THE
PARLIAMENTARY DEBATES
(OFFICIAL REPORT)
IN THE
SEVENTH SESSION OF THE FIFTH PARLIAMENT
OF NORTHERN IRELAND.
APPOINTED TO MEET AT STORMONT ON THE FIRST DAY OF FEBRUARY,
IN THE EIGHTH YEAR OF THE REIGN OF
HIS
MAJESTY KING
GEORGE VI.
VOLUME XXVII.
THE SENATE.

---

Tuesday, 1st February, 1944.
The Seventh Session of the Fifth Parliament of Northern Ireland appointed to
meet on 1st February, 1944, in the
Eighth year of the Reign of King George
VI., was opened by the Governor (His
Grace the Duke of Abercorn, K.G.,
K.P.) in person.
The House met at Five Minutes before
Twelve of the Clock noon, Mr. SPEAKER
(the Right Hon. Viscount Bangor,
O.B.E., D.L.) in the Chair.
KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor, being seated
on the Throne, commanded the acting
Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod
(Brigadier-General H. G. Young, C.I.E.,
D.S.O.) to let the Commons know "It
is His Grace's pleasure that they attend
him immediately in this House."
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, his Grace delivered the
following speech from the Throne: -
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
Since I last addressed you the Forces
of the United Nations have been
enabled, by God's Providence, to assume
the offensive in all theatres of war with
results which justify high hopes for the
future. Northern Ireland, as part of
the United Kingdom, is willingly and
proudly contributing to the mighty
effort of the Allies and I feel confident
that our people, in company with ull
other freedom-loving peoples, will
maintain their whole-hearted efforts to
bring about that complete and final
victory which we are resolved to
achieve.
The happiest relationship continues
to exist between my Government and
the various arms of the Allied Forces
in Northern Ireland, and I have noted
with deep satisfaction the many manifestations of good feeling between the
members of those Forces and the civil
population.
Two years ago my Government
extended cordial greetings to the
American Troops on their landing in
Northern Ireland These were the
vanguard of mighty Forces which have
since crossed the ocean to take their
share in the battles against the enemy
on this side of the Atlantic. Many of
them are now in our midst, and we
welcome them as the representatives of
a great Ally whose friendly co-operation and goodwill have been abundantly
shown in many branches of our
common war effort. I trust that in
the years to come our comrades-inarms from the United States will
treasure the memories of their stay in
Northern Ireland as an abiding proof
of the respect and friendship of our
people for their great Nation.
I rejoice to learn of the gallant
exploits of Northern Ireland units on
the fighting fronts and have noted
A
King's Speech
King's Speech
THE SENATE

with pride that many volunteers from
this part of the United Kingdom,
serving in all branches of the Forces
and in the Merchant Navy, have been
awarded decorations for bravery and
devotion to duty. The demand for
more recruits remains urgent, and it
is my earnest hope that our young men
and women will answer the call in
ever-increasing numbers.
The Ulster Home Guard, under the
guidance and direction of the military
authority, has become a well armed
and well trained Force. I am confident that its members can be relied
on to meet with efficiency and devotion
the calls that may be made on them.
The Civil Defence Services are being
maintained in constant readiness to
deal with attacks which the enemy may
launch against us. I am glad to
know that the risk of loss of life and
property has been reduced by greatly
improved fire-fighting facilities.
Our farmers have made a splendid
response to the appeal for increased
food production during the past year.
It is due to their efforts, supplemented
by the help of unemployed industrial
workers and volunteers from the towns,
that such a large crop has been safely
gathered in, notwithstanding the unfavourable weather during the harvesting season.
my
Although the area under the plough
is not to be increased during the
coming season,
Government confidently hopes that the more extensive
use of artificial fertilisers will result in
greater food production for which there
is still a pressing need. A greater
acreage of flax is essential to meet the
increasing demands of our
factories; and I have no doubt that
our farmers will make an even larger
contribution in the present year.
war
I am gratified at the continued progress made towards the maximum
mobilisation of the industrial resources
of Northern Ireland for the prosecution of the war, resulting in a
substantial increase in the production
of ships, aircraft, munitions and
textiles. My Government is glad to
welcome United Kingdom Ministers
who come to Northern Ireland to
obtain personal knowledge of our
problems and I am confident that these
visits have contributed greatly to the
smooth co-ordination of our efforts.
I observe with satisfaction that
increased interest is being taken by the
managements of factories in problems
of industrial welfare, the solution of
which will be an important contribution to increased production and more
harmonious relationships between
workers and employers. The substantial reduction in the number of persons
unemployed is welcome evidence of the
increasing absorption of available
labour in war industries.
Legislation will be laid before you
to provide additional financial assistance for the alleviation of the difficulties
which local authorities have experienced, through the operation of
derating legislation, in carrying out
necessary works and supplying certain
services; to authorise the payment of
housing grants in respect of houses
erected during the war; and to enable
vacant premises to be requisitioned in
order to provide homes for persons inadequately housed.
The Disabled Persons (Employment)
Bill now before the Parliament at
Westminster empowers the Parliament
of Northern Ireland to pass complementary legislation to enable persons
handicapped by disablement to secure
employment, and the necessary Measure
will be submitted to
appropriate time.
you at
the
Other Bills will be introduced regarding the following matters:-
Belfast County Borough Council,
Local Government Officers,
Shops,
Juvenile Delinquency,
Rent Restriction (Defective
Tenancies),
Criminal Law and Procedure,
County Courts,
Electoral Registration,
The Report of the Select Committee
on Ministerial Salaries and Members'
Allowances has been laid before the
House of Commons and will come forward for consideration at an early
date. At the request of my Government Sir Francis Floud recently
examined the scope of the duties of the
Civil Departments. A redistribution of
the functions of Ministries is deemed
desirable and legislation will be pro-
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
1 FEBRUARY 1944
moted embodying the proposals of the
Government.
Members of the House of Commons.
The Estimates for the Public Services
will be laid before you in due course.
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
The transition from war to peace
conditions will create intricate problems
affecting the entire community. I am
therefore gratified to learn that my
Government continues to devote unremitting attention to the framing of
post-war policy. On a number of the
wider issues involved in the formulation
of plans for the future my Ministers are
maintaining the closest contact with the
Imperial Government. Every endeavour is, therefore, being made to prepare for the reconstruction period and
to ensure the development and
expansion of our industries within the
economic structure of the United
Kingdom.
Some post-war questions will, however, fall within the jurisdiction of the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, and I
have learned with satisfaction that
such
many
matters have already
received detailed attention from my
Ministers. It has been found possible
to make a start in dealing with the
housing problem, and I am confident
that, so far as the supply of building
material permits, further progress will
be made in this direction. The Report
of the Select Committee on Health
Services has now been issued and will be
of value in the moulding of future
policy in matters relating to the physical
well-being of our people.
The development of Social Insurance
and other Social Services is closely
engaging the attention of the Imperial
Government. Any action taken as a
result of those deliberations will have
its counterpart in Northern Ireland in
pursuance of the frequently expressed
policy of my Government to maintain
equal standards with Great Britain in
these matters.
Also prominent amongst the matters
which are receiving consideration is the
position of the linen industry, into
which an expert Committee is conducting an investigation.
The education system of Northern
Ireland is under comprehensive review
by my Ministers, whose earnest resolve
is to bring about a marked advance in
the provision of educational facilities.
A White Paper dealing with this most
important matter is being prepared and
will be laid before you in due course.
I rejoice to learn that law and order
have been well maintained and I desire
to commend the skill, efficiency and
high sense of duty displayed by the
Royal Ulster Constabulary and the
Special Constabulary under difficult
war conditions.
I pray that the blessing of Almighty
God may rest upon your deliberations.
Then his Grace was pleased to retire,
and the Commons withdrew.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.
Paper Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Labour:
National Health Insurance (Arrears)
Amendment Regulations, 1943.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act) and ordered to lie on the Table.
Address in Reply to His Majesty's
Most Gracious Speech.
Mr. SPEAKER having read
Kings Speech,
the
Sir ROLAND NUGENT (in morning
dress): I beg to move, That an humble
Address be presented to His Grace in the
following words:
Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's dutiful and loyal
subjects, the Senate of the Parliament
of Northern Ireland, beg leave to offer
our humble thanks to your Grace for
the speech which your Grace has addressed to both Houses of Parliament.
I feel deeply honoured by having been
invited to move this Address, and very
grateful to the Prime Minister and to the
Leader of the House for having asked me
to do so. I think I am more fortunate
than my recent predecessors in this task
in that for the first time for some years
we are enabled to look to the future with
some modest degree of confidence. This
atmosphere of hope gives a special
significance to the passage in the Gracious
Speech which referred to the problem of
reconstruction, and I think it would not
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
be perhaps inappropriate for the Mover
of this Address to invite the attention of
the House for a brief time not, as before,
to the problems of war, but to the
problems of peace. I am personally
reasonably optimistic as to the immediate
post-war period in Ulster, or perhaps I
should say not optimistic but rather less.
pessimistic than I am about the subsequent stages.
It seems to me there must be a tremendous world shortage of goods piled up
during five years of war. That shortage
of goods means, whatever the difficulties
in trade, a very active demand for our
products, and great though the difficulties
of transitional reorganisation may be, a
full order book helps one to get over a
great number of difficulties. I cannot see
that the great problems of mobilisation
for peace should be more serious than
mobilisation for war, and if that is so
hard work, good-will, and a little
patience will get over it. It is when we
turn to the second stage of reconstruction,
when that world hunger for goods has
⚫ been appeased, that my mind is filled with
apprehension.
We all know that our objective is a
better standard of life for all our people,
and we have got to obtain that better
standard in a world shattered and disorganised by war, and impoverished
beyond our conception. It will always be
difficult to raise rapidly the standard of
living of the people. Theoretically it is
possible when your country is selfsupporting in raw materials and food. I
say theoretically because I believe in
actual practice, except perhaps in the
United States, it has never been done.
Theoretically you can so expand your internal structure by a sort of qualified
process of taking in each other's washing
that everybody reaches a better standard.
Unlike those happy countries, Russia and
the United States, we have not vast
resources of food and raw materials waiting to be exploited. We have got to
import, and that to my mind is the difficulty of the United Kingdom generally
and Ulster.
We cannot put up our standard of
living without importing more, but how
are we going to pay for those imports?
Before the war we had considerable
foreign investments, but those investments have gone. We had considerable
known as
other sources of what are
invisible exports-profits of banks,
shipping, insurance, and so forth. Those
in the post-war world will be smaller.
The most optimistic estimate which I have
seen is that we will have to increase our
exports of manufactured goods by
approximately 40 per cent. to get the
pre-war standard, and if we are going
to raise the standard of living we will
have to import more, and consequently
export more manufactured goods. Any
manufacturer or merchant will realise
that to put up one's foreign sales by 50
per cent. or 60 per cent. is a very formidable task, and very definitely a mansized job. It seems to me that we are up
against the age-old problem of how to
make the goods our customers want at
the price our customers can afford to pay,
and the answer to that, of course, is by
the highest possible level of industrial
and commercial efficiency.
I had better relieve the anxiety of the
House at once by saying that I am not
going to take them through the hundreds
of problems which are embraced in
the
term "technical and commercial
efficiency," but I would like to make two
points, partly because I think they are
long-term problems, and if we are going
to tackle them we should tackle them
scon, and partly for another reason which
I will give later on in my speech. My
first point is the question of technical and
scientific research. It is, I think, very
generally admitted that in the early days
Great Britain led the world in all new
industrial developments. It is, unfortunately, equally agreed that in recent
years Great Britain lost that lead, and
she lost it largely through a certain
neglect of research. It is not that British
scientific and technical thought is any
less original than it was. The developments in the war show very clearly that
as far as technical thought is concerned
we still lead the world, but it has been
the failure not of quality but of quantity.
I have seen it estimated that in the
United Kingdom as a whole we were
spending only one-twentieth of the
money devoted to applied research in the
United States, and I believe that in
recent years our volume of applied research is very far below that of Russia.
When you think that every new product,
every new process, every new material,
and every new method in industry must
be derived originally from research you
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
1 FEBRUARY 1944
These
can see its immense importance.
things must come from research, for
there is nowhere else they can come from.
When you are faced with the task I have
outlined I think you will agree that it is
of immense importance. You may perhaps have noticed that every single one
of the Bank chairman of the Big Five
referred to research
as an important
matter, and bank chairmen have a way
of being right about
these things.
Research is not a thing which you can have
for the money in your hand. You cannot go and ask for one hundred pounds'
worth of research over the counter. It
is a matter requiring the most careful
picking of your team, the most careful
organisation of that team's work, and the
fitting of it into the framework suited to
your industry and to your community.
re-
In our case, in this country we have
got the problem of linking our
search with research in England
other
and
countries, which is a
most complicated and difficult thing. It
is not, thank goodness, that we in the
North of Ireland have lagged behind the
United Kingdom in research, but rather
the contrary. We have an excellent
nucleus, but it is to my mind most
imperative to expand that nucleus to a
far bigger structure. You have got to
think of research as very nearly the most
important thing in connection with industry in the future. We have got to
expand and develop that nucleus.
Examination must obviously be made
to determine how that is to be done, and
what part, if any, the Government are to
take in doing it. I think the examination could only be conducted in the first
instance by some central and impartial
body like a Commission or Committee
appointed by the Government. I think
that in the investigations the impetus
must come from the Government. I believe it would take a Commission at least
a year's hard work to produce a plan.
When that plan is produced, then you
have got to begin applying it to your
organisation, and that may take many a
year of additional work and experiments.
Research organisations are not found
every day. That is my first point, which
I consider of very great importance, not
merely from the material side, but also
from the psychological. The existence of
an active centre of research under the
right man, or rather not so much a
centre, for that implies a small body, but
an organisation for research working in
the closest possible contact with industry,
could be a most tremendous intellectual
stimulus.
It could permeate the whole
of your industrial structure so that the
whole of your industry is full of the right
spirit of enterprise and eager to reach
forward in technique, in equipment, and
in method. Such a mental stimulus
would be of immense value to any community.
Talking of psychological stimuli brings
me to my second point, which is education. This seems to me to be a long term
problem. I am sure we were glad to
learn from a recent speech by the Leader
of the House that he is well ahead with
his plans for the improvement of education. I do not propose to run through
all the interesting things of which, no
doubt, he is thinking, but I do want to
make one point, because I think it has
sometimes slipped into the background.
I am not sure it has not done so even in
the very interesting development which is
taking place in England. I refer to the
enormous importance of your teaching
staff. I think sometimes that the
Ministry of Education tends to get a little
overwhelmed by the material side of the
problems, the bricks and mortar side of
the job. They lose sight of the fact that
their teaching staff is the absolute backbone of the educational system. It is
quite possible to have a first-class education given in a broken-down barn by a
good teacher. If you have not a good
teacher, all your palatial schools, free
milk, dental clinics, and so forth, will be
of no value whatever. We are lucky in a
very excellent body of teachers, a competent, devoted body of men and women,
but are there enough of them? Not by
50 per cent. as we all must know. Are
we giving them every chance we can to do
their work as well as they possibly can?
I do not think we are.
Remuneration is one point. While I
am sure no teacher expects, or would demand, riches, to a man or woman doing a
teacher's work, it seems to me essential
that their remuneration should be sufficient to enable them to live, and if necessary bring up a family in modest comfort.
No man or woman can do intellectual
work of any value if worried over their
financial affairs. I think the remuneration should be looked into. I do not think
it is very ungenerous at the moment, but
the thing could be looked into. Better to
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
err on the generous than on the
ungenerous side.
If the teacher puts all he or she has
got into it and nothing else is worth
while it is a very severe mental and
spiritual strain. The teacher's constant
danger, as everyone who has taught
children or trained troops knows, is
staleness, becoming a mere teaching
machine. That is a thing very difficult
to avoid, and very specially is that so of
the teacher in the country districts, who,
of necessity, gets very little opportunity
of brushing up his brains against his
intellectual equals. I think very careful
thought should be given to this question
of teacher's staleness.
our
I think one thing which would be of
immense advantage, if it were possible,
would be if there could be a large scale
system of exchange between our teachers
and teachers, say, in the Dominions and,
if possible, in the U.S.A. As well as
being of immense advantage to
teachers, I think it would be of considerable advantage to our children. I think
a new teacher for six months or a year
would liven them up, and I am quite sure
if the teachers were Australians, and their
spirit was anything like the spirit of the
Australian infantry, it would waken the
children up quite a lot. I think that is
worth very serious consideration, but
obviously it would not be possible to
arrange for teachers to go abroad very
frequently in their lives. The most we
could hope to do would be to send them
once, for say one year, and I think very
careful consideration should be given therefore to the question of teachers' holidays.
I believe a good deal could be done in
giving them a change of mental atmosphere. It may need a little financial
assistance, and I am sure a good deal of
arrangement. If they were allowed to
visit Italy and the art collections there,
for instance, I cannot imagine anything
more refreshing or more likely to do them
good.
These are only casual suggestions, and
perhaps I have said enough to show what
I am driving at. Your teachers are the
very marrow and bone of your education.
You must see that you have the best, and
you must give them the chance of giving
of their best. It is far more important
than anything else. After all, it is the
man behind the gun, not the gun, that
matters. I have dealt with these two
points partly, as I said, because they are
long-term problems, problems which must
be tackled early if they are to be tackled
at all, and also because of the peculiar
and particular structure and nature of our
Province and its industry.
on
We forget sometimes that our industries were not based, as those of
most other countries were, on great
natural resources like coal, iron, oil,
and forest. We had nothing of that
kind. Our industries were based
an intelligent and adaptable population,
and the leadership of a few very remarkable men. Other countries lavish
money on developing their natural resources like water, mines and forests.
Ought we not to spend money developing
cur natural
resources-the men and
women of Ulster ? If we do, I feel sure
we shall be repaid many times over. If
we give them a chance and start them
with a good education, help them by
research and experiment, give them the
best methods, the best tools and the best
equipment, I feel sure that we can trust
them to do good work.
Mr. BROWNE (in morning dress):
The duty of seconding the Address in
reply to the King's Speech has been entrusted to me. That is
a very high
honour, conferred on me by the Prime
Minister and Cabinet, and it is one which
I greatly appreciate. It is no easy task
to follow a cultured and experienced
gentleman like the hon. and gallant
Senator who has just spoken, and who has
so ably moved the Address.
It is scarcely necessary to say that we
in Northern Ireland are passionately devoted to His Majesty the King. In no
part of the United Kingdom has His
Majesty more loyal subjects. That fact
has been demonstrated
on numerous
occasions, and I am certain that in the
days to come, those feelings of loyalty
will not become less ardent but rather
strengthened. Perhaps it may not be out
of place to say that we are proud and
thankful that the head of a great Ulster
family continues to be the Sovereign's
representative in Northern Ireland. The
Duke of Abercorn has held that exalted
position since the establishment of our
Parliament, and we trust that both His
Grace the Governor and the Duchess will
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
1 FEBRUARY 1944
be long spared to the people of this
Province, in whose welfare they take such
a deep interest. A good many things are
referred to in the Gracious Speech, and,
judging by the amount of legislation foreshadowed, the Session promises to be
fairly busy.
are
of
The first reference is naturally to the
dreadful war in which we are engaged.
The outlook for 1944 is bright in comparison with previous years.
The road
to victory will no doubt be hard,
hazardous, and dangerous, but we
assured by General Montgomery,
Eighth Army fame, that the end of the
war is definitely in sight. Let us hope
that the present year will see the crushing
defeat of Hitler and his gangsters. At
the end of the last Great War we failed
to make the most of our opportunities.
Those opportunities were great, with the
German Army battered to its knees and
with the leaders hastening for shelter to
neutral countries. But those opportunities
were not grasped. Such a mistake should
not be made this time. There should be
no weak or temporising policy.
We are proud of the part which has
been played and is being played by
Ulster in the conflict. Without conscription our young men have joined His
Majesty's Forces in their thousands, and
on land, sea and in the air, they have
displayed courage, determination and
skill, and have gained many distinctions.
Alas, many of them have paid the
supreme sacrifice, and our heartfelt sympathy is extended to those whose homes
have been darkened by heavy bereavement. Especially does our sympathy go
out to the Prime Minister and to those
Members of the Senate who have lost
their very gallant sons. Our women too
have been no less keen and eager to serve
their King and Country, and in the
Services open to them, they have shown
their worth and value.
On the industrial front the workers of
Ulster, both men and women, have come
up to expectations indeed have far surpassed them, and have supplied the
weapons and munitions so urgently needed
by those in the fighting ranks. Nor must
we forget the farmers who have played
such an important and vital part on
the food front. Last year's production reached a record high level,
and it is hoped and expected that
this year's
year's effort and achievement
will exceed all previous ones. We should
all be glad that the Ulster Home Guard
and the Civil Defence Services are in an
efficient state and ready to meet any
enemy attack, because we cannot fail to
recognise that our area is not entirely
free from danger. That has been proved
by the fact that only last December
German aircraft were able to fly over
Southern Ireland and drop spies by
parachute.
our
We in Ulster are particularly delighted
to have in our midst the American troops.
They are
comrades-in-arms.
We
welcomed them to our soil two years ago,
and we are proud to have them still with
us. To quote a sentence from the King's
Speech, we trust that in the years to come
they will-
"treasure the memories of their stay in
Northern Ireland as an abiding proof
of the respect and friendship of our
people for their great nation."
Distinguished visitors from various
parts of the Empire, the U.S.A. and
Europe have spoken in praiseworthy terms
of Ulster's war effort. Here is Mr.
Winston Churchill's tribute in a letter
which he wrote to Mr. J. M. Andrews in
May, 1943:
That was a dark and dangerous hour.
He was referring to December, 1940.
We were alone and had to fight singlehanded with the full fury of the
German attack raining down death
and destruction on our cities and,
still more deadly, seeking to strangle
our life by cutting off the entry to our
ports of the ships which brought us our
food and the weapons we so sorely
needed.
Only one great channel of entry remained open.
That channel remained
open because loyal Ulster gave us the
full use of the Northern Irish ports and
waters and thus ensured the free working of the Clyde and the Mersey.
But for the loyalty of Northern
Ireland and its devotion to what has
now become the cause of 30 Governments or nations we should have been
confronted with slavery and death, and
the light which now shines so strongly
throughout the world would have been
quenched.
What a striking testimony to the people
of Northern Ireland from the greatest
war Prime Minister of all time. Not
only must we achieve victory on the
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Mr. Browne]
our
battlefield, we must also establish peace.
Win the war, crush the
aggressor nations,
free the world from oppression and
totalitarian terror-such must be
foremost aim and nothing should distract
our attention from that object. But we
must take care that we do not lose the
peace. The close of hostilities will leave
us with a jaded, impoverished and shattered world, and we must prepare for
reconstruction. Senator Sir Roland
Nugent has referred to this most important subject and he is well qualified to do
so from the industrial and economic point
of view. We cannot stress that matter too
much. Preparatory work must begin
now if we wish to avoid a perhaps irreparable catastrophe. In so far as
define our post-war conditions and in so
far as we state clearly what we intend to
do with our victory, we help to obtain
that victory.
we
It is only natural that anxiety should
weigh heavily on the Prime Minister and
the other members of the Government at
Stormont, and on those who think of the
disturbances likely to attend the transition from war-time to peace-time
economy. There is the fear of a new and
widespread crisis of unemployment. The
memory of the last war is still too vivid
with many of us to prevent us from wondering what will happen after the present
conflict. It would be extremely dangerous
to promise work, better wages, better
housing, better food, longer and more
enjoyable holidays, without studying and
preparing now and being in a position to
apply those measures which alone will
fulfil our desires and prevent disappointment.
our
The end of the war should see us ready
for the reconstruction of our devastated
areas, the restoration of economic activity,
the changing over of industry to the
needs of peace, the reopening of trade
outlets, and the resettlement of
workers and their families. Those in the
fighting and other services who have saved
us from annihilation deserve first consideration. I notice that across the water
measures are being taken to reinstate ex-
Servicemen in their jobs. Easy it will
not be, but I feel sure we can depend on
local employers to be considerate, helpful
and resourceful, and to provide as much
employment as possible.
We cannot but admire the energy and
perseverance of our Prime Minister in
connection with all these matters. He
realises the difficulties that lie ahead, and
is doing a splendid stroke of work. It
will not be his fault if Ulster lags behind,
if our old industries are not revived and
new industries established. A new brand
of courage is required to face the reconstruction of the old life, on a fresh basis.
We are gratified that much anticipatory
work on post-war problems has already
been done by the Planning Advisory
Board which was formed in 1942, and its
recommendations cannot fail to be of considerable assistance to our Government.
In the past, sufficient attention was not
paid to the planning of our cities and
towns. Unregulated and ill-considered
development has left us with a legacy of
congestion, over-crowding, and insanitary
conditions, with their inevitable consequences of ill-health. A rare opportunity
is now afforded of reviewing many things
which relate to sound urban life. Rural
conditions are not all that could be desired
and it should be our aim to encourage
people to remain in the country and not
migrate to the cities and towns.
Of
We should go even further and get our
school teachers to encourage young people
to take up agricultural careers.
course, they should be assured of a decent
living, with amenities equal to those of
the town. Without sound, healthy farmsteads where will our country drift? We
all know that farming is the backbone of
our Province and that the agricultural
labourer is the backbone of farming. The
supply of water and the provision of
sewerage facilities are essential to healthy
living. Yet I understand there are towns
in Ulster with a population of over 1,000
which have no piped-water supply; many
more such towns are without adequate
sewerage facilities, and of 109 communities with a population of from 250 to
1,000 the great majority have neither a
piped-water supply nor sewerage facilities.
If we are to have a proper standard
from the point of view of public health,
vigorous steps will have to be taken to
increase and improve those essential public
services in the Province. Whether or not
the Planning Advisory Board has made
recommendations on these matters I am
not able to say, but something should be
done. Of course, I recognise that some of
our sanitary authorities may of themselves
be too small to deal with these projects,
but what objection can there be to large,

co-ordinated schemes. Much greater coordination on the part of the responsible

This
We have all received copies of the report of the Select Committee which was
set up in 1942 to inquire into the Health
Services of Northern Ireland. The Committee was given an
onerous and responsible task, and its members, especially
the Chairman (Mr. Howard Stevenson)
must be congratulated on the care and
thoroughness with which they have
carried out their duties. They had no
fewer than 82 sittings. The report, as
might have been expected, is a comprehensive and valuable one and the Blue
Book, containing the recommendations
and transcript of evidence, runs to some
800 pages a very bulky volume. I do
not think any more important document
has been issued since our Parliament was
established in 1921, and it has aroused
widespread interest and attention.
is not the time to criticise or review the
recommendations, which are of a farreaching character. They will require
careful and detailed examination on the
part of the Government. There should
be no hasty decision. I might say, however, there is a strong feeling that
Ministry of Health is required. Under
the existing system our health services
are spread over far too many authorities.
There is little or no co-ordination and a
central controlling authority with power
to carry out schemes, deemed necessary
and desirable, would be welcomed as
tending towards reform, improvement and
efficiency. That a radical change is
needed in many of the services goes without saying. However, there will doubtless be opportunities for debating all
these matters later on.
a
Nobody, I fancy, will attempt to
minimise the seriousness of the housing
shortage, especially in Belfast. During
the war it is not possible to embark on
large housing schemes, but it is satisfactory to know that in the meantime
we are to have 750 houses erected in the
city. Good health depends very largely
on good housing, and it is up to us to
see that the people are properly housed.
It is also gratifying to know that the
Government intends to introduce legislation to enable vacant premises to be requisitioned in order to provide homes for
persons inadequately housed. My observavations apply not only to the cities and
towns but also to the rural districts. We
have been told by an hon., learned,
and gallant Senator that young farmers
are not in a position to get married owing
to the want of housing accommodation.
This is a drawback that should be
remedied. We are promised legislation
dealing with the Belfast County Borough
Council. I presume that refers to the
Bill which is intended to restore certain'
powers to the Council, and to confer
certain functions on the Town Clerk and
Town Solicitor of Belfast. Whether this
measure will have an easy passage it is
impossible at present to say, but it has
the merit of being, more or less, an agreed
Bill between the Government and the.
Belfast Corporation. If it tends to wipe.
out the friction that has existed for so
long, to restore harmonious relations, and
to place the Municipality on a higher
the
level,
Bill should be cordially
welcomed.
Members have no doubt noted with
pleasure that the Disabled Persons
(Employment) Bill now before Parliamentat Westminster empowers our
Government to pass complementary legislation to enable persons handicapped by
disablement to secure employment. We
are also told that the development of
social insurance and other social services
is engaging attention, and that any action
taken at Westminster on these subjects
will have its counterpart in Northern
Ireland in accordance with the step-bystep policy. That is satisfactory.
It is gratifying to know that the
thoughts of the Minister of Education and
his Ministry are directed towards reform
and improvement in our educational
system. We are told that a White Paper
is being prepared on the subject, and we
shall await its appearance with interest.
Many of us would like to see the basis of
the scholarship schemes widened and
strengthened, the system of technical
education enlarged, physical education
given its rightful place, the number of
nursery schools increased, and the schoolleaving age raised. We cannot expect our
Government to copy too closely the proposals in Great Britain. Whatever
scheme is adopted in Northern Ireland
should suit our own needs and requirements, and I fancy even that will mean
complications of
an administrative,
educational, and financial character.

The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): In accordance
with long standing convention and obvious
duty, I would like, first of all, to say how
interested I was, as I am sure all other
hon. Members were, in the speeches we
have just heard. From the hon.. and
gallant Member behind me we had a
most illuminating and realistic glance
into the future. He stressed the lines of
procedure which will be not only important, but vitally necessary if we are to
enter into that inheritance of well-being
which many of us are promising ourselves
and our people. He referred to scientific
research and to education, one of them
dealing with the material side of life, and
the other with the mental and moral side
of life, and I am confident that neither
of them will be neglected by those who
hold responsible positions in the North of
Ireland.
From the hon. Senator, who seconded
the Motion for the Address, we had a
very comprehensive survey of the actual
topics which are in the minds of the
people to-day. He referred to them with
great soundness of judgment; indeed, he
did that part of his task so thoroughly
that I am afraid he has stolen a great
deal of my thunder, if indeed he has not
left me with almost nothing to say at all.
In concentrating my attention upon the
Speech, as I have had to do on several
occasions within the last few days, it
seemed to me that the duties and responsibilities of government in the exceptional
circumstances in which we find ourselves
to-day, are obviously three in number.
First of all it is the duty of the Government to do everything possible to assist
in the successful prosecution of the war
to a victorious consummation. A further
obvious duty is to carry on the normal
activities of government, to maintain
peace and order in the community, and
to relieve obvious and outstanding cases
of hardship, which are the ordinary
duties of our Government in peace time,
and to do all these things to-day without
distracting attention or side-tracking the
strength and effort and energy of the
people from the first great task of saving
our civilisation from destruction.
Then it seemed to me that there was a
third duty, and that that had not been
neglected either, namely, the duty of
taking a glance forward and thinking of
the future in many directions along the
same lines as has been suggested by the
Mover of the Motion. I think you will
gather from the Gracious Speech that in
all these ways the Government has not
been remiss, and the Government has been
ably backed up by the good-will, the toil,
and the united effort of the entire people
of Northern Ireland. I do not want to
develop these various responsibilities at
any great length. They are referred to
in the Speech dealing with the war effort.
As Senator Browne has indicated, we
have received our American comrades in
arms-I will not say with open arms, but
with a great gladness of heart. We have,
metaphorically speaking, thrown our hats
in the air and have said, "Here are true
tokens of friendship, and here are indications of final and complete victory over
these aggressors, over these international
gangsters. If these visitors amongst us
sometimes think we are a little bit formal,
as obviously we had to be to-day, I would
assure them that every time we pass one
of them in the street our heart leaps
afresh with a new welcome and a new joy.
The Speech also indicates that our
people have given great and material
assistance to the war effort in such things
as Home Guard and Civil Defence duties,
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
1 FEBRUARY 1944
and appreciative references are made to
the work of our farmers, who have done
famous things really in providing the
foodstuffs, not only for ourselves, but for
the people of Great Britain in these days
of anxiety and danger. Allied with them
are
our industrial leaders and manufacturers and workers, who have at very
short notice turned their machinery to the
tasks of war, and are providing those
munitions of warfare without which the
future for us would be very dark indeed.
So much then for the contribution which
our Government is making to the war
effort, and which it promises to continue
to make during the coming year.
as
I
The second function of Government is
to carry on the normal duties of those in
authority with as little distraction
possible from this first great task.
think we can congratulate ourselves that
we have had, since the war began, a
strong and resolute Government so far as
peace and order are concerned. Any misguided elements in the community have
been kept in control, so that the minds
of our people have not been disturbed or
made unduly anxious in that connection.
The Government promises a number of
pieces of fresh legislation which also form
part of the ordinary responsibilities of
government. I need not go into these
matters in detail.
For example, there is a Bill to enable
the authorities to requisition unoccupied
premises at this period, when the housing
shortage is very extreme. That is being
done for the purpose of preventing
owners from behaving in a somewhat dogin-the-manger fashion, and holding property empty when other people are in
desperate need of it. There are also a
number of small Measures, none of which
will, I think, create any great controversy. For example, local authorities
who should find themselves in some difficulty owing to the operation of derating
will be helped by legislation which will
be introduced in order to make their task
- possible in the light of those derating
operations.
Then a Bill will be introduced dealing
with the Belfast City Council to, so to
speak, complete the work of reconstruction which was begun some time ago. We
have in our Corporation many men of
the type of our Lord Mayor, who
ordinarily sits with us here, who have
done great service for the city, and indeed
for Northern Ireland. We hope that this
reorganisation and reconstitution of a
somewhat modified local authority will
give to such men even greater power in
the days that are to come. Then there is
a Pharmacy and Medicines Bill, the purpose of which is to prevent the advertising
of quack medicines which are obviously
of no value, but by which thoughtless and
improvident people are sometimes
befooled and led to waste their money
with, perhaps, injury to their health.
Those are a few of the outstanding and
largely non-contentious pieces of legislation which the Government feel that, even
in these strenuous days, they are justified
in bringing forward.
The third duty is to look to the future,
and in this connection the Government
have felt it necessary to do certain things.
They are introducing a Bill to make it
easier for wounded ex-Servicemen to find
employment when they return from the
wars. There are some things related to
the future that we can do immediately,
and that is one of them. We can prepare
for these men a more optimistic homecoming than they had after the last great
war.
I
recon-
Then there is a Bill to provide for electoral registration. That is a very complicated matter I need not go into now.
Its necessity is pretty obvious. Across
the water the Imperial Parliament is
adopting a similar measure, so that we
shall have a Parliament that in the coming
days will not only represent the people,
but will be felt to do so. There are other
spheres where preparations must in the
meantime be somewhat tentative.
industrial
refer particularly to
struction, which has been so ably dealt
with by the hon. and gallant Senator who
moved the Address. When I came into
this Chamber to-day there was put into
my hand a work dealing with this very
subject, produced by a very able committee in England, which I have no doubt
will interest all of you. That is perhaps
the biggest and most difficult work this
country will have to set its mind to after
the war if we are not to be cursed by a
repetition of mass unemployment such as
we had after the last war.
Housing is also to receive consideration,
and the health services will be reviewed in
the light of the valuable Report of the
Select Committee. There will also be new
legislation on education. I do not wish to
Address in Reply
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
develop this subject to-day, as I shall
have ample opportunity of doing so at a
later date. I may say that the Bill introduced in England, while it will not in all
details serve our purposes in Northern
Ireland, may, perhaps to a very large
extent, indicate the lines on which we
might well amend our educational system.
We might give our children from 11 to 15
a transformed and wider education, and
we might give boys and girls a part-time.
education, one day in the week, which
will be of tremendous value to them, from
15 to 18. We might also deal with mental
defectives in a way we have power to do,
but have never yet done to any great'
extent. I am told there are about 9,000
of these educable unfortunates in our
midst, many of whom would become useful
members of the community if a little
extra trouble were taken with them, and
a little more money spent on them, but
who, left to themselves, would become a
serious social and moral liability to our
people.
I am sorry we have been so tardy in
Northern Ireland in facing up to our
responsibilities in regard to this question.
We have, I suppose, 2,000 or 3,000 of
these people in Belfast-the complete
number has not been ascertained as yet
-and how many are we helping by special
means? I would almost be ashamed to
give you the number, but I am hoping
for better things to come, when we will
give special education to these unfortunate and feeble folk, who will never be
anything more than children in many
respects, but who could be made useful
citizens if, as I say, a little trouble is
taken and a little money expended upon
them.
Many of these things may seem to some
thoughtless people Utopian, but I am
not ashamed of that word. H. G. Wells
has said that the world makes progress
by building Utopias. It is a good thing
to see visions of better things, even if we
may not reach them in their completeness. This subject is like a clarion call
with a sincere ring to our people to gird
themselves for the final ordeal in this
great war, and for the building of a
better future for all our people in Northern Ireland. I commend the Gracious
Speech to the House, and I want to express my appreciation of the very interesting and stimulating speeches we
have had from the Mover and Seconder
of the Motion for the Address.

Thursday, 3rd February, 1944.
The Senate met at half after Two of the

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
The Rents Tribunals.
The following Question stood on the
Order Paper in
in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state what representation
(if any) Roman Catholics in Northern
Ireland have on the Rents Tribunal.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: This is a question with regard to the constitution of
the rents tribunals. I notice that there
are ten resident magistrates, and one of
them, of course, and one only, is a
resident magistrate from our side of the
House. But, on the rents tribunals, I
find there may be one Catholic on the
whole panel of 60, not including the ten
chairmen, but beyond that I fail to trace
any others. I do say that where the rents
tribunals are concerned, it is surely not
asking too much that our Roman Catholics
in the Six Counties should at least have
the representation to which they are
entitled on these tribunals. I do not
wish to be long, but it looks to me that
the Government have definitely decided to
boycott Catholics by not giving them fair
representation on the rents tribunals and
in other cases.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Rev.
Professor Corkey): In reply to the question put by the hon. Senator, the Excessive
Rents Tribunals, which operate under the
Excessive Rents (Prevention) Act, 1941,
are constituted by the Minister of Home
Affairs as and when required. Each
Tribunal consists of three members; a
resident magistrate and two other lay
members.
When the Excessive Rents (Prevention)
Bill was passing through Parliament,
Members all Members, including Protestant and Catholic-were invited to
submit to the Minister of Home Affairs
names of suitable persons to serve on the
Excessive Rents Tribunals. Three Members of the Opposition responded to this
invitation to submit names, and I understand that those whom the hon. Senator
represents made no effort to provide
names as they were asked to do.
Subsequently, on the 31st March, 1942,
in reply to a question in another place,
the Minister of Home Affairs took the
opportunity of thanking those Members
who had submitted names of persons and
of making a further request to hon.
Members to submit
names during the
coming Recess. No replies were received
to this second invitation.
The
members appointed by the
Governor were not chosen on religious
grounds and I have no information as
to the particular religious beliefs of the
individual members.
It may
be necessary to constitute
further tribunals in the future and the
Minister of Home Affairs will be happy
to receive from hon. Senators the names
of persons suitable for appointment as
members of tribunals.

THE KING'S SPEECH.

words:

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
should first like to congratulate the Mover
and Seconder of this Resolution. Both
of them made speeches of distinction, Sir
Roland Nugent by evading the direct
issues presented by the Gracious Speech,
and Mr. Browne by recapitulating all the
policies which from time to time various
Members of this House have advocated.
In presenting his own policy Sir Roland
Nugent suggested that we should have
something in the nature of a general staff
for planning in industry. He seemed to
want a general staff for industry, and if
I am not mistaken his suggestion received a great deal of approval from
Members of the House who felt that his
idea should be adopted so far as might
be possible, and although I am not one
of those who find it easy to believe that
this war is likely to end very soon, we
all realise that it must end some day,
and the date of its ending does not really
affect very much the question of planning
and reconstruction. As always in the
case of foreign policy, I think it is only
right to point out that a military victory
is not likely to achieve anything like a
final victory. A great deal more has to
be done after the actual fighting has
finished. After the fighting is over, in
addition to our own internal problems of
reconstruction, we will have to take part
in the reconstruction of large areas, perhaps the whole of Europe and of the
Middle East.
We could never wage a major war
without a general staff continually in
session investigating
investigating through various
channels inventions, improvements and
so on that take place from time to time.
We could never run a war with ad hoc
committees in the way the Government
seem to think they can run the peace,
and that general staff idea with a section
continually doing research work to see
how the state of the country could be
improved seems to me a very reasonable
one in view of the fact that it is the one
always used when the nation is in a state
of crisis. The great difficulty about
ad hoc committees is they do not have
time to make proper inquiries. The time
factor is always against them. There is
only the point of time when someone
realises that something must be done, but
the length and quality and quantity of
the studies are always limited by the fact
that within a reasonable time they are
expected to produce a report.
The Gracious Speech produce no practical reconstruction policy. That seemed
to me to be rather a remarkable omission
in view of the fact that the Government
appear to think that this war, or the
fighting part of it, may finish during the
present Session. Out of the 22 paragraphs in the Speech only one relates to
legislation; the other paragraphs simply
tell us what has gone before and might
be summarised by the expression, "The
Ministers are still thinking."
Out of the nine Bills projected by this
Speech only one seems to me to be of
first-rate importance; that is the one
relating to juvenile delinquency. The
Government of Northern Ireland have
had the question of juvenile delinquency
before them for many years. Years ago
the Lynn Committee made a long, exhaustive, and thoroughly worked-out
report, and nothing has been done until
this year. We are 35 years behind the
times in the matter of juvenile delinquency, and I think that for us to have
been so much slower than England and
other countries to improve this very im-
'portant part of civil administration is a
thing in regard to which we cannot feel
anything but shame. I do not think that
any legislation simply to improve the
children's courts will be effective unless
we have a very great improvement in the
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
3 FEBRUARY 1944
health policy of the Government. I think
this is a case in which a Ministry of
Health would improve the situation very
much, because the health of the children
and their behaviour are
so very much
bound up together that I do not think
you can put child delinquency down as
merely a matter to be got rid of or dealt
with by a Bill relating to children's
courts.
It is necessary that we should have, in
addition, a system of
system of child guidance
clinics. In this connection we should
have a number of trained psychologists
working amongst children, and the suggestion has been made in the past that
the Belfast University should be assisted
and encouraged to help in this matter. I
hope the Government are not neglecting
that side of the matter, and that they
will make the necessary arrangements to
get the assistance of a school of psychology in the Belfast University before
they produce this Bill.
In
Housing also is of vital consequence to
the question of child delinquency.
the overcrowded state of our society we
cannot expect children to develop their
growing powers of mind and body without in some cases running the risk of
interfering with the rights and property
of other people. If they get into the
country districts far removed from
windows, milk bottles, and so on, on
which they can practise in town, nothing
is heard of the expansion of their muscles
and their minds, but the moment they
start expanding their muscles and their
minds in towns they damage windows or
milk bottles, or get into trouble by
running in front of motor cars. The
question of child delinquency seems to be
very largely a responsibility for the
whole of the community. We put children
in these over-crowded areas inadequately
provided with recreation facilities, and
then we expect them to behave in a suitable way. They never will, and until we
have some way in which they can be
encouraged to develop along rational lines
without interfering with the property and
rights of other people we shall always
have juvenile delinquency. I hope that
the Government in bringing forth their
Bill will keep this very important side of
the matter in mind.
That is really all I have to say about the
Gracious Speech, but in his speech the
Leader of the House forecast certain additional legislation. Whether he was doing
this in connection with the question of
child delinquency or not I am not quite
clear, but he seemed to think there was a
possibility that we might have a Mental
Deficiency Bill. That is one of the most
important things that this House might
ever have to consider. The fact that he
has made that remark will give great encouragement to those who are charged
with the welfare of the insane. There,
again, we are years behind England in the
matter of mentally deficient children, and
it is high time the Government did produce a Mental Deficiency Bill following
the lines of the English Act. But if they
do they will have to make it quite clear
that they have the necessary institutional
and medical treatment.
At present the Haypark institution is
the only one that is available for the whole
of the Province, and I think I am right
in saying that it has tended to take in and
look after the highest grade mental defectives. The Minister, in making his
speech, referred to the possibility of curing
these mental defectives, and I am sure that
many of them can be cured, and that many
more could be cured if they were given the
proper treatment. But I would point out
that we have also a very great responsibility to those unfortunate children who
are incurable and whose only destination
at present is the adult mental hospital,
which is not primarily equipped for
children. Until we have homes to which
these permanently disabled children can
be sent, I say frankly that the Government will not be doing its duty either to
the country or to the community. It is
absolutely essential that these children
should be in institutions where if there is
any capability of improvement, that can
be brought about under conditions which
are suitable to the children.
Then, again, no Mental Deficiency Bill
would be of any use unless the Government
have the courage to compel parents who,
misguidedly, but naturally perhaps, are
reluctant to part with mentally defective
children. That is one of the great difficulties that have been experienced in the
past. These children should be treated at
an age when some change in their condition is possible. I say it is very natural,
but it is a serious disadvantage to the
children themselves, if parents will hold
on to them and bring them up as if they
were ordinary children, when in actual
31.

The last thing which I think the
Government are most likely to deal with
in this Session is the question of education. I gather that it may be some considerable time before any change is
brought about following the lines of the
White Paper issued in Westminster, but
I would point out that something should
be done in education without waiting for
the permanent post-war scheme. After
we are
this war there will have to be a different
outlook, not because we want to have a
different outlook, but because
forced into the position, as Sir Roland
Nugent said the day before yesterday, of
finding that so much has been destroyed
-our foreign capital has been used upthat we shall have to reconsider our ideas
of living. The whole of this will fall
upon the generation which has just left
school, or is just leaving school, and any
new educational policy which the Goverament may bring forward will not affect
those people for the next 25 years.
Something must be done to help those
younger people who have not had a great
experience of life to realise the great
changes which are coming upon them,
and I would suggest that the Government,
again if possible with the help of the

leaders very much on the lines of the

explained. I do not think that any idea
is so profound or any problem so confusing as to be quite incapable of some
measure of explanation provided you get
the right people to do the explaining.
Though it might be difficult at the

can be made, and if it had sufficient
backing and had the authority of the
Ministry of Education and the Belfast
University, or some other bodies, it
would do a great deal to help in the
education of the younger members of the
community.
The Leader of the House warned us
not to expect any Utopia after this war.
It was many years ago that I read the
book, and then only under protest at the
dictation of my elders. At the same
time I read another book called The
Republic, and I came to the conclusion
that in any state of society such as that
put forward by the book, life would lose
most of its interest and practically all
its value because we are able to progress
by trial and error, and an ideal state of
society is not what any of us would wish
to have too quickly. I am sure that the
speed at which we can improve society
after the war in the line of social betterment will depend not so much what
upon
the country can give, but upon what
power we have to make the best use of the
little it can give.
I think that is very much within the
scope and province of the Minister of
Education. The more one thinks of the
matter the more one is convinced that
education is absolutely the key for the
advancement of the people after the war.
We have got to co-operate with our
neighbours. No Government by itself
can possibly achieve the betterment of
the people, especially in Northern
Ireland. The success that we will have
in raising the standard of the country
depends very largely on the co-operation
we get from the British Government.
But we can and ought to improve the
minds of the people to make use of what
little there will be to go round after the
war is over. If I have any complaint
against the King's Speech it is that it
does not provide the necessary means
for preparing the people for the mental
shock that is coming to them after the
A lot of people in this country
think that after the war they will go
back to the state of things that existed
before the war. I do not think there
will be the same amount going round,
and I would urge the Government very
seriously to consider the question of
carrying out the suggestions and proposals they have to carry out according
to the Gracious Speech. I beg to support the Motion.

I have read the findings of the Select

for themselves, by themselves, and suitable for them. As a matter of fact those
who are receiving any treatment at all
have to be sent to Southern Ireland for

ensure that no matter what the consequences the Government will not be
allowed to pigeon-hole the findings of a
Committee which are all so vital and
necessary for the health and well-being of
the country. If people are not healthy
they cannot give a return to the State.
Money reasonably and properly diverted
towards public health is bound to repay
itself in better health and better service.
Major DOBBS: May I be allowed to
add my congratulations to the Mover of
the Motion for his speech which was
so quietly delivered and so intensely interesting, and also to the Seconder of the
Address for his forthright, straightforward, sober speech? The few remarks
I am going to make will be entirely on
agriculture. I am sorry that no mention
or reference is made in the Speech to the
production of butter, eggs or bacon.
Much was done in regard to these commodities in the past, and it would seem
to be that much more will have to be
done in future if the Province in the
post-war years is to be prosperous.
The Gracious Speech makes the point
that the area under the plough is not to
be increased. That is very good so far
as it goes, but I should like to put some
remarks to the House with regard to
those farmers in districts not too favourably placed-districts with low lying,
heavy land such as the district between
Carrickfergus and Larne of which I have
some slight experience.
I think my
remarks may be of some interest to my
brother farmers in this House, and I
hope they will not be too dull to other
Members. There are two fundamental
facts I should like to put before the
House. The first is that our harvest
starts when the County Down harvest
ends. Another fact, which may seem
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
rather incredible to some farmers here,
is that since the 26th September I have
had a plough and tractor standing by
to plough. It is true we have opened
three fields, but since that date until
yesterday we have not been able to plough
a single acre of land. The ground is
saturated, probably owing to the lack of
frost and sun. It may possibly be due to
the closeness of salt from the sea which
makes this heavy land very sticky. It
requires days after the cessation of rain.
before the land becomes dry.
I know everyone here realises how
heartbreaking it is to farmers in those
districts. The farmer cannot plough,
he cannot sow, and he cannot reap at the
appointed times, although he knows
perfectly well that in other slightly more
favourable districts the farmer is ploughing, sowing and reaping. After the last
war farmers in my district cut down
their tillage to very meagre proportions.
I did the same because it was not
economical to do more ploughing. Two
years before the war I had the very
greatest difficulty in saving six acres of
oats. The difficulty was so great that I
said I would never plough another acre
of land in this district as long as I lived.
Then the war came. Last year I ploughed
200 acres. I take no credit for that at
all, because it had to be done. I put
some tons of artificial manure
on the
fields. In the Gracious Speech the hope
is expressed that the more extensive use
of artificial manure will result in greater
food production. I only hope that that
may be true. I am a little bit sceptical
about it because, as I have stated, I put
some tons of artificial manure on to the
tilled land—my fields were clean--but the
resulting crops were poor. The ground
remained saturated. Strangely enough,
the turnips were a failure for the first
time in living memory. The farmers in
my district put it down to the absence of
frost and sun. If you have not sun in
the summer you will not have frost in the
winter.
Strangely enough, the district about
which I am talking was a famous one
for growing wheat some 80 or 90 years
ago. It was one of the best wheat producing districts in the whole of the North
of Ireland. Thirty or forty years ago if
you had asked a farmer why he did not
grow wheat he would have said, "It is
true our fathers grew wheat, but then
they had more sun than we have now."
I think that is perfectly true. Some years
ago I came across some old letters written
by a steward to my ancestor who was on a
visit to England. In one of those letters
the following appears: "I have had great
difficulty in getting labour to cut the ice
on the lake in order to get it into the
ice house. I have had to pay double the
usual price for the whisky for those men.
I suppose it must be the war." The war
referred to was the Crimean war of 90
years ago. Another of those letters
stated: Councillor Stewart came out
from Carrickfergus on Tuesday to skate
on the lake. He fell in and he has not
been out since."
In those days, that is some 90 years
ago, there was a real thickness of ice.
During the past 30 years I cannot remember any ice on that lake except for a
thin skin. I am quite certain that in the
days to which the letters I have quoted from
refer, there was more frost and more sun
than is the case to-day. I do not think I
have seen the dust flying three times
within the past year. I think that will
appeal to farmers here.
In 1941 an Army officer from the
Eastern counties of England, who is an
expert farmer, came out to my place, as
he was interested in what we were doing.
I took him over a field and said to him: :
"We have just cleared the wheat off.
What would you do now?" He said he
would put in
a catch crop, and he
would then do so and so, or perhaps
such and such a thing. I waited
until he had finished, and then
I asked: "Do you realise that this is
the last day of November and not the last
day of August?" He turned round,
looked at me, and said, "I do not understand how you good people can keep
carrying on here.”
If you give the farmer the kind of
weather he wants, he will deliver the
goods. If you cannot give him the proper
weather, surely something should be done
to restrict the amount of tillage required
in those districts which are not wellfavoured and concentrate on improving
the grass. Farmers in my district asked
me if I could do something about it. I
have never heard a grouse from one of
those farmers, although they are losing
money.
On Tuesday morning my wife received
a letter from an old friend. In her letter
King's Speech-
Address in Reply

A
the lady said, "Our only son has just been
invalided out of the Navy,' He was invalided out because he had broken his
back; a very bright young fellow.
cripple for life. Farmers realise there are
worse things than losing money. I beg
to support the Motion.
Mr. LESLIE: It has been given to me
to be present at a good many debates in
reply to the Gracious Speech from the
Throne. I may say quite honestly that
I do not recollect an occasion on which
I have felt more interested than I
was on Tuesday when I heard the
speeches delivered by
by the proposer
and seconder. The speech of the hon.
and gallant Senator who opened the
debate was, if I may say so, one of the
most informative and one of the best
thought-out speeches to which it has ever
been my good fortune to listen. The
speech of the hon. Senator who seconded
showed evidence of very careful thought
and of very careful and meticulous
preparation.
Passing from the speeches which
attended the Gracious Speech, one comes
automatically to the Speech itself. The
Gracious Speech is of considerable length
and contains a good deal of matter with
which I do not propose to deal. Of the
matters to which I do propose to devote
attention for a few moments, I come, first
of all, to agriculture. One is pleased to
recognise that it is stated that the harvest
of last year was safely gathered in. One
would like to be assured that it is not only
safely gathered in, but has been satisfactorily threshed. Although the product of
the bountiful harvest can be safely
brought to the yard, there is considerable
doubt as to whether a good proportion of
it will not be found to be a good deal below
sample. It seems to be agreed that last
year's flax crop, as regards the quality of
the product, was rather in advance of the
year before.
As regards the management of agriculture in the future, one is glad to see
that there is no attempt made to increase
the actual area put under the plough.
The fault of our arrangement as regards
agriculture seems to be-I have thought
this for the past two or three years--too
much generalisation. That is not a fault.
peculiar to ourselves, because anyone who
follows the debates of the Parliament at
Westminster regarding farming prices
cannot fail to have observed that the
arguments put forward by the responsible
Minister were based on generalisation, on
the average
this and the average that.
Farmers to some extent, as some of us
know to our cost, do depend on average,
but it must be realised that the farms are
not uniform.
I have some land which is low-lying and
it is saturated at the present time.
Something has been done. I was amazed
to receive a few days before Christmas a
letter from our county agricultural officer
to the effect that before the 1st January
next I was required to plough X acres.
With the best intentions in the world,
and with a tractor hanging about in the
store or wherever it is kept, it would have
been utterly impossible to plough the land
at that time. Since then the land which
I set to another man, and which he had
set to another man to do the ploughing
for him has been ploughed. I was told
that a neighbour came out to measure the
amount of mud produced. Many of us
own land which is saturated, and that does
not give us any hope whatever of success.
Some attempt to mitigate uniformity
of the tillage requirements was made last
year for the first time, when some of the
dairying districts were relieved from full
compliance with the Order, but one hopes
that in the not too distant future it will
dawn on the Ministry of Agriculture that
there is such a thing as heavy land and
that the best use to which some of that land
could be put is not the growing of crops
but the production of grass. The question of grass is one of the very greatest
importance. It is all very well to require
a minimum proportion of tillage, but you
must be very careful to see that you do
not set the standard too high, as I am
afraid happened in too many cases last
year and seems to show every prospect of
occurring again this year, since the object
is not to plough so much as to produce
the crop.
Passing from that, one is pleased to
observe that there is an intention to give
effect to the proposal adumbrated once
or twice last Session to give the local
authorities rather more assistance in the
way of money than they have had in the
past. We all know that the duties cast
upon the local authorities, whether they
be county councils, or the minor councils,
the urban and district councils as the case
may be, are increasing and becoming
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
more intensive with the natural result
that they put a pressure on the rates.
It is usually said that it is the duty of
Parliament to save the taxes at the expense of the rates. No doubt that point
of view still prevails in another place.
We are, perhaps, more happily situated,
and we can look at the matter as it affects
the people as a whole. We have to remember that the ratepayer and the taxpayer is one and the same person, and
that although he has two pockets he has
only one pair of trousers. Attempts
have been made before to strike a balance
as between the finances of the local
authorities on the one hand and national
taxes on the other. One is glad to see
that another attempt is going to be made.
We are promised, as usual, a certain
number of Bills. Nine of them are
specified in the Gracious Speech. We have
heard of another one this afternoon which
is not in the Gracious Speech. Of those
nine there are only three to which I
propose to direct attention for a moment.
The first of them is the Bill dealing with
the borough of Belfast. A Bill dealing
with the arrangements of the Borough
Council was brought in in another place
last Session, but it did not get as far as
this House. Consequently, one does not
know whether the Bill that is to be
introduced this Session is going to be the
same as that one, or whether changes are
going to be introduced. One can only say,
looking back, if one may, on last Sessions's
Bill that there was one point in it, and
only one point, which would attract
general attention, for the rest of the Bill
would keep it in the category of a local
Bill rather than a public Bill.
There is one point which was of important application as dealing with the
general question of interference with local
government, interference, in fact, with
the domain of the local government
electors. I am sure hon. Members listening to me will know the point to which
I am referring. All one can do at the
moment, when the Bill is not before us,
and may never be before us, is to express
the hope that if it is intended to appoint
executive officers, as was suggested in the
last Bill, care will be taken in the Bill
to make it perfectly plain that these
executive officers are to be strictly
executive. They are not to be originating
officers. Their duty should be to carry
out, and no doubt without interference, a

policy, or to further a scheme which has
been approved by the Council, which is
the direct representative of the rate payers.
We ought not, I think, to take advantage of what we hope may be a passing
phase to impose on the constituents of
the Corporation of Belfast a system, not
so much of government as a system of
administration of government, which,
although it may be agreeable to or acceptable by the Council of the day, has not
been approved by the electors of Belfast.
Belfast, like other places, is at the moment
governed in theory-I am not speaking
of the temporary arrangements as regards
the administrators-but in theory the
Belfast Corporation is, like other bodies,
in a state of suspended animation. It
has not been renewed by the electors for
more than the normal period, and we
know there are certain reasons beyond our
control putting local government elections
at the moment out of the question. But
the fact that those local government elections are already overdue ought, I think,
to make one very careful as regards the
imposition of any new system until the
local authorities have had their normal
chance of replenishment or rejuvenation,
as the case may be.
Another Bill promised will deal with
juvenile delinquency. That is a subject
which my hon. and gallant Friend (Major
Perceval-Maxwell) has made his own, and
I do not propose to detain the House by
saying very much about it, except that I
could not quite follow my hon. and gallant Friend when he said we were 35
years behind England.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: The
1907 Act.
Mr. LESLIE: I was thinking of the
1908 Act. The 1908 Act runs in Northern Ireland, but I do not recall the 1907
Act at the moment. However, that is a
matter of detail which need not detain
the House. There is a proposal to bring

procedure. That again is not suitable
for discussion until the Bill is before us,
but I venture to hope that in the Bill we
shall find that opportunity has at long
last been taken to bring into force in
Northern Ireland a measure on the lines
of the Indictments Act of 1915, which
has been operating for practically 30
years across the water, and which has
been found of great assistance to those
who are concerned in the administration
of criminal procedure in the courts.

In dealing with these schemes of the

housing, in the past few years I am not
able to hold the Government as free from

have been done in the past year. It is
stated that there is a shortage of timber,
amongst other things. No doubt that is
so, but a certain amount of timber is
grown in Northern Ireland, and although
some of our fir trees may not be as good
as those imported in more fortunate times,
if the question of the provision of timber

arrangements made for using Northern
