that timber might have been seasoned,
and, therefore, would have been available
at the moment.
I have been given to understand that
there are methods now for the artificial
seasoning of timber, methods which are
relied upon as excusing cutting being done
at times when it used not to be thought
right to cut them because of the sap of the
trees. Whether that method has been
given up entirely I do not know, but the
method was there, and it does not seem to
have been made use of. I must say that,
in my judgment, better housing of the
people is one subject on which the people
of the Province are united, and one subject on which they are determined that an
advance, and a speedy advance, shall be
made. When the day of reckoning comes
it will go ill with the Government, and
with those Members who have not done all
they could in the furtherance of that
natural and very proper desire, the desire
for more houses.

Report. Already a difference of opinion

That, without presuming to pre-judge

It is really necessary, I think, that a warning should be given, and one hopes it will

schemes until one is quite sure that the

Rev. Professor CORKEY: There is not
very much in the debate that necessitates
a detailed reply. All the speeches that
have been made have been constructive
and generally appreciative of the programme of legislation which has been put
before the House by the Government.
There is one point, however, in which I
am myself particularly not only interested
but concerned. Several references were
made to the educational reform which is
promised by the Government, and about
which, in the first instance, we are to
have a White Paper. It was suggested
to-day, and it has been suggested frequently of late, that we are a Parliament,
an independent body, that we have
certain powers and authority of our own,
and that we ought to be able to produce
an educational scheme for ourselves and
go ahead with the task of reforming our
educational system without waiting to see
what England is going to do. It would
seem as though we were very pusillanimous
in having to wait until we see what our
larger partner is prepared to do, but
there are facts in the situation which are
very simple, and which are very familiar
to all those who have thought about this
question; facts which counsel us of the
prudence of waiting to see what the plans.
for educational reform in England are
to be.
We are a poor country comparatively.
I am told that the income per head in
Northern Ireland is only about half what
it is in England. The elemenary fact in
regard to all social reform initiated in
Northern Ireland is that it makes large
demands not only upon our own
Exchequer, but upon the Exchequer of
the Imperial authority at Westminster.
King's Speech-
Address in Reply
3 FEBRUARY 1944
It
An old proverb in which there is great
truth, and which is applicable to this
problem of educational reform, says that
he who pays the piper has some right to
a say in the calling of the tune.
would seem to be very high-handed on
our part if we prepared a very fine and
necessarily expensive educational programme, and proceeded to put it into
execution without discovering first of all
what the ideals of the larger partner were
and what he was prepared to spend in
England and Wales on his own educational reform.
I would like to say that Northern
Ireland has always been dealt with most
generously, and even handsomely, in the
matter of social reform. At the same
time, if we set up a standard here which
which seem to those across the water to
be grandiose, or which was out of step
to any extent with what they were proposing for themselves, we might get perhaps, not a blunt refusal, but in any case
we might receive discouragement, which
no Government would like to risk. Therefore, although we may seem to be dilatory in putting forward our proposals,
for these simple and very intelligible
reasons it has always seemed right that
in regard to reforms where great expense
would be involved,
Government
should wait and see what is going to be
done on the other side. We are an independent Parliament, and we can undertake reforms on our own, but when large
expense is involved, that expense falls not
only on our own Exchequer, as I have
said, but ultimately upon the Exchequer
across the water I think it right that I

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I understand that it is in accordance with precedent that in such a situation as I occupy
to-day I should make a statement.
not prepared to-day to make that statement in regard to circumstances which
arose, and which made it necessary for
me to resign my position as Leader of
this House. I would like on this, the very
first opportunity possible, to express my
very warm thanks to you, my Lord, as
Speaker of the House, and also to all the
Members of the House, for their great
consideration, tolerance, and kindness towards me while I had the honour to
represent the Government here. I came
here, as you know, as a perfect novice. I
had had no practice in the art of presenting Bills to a Senate such as this, and quite
often the texts which I had to expound
were very obscure and difficult, and,
indeed, not always very acceptable to all
the Members of the House. But I am
glad to say that on the few occasions on
which you did put me somewhat in fear,
or, as the common expression is, put the
wind up me, you did it in such a way
that there was no possibility of misunderstanding on my part, and you indicated
that in your criticism, and in your
differences, there was always a constructive
intention and spirit.
This is all I wish to say to-day, except
perhaps this that I am gratified that, as
Leader, I made no grievous blunder and
irritated no susceptibilities. I would also
like to say that so long as I am a Member
of this House I shall always try to manifest the same reasonable spirit as the
Members have found in me in the past.
More will have to be said, and perhaps
you, Mr. Speaker, would afford me the

Marriage (Declaration of Law) Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
The PARLIAMENTARY SECRE-
TARY OF THE DEPARTMENT OF
THE PRIME MINISTER (Sir Joseph
Davison): This Bill may be described as one for protecting the
conscientious objections of clergymen
and for the removal of doubts as to the
effects of certain enactments or rules of
law in relation to the solemnisation of
marriages of persons whose former
marriage had been dissolved, or of persons coming within certain degrees of
affinity who, by civil law, are entitled to
be married.
The provisions of the Bill are not confined to any particular denomination,
though it is possible that the difficulties
which the Bill will set right may not be
of general application.
The Government feels that clergymen
should not be compelled to act in any
manner which is not in accordance with
ecclesiastical law and practice, and the
object of the Bill is to remove any doubts
which may have arisen by reason of the
complicated enactments governing certain
marriages.
Clause 1 of the Bill deals with the remarriage of divorced persons, and makes
it clear that a clergyman shall not be
compelled to solemnise or to permit his.
church to be used for the solemnisation
of such a marriage. The House will
remember that prior to 1939 dissolution
of marriage could only be obtained by a
private Act of Divorce, following a degree
of divorce a mensa et thoro. The Matrimonial Causes Act (Northern Ireland),
Marriage Bill
Adjournment
22 FEBRUARY 1944
1939, applied the main principles of the
English matrimonial jurisdiction to
Northern Ireland, substituting a judicial
for a legislative system of divorce and
enumerating the several grounds of
divorce. Under this Act, when a decree
for divorce is made absolute, either of
the parties may marry again as if the
prior marriage had been dissolved by
death. It is by no means clear that
ministers are bound to solemnise the
marriage of persons entitled to be married
under this Act, but if they are so bound
by the civil law, this Clause will release
them from that obligation.
some
Clause 2 deals in a somewhat similar
manner with marriages within certain
degrees of relationship. In this connection the Marriage Act of 1835 provides
that marriages between persons within the
prohibited degree of consanguinity or
affinity shall be null and void. This Act,
however, has been modified to
extent by the Deceased Wife's Sister's
Marriage Act, 1907, the Deceased
Brother's Widow's Marriage Act, 1921,
and by the Deceased Brother's Widow's
Marriage Act (N.I.), 1924. The effect of
these modifications is that a man may
marry his deceased wife's sister, and a
man may marry his deceased brother's
widow. The marriages of such persons
may not be in accordance with.
ecclesiastical law and practice and this
Clause will relieve ministers from any
obligation to solemnise the marriage of
such persons or to permit their churches
to be used for that purpose.
The Bill is a short one which I believe
will commend itself to the Members of
this House. I beg to move, "That this
Bill be now read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I am inclined to re-echo
the concluding words of the speech of the
•
Parliamentary Secretary in thinking that
this Bill is one which will commend itself
to the House. This is, as has been said,
simply a Bill for the quieting of consciences and for the removal of possible
difficulties. It may be remembered that
before the Act of 1939 was discussed in
this House attention was drawn to the
possibility of difficulty arising, and to the
desirability of inserting a conscience
Clause, but the Government of that day
did not apparently think it was necessary,
ånd nothing was done. Since then it has
apparently been represented that probably, particularly in view of Section 20
of the Church of Ireland Act, 1869, there
is some doubt arising, and therefore it is
desirable that ministers and clergymen
should be relieved of any possible obligation to solemnize a marriage which in
their conscience they cannot possibly.
approve. Accordingly the Bill is now
before us, and I think it will meet with
general approbation.
Before I sit down I may mention that
there is a curious printer's error on the
third page in line 6. The word "public"
has been accidentally left out before the
word "worship". As it is merely a
printer's error I think it can be corrected,
and a formal Amendment at a later stage
will be unnecessary. I support the Second
Reading.
On Question, Motion agreed to
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.
Resolved, "That this House do now
adjourn until Thursday".-(Sir Joseph
Davison).
Adjourned accordingly at Fifteen
minutes before Three o'clock
until Thursday next.
Questions to Minister

Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Thursday, 24th February, 1944.
The Senate met at half after Two of
the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the Chair.
QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
Housing Subsidy.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether recent statements in the Press
that the subsidy to be given for the
houses which are shortly to be built in
Northern Ireland is the highest per
house of any subsidy given in the
United Kingdom are correct, and, if
so, what is the amount by which this
subsidy exceeds the average subsidy
given for comparable houses in the
rent of the United Kingdom and what
is the reason for this excess.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I put this
question down, but it may not be a matter
of very great importance. It is just possible that this large subsidy is due to
building costs in this country being
higher than building
building costs in the
remainder of the United Kingdom. If
that did happen to be the case it would
obviously be a matter for very serious
consideration. If, as seems probable, it
is only an isolated case it is, of course, a
matter of no importance.
The PARLIAMENTARY SECRE-
TARY OF THE DEPARTMENT OF
THE PRIME MINISTER (Sir Joseph
Davison): The subsidy of £390 per house
to be paid by the Government to the
Belfast Corporation in respect of the 750
houses to be built in Belfast is equal to
the highest subsidy paid in Great Britain
in respect of urban housing.
In England and Wales the subsidy payable for urban housing is £5 10s Od per
annum per house for a period of 40 years.
In exceptional cases the subsidy may be
increased to £6 10s 0d per annum per
house for 40 years in non-county boroughs
and urban districts where the level of
working class rents is substantially below
the average for the particular type of
district and the financial resources of the
district are small in relation to the
housing work to be done. The capitalised
value of £5 10s Od per annum for 40
years at 3 per cent. per annum is
approximately £123.
In Scotland the rate of subsidy for
urban housing ranges from £10 10s Od per
annum for 40 years per house to £13 per
annum for 40 years per house according
to size. The capitalised value of £13 per
annum for 40 years at 31 per cent, is
approximately £290. To this sum of
£290 the Imperial Government have
agreed to add a war-time subsidy of
£100, making £390 per house altogether.
The high rate of subsidy payable to
Belfast Corporation is justified because of
the high cost of building in Belfast at
the present time.
Taxation on Agriculture.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether his attention has been called
to a statement recently made by the
Chancellor of the Exchequer that the
Board of Inland Revenue have been
instructed in the course of their investigation of the effects of taxation on
industry to give special consideration
to the difficulties of agriculture, and
whether the Northern Ireland Government will take the necessary steps to
that the Board are fully
supplied with all the information they
need in respect of the effect of taxation
on Northern Ireland farmers, and in
particular to draw their attention to
any points in which these effects may
differ from those in Great Britain,
owing to the smaller average size of
Northern Ireland farms, and the fact
that the majority of Northern Ireland
farmers are owners and not, as in
England, tenants.
ensure
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The second
question standing in my name I have put
down chiefly to call attention to this
point. Those of us who in the course of
the war have had some experience of
dealing with land under war and other
Regulations passed and administered
mainly in Great Britain realise that the
Imperial authorities working in Great
Britain very seldom appreciate the
Personal Statement-

24 FEBRUARY 1944
Professor Corkey
immense difference which is made in many
respects by the small size of farm holdings in this country, and by the fact that
they are farmed by their owners and not
by tenants of large estates. This may possibly effect the inquiry now being carried
out by the Board of Inland Revenue on
agricultural land values, and I am anxious
that this question should be kept before
the Board of Inland Revenue and the
Imperial authorities.

suggested by the hon. and gallant Senator
were taken some time ago, and the fullest

Board of Inland Revenue. Representatives of the Ulster Farmers' Union were
present at discussions in London with the
Board.
MINISTER OF EDUCATION'S
RESIGNATION.
Personal Statement by Professor Corkey.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: In accordance with my request to you, Mr.
Speaker, on the last day on which the
House met, I would like now,
with your
consent and the consent of the House, to
make a personal statement in explanation
of why I have moved from one side of
the House to the other, and in explanation of a number of other things which
have happened to me. in recent date.
am sorry to take up the time of the House
with what may seem at first sight to be a
purely personal matter, but there are two.
facts which justify me in making this
personal statement.
I
First, my honour as a Cabinet Minister
has been impugned by an entirely misinformed statement made by the Prime
Minister regarding my work in the
Ministry of Education, and I feel bound
to defend myself; and, in addition, a
great principle in which the people of
Ulster are interested is at stake.
Let me deal right at the outset with
the statement of the, Prime Minister that
in August he had warned me that I ought
to give more attention to and keep in
touch with the Ministry. I am positive
that on that occasion he made no reference
to my visiting or failing to visit Portrush.
I gathered, during this August interview, that he had been informed by someone that it would be impossible for me to
carry on my work as professor in the
college and also the work of the Ministry
at the same time. I was, however, able
to assure him that I had been released
from practically all my college work.
was able to give virtually all my time and
thought to the work of the Ministry.
I
Our interview was quite friendly and
he seemed satisfied. Had he mentioned
to me on that occasion that he wished me
to be in constant attendance at Portrush,
I would certainly have sacrificed other
work of the Ministry to do so.
To begin with, I would like to say that
up to the 11th February, when I had my
recent interview with the Prime Minister,
no complaint of any sort ever reached me
regarding my relationship with the Ministry or the effectiveness of my work in it.
On that date the Prime Minister asked
me to meet him, and without giving me
the slightest warning or any opportunity
of stating my case, he asked me for my
resignation on the grounds that I had not
gone frequently enough to Portrush. He
added immediately that my Parliamentary
Secretary had resigned. I am quite positive on that point. Indeed, I referred to
her resignation in my letter to the Prime
Minister, written the day after the interview, and in his subsequent reply to me
the Prime Minister neither questioned nor
repudiated that statement. So far for
that. I would like to stress the fact that
my resignation was asked for before I
was given any opportunity of putting
before the. Prime Minister the true facts
of the case.
I would now like, with, the permission
of the House, to correct some of the misinformed and misleading statements
which, I regret to say, were. made by the
Prime Minister in explaining in another
place why he had asked me to resign.
The main ground given by him, as is
known to hon. Members, for this sudden
and drastic action was that I had not
visited Portrush often enough. In elaborating this point, the Prime Minister
made no reference to the fact that the
office in Belfast was the only office assigned
to me for doing my work when I took
up duties as Minister, and was therefore
my headquarters. My secretary and
clerical staff were located there, and also
the entire department of Youth Welfare
work. My correspondence came to that
office in Belfast, and I had to be, and
Personal Statement-
Professor Corkey
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
actually was, in daily attendance there
for interviews and inquiries of various
kinds connected with my work as Minister, in addition, of course, to handling
such correspondence as came, to hand.
In that office I had consultations
frequently with the officials of the permanent staff when any important matter
I called for consultation. I was also in
'constant telephonic communication with.
the part of the Ministry which was at
Portrush, when any matter of lesser importance demanded consideration. I feel,
therefore, it was most unfair that the
Prime Minister in making his statement
did not give any indication of the important nature of the office in Belfast,
and that he did not make it clear at the
same time that no office was provided for
me at Portrush.
The Prime Minister stated that
sufficient attention had not been given by
me to the Ministry because I had not
been "in constant attendance," as he put
it, at Portrush. It would have been
absolutely impossible for me to be in
constant attendance at Portrush and at
the same time discharge my manifold
duties as Minister and Leader of the
Senate and as a Member of the Cabinet.
In addition, anyone who knows anything
about the Ministry of Education will
understand that, with one or two exceptions, the staff in Portrush is normally
engaged in merely routine work, important, of course, in its way-the oversight of which was not part of my duties.
Indeed, on the occasion of my first visit
to Portrush I was informed by those in
authority that I would have nothing to
do with the junior staff, except when any
member of it was asked to put before me
specific information on some detail of the
work. "Constant attendance" at Portrush would merely have been meddlesome
and useless interference with the work of
an efficient staff.
Further, the Prime Minister made
much of the fact that I had only been to
Portrush three times in six months. The
Prime Minister's statement was true, but
entirely misleading. One of those months
-July-was the school holiday month.
During August I offered to go to Portrush, although I was on holiday at the
time, but was told by the Permanent
Secretary that there was no business
requiring my attendance. I was there
during September. During the latter part
of the year, although suffering from the
effects of influenza, I again suggested
going to Portrush but was again told by
the Secretary that there was no need for
me to be there at that time.
The Prime Minister said he did not
wish to be hard on me. I do not ask for
mercy; I ask for justice. I think it was
very unfair of the Prime Minister in
making his indictment to withhold from
Parliament and the country the fact, well
known to him, that I had been a victim
of influenza during part of this period,
and that as a result of my eagerness to
resume my duties in the Ministry I had
suffered a relapse.
I may add that I
visited Portrush twice during January
when there was business to be done, and
I had arranged to be there for two days
in the very week in February when I was
informed that I was no longer Minister.
the
The Prime Minister, has, of course,
right to dispense with a Minister without
giving any reason, but since he has
chosen to specify the infrequency of my
attendance at Portrush as the sole reason
for my dismissal from office, I cannot
help saying that it seems to me, in the
light of all the facts, to be feeble and
unworthy of him, and it will, I am sure,
seem very inadequate to anyone who
knows the facts. I can honestly say that
no matter connected with the Ministry of
Education that required my attention was
ever neglected. No complaint, so far as
I know, ever reached the office.
The Prime Minister stated that I was
not able to give sufficient attention to the
Ministry, but he made no reference to the
fact that since. my appointment, in order
to gain first-hand information about our
system of education, I spent considerable
time in visiting primary and secondary
schools, and in carrying out public duties
under the Ministry connected with youth
clubs, teachers' associations, regional
committees and school speech days in
various parts of Northern Ireland.
Neither did the Prime Minister make
any reference to the fact that I made an
extensive tour in England in mid-winter
to make myself conversant with the new
movements that are afoot for the advancement of education in England.
I cannot understand how, in any fairness, in face of these facts, the Prime
Minister could say that I had not shown
Personal Statement-
Professor Corkey
24 FEBRUARY 1944
keenness in my work, unless he had been
misinformed. Possibly if I had been less.
keen and had been satisfied to play the
role of a mere yes-man reports of a
different character would have. reached
his ears.
""
I come now to the second main circumstance in the situation which justifies me
in making my statement here to-day.
What this circumstance is I shall briefly
indicate. In 1930 Lord Craigavon's
Government, in fulfilment of an agreement with the Orange Order and the
Protestant Churches, placed on the
Statute Book an Education Act which
secured a
definite place. for religious
instruction in all our provided and transferred elementary schools where parents
wanted it.
In December last, Mr. Butler introduced a Bill into the. House of Commons
at Westminster which he had foreshadowed in a White Paper published
earlier. In this Bill Mr. Butler proposes
to make provision for the securing of
religious education in all schools, both
primary and secondary, wherever the
parents wish it, along lines, I may say.
very similar to our 1930 Act. Moreover,
in reply to a letter from our Ministry, he
has indicated his intention to apply the
same principle to direct-grant schools in
England, which correspond in many ways
to our secondary schools in Northern
Ireland.
Since Mr. Butler declared his intentions
on this matter, I, as Minister of Education, received from the Orange Order and
Protestant Churches several resolutions
asking me to secure a place for religious
instruction in our secondary schools in
Ulster comparable to the place now being
given to it in secondary schools in
England. Having received these resolutions, and being interested in my work,
I felt that this question demanded careful
consideration. When I discussed the
matter, as I did, with the Ministry and
with the Parliamentary Secretary, I discovered difficulties.
The Prime Minister states that there
was no disagreement between him and me
on this question as the matter had not
been discussed with him. That is quite
true. I considered that the discussions in
the Ministry had not gone sufficiently far
to justify me in bringing the matter
either before the Prime Minister or the
Cabinet as yet. But when he went on to
say that there had been no difference
between me and the Parliamentary Secretary on this matter he was inaccurate
and had been quite misinformed.
In a letter to the Prime Minister on
12th February, before my resignation had
been accepted, I wrote to him about this
very matter in these terms:
On all occasions, when I raised the
question of securing a more definite
place for religion in all our schools, as
is contemplated by Mr. Butler for all
schools in England, I was met only
with difficulties and objections on the
part of the Parliamentary Secretary.
I may say that in these discussions I
made no secret of the fact that my purpose was to give to all the children of
Ulster, so far as possible, the benefit of
Mr. Butler's policy both with regard to
secular and religious instruction.
Like the political leaders in England
who backed Mr. Butler's Bill-I may
include Mr. Churchill, Mr. Bevin, Mr.
Atlee and a number of other distinguished
Statesmen-I am aware of the great need
of our young people to have instruction
and guidance in matters concerning our
Christian moral standards and our
Christian faith. At the present time our
young people must have such help in our
schools if our Christian heritage and our
democracy are to survive. Mr. Churchill
speaking recently on this very point said,
"This fundamental element must never be
taken from our schools."
&
Sir
In passing, may I say that Sir Basil
Brooke in his reference to this subject on
Tuesday does not seem to be aware of the
precise issue involved in adopting the
policy of Mr. Butler in our secondary
schools, as we are urged to do by the
Orange Order and the Churches.
Basil says that this Government will
always see to it that the teaching of
religion will always be "allowed" in
schools in Northern Ireland. There is a
vital difference between an educational
system which "allows" religious instruction, and a system, such as Mr. Butler
proposes, which "provides" religious
instruction where parents want it.
All educationists will recognise the
vital difference. The pledge given by the
Prime Minister on Tuesday has

for me

When the time came
appoint 12 members to the new Youth

people.
In conclusion, I am naturally disappointed at the turn that events have
taken, and I regret that I shall not be
able to complete a work to which I had
given so much thought during the nine.
months I was in office. I had hoped to
have the sympathy and support of the
Prime Minister in carrying out the heavy
duties to which he had called me, and I
shall always regret that instead he felt
called upon to accuse me publicly and
wrongfully of neglecting my work. I am,
however, not embittered, and I shall continue to serve the people, of Ulster to the
best of my ability. (Hon. Members:

my Lord, for your indulgence.

QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
Northern Ireland Planning Commission.
The following Question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Government will consider
adding further representatives of agriculture and of rural land-owners to the
Northern Ireland Planning Commission
and take any further steps which may
be necessary to ensure that the interests
of the users and owners of rural land
are fully safeguarded before any final
decisions are taken as to the planning
of any area.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: It seems to
me that there is a danger in leaving the
Planning Commission or any similar body
to be exclusively an urban-minded body.
As far as I can make out, the present
Planning Commission, with the exception
of one gentleman, is composed almost entirely of gentlemen whose natural bias.
would be more to the town than to the
country. Owing to the war, and to
the construction of aerodromes, camps,
etcetera, we have lost a great many
thousands of acres of agricultural land
which it would be impossible to return to
any agricultural use in an economical
way, and it is very important that we
should not lose more agricultural land to
bricks and mortar than is absolutely
necessary. There may be some conflict of
interest arise in the course of planning as
between the country and town interests,
and it seems to me to be very desirable,
for that reason, that country interests
should be well represented on any body
dealing with that matter.
The
PARLIAMENTARY SECRE-
TARY OF THE DEPARTMENT OF
THE PRIME MINISTER (Sir Joseph
Davison): In reply to the question
of the hon. and gallant Senator, the
Northern Ireland Planning Commission was constituted in order to perform
certain advisory functions in regard to
the more technical aspects of the utilisation of land and the preparation of
planning schemes. Accordingly, as might
be expected, the members of the Commission are all-with the exception of the
Chief Technical Officer of the Ministry of Agriculture either architects or
engineers or both.
It was never intended that the Commission should be a body representative
of various types of users of land, and it
would not, therefore, in the Government's view, be appropriate that users
and owners of rural land, as distinct
from land in urban area, should be given
special representation upon it.
Under the Planning Acts (Northern
Ireland), 1931 and 1944, local authorities.
are responsible for the preparation of
planning schemes, and, as in rural areas
these authorities may be assumed to be
representative of the users and owners
of rural land, there is every reason for
believing that schemes in respect of rural
areas will have due regard to the interests
of the users and owners of such land.
The hon. and gallant Senator, therefore, need have no fears lest the special
problems relating to land in rural areas
may be neglected. As I have mentioned,
the chief inspector of the Ministry of
Agriculture is a member of the Planning
Commission and will be available to
advise the Commission on any technical
questions arising in connection with the
utilisation of land for agricultural purposes. Moreover, on questions relating
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
29 FEBRUARY 1944
to the planning of rural land generally,
there will be effective consultation between the Ministry of Agriculture and
the Ministry of Home Affairs, which is
the Department responsible under
Statute for the examination and approval
of planning schemes submitted by local
authorities.
In these circumstances the Government
consider that the interests of users and
owners of rural land are fully safeguarded
and that no further steps are necessary
for this purpose.
Mr. LESLIE: Is it really understood
and recognised that no urban authority
can possibly extend the scope of its
operations without encroaching on rural
land? That is a point that seems to have
been left somewhat in the air. There
are various aspects of the uses of agricultural land. There is the use for agriculture and the use of the land with due
regard to the scenic amenities of the
neighbourhood. What I think we want
to know, and what was perhaps one of
the supports of the hon. and gallant
Senator's question, was whether proper
regard has been had to matters of that
kind from the point of view of the
dwellers in and the owners of land in the
rural areas.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: As I have
said, the chief technical inspector of the
Ministry of Agriculture is a member of
the Planning Commission, and if any
representations were made to him by any
planning authority throughout the rural
areas I am sure they would have every
consideration from him and from the
Planning Commission.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The chief
inspector of the Ministry of Agriculture
is only one man on a very large Planning
Commission. Obviously he would not be
put there unless the Government assume
that questions affecting rural interests.
would arise before that Commission. All
I am asking is that he should have somebody to assist him. On a day when a
question affecting rural interests would
arise he might be absent through illness
or some other cause. It is not very much
to ask that there should be two or three
additional persons with a knowledge of
rural conditions appointed on what is a
very large Commission. I honestly think
the point is worth consideration, if only
to satisfy the uneasy minds, of which I
know there are a good many.
Agricultural Enquiry Committee.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the Commission now inquiring
into the future of agriculture has investigated the possibility that more
permanent capital may be required by
farmers as a consequence of recent
developments, and in particular by the
increase in machinery, in tillage, in
farm wages, and the arrears of maintenance which will have to be made
good after the war, and whether if this
point has not yet been investigated the
Government will instruct the Commission to do so and to make suggestions
as to how such capital, if required,
could most suitably be made available
to farmers.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: My second
question on the Paper deals with a matter
of very considerable difficulty. It is
causing a certain amount of anxiety in
England. There have been letters in
The Times and other newspapers from
very leading members of the agricultural
industry in England and from leading
accountants on agricultural matters.
There appears to be a very general opinion
that after the war farmers may find
themselves short of capital, but it is
extraordinarily difficult to say how much.
In my own case, as far as I can make
out, I am using £4 or £5 an acre more
capital than I did before the war. It is
very difficult to disentangle the changes
in one's procedure on the farm and the
actual causes, but the danger is that if
there is a shortage of capital it will only
make itself felt after the war in delays
in necessary repairs to farm buildings and
roads, and in the stocking of farms, and
a very great deal of harm might be done
before it was realised that it had been
done. As we have a committee inquiring
into all these matters I think it would
be a very good thing if they went into
the point, if they have not already done
So.
Major DOBBS: I hope the Deputy
Leader of the House, in answering this
question, will be able to tell us that this

Rent Restriction (Defective Tenancies)
Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: The object
of this Bill is to give the protection of
the Rent Restriction Acts to certain persons who occupy dwelling-houses
on
holdings purchased under the Land
Purchase Acts in pursuance of tenancy
agreements which are not legally valid.
The effect of Section 30 (1) of the
Northern Ireland Land Act, 1925, an
Act of the Imperial Parliament, is to
make void any attempted letting of a
holding or part of a holding subject to a
land purchase annuity where the proprietor has not obtained the consent of
the Ministry of Finance to the. letting.
Several cases have come to the notice of
the Ministry where the proprietor has
purported to let a dwelling-house on the
holding without first obtaining the prescribed consent of the Ministry of
Finance, and has subsequently been able
to regain possession of the dwellinghouses on the ground that, as the purported letting was invalid, there was no
tenancy to which the protection given to
a tenant under the Rent Restriction Acts
could be afforded.
By the provisions of the Bill, the Rent
Restriction Acts will be made applicable
to a letting of this type, and consequently
the person who has taken the house will
be protected against any eviction at the
instance of the proprietor on the ground
that the attempted letting was void by
reason of Section 30 (1) of the Northern
Ireland Land Act, 1925.
I beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I believe that the late
Sir Rudyard Kipling remarked, in one of
his admirable short stories, that all the
stories ever told in comparatively recent
years were merely variants of the original
stories whispered by the serpent into the
ear of Eve in the Garden of Eden. If that
be so, it seems to me that this Bill is, in
a way, an exemplification of the truth of
the statement, because the Bill is merely
a variant of the well-established maxim
that a man may not profit by his own
fraud. There is nothing whatever that I
can see against the Bill. It is well within
our powers, in so far as it approaches the
border line of which it is a long way off
-of the Government of Ireland Act as
regards the subject matter of the law
relating to land purchase, and it is, as it
seems to me, a good deal short of what
we did in the Second Schedule to the
Accidental Fires Bill-

29 FEBRUARY 1944
Local Government Act of 1934. I cannot
see any single objection to the Bill.

Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Accidental Fires Bill.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: This short
Bill deals with one aspect of the consequences of accidental fires. Up to the
year 1715, if a fire starting by accident
on any premises spread to and caused
damage to other premises, the owners of
such other premises might seek compensation from the person in whose house
the fire commenced its work of destruction. In that year an Act of the Irish
Parliament was passed to give relief in
such cases, and it is still in force in
Northern Ireland. This Act says that—
No action, suit or process whatsoever
shall be had, maintained or prosecuted
against any person or persons, in whose
house or chamber any fire shall . . .
accidentally begin, or any recompense
or satisfaction be made by such person
or persons for any damage suffered or
occasioned thereby; any law, usage, or
custom to the contrary notwithstanding.
I invite the attention of hon. Senators
to the words, " in whose house or chamber
any fire shall accidentally begin." Up
to a year or so ago it was assumed that
the words "house or chamber" did not
limit the relief to dwelling-houses, but a
legal decision in Eire, where the Act of
1715 also applied, raised a doubt as to
whether they might be held to cover
business premises in Northern Ireland.
There cannot be any difference in
principle in the application of the relief
to dwelling-houses or to business premises,
and so this Bill has been put forward to
clarify the law and to bring it into conformity with the law in England, for in
that country an Act was passed in the
year 1774 in much wider terms than those
of the Irish Act of 1715.
Clause 1 of the Bill says that-
"no legal proceedings shall be instituted against any person in or on
whose building or land any fire has
(whether before or after the passing of
this Act) accidentally begun, in respect
of damage caused by reason of such
fire."
There is a proviso to Clause 1 which
excludes certain exceptional cases, such as
negligence or bringing dangerous things
into or upon the building or land.
I think that what I have said, taken in
conjunction with the memorandum printed
on page 1, will inform hon. Senators as
to the meaning and intention of the Bill,
and accordingly I beg to
the
Second Reading.
Question proposed.
move
Mr. LESLIE: I do not imagine that
anybody in this House will raise any
objection to what I call the principle of
the Bill before us, but I must be excused
for pointing out that it is one of the
most abstruse little Bills that have been
presented to this House for a very long
time. The Bill owes its origin to a decision
in the neighbouring district known as
Eire. A case arose in that district which
before the Supreme
ultimately came
Court, and a decision was taken by a
majority in a court of five. The majority
was three to two, and in the two was
their Chief Justice. So it might be possible that anybody here who was disposed
to inquire into the necessity or desirability of supporting a judgment in that
court elsewhere might be in some doubt
about it. Accordingly it has been thought
well to bring a Bill in here in order to
prevent there being any doubt on the
subject, and to produce the same result
in Northern Ireland as has already been
in existence since 1774 in Great Britain.
For myself I must confess to some doubt
as to whether the Bill before us would not
have been just as good if proviso (a) to
the First Clause had not been inserted,
because such a proviso does not occur in
the Act passed in the neighbouring State
to which I have referred in consequence
of the judgment to which I have also
referred. I believe myself that it makes
no difference whatever whether we put
that proviso in or leave it out, but I
think it may well be argued that the insertion of that proviso does put up a sort
of notice board that it is not intended in
Northern Ireland to do anything that
can possibly interfere with the common
law as it has existed in Great Britain since
1774, or perhaps earlier. I say that
because, so far as I have been able to

ascertain, the really important cases

CONGRESS OF THE U.S.A.
Invitation to Delegates to Visit
Northern Ireland.
Mr. STEVENSON: I beg to move,
"That this House, desiring to promote a closer association between the
Parliament of Northern Ireland and
the Congress of the United States of
America, directs the Speaker on its behalf to invite the delegation of Congress
during their stay in Great Britain, to
visit Northern Ireland as the guests of
this Parliament, and thus further
advance the cordial relationships which
have been strengthened by the presence
of the United States Forces in this
area."
This Resolution, which I have the
privilege to move, has an object which
makes my duty a very pleasing one. For
over 200 years there has been a bond of
friendship and kinship between the
peoples of the United States of America
and ourselves, a bond which has been
immeasurably strengthened during the
present war. The presence of so many
American troops in our midst has
established mutual understanding and
warm-hearted friendship. It is important
that we should foster this common bond,
and so establish a good-will that will
cause good feeling amongst all nations.
I therefore hope that this Resolution,
which springs from this desire, may have
the hearty support of this House.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
have the greatest pleasure possible in
seconding this Resolution. I think it
would have been a mistake if the opportunity had not been taken when this
delegation from United States Congress
was visiting London to invite them to
Ulster. We have very strong ties with
the people of America, stronger indeed
than any other part of the United
Kingdom. Now that they are our allies
and are doing their part in trying to win
this war, I think we should let them see
how much we value their assistance in
bringing about victory.
We have in the past contributed much
to the progress and welfare of the United
States. Many of the best men of the
United States have left these shores and
have helped to build
great
up the
democracy of America. Therefore, I feel
that this is a very good opportunity of
letting the people of the United States
see how much we value their friendship
and co-operation, and that we are anxious
to improve and further cement the bond
of friendship and kinship that already
exists between us. I have the greatest
possible pleasure in seconding this
Resolution, which I hope will be
unanimously accepted by the House.
On Question, Motion agreed to nemine.
dissentiente.

Housing Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I have been
asked by the right hon. Gentleman, the
newly appointed Leader of the Senate, to
move the Second Reading of the Housing
Bill. I believe. I am voicing the opinion
of all Members of the House when I say
we hope the right hon. Gentleman will
have a happy time in his new position.
(Hon. Members: Hear, hear.)
This short Bill has two main objectives.
The first and immediate objective is to
give statutory authority to the Ministry
of Home Affairs for the payment of a
subsidy to the Belfast Corporation in
respect of 750 houses which are about to
be built in Belfast, and for which a release of materials has been obtained.
Hon. Senators are, I feel sure, well aware
of the arrangements which have been
made, and under which it is proposed to
pay a subsidy not exceeding £390 per
house.
The Government is, however, not without hope that, as money becomes available
and the supply of essential building
materials becomes more plentiful, it may
be possible, later on, to arrange for some
further war-time building, and the Bill
has, therefore, been drafted in general
terms so that fresh legislation should not
be necessary whenever this extension of
building becomes possible, or if it should
be necessary to alter the amount of the
subsidy.
The second main object of the Bill is
its provision to enable those local authorities which have power to acquire otherwise than by agreement any land for the
purpose of the Housing Acts to use the
vesting order procedure for the purpose of
acquiring land for housing schemes. This
procedure has been in operation since the
passing of the Local Government Act
(Northern Ireland) 1934 and has worked
satisfactorily in cases where it is necessary
to acquire land compulsorily for public
health purposes or in connection with
roads, but it has not been applicable to
land acquired for housing. It has the
great advantage of enabling the local
authority once a vesting order is made,
to enter on the land almost immediately,
leaving the question of the compensation
to be paid for the land to be arranged for
subsequently, either by agreement, or, if
agreement cannot be reached, by arbitration in the usual manner.
as
I feel sure it is unnecessary for me to
urge upon hon. Senators the desirability
of these powers being afforded to local
authorities as soon as possible. It is
vitally important that and when
materials become available local authorities should be in a position to get on with
their housing schemes without delay, and
this, of course, they cannot do if elaborate
procedure has to be followed before they
are in a position to enter upon land.
The
It will be noted that in the Bill provision has been made for the payment of
subsidies to housing associations as well
as local authorities, in which respect the
Bill follows the precedent of previous
legislation. Further provisions of the
Bill in regard to housing schemes which it
covers also follow the usual lines.
schemes must be submitted to the Ministry
of Home Affairs for approval and grants
may be made upon such terms and conditions as may be prescribed under the
Regulations. Grants under the Bill will
be payable in respect of houses erected in
pursuance of a housing scheme where the
scheme was approved by the Ministry and
the works commenced on or after 1st
April, 1944, and before the 1st January,
1947.

I beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I would
like first of all to express my own satisfaction and, I am sure, the satisfaction of
the whole House with the new Leader
whom we have secured to guide us in our
deliberations in this House. (Hon.
Members: Hear, hear.) Under the circumstances I do not think a better ap-
Housing Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
pointment could have been made. It
would be presumptuoos on my part to
attempt to indicate the many ways in
which Sir Roland Nugent is qualified for
this position, and I am quite sure he will
be able to introduce to us the business of
the House in a very lucid, intelligible and
possibly also generally persuasive way. I
wish him not only happy days, but also
length of days in this appointment which
has come to him.
With regard to the Bill which is now
before the House, it seems to me to be a
good Bill, and I can say so with more
confidence inasmuch as I had some responsibility for it when the Bill was first
drafted. The primary object of the Bill
is, as the Deputy Leader of the House
has indicated, to give authority to the
Government to pay a very large subsidy
on 750 houses for which it appears the
material can now be obtained. As the
Deputy Leader has indicated, the Bill has
been prepared in such a way that not only
the Belfast Corporation, but other local
authorities, as well as building societies
that are not local authorities, may be able
to take advantage of the terms and inducements offered after the war as
materials become available.
There are just two observations I would
like to make on this Bill, and they have
reference to post-war building, as the
Bill also has. First of all, clamant as is
the need for houses in Belfast, I would
venture to express the hope that not one
of these houses will be built until the
city is possessed of a plan of development
for the future. There is a Planning
Commission at work, and I trust that this
Planning Commission will have their proposals framed, in outline at any rate, so
that this new building will be able to be
fitted into their plan in such a way that
these new houses may not possibly be the
nucleus of fresh slums. In a report in a
newspaper to-day there is a very frank
and sound statement by the
architect of the Ministry of Home Affairs,
Mr. Winston, which I would commend to
all those who are interested in the housing
of the people. He insists with all the
strength possible that prior planning of
the layout and development is essential
if the people are to be housed in a satisfactory way after the war. Indeed, he
chief
says that new housing estates started
without planning to fit them properly
into the town were doomed to failure,
and for that statement he cites the experience of England and Wales. He also
says that efforts made for the betterment
of the people-and he referred particularly to religious instruction in
schools-will largely be in vain unless
their homes are built and sited in a
proper way.
I would go further along that same
line, and I would say that all our public
health systems, all our child welfare
schemes, all our philanthropic organisations, all our missions, and the activities"
of all our churches will to a very large
extent be in vain if the people are condemned to live in the ugliness, depression,
and squalor which has been the fate of
many of our dwellers in the towns in the
past. I hope the Planning Commission
will do their work in time to enable this
new scheme to be an ideal scheme, so
that the houses will be not only well built
so far as the individual houses are concerned, but also well laid out so far as
the sites are concerned.
The other observation I want to make
is of a still more general and tentative
nature. It is that I hope the new houses
that are to be built will be financed in
such a way that they will be within the
reach of the average working man. Great
efforts were made in this direction after
the last war, but, as we all know, the
rents of the houses were rather beyond
the possibility of the average wage earner,
so that he had to starve his family if he
were to avail himself of one of the new
houses built between the wars. I know
the problem is exceedingly difficult, and I
know also that the Belfast Corporation
did splendid work not only in the matter
of the layout of the houses but also in
regard to financing them. This £390
seems a very big subsidy that is being
offered per house, but we are told that
these new houses may cost about £1,000
each to build, and if that is so I am afraid
the ultimate inclusive cost, including interest and sinking fund, ground rent and
rates, will come to anything between 15s
and £1 per week. That will make a very
serious demand on the wage of the
average working man. I am hopeful,
however, that by something like mass production the cost of building may be very
considerably reduced, and I hope also
that by means of some scheme such as
that suggested in the Uthwatt Report the
price of land will be so controlled, in an
Housing Bill-
Second Reading
21 MARCH 1944
equitable way, that the ground rent may
not be a very heavy responsibility for
the tenants of the houses.
I trust that this preliminary scheme
for which we are now to have the
necessary materials will indeed set an
ideal before the country which will be
worthy of being followed not only by
Belfast but by other municipalities and
other associations. I wish those interested
in this scheme all possible success in the
work they have undertaken.
Mr. LESLIE: This afternoon appears
to be mainly devoted to welcomes of one
kind and another. First of all I should
like to join in the welcome already expressed to an old friend in a new position
-(Hon. Members: Hear, hear)-and to
reiterate all the good wishes that have
been expressed. The other welcome is a
welcome to the Bill with which we are
now dealing. We are all very glad to see
something started, or put in the way of
being started, to deal with this question
of housing. For a long time there has
been a great deal of talk, a very great
deal of talk, and very little result. Now
we may hope that something is really
going to be done.
The Bill, as we have it, is a general
Bill. It is not limited to Belfast; still
less is it limited to the 750 houses of
which we have been told. Seven hundred
and fifty houses is not an inconsiderable
number. They will occupy a certain
amount of ground. I do not know
whether the Parliamentary Secretary is
in a position to tell us how many houses
it is proposed to build per acre, and
therefore how many acres in Belfast will
be required for these 750 houses. Perhaps
the Parliamentary Secretary will be able
to inform us also whether the Bill goes
so far as to allow land for houses to be
acquired outside the city boundary, or
only within it. Neither of these points
are given any indication in the Bill before
us, but there will be other opportunities
wherein the information can be given.
I do not know how far it is expected
these 750 houses will go. We hear all
sorts of reports as to the number of
houses required, not only in Belfast but
throughout the Province. It has been
suggested, I think, that 100,000 houses
are wanted throughout the Province, as
a whole.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think the
Minister of Home Affairs gave that figure
recently.
Mr. LESLIE: That seems to be rather
a large proportion, because, after all, the
present population of the Province is
approximately 1,300,000, and I find it
rather hard to believe that a new house
is required for every one in thirteen of
the population. I am aware that many
of the population are married, and you
cannot build one house for a man and
another for his wife, or separate houses
for each of his children. It seems to me
that 100,000 additional houses for a
population of 1,300,000 is a little on the
large side. However, that does not
concern us at the moment, especially as
the Bill is limited to the urban districts,
and a good deal of our population is
resident outside the urban areas.
So we
may hope that the 750 houses will be,
shall we say, something more than a sop
to Cerberus, but unfortunately they will
be only a sop if unprogressive.
The right hon. Gentleman, who spoke
last spoke about the desirability of cutting
down the cost of ground rents in order
that too severe a burden might not be
placed on the pockets of the tenants. As
I read the Bill, and as I understand the
housing position generally, judging from
past proceedings under various Statutes
there will be no question of ground rents
at all. Land for houses will be bought
right out and there will be no ground
rent. The local authority may have
some sort of notional charge for ground
rent in their sinking fund payment, but
there will be no ground rent for the
tenant to pay.
The tenant will pay
ordinary house rent, and that will be
that. That makes a considerable
difference. As regards the time when
this scheme is to take effect, we are given
no indication.
Passing from that, one point in the
Bill which does give me very serious
thought is this. I have not had time, and
I am not sure that I could have had the
opportunity to find out exactly the position of all the varied positions in which
this problem of the acquisition of land for
houses for the working classes now stands.
The House will remember the various
provisions in Parts 1, 2 and 3 of the
Housing of the Working Classes Act,
1890. Under at least one of those Parts,
Housing Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
you
before could get land for these houses
you had to rely on a Provisional Order.
That, as the House will remember, means
that you had got to come before Parliament before you could get hold of the
land. Other schemes have been put forward under rather different conditions.
Going back to the Public Health Act,
there are opportunities there, but a Provisional Order has always been rather in
the background.
Then you come to the 1934 Local Government Act and the principle of what
we call a Vested Order applicable within
certain spheres, and the housing of the
working classes is not within those spheres
The Vesting Order from the financial
point of view has given complete satisfaction. I have not heard of any wellestablished complaint against a Vesting
Order on the ground of the compensation
paid, and on that universal satisfaction
has been given. But when we come to
the first step of all, the acquisition of the
land, I am very doubtful as to whether
a Vesting Order is the right procedure.
The House will remember that under the
1934 Act, I think Section 22, the powers
contained in the Order were to be limited
to acquiring land for some purposes of a
county council, or for some purposes,
of a
sanitary authority under the Public
Health Acts. Those are rather limited
purposes, and they dealt with comparatively small parcels of land. There is
nothing that would allow you to take hold
of land specifically for building 750
houses, which is likely to be a parcel of
some size.
I do not suggest that these 750 houses
will all be built in one block. They will
not. They will be scattered in several
blocks; how many
I do not know. At all
events, they will not be in one piece of
land. The land will be provided, either
by agreement or compulsion, by more than
one owner. The facts remains that as this
Bill goes on you will have throughout the
Province cases where you cannot get land
by agreement. The refusal to part with
land by agreement springs, generally, from
various causes. First of all there is the
person against whom any form of compulsion is usually directed, the person who is
acting in what is called a cussed way, and
happily there are not many of them.
But there are others who do not want to
part with that bit of land or if they do
they do not want it used for the building
of houses of this class. Those people have
rights in property and ought to be protected. I want to be assured, before this
Bill passes, that the interests of those
people are safeguarded, and that they
have that power of appeal from the
Ministry of Home Affairs to the courts
to which, as I hold, they are entitled.
I am not quite sure of the whole mass
of various regulations under which land
can be acquired at the moment, and therefore I propose, when we. reach the Committee stage-which I imagine will not
be until next week-to put down a short
amendment to leave out the Clause-I
am alluding to Clause 5-in order that
we may get a good discussion on the
subject. I am not sure that the House
will desire to leave out the whole Clause,
but they may desire to introduce some
modification in order to protect the
interests of the man who for some
reason or other does not want his
land
taken, and who desires to
have an appeal to some authority outside the Ministry of Home Affairs. I do
not think there will be any serious objection to the methods of compensation. It
ought not to be beyond the bounds of
possibility to produce a Bill that will give
satisfaction all round, and which will not
require the imposition of unnecessary
delay. The delay about which so much
is made in getting possession of the land
is really a very small item in the time that
is taken in getting the house built. It is
true you cannot start building until you
get on to the land, but I think anybody
who has any experience of building knows
that the time in which it is promised the
house shall be built and the time in which
the house is built is very often different.
Again, a great deal is made about the
cost of the land. I think anybody who
knows anything about house building
knows that the cost of the land bears very
small relation to the cost of the house.
In the old days the cost of a house was
lower than it is now because, for one
thing, wages were a good deal lower. I
do not know if the building operatives
abstained from strikes any more then
than they do now, or whether
building operatives
given
to striking now. I think experience
in other parts of the British Isles
shows that strikers are not labourers
engaged in house building, but that they
occupy another position below or above
are
more
87'

Housing Bill-
Second Reading
21 MARCH 1944
ground. There is no outstanding reason
that I can see why there should be any
great delay in getting possession of the
land.
It seems to me that the Bill is a very
good Bill on the whole, and the provision
of public funds which is required to meet
the £390 subsidy per house is a very
generous contribution on the part of the
taxpayer, for which the Government is the
channel, and for which no doubt they
will desire to take the credit. Although
I-do not begrudge them that credit, I do.
want to point out that the Government is
only administering money belonging to
other people, and that is the taxpayers.
There is no secret fund from which the
cost of house building can be financed.
However, I think the Bill has the germs
of success rather more than that of the
maturing germs.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I intend to pass
a few general remarks on the Second
Reading of this Bill. I had proposed to
look into the Bill carefully, because the
question of housing deserves the most
careful study. But circumstances prevented me doing other than taking the
most cursory glance at the Bill before us.
With regard to the congratulations that
have been extended to the new Leader of
the House. on his appointment, I do not
intend to follow the same course as the
previous speakers. Instead of congratulating the right hon. Gentleman, I shall
congratulate the Government on having
secured the services of Sir Roland Nugent
to represent it in this House. I have
great hopes that he will instil into the
Government.those constructive democratic
ideas that he has so effectively voiced in
this House. I have no doubt that the
association we have had with him in the
past will encourage those on both sides of
the House who wish to voice constructive
and democratic ideas.
With regard to the Bill itself, this is
a very preliminary step in the building
of houses. The question of the housing
of the people is a clamant and urgent
one that cannot be further postponed.
It is the duty of the Government to house
the people and to see that they are housed
in a reasonable Christian manner. If the
Government themselves do not do it it is
the duty of Parliament to urge the Government to take all possible steps to see
that the people are reasonably and properly housed. I myself believe that if
the Government and Parliament fail to
cope with such a vital question affecting
the lives of the people, then the people
themselves will after this war take steps
to see that they have a reasonable opportunity of rearing their families under
Christian conditions. I hope-and this
is not mere political window dressingthat there will be a serious effort made
by the Government to immediately get
to grips with this problem.
I more or less disagree with the two
previous speakers. I think that valuable
preliminary steps can be taken at once
to develop housing schemes. If you are
going to acquire land it will be land on
which there is no sewage or water supply,
and if land is obtained at once the work
of putting in these essential services could
be proceeded with, so that immediately
building materials became available the
houses could be built without delay. With
regard to the remarks of the right hon.
and learned Senator about the payment
of compensation, as I have said, I have
not had an opportunity of studying the
provisions of this Bill and I do not know
what methods of paying compensation it
contains, but perhaps I am the only
Member of this House who can speak
with practical knowledge of the working
of these schemes. I have been engaged
by landowners to protect their interests
where land has been acquired by local
authorities for various purposes, and when
the arbitration was over and the
arbitrator had made his award I have
felt that the landowner suffered a
grievious wrong in many instances
through not getting adequate compensation for the land that was taken over.
The principal reason was that in those
cases he had not the right of appeal to
the court, which is the one independent
tribunal that can deal impartially and
to the satisfaction of the public with these
matters, because the courts are trained
to measure evidence and come to a proper
and just conclusion. I remember one
particular case where a man bought a
holding. It was not a very large holding,
but it contained very substantial buildings, including a dwelling-house and outhouses. It was convenient to a market
town. The local authority took a very
substantial part of the holding from him
and thereby rendered it entirely uneconomic from an agricultural point of
view. In my judgment that man received
Housing Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
nothing in compensation but the common
agricultural value of the land. That is
a state of affairs against which it is up
to us to protect people. The Government
have got the courts to which appeals can
be taken, and the public generally would
be satisfied to go to the courts.
There is another point. I believe,
although I do not say I am right in that
belief, that this Bill does not confer any
powers on rural district councils, and does
not give them a share in any money that
is to be disbursed by the Government for
the building of houses. That is an
omission that requires a good deal of
justification on the part of the Government, if I am right in believing that the
Measure does not apply to rural district
councils. We have heard that the housing
situation in Belfast is very deplorable,
and we all sypmathise with Belfast, but
we who live in the country know of the
leaking thatched roofs and earthen floors
of dwellings in the rural areas. Surely
the rural population requires some consideration as well as those in the city of
Belfast. These are matters which I hope
will be further ventilated at a later stage
in the Bill.
new
The LORD MAYOR OF BELFAST
(Sir Crawford McCullagh): Perhaps I
may be allowed to join with the previous
speakers in congratulating the
Leader of the House on his appointment.
Not only will he have the good wishes of
this House, but I am sure his appointment will give general satisfaction to the
public. (Hon. Members: Hear, hear.)
As far as this Bill is concerned, I will
whole-heartedly support it, and I wish to
express my appreciation of what the
Government has already done. Seven
hundred and fifty houses may not seem a
great many, but still it is a fair proportion of what England and Scotland are
getting, although we are entitled to more
than our proportion of the number of
houses allotted to England and Scotland
because we have not had our quota in the
past. However, the Government have
gone a long way, and, in my opinion, the
subsidy of £390 per house is very
generous.
The right hon. Gentleman opposite
(Rev. Professor Corkey) seemed to think
that these houses should not be commenced until the Planning Act is put
into operation. These houses are wanted
at once. You have no idea of the
shortage of houses in Belfast. To erect
750 is only touching the fringe of the
need. We want many, many thousands
of houses, and consequently I hope that
nothing will interfere with the passage
of this Bill that will result in stopping
the immediate building of these houses.
The right hon. and learned Gentleman
opposite (Mr. Leslie) talked about the
cost of land and said there would be no
ground rent to pay in respect of these
houses. There is bound to be a ground
rent whether you rent the land or purchase it outright. If you purchase the
ground you have to provide for a ground
rent in fixing the rent of the house, but
the ground rent is not a very serious item
in the cost of the house; it is the cost of
construction.
This Bill deals with another important
question-the compulsory acquisition of
land. Anyone in public life knows very
well that the minute the Corporation
becomes
want ground it
the most
valuable piece of land on God's earth,
and up goes the price. People seem to
think they can get whatever they ask, and
some people, at any rate, seem to think
that the more they ask the more they
will get.
What is the position in Belfast, and
am speaking only for Belfast. Owing t
the confined space in our city, and to the
want of an extension of the boundary,
which is long overdue, the building ground
in the centre of the city is very limited,
indeed. One line of thought seems to
be that we should build immediately on
part of blitzed sites, but, rightly or
wrongly, I hold the opinion that a broad
view should be taken, that our city
should be planned on altogether different
lines as far as possible, and that these
congested areas should not be built on.
That is my view. Then you come to the
question, where are you going to get
ground in this city? It cannot be had,
because the central ground is too valuable
for workers' houses. What are you going
to do? I take it that this Bill gives
power to go outside the boundary to
secure ground, and that is what will have
to be done, because I do not believe that
ground can be got in this city to build
the houses required. You might possibly
put up 750 houses in the suburbs, but you
have not got the ground to build all the
houses that are needed.

Sir JOSEPH DAVIDSON: In reply to

has been nothing definite decided in that
direction up to the present, and it could
not be decided until sites are acquired.
As regards the question of going outside

existing law a local authority can build.
outside its boundary with the consent of
the adjoining local authority. Another
point was raised about the owner of land
who is dissatisfied with the award of the
arbitrator. Under the existing law, if
the owner of the land is dissatisfied with
the arbitration award, and the amount
exceeds £100, he may appeal to the
county court under the Local Government
Act of 1934. In reply to a point raised
by Senator McAllister, rural areas
secure financial assistance under the
Labourers Act up to 40 per cent. of the
loan charges, which is practically the same
as the local authorities will receive under
this Bill.

Constabulary Bill.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time".-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: May I take
this opportunity of thanking the House
and all those right hon. and hon. Members who have spoken of me so kindly.
It gives one a little heart in a rather
am afraid I
unnerving experience. I
shall require more than even the usual
indulgence the House extends to a new
Leader, because I am learning two new
jobs simultaneously. One of them appears
to be an almost unlimited job, but I am
sure the House will be kind to me.
This Constabulary Bill has been largely
occasioned by the fact that the war interrupted the building of police barracks in
Northern Ireland. Owing to the war the
construction of police barracks had to be
suspended, and it seems probable that in
the future the demands for building
material for other purposes will be so
great that the programme of constructing
new barracks will have to proceed very
slowly. It is for that reason that in this
Bill the House is asked to extend the
Ministry's powers to acquire land compulsorily. In 1924 an Act called The
Constabulary (Acquisition of Land) Act
was passed giving power to the Ministry
of Home Affairs to acquire lands compulsorily for the purpose of providing
accommodation for the Royal Ulster
Constabulary Bill
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
Constabulary. The operation of the Act
was limited to five years, but it has been
renewed from time to time. It is due to
expire shortly and the Bill before us is
intended to extend the Ministry's powers
till 1954.
I am not aware that there has ever
been any difficulty in regard to the
acquisition of sites for police barracks.
My own personal experience in this connection has been a happy one. The police
asked me for a site, I suggested one, and
they made me an offer for the land which
seemed to be extremely handsome. In any
case, police barracks in the country are
not very large buildings, and the amount
of land taken is not very great. I beg
to move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: When on a previous
occasion an extension of the Constabulary
Act was made we were told that during
the period there had been only two or
three occasions on which it had been
necessary to exercise compulsory powers.
Consequently there is no need to suppose
that there will be a great many cases in
the future, and I think the House ought
to limit the further extension to five
years. You are dealing with an Act of
1924, and it is now 1944. There have
been twenty years in which to get this
matter of constabulary barracks in order.
It is true that of those twenty years five
have been obliterated by the war, but
another five years ought to see the programme of barrack construction completed.
There cannot be many constabulary
barracks left to be built, or much land
suitable for barracks. It is rather a
hardship on a man within that uncircumscribed area that he does not know
whether he will be jumped on or not. I
do not think there is any reason to suppose that, if the Ministry of Home
Affairs makes up its mind, it cannot get
as many barracks as it wants built in the
next five years. If it cannot do the job
in the twenty-five years that will then'
have elapsed I do not think there is any
case for compulsion. The possibility is
always open to the Government to come
to Parliament again if it is necessary.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday.
