CLAUSE
1 (Further extension of
period for compulsory acquisition of
land).
The power of the Ministry of Home
Affairs to acquire land compulsorily for
the purposes mentioned in sub-section
(1) of section one of the Constabulary
(Acquisition of Land) Act (Northern
Ireland), 1924, may be exercised at any
time not later than the twenty-eighth
day of May, nineteen hundred and
fifty-four, and the proviso to the said
sub-section, as amended by section
seven of the Constabulary Act (Northern Ireland), 1928, and section two
of the Constabulary Act (Northern
Ireland), 1933, shall be further amended
accordingly.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in
Clause 1, page 1, line 10, to leave out the
word "fifty-four" ("nineteen hundred
and fifty-four") and insert instead thereof
the word "forty-nine." I mentioned this
little matter on the Second Reading
of the Bill. The proposal is, as hon.
Members will see, to extend the life of
this Bill, or at least the life of so much of
it as gives power to the Ministry of Home
Affairs to compulsorily acquire land, for
a further period of ten years. The Committee will remember the past history of
the Bill. The Act was passed in 1924
for a life of five years.
The work conthe
templated under
Bill was not
completed during those five years, and
Parliament, in its generosity, gave the
Ministry of Home Affairs another five
years to finish the work. The Ministry
of Home Affairs does not seem to have
been able to accomplish the work even in
ten years, and Parliament gave them another ten years in 1933.
When that proposal was before the
Committee I pointed out that in my
judgment ten years was rather too long,
and I said I thought five years was
sufficient extension to allow. The then
Leader of the House was not unsympathetic to my suggestion, but it
seemed that, on the whole, the damage
likely to be done was not very great, and
the proposal was not pressed. Now the
ten years have expired, and the present
is
position
that
of
the Ministry
Home Affairs has had twenty years to
build its barracks in Northern Ireland.
It is fair to remind the Committee that
of those twenty years five have been lost.
Constabulary Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
owing to the war. But that means that
they have had fifteen years, and one
wants to know what steps were taken
between 1929 and 1933, or, even more,
what steps were taken between 1933 and
1939 when the war began. In 1939 the
Home Office had had fifteen or sixteen
years to complete their programme of
barrack building, and now they ask for
a, further ten years. Having regard to
the war one is not disposed to quibble
about a few years, and I am prepared to
consent to them having another five
years, which is the proposal in the
Amendment, but I do not think we
ought to go as far as ten years, because,
if we do, that is admitting that the
power once given for five years is to be
extended practically indefinitely.
The
If we give the Ministry ten years now,
that will have given them thirty years to
accomplish work which was originally
supposed to be done in five years.
General Post Office, which has establishments of one kind or another in every
town and village in Northern Ireland,
manages to acquire its premises as it
requires them without compulsory powers,
and it seems a little difficult to understand
why the authority cannot get them for
police barracks. Also there is this difficulty; that, if you leave the compulsory
powers in the air, a man within a reasonable distance of where a police barracks
might perhaps be established is left in
some doubt as to whether his land is or
is not going to be taken. That unfortunate position may not affect him very
greatly, but the possibility of having your
land compulsorily taken is still there, and
although it is sometimes in the interests
of the country that it should be taken,
the duration of the period ought to be
circumscribed as much as possible.
If the Committee agrees with me, and
if during those five years the Ministry is
still unable to complete its programme,
there is nothing to stop them coming
back to Parliament and asking for further
time, and I think that is the course that
should be taken. But thirty years is too
long, and accordingly I beg to move my
Amendment to limit the period to five
years.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): The real question is
one of convenience. It is impossible at
the moment to foresee when the
barrack reconstruction programme can be
resumed. It obviously cannot be resumed
until the war is over, and it is equally
certain that after the war there will be a
very serious shortage of building material
and labour. What will be available will
have to go to the building of domestic
houses. Consequently it is probable that
for some years at least after the war this
barrack reconstruction programme will
enjoy a very low priority for materials.
and labour. Therefore, it is highly
probable that very little can be done in
the first five of the ten years for which
the Government is asking in this Bill.
That being so, it is almost certain that in
fact we would have to come back to the
House in five years time and ask for a
further extension.
The compulsory powers provided for in
this Bill are not really very serious. I
understand there are only three instances
in which they have been exercised in the
past, but it is always possible, in the case
of police barracks, that it may be necessary, for what I may call strategic reasons,
that a barrack should be in a particular
place, in relation to a town. It is really
for those cases, where you might possibly
find an obstinate owner of land, that these
powers are needed. As I have said, it is
really a matter of convenience. If the
Committee feels that it would be more
reasonable to shorten the period I am
prepared to accept the Amendment. But
I do honestly think that, in that case, we
shall have to come back to Parliament in
five years time, and it is really a case of
saving yourselves or your successors
certain amount of trouble then. However, I am quite prepared to leave it to
the Members of the Committee.
a
Mr. LESLIE: I still think that if it is
unfortunately necessary to continue after
the five years the Ministry ought to come
back to Parliament. I think we ought
to be assured that they have done everything possible to carry out their work
within the time set by Parliament. This
sort of question is frequently before
Parliament. One thinks of cases in which
Boards are given so many years to raise
their capital and carry out certain worką,
and they have not done it and have had
to come back to Parliament. We have
to maintain this principle of control by
Parliament. We originally gave five

Housing Bill.

In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair].
CLAUSE 1 (Grants to local authorities constructing working-class houses).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill."
Mr. LESLIE: Before we pass from
this Clause I would like to ask the right
hon. Gentleman the Leader of the House
whether he could give us what I might
describe as an up-to-date explanation of
the expression, "persons of the working
classes."
As the Committee knows, we
have had this question up and down the
Statute Book for very many years, and
it has been considered in relation to all
sorts of things. Sometimes it has been
the value of the house, sometimes the
amount of garden to be attached to the
house, and I think that once, and only
once, has it been related to the wages
drawn. The one exception where the
wages were referred to one that I have
been unable to lay my hands on- -is of a
good many years standing. It is the Act
of 1903, which has since been repealed,
and which gave a very comprehensive
definition of artisans as being those whose
wages did not exceed 30s per week. The
30s a week has, so to speak, gone west a
long time ago.
We had in the Settled Land Act a very
good definition of "persons of the working classes" as including everybody who
got their livelihood by wages or salary.
That is a very wide definition and would
include anyone earning up to the limit
of the National Health Insurance Acts,
which I think is £420 a year, and that
would take you into the class of persons
whom you might expect would have saved
sufficient to enable them to buy a house
for themselves. As it is now, with wages
in some cases at fantastic rates, we seem
to be left with nothing like a modern
definition. The point is of some importance because complaint is so often made
-in fact it was voiced a few days ago in
another place that persons for whom
these houses were intended are not, in
fact, getting them, and that they are
going to persons of a rather different
standard, perhaps too much to-to use
Lord Salisbury's phrase the man who
wears a black coat, and so on. That has
been the complaint not only in the towns,
but in the country under the Labourers
Acts, so that if we could get a definition
of what is now in the Government's mind
it would be desirable to put it on record.
It is not a case for inserting any particular Amendment in this Bill.
Mr. BROWNE: I also had intended to
ask the Leader of the House for a definition of the term "working classes". I
should also like to know if private individuals, in addition to housing associations and local authorities, are entitled
to this subsidy if they embark upon
housing for the working classes and, of
course, conform to the Statutory conditions.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In reply to
Senator Leslie, a definition of "working
classes" was given in Section 16 of the
Housing of the Working Classes (Ireland)
Housing Bill-
Committee
28 MARCH 1944
Act, 1908, which was very similar to that
given in the Act of 1903, to which the
right hon. and learned Senator referred.
This definition was amended by Section
24 of the Housing Act (Northern Ireland), 1939. The amended definition
reads as follow:
The expression, "working classes"
shall include mechanics, artisans,
labourers, and others working for
wages, hawkers, costermongers, persons
not working for wages but working at
some trade or handicraft without
employing others except members of
their own family, and persons, other
than domestic servants whose income in
any case does not exceed an average of
45s a week, and the families of any of
such persons who may be residing with
them.
I am informed that this definition is
not an exclusive one. It states that the
expression "working classes" shall include
the list I read, but that does not mean
that other persons cannot be included
within the scope of the expression. An
almost identical definition of the term
appears in the English Housing Act of
1936. Speaking personally, I think the
definition of "working classes" is a
Victorian definition that does not apply
to the present day, but the definition I
have read will give a general indication of
what is in the mind of the legislature.
With regard to the point raised by
Senator Browne, I understand that, as
far as this Bill is concerned, it gives the
subsidy only to local authorities and
housing associations as defined in the Bill.
Mr. MCALLISTER: When the Leader
of the House was giving his definition of
"working classes"-I think the last
definition was given in 1906-
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In 1939.
Mr. MCALLISTER: It did occur to me
that the value of money and the standard
of wages have definitely altered since
1939, and therefore I think the 1939
definition of "working classes" would not
and could not apply to-day. Many
of the working classes who were
included in the 1939 definition would be
exempt to-day if that definition is
retained.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: If I read
this definition rightly, "working classes"
"
includes the long list of artisans I read
out. They are included in any event,
whatever their wages may be, and in
addition those other persons whose wages
are not more than 45s a week. As I
have said, this definition is not exclusive,
and other persons can be included.
think that what the definition really
means to cover is persons with fairly low
incomes.

CLAUSE 5 (Vesting orders for compulsory acquisition of land for purposes
of the Housing Acts).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:-
At the end of Clause 5 to add the
following proviso:-
Provided that, before making any
such order in pursuance of the power
conferred by this section, the Ministry
shall have regard to the effect which

the immediate neighbourhood of the
land proposed to be acquired.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: This Amendment is put down in the hope of, in
some degree, meeting the views expressed
by hon. Members on the Second Reading
debate. I am quite aware that it does
not completely meet all the points raised,
but we were in some difficulty. The
whole object of this measure is to
accelerate the procedure in the acquisition of land. We have not included in
the Amendment eny appeal to the
Committee
Housing Bill-

THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
county court, because it might involve
considerable delay. There is the further
objection, that if there was an appeal to
the county court before a Vesting Order
was made, only the owner of the land it
was proposed to acquire could appeal,
while the neighbouring owners who
might be affected would not be able to
appeal. We thought that, on the whole,
the best thing to do would be to insert
this proviso, which will, at any rate,
draw the attention not only of the Ministry but of the local authorities to the
necessity of looking very carefully into
the possible consequential damage they
might do.
I think that in all these cases where
the compulsory acquisition of property
is involved there is always a certain
danger of hardship or injustice to individuals, and one must consider that
danger against the whole general interest
of the public, which in this instance is.
that the acquisition of land should
proceed expeditiously. I think that when
we remember that two very responsible
bodies, the local authority and then the
Ministry, are going to look into this
matter, having had their attention drawn
to it, it will be agreed that we are going
as far as we can to avoid hardship.
Another point raised in the course of
the Second Reading debate was the question of the compensation to be awarded.
Under the procedure already laid down.
there is an appeal to the county court if
the award is more than £100. I beg to
move.
an
Mr. MCALLISTER: I would like to be
told what is the legal interpretation of
the words in the Amendment, "lands in
the immediate neighbourhood." Passing
from that, there is another point to which
I want to address myself. As the Leader
of the House has stated, there is
appeal to the county court if the amount
of the award is over £100. I take it that
the reason for allowing an appeal at all
is because it is an appeal from the personal
opinion of the arbitrator, who is not
infallible and may be wrong.
He may
be equally wrong whether he is giving
an award up to £100 or over £100. The
person who receives only £100 may very
often be a man of small means, and £100
would be more vital to him than would
several hundred pounds to the man who
is in a better financial position.
If the right hon. Gentleman intends to
persist with the Clause as it stands and
refuses to allow a general appeal irrespective of the amount of the compensation,
I would like him to indicate to the Committee by what process of reasoning or
logic did the Government arrive at the
conclusion that where the arbitrator's
award is under £100 there should be no
appeal, but where it is over £100 there
should be the right of appeal. In assessing compensation several factors, apart
from the actual value of the land, may
arise. There may be amenities and easements of water or rights-of-way affected,
but if the arbitrator comes to the conclusion that the only damage suffered
amounts to under £100, the owner of the
land is deprived from going to the county
court judge, who might well find that,
apart from the value of the land, the
damages in respect of these amenities and
easements may be £150, £200, or even
£300. Yet that man would have no right
of appeal from the personal opinion of the
arbitrator.
I do not know what will be the reply
of the Government to this argument, but
in case it should be that an award of
£100 or under does not justify an appeal
to the county court, I would like to say
that after many years of a pretty wide
experience of the county court I think it
is an ideal poor man's tribunal. I do not
think it could be improved upon for
adjusting differences between people. The
costs are infinitesimal, so that that should
not be allowed to enter into the question.
On the other hand the old rule,
"To the
victor the spoils," should apply, and if
a man appeals from an award of under
£100 and does not succeed in getting it
increased, he is rightly entitled to pay
the costs of the appeal, which would be
very small. This question might affect
very many people, and I would strongly
press my personal views and long years.
of experience on the Government and ask
them to amend this Clause by making the
appeal to the county court general instead
of confining it to awards of under £100.
Mr. LESLIE: With regard to the last
remarks of Senator McAllister, I think,

of giving an appeal to the county court
when the compensation is less than £100
was discussed in this House when the

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Acreage Payment for Potatoes, Flax
and Cereals.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether the acreage payment for potatoes, flax, wheat and rye are in the
nature of an insurance to the farmer,
or are they devised with a view to
keeping down the price of the crops
produced to the consumer.
Major DOBBS: In asking this question about the acreage payment for
potatoes, flax, wheat and rye, I should
like, first of all, to make a remark or
two about the acreage payment for
potatoes. When this payment for potatoes was first announced the farmers considered that it was a special inducement
to them to grow potatoes. Potatoes were
wanted. They looked upon the payment
as an insurance. The acreage payment
just paid for the cost of the seed. They
understood that provided they grew
potatoes in a bona fide manner they
would get the subsidy. If they had bad
weather-and bad weather has been the
crux of the matter during the last year
or so they thought they would still get
the subsidy. I speak on behalf of many
farmers in my district and in other districts who were beaten by the weather,
and in these cases the payment was
refused. I do ask that a special allowance may be made for those farmers, at
any rate in the future.
It should be remembered that these
farmers are working right up to the top
of their capabilities. Each has cultivated five times, perhaps ten times,
perhaps more, the amount of land that he
cultivated in peace time. With these
men about whom I am speaking it has
been a question of plough, plough,
plough; not a question of just scratching
the land. The Ministry of Agriculture
talk about good husbandry. That is
excellent, but I am afraid that their good
husbandry is taken from what is good
husbandry in the south-east counties of
England, not in Ulster. If a farmer in
Hampshire were to cut his oats in September he would be the mark of the whole
county. I have cut oats in September
and have been thankful to be able to do
So. If these acreage payments are not in
the nature of an inducement or an insurance to the farmer they appear to be made
so that the maximum price which the
farmer would obtain for his potatoes can
be reduced. If this is the case I think
we should be told so, and it should be
recognised that the Imperial Parliament
are benefiting, not the farmer, but the
consumer.
of
There is another small question,
which I gave private notice to the right
hon. Gentleman, the Leader of the House,
that I should very much like to ask with
regard to the acreage payment for wheat.
Take it that winter wheat is sown on some
field and then, owing perhaps to heavy
rain and severe frost coming close
together, when the wheat appears in the
spring it seems to be going to be a partial
failure. The good farmer would naturally
sow some oats over his wheat in order to
get the biggest yield of grain when he
comes to the harvest. The result would
be dredge corn. The question I want to
ask is, will those acres still be eligible for
the wheat acreage payment?
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Sir Roland Nugent): Perhaps I had
better first give the official answer, and
then I think I can add a certain amount
of information, because I have had the
benefit of the advice of the Department.
The official answer is:-
In cases where a crop failure is due
to the non-observance of the rules of
good husbandry acreage payments are
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
not payable, and cannot, therefore, be
regarded as an insurance to farmers in
such cases.
As regards the second part of the
hon. Senator's question, the acreage
payment to the grower is one of the
methods of reducing the cost of the
crops to the consumer. In addition, it
provides an advance payment to those
growers who are unable to market their
crops until late in the season.
In
I think the full meaning of that
answer will be a little clearer if I explain
the way in which the acreage payment
system came into being. As the House
will realise, the acreage payment system
is not peculiar to Northern. Ireland. It
is a United Kingdom system which is
merely in force in Northern Ireland, not
particularly because it is appropriate to
Northern Ireland, but because we must
follow United Kingdom measures.
the United Kingdom, unlike Northern
Ireland, the farmer can be ordered to
grow certain crops in certain fields, and
the primary object of the acreage payment was to provide some partial compensation to the farmer who was ordered
to grow a crop on land which was not
really suitable for that crop. Here the
farmer is free to grow what crop he
wishes, provided his total tillage area is
sufficient.
Looking at the matter in the light of
that information, the acreage payment is
in
a limited sense an insurance to the
farmer provided he observes the rules of
good husbandry, because it protects the
farmer in England against having to
grow a crop in unsuitable ground, and in
this country it protects the farmer
against uncontrollable misfortune, but it
is not an insurance against mistakes in
his own husbandry. It is impossible to
lay down a general rule which would
cover all possible cases that might arise
under the heading of good husbandry.
In one of their circulars to farmers the
Ministry of Agriculture set out the main
points of what they regard as good
husbandry in Northern Ireland. That
circular says :-
Good Husbandry includes:
1. Growing each crop on suitable land.
2. Preparing the land properly.
3. Sowing or planting at the right time.
4. Using good quality seed.
5. Manuring adequately applying
1-4-1 mixture to potatoes and potash
to flax.
6. Cultivating, weeding and spraying
potato crops thoroughly.
7. Weeding flax.
8. Harvesting properly and in good
time.
Obviously each case has to be judged
on its merits as to whether husbandry
has been good or not, and I am informed
that the Ministry always take into
account any relevant factors which may,
in spite of good husbandry, have caused
a crop to fail.
Before they reach a
decision the land concerned is inspected,
first of all by the tillage officer. After
his report is received, and before a final
decision is taken, the land is inspected
again by a senior officer of the Ministry.
In the end the opinion of the farmer and
the Ministry may differ as to whether
good husbandry has been practised or not,
but it is clear that the Ministry do
exercise very considerable care.
Turning to the second question asked
by the hon. and gallant Senator, we come
again to the question of good husbandry.
If in the sowing of winter wheat the
normal rules of good husbandry were
observed, and in spite of that, owing to
the stress of weather or some other reason
the crop is unsatisfactory, the farmer will
be entitled to his acreage payment.
Secondly, he may then, if he wishes, oversow with oats, or, if he desires-and I
am informed this was done last yearhe can plough up again for early spring
and put in a crop of potatoes or another
crop. If he does put in
a crop of
potatoes he will get the potato subsidy
as well as the wheat subsidy. There were
actual cases of that being done last year.
There were actual cases of two crops of
potatoes being grown and the double
subsidy given, and quite rightly, because
the acreage payment is part of the price
of potatoes. Always subject to good
husbandry, the farmer will be entitled to
his acreage payment.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
think the House and the farmers will be
obliged to the right hon. Gentleman for
his very lucid answer. However, I still
think the point which the hon. and
gallant Senator made so clearly has not
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
30 MARCH 1944
The
been completely answered by the Department. What is good husbandry?
first principle of good husbandry in farming, as in business, is that a man does
not over-trade. In other words, he does
not attempt to plough up more than he
can plough up having regard to the
weather and the state of his land. But
the Ministry themselves are responsible
for a breach of the first and most important principle of good husbandry, and
as long as they maintain their ploughing
regulations as they are at present no
farmer can be held responsible for bad
husbandry. If you plough up a certain
amount of land and put it in cereal crop
you cannot go on growing that cereal crop
year after year off the same land. That
means that the following year you must
put in some other crop, a root crop for
instance.
There are only certain crops that can
be sown in this country. There is a glut
of cabbages in the market, and it is quite
impossible to make cabbages pay unless
you are living near Belfast or some other
town. It therefore follows that whether
the land is suitable or not the farmer must
grow a large quantity of potatoes, and
there is a great amount of land in Northern Ireland, particularly in the area
from which the hon. and gallant Senator
(Major Dobbs) comes, that is not suitable
for potatoes. I am quite convinced that
the heavy clay soils of Northern Ireland
should not be asked to grow potatoes, and
by making these soils bear year after year
crops of cereals, the Ministry of Agriculture are definitely preventing the farmer
from complying with the rules of good
husbandry. There are a number of cases
in which the Ministry should be more
selective in their plans.
There was a time, perhaps at the
beginning of the war, when it was necessary to grow grain on land which would
produce only five hundredweight to the
acre, but now that is nonsense.
We are
now beginning on the second rotation of
crops, and it is high time the Ministry
adopted their own principles which are,
as I say, that land should be cultivated in
a good way. The first principle of farming is that the crop should be selected on
rotation, should be organised according to
the state of the land, the type of weather,
and other limiting factors which all
farmers have to deal with, and until the
Ministry realise their responsibilities in
the matter no farmer can be blamed for
bad farming.
I

would like to know if, in arriving at an

Mr. LESLIE: What I would like to

specifically referred to are part of the

the whole object of requiring a particular

of the farmer, who has to go on with the
dice heavily loaded against him. Is it
not the fact, as we have seen during the
last two years, that many farmers have
had to till land which they know from
their own experience on their own farms,
is not suitable for tillage. I do not think
there can be
any doubt that in the past two

put under the plough which would have
been much better left in grass. The point
is that the system has gone on for four
years now, and land that has been broken
once, and carried on without a break
through two very wet years has sometimes
been sown out in grass. The time is
almost coming when, if more care is not
exercised in applying the general rule of
tillage throughout Northern Ireland,
that land sown out in grass since the
beginning of this war will have to be
ploughed again. Meantime the farmer,
following the rules of good husbandry
as far as he understands it, has perhaps
put in a fairly expensive selection of
grass seed, which has risen in price
enormously since the war started. The

cost of sowing a field now is very much

As regards the other matter of the

Apparently the Department, and I suggest to the House that good husbandry
here and there are not necessarily the

Major DOBBS: I am asking if the

ORDER OF THE DAY.
HOUSING BILL.
Order for receiving Report of Amendments read.
Resolved, "That this Report be now
received."(Sir Roland Nugent.)
The said Amendments were accordingly
reported and agreed to by the House.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read the
Third time."
Question proposed.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I would just like to
say as shortly as possible that this Bill
will not directly contribute to the erection of houses in Northern Ireland. At
the same time it is a very necessary and
essential preliminary to that object. I
would like to impress upon the Government the urgency of this question of
housing, and I say that this Bill will be
a test of their earnestness and bona
fides as to whether they are prepared to
face up to this very serious problem. This
Bill on the face of it delegates to a great
extent the responsibility of providing
houses to the local authorities, but the
Housing Bill-
Third Reading
30 MARCH 1944
Government cannot absolve itself from
the responsibility and moral obligation
of housing the people of Northern Ireland in a reasonably proper manner.
This Bill provides procedure whereby
land can be acquired in a few days after
the passing of the Bill for the purpose
of carrying out building schemes.
While the material for the building of
houses may not be available, the bona
fides of the Government can be tested
during the next few months by their
work in preparing sites and laying down
drainage, water, electricity, and gas
mains preparatory to the actual erection
of the houses when the material becomes
available. I hope the Members of this
House, and the general public, will after
the expiration of a reasonable time, and
after giving the Government an opportunity of carrying out this preparatory
work, raise the question again if the
Government prove to be deficient in their
efforts.
Mr. BARCLAY: I would like to ask
the Leader of the House one question.
Will the housing association be responsible
for making roads and avenues through
the land they take over. In the past
that has not been the case in the rural
districts. I know of one avenue with
over 45 houses, the tenants of which are
paying rates to the county council. The
owner of the land had to make the road
to a specified size, put metal on it, and
have it tarred, and when he had done
all that and asked the county council to
take it over they would not do so because
the avenue led nowhere-it was a dead
end. It did not lead out to any public
road. There are 45 houses in that
avenue, each with a valuation of between
£30 and £40, and the tenants are paying
rates and, I suppose, taxes. I would like
to ask if this housing association will be
responsible for making roads and avenues
with dead ends, and keeping them in
repair.
Mr. LESLIE: At this stage in the proceedings I must not, of course, deal with
or allude to anything that is not in the
Bill. We are confined to what is in the
Bill, and I do think the occasion is, perhaps, not unsuitable for pointing out once
more two rather salient facts. One of
them is that the Bill is limited to urban
district councils of one sort and another,
starting with the Corporation of Belfast
and going down to the smallest urban
district in the Province. Therefore,
there is nothing in the Bill about the provision of houses in rural districts.
Another point I want to lay stress on
is that there is nothing in the Bill which
derogates from the importance of schemes.
That is to say, before a local authority,
which must be an urban district council,
can do anything, it has got to have a
scheme ready, and it. has got to get that
scheme approved. That is very important. There was an illustration in the
newspapers this morning, where it was
stated that in the immediate neighbourhood of the city of Londonderry there is
a plot of land of 70 acres which it is
proposed to make use of for housing purposes, and it was stated in the paper that
a great many of the houses on that land
were to be built by private enterprise.
The others, I presume, are to be built
under the authority of the Corporation of
Londonderry.
As this Bill stands, the private enterprise man, if he in fact exists as to which
I have no knowledge-would not get a
grant, but his case, so to speak, would
stand over. Unless there is a scheme put
forward by that local authority, the local
authority will not get anything either.
Those two points have to be remembered,
and I hope it will be remembered, when.
this Bill is passed, that it is limited to
urban district councils, and secondly, the
importance of preparing a scheme must
not be overlooked.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I would like
to call attention to the very important
statement bearing on this question made
by Mr. Churchill in his speech on Sunday
night. His statement is very relevant to
our problem here. In England, just as
here, land for housing, and particularly
for working-class houses in the neighbourhood of large cities, is becoming very
scarce and increasingly dear. That fact
has been before the minds of the Legislature in England, and I think it is a
circumstance which is not unknown to our
own Government. However, in England,
as Mr. Churchill has pointed out, they
have taken steps to deal with the situation, which was becoming increasingly
difficult as land became increasingly
scarce. Mr. Churchill says they have
fixed a ceiling to prices for land for public
purposes and for housing, and that they
Housing Bill
Strike in Belfast
THE SENATE
[Rev. Professor Corkey]
will introduce legislation in the present
Session to enable local authorities to purchase land for these purposes at the prices
that prevailed at the 31st March, 1939.
Now the price of everything has been
soaring since then, and Mr. Churchill
recognised that fact, but he says, in
justification of this declaration of his,
that land is peculiar in several ways, and
that the Government feel justified in
making this declaration. I need hardly
read his words, as I have no doubt that
most of the Members of this House heard
his speech, but I may just read a couple
of sentences :
Then we are told by the busy wiseacres: How can you build houses
without the land to put them on?
When are you going to tell us your
plans for this? But we have already
declared, in 1941, that all land needed
for public purposes shall be taken at
prices based on the standards of values
of March 31st, 1939.
May I express the hope that in this
matter, as in others, our Government will
keep in step with England. It is a most
important matter. The price of land will
send up the rents of houses, and I think
our Government would be justified in
adopting the same policy here as has been.
adopted by the Parliament at Westminster.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: With regard to the point raised by Senator
Barclay, I am informed that no houses
have been built by housing associations
outside Belfast, so that the houses he
referred to must have been built by
private enterprise.
Mr. BARCLAY: I understood that
this housing association will be extending
its activities to the rural districts. These
houses are in a rural district, and I
would like to know if this housing
association will be responsible for the
making and the upkeep of roads, seeing
that the county council will draw rates
from the houses.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: These houses
were not built by a housing association?
Mr. BARCLAY: No, by a private
builder.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Any houses
erected under this Bill will have to be
built under an approved scheme, and it
is hardly conceivable that the Ministry
would approve of a scheme that did not
include roadmaking. With reference to
the question raised by Rev. Professor
Corkey regarding Mr. Churchill's statement about the price of land, I am sure
he will not expect me to deal to-day with
such a big principle, but I am certain
it is a point that is present to the mind
of the Government here. At the same
time I would like to point out that Mr.
Churchill was careful to say that all land
needed for public purposes shall be
taken "at prices based on the standard
of values of March 31st, 1939." To my
mind that means that the 1939 prices
would be the basis, and that they would
be liable to adjustment to suit the
circumstances of the time.
It is a very
difficult problem, and it would be very
easy to inflict most serious injustices, but
I am sure it is a point that has been or
will be examined by our Government.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

pass." (Sir Roland Nugent.)

Strike in Belfast.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, "That this House do now adjourn

Question proposed.
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
desire to raise the matter of the deplorable
strike that is at present taking place in
our city. I am sorry I have not been able
to give the Leader of the House due and
timely warning, but after the tributes
paid to him to-day on the manner in
which he has dealt with questions I am
sure he can answer anything. I am much
concerned about this strike. The men concerned are men I formerly worked with,
and for many years I was one of the
Strike in Belfast
Strike in Belfast
30 MARCH 1944
principal officials of their organisation. I
am extremely sorry to see that they have
taken drastic action by ceasing work
before all the negotiations were completed and the terms of settlement
announced. It can only be described as
an illegal stoppage, but I am more concerned about the reputation of our city.
The workers here have won very high
praise for the work they have done during
the past four years. They have worked
very consistently, and they have worked
on Saturday afternoons and Sundays and
extra hours on other days, and they were
given credit for that by some of the
officials across the water. I am, therefore,
sorry that action has been taken which is
likely to rob the men of that good reputation.
I am even more anxious about the effect
it will have on the war effort. I am afraid
this stoppoge of work in the aircraft
factories and the shipyard and other works
will have a serious effect on the invasion
plans, because the Government will undoubtedly be in need of all the planes and
ships they can get. I really think the
members of the Amalgamated Engineering Union have been ill-advised to take
this step at the present time. I would like
to ask one question-are the engineers in
Belfast paid as favourably as they are in
any other port in England and Scotland?
When I speak to the strikers they tell me
they are not, and I should like that statement either confirmed or contradicted. I
want to ask the Leader of the House
whether the engineers employed in other
ports across the channel, as well as the
engineers employed in the repair shops in
this port, are paid the extra 6d per hour
in lieu of piece rates which is now claimed
by the engineers employed by Messrs.
Harland & Wolff, Limited? If they are
more favourably treated in other ports in
England and Scotland, and even in some
of our
own repair shops, then the
engineers employed by Messrs. Harland &
Wolff are justified in putting forward a
claim for similar treatment, and they
have a genuine grievance.
Perhaps the Leader of the House will
be able to give us some information on
that point, and, if the men's statement
is correct, I cannot think that the
Ministry of Labour or the Government
would decline to press Messrs. Harland &
Wolff to do something that would satisfy
the men and bring an end to this deplorable dispute. I would be very glad if I
could do anything to induce the men to
return to work, but there is nothing that
I can see can be done, unless the men are
as favourably treated as are engineers in
other ports. We are part and parcel of the
United Kingdom, and what is paid in
one port should be paid in another. The
engineers here are just as good as those
in any other port, and they should be
treated in a similar way.
Mr. LESLIE: Is it not true that,
whatever grievance, real or fancied,
these men may have, the fact remains
that striking during war time is an
offence against the law. If so, I think
it is desirable that that should go out to
the public. I would like to ask the
Parliamentary Secretary whether he expects us to sit more than one day next
week.

According to the information in the

23s, 6d per week paid by certain firms

any other shipbuilding and ship repairing district of the United Kingdom.

verify this information, but in the
absence of such verification it may be
accepted as correct.
Was that your point?

per week paid by certain firms at the
Pollock Basin is not paid in any other

of the United Kingdom, as far as the
Ministry of Labour is aware.

Destruction by Foxes

Destruction by Foxes.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I wish to draw the
attention of the Government to the fox
menace, all over the Six Counties. Last
year I drew the attention of the Ministry
of Agriculture to the fact that great
losses took place, but nothing very drastic
was done by the Ministry during the
season. There is again great destruction
of stock by foxes at the present time.
Foxes have increased to an alarming extent, and now that the lambing season is
upon us there is great danger of further
loss. A great many losses have already
taken place in my own district. Four
farmers convenient to me have lost a great
many lambs, and it is very difficult to
ascertain the real loss of lambs. A ewe
may have lambs during the night and a
fox may come along and take them.
Before the war the Ministry appointed
an experienced trapper, who did great
work all over the Six Counties. He
materially reduced the number of foxes,
but since the war no trapper has been
engaged. Apparently the responsibility
for destroying foxes is put on the farmers.
Last year there was a reward of 6s for
adult foxes and 3s for cubs, but farmers
have not time to sit up all night and hunt
for foxes. That reward would not meet
the expense and the time lost.
I would suggest to the Ministry that a
trapper should be appointed for each of
the Six Counties. It would not entail
great expense. A sum of £2,000 to
£3,000 would be sufficient to cover the
work for six to nine months if one trapper
was appointed for each county, and that
money would be more than repaid by the
saving of stock and food. I myself have
suffered enormous losses by foxes.
•
In one
night I lost 60 laying fowl, and on another night 50 goslings and 20 lambs.
Last season, on my land alone, between
trapping and shooting, I took 15 foxes.
That will give you an idea of the fox
population in the different areas, and I
think it applies to every county in Northern Ireland, according to reports I have
received. It is a very serious matter,
and I appeal to the Ministry to do something immediately; otherwise there will
be great losses this year, because foxes
have increased to a greater extent than
last year.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Might I say that
I can confirm what Senator Maguire has
said? I know there is considerable
anxiety among a great many farmers
about the destruction done to stock by
foxes. There is another aspect of the
matter that appeals to me at the moment,
and it is this. If this increase in the
fox population is not stopped and it is
allowed to go on increasing, what is going
to happen? Will it not cost more to
deal with the menace then than it would
if steps were taken to check it now? I
was rather surprised to hear from Senator
Maguire that for sitting up all night to
protect his property from foxes a farmer
is paid only 3s, although stock to the
value of thousands of pounds may be in
jeopardy..
There is a feeling amongst the farmers
that these professional trappers can do
very efficient work, and I think that now
is the time to employ a sufficient number
to prevent a further increase in the fox
population. The danger should be dealt.
with now before it gets out of bounds
rather than allow it to increase, when it
will be so much more difficult to deal
with.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Facts and
figures given by the Ministry of Agriculture show that since the scheme for
the destruction of foxes was revived on
1st February, 1943, until 29th February,
1944-13 months-rewards have been
paid for the destruction of 5,611 foxes.
The sum of £1,518 16s Od has been paid
in rewards. The amount of the rewards
was increased at the request of the
county councils from 6s to 10s per head
for adult foxes, and from 3s to 5s per
head for cubs.
Mr. MCALLISTER: Five shillings for
the work of a whole family?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Not if one
member of the family knows his job
sufficiently well. The Ministry is of the
opinion that the effect of the scheme will
be cumulative, and that it will achieve a
reduction in the number of foxes. Pre-

30 MARCH 1944
In
vious experience. has shown that a scheme
of rewards is much more effective and
much more economical than the employment of trappers. I only want to add
that it seems to me that this fox menace
is a new thing in this country.
England, where they are more used to
foxes, the farmer is up to various dodges
for protecting his stock, and I believe an
improvement will be noticeable when our
own farmers get more accustomed to
coping with foxes and their ways. The
Ministry feel pretty certain that the offer
of a reward is the most effective way to
deal with the matter. They have. had
experience both of using trappers and
giving rewards, and therefore we must give
some weight to their opinion. I know it
is a very worrying thing. We have foxes
down in the Ards, but my own keeper is
a very good trapper and got about 13 last
year.
In reply to Senator Leslie, we shall be
sitting on Tuesday of next week, but I do
not think we shall sit again until after
Easter. We shall probably have to meet
again soon after Easter, probably a week
or so after, but we cannot quite settle the
date yet.

Death of Senator McLaughlin.
Mr. SPEAKER: It was with feelings
of the greatest regret that I heard this
morning of the death of Senator
McLaughlin. Senator McLaughlin had
been a Member of this House for eleven
years. I had the greatest regard for him,
and looked upon him as a personal friend.
He was one of my supporters when this
House was good enough to re-elect me
some years ago. Senator McLaughlin had
the courage of his convictions but was
never bitter, and I feel perfectly certain
that he will be very much missed in this
House by every Member of the House. I
am sure that all our sympathies will go
out to his widow and son who mourn his
loss.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I feel that I should
express from the Government side of the
House the very great regret and sorrow
with which we have heard the sad news
you have just announced to the House.
Senator McLaughlin was one who held
strong views, and expressed them
strongly, but always with the greatest
fairness and kindness. He was courteous
and helpful in debate, and at all times.
he was a valuable Member of this House.
The House will be the poorer for his
passing.
the
Mr. MCALLISTER: The death of
Senator McLaughlin has come upon us
with very tragic suddenness, and I do not
think I could improve upon
sentiments that have been SO very
beautifully expressed by you, Mr.
Speaker, and the Leader of the House
with regard to Senator McLaughlin. He
was a man of very fearless character. He
•
was a man, as has been said, of great
moral courage. He vigorously maintained
the views he believed in so strongly, but
if he did he always, as the Leader of the
House has said, fought fairly, and in the
cleanest possible manner. He never
stooped to undesirable methods. We on
these benches knew Senator McLaughlin
as a very loyal colleague. I may say that
I feel I have lost a very valiant, true,
and trusted friend, and what more can
one say on behalf of another public representative.

Message from the Commons.
The Commons agree to the Amendments made by the Senate to the Housing
Bill without amendment.
They have passed the Clogher Valley
Railway Company (Winding Up) Bill, to
which they desire the concurrence of this

ORDER OF THE DAY.
CLOGHER VALLEY RAILWAY
COMPANY (WINDING UP)
BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time".-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time.' Hon. Senators will recall
that in 1941 the Clogher Valley Railway
and Roads Act was passed, and that this
Act brought to an end the operation of
the Clogher Valley Railway. The Clogher
Valley Railway Company, which had
ceased to take any part in the management of the undertaking for some years,
was not affected by that Act and remained
in being. This Bill now brings the
process initiated in 1941 to its logical
conclusion, and provides for the winding
up of the company and the extinguishing
of its share capital. The company has
been in existence since 1884, when it was
established by a Provisional Order made
131 Clogher Valley Railway Bill
4 APRIL 1944

Adjournment
under the Tramways and Public Companies (Ireland) Act of 1883.
The Provisional Order provided that
the payment of interest at the rate of
5 per cent. on the paid up share capital
of the company should be guaranteed by
certain baronies in the counties of
Tyrone and Fermanagh. It also provided,
when the baronies were called upon to
fulfil their guarantees, that Government
assistance should be forthcoming to a
limited extent. As the House is aware,
the guarantees have had to be invoked in
almost every year of the company's
existence, and the only change that has
taken place has been the progresive
shifting of the responsibility for meeting
the guarantees from the ratepayers to the
taxpayers. Under the 1941 Act, to which
I have already referred, this movement
was completed and the Government became wholly liable for meeting any
deficiency in the company's ability to pay
these dividends. The share capital of the
company is £123,310, and the sum which
has thus to be found each years is £6,166.
For some years past the shares of the
company have been bought up by the
Government under their normal investment powers, and the position at the
moment is that slightly over 99 per cent.
of the shares of the company is held by
the Government. This Bill, therefore,
provides for the winding up of the
company, and for extinguishing its share
capital. It is entirely a book-keeping
transaction as regards 99 per cent. of
the shares. The Bill also provides for
dealing with under 1 per cent. of the
shares which are held by private holders
with whom the Government has failed to
come into contact. The Bill is really a
formality, and finally completes the
process begun in 1941.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed."(Sir Roland Nugent.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."-(Sir Roland Nugent.)
Resolved, "That this Bill do now
pass." (Sir Roland Nugent.)
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill without any amendment.
Royal Assent.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I have to
report to the House, That His Grace the
Governor of Northern Ireland, in the
name of and on behalf of His Majesty the
King, has been pleased to give his Assent
to the following Bills agreed upon by
both Houses:-
Constabulary,
Housing.
ADJOURNMENT MOTION.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, "That this House do now adjourn
until Thursday."
Question proposed.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Perhaps the
House would like to know that on
Thursday there will probably be only the
Royal Assent to the Bill which the House
passed to-day. It is necessary to have
the Royal Assent on that day, because
the Royal Assent must be given in both
Houses on the same day, and Thursday
will probably be the last day on which
the House of Commons will sit before
the end of the month. In addition, as
hon. Members will have noticed, the Bill
winds up the Clogher Valley Railway
Company on 30th April, and it would
be undesirable to wind the company
retrospectively. It is just possible that
we may take the First Reading of another
Bill on the same day, but I think it is
very unlikely.
up

QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
Hill Cattle Subsidy Scheme, 1943.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I beg to ask the
Leader of the House whether he can state
if the conditions of the Hill Cattle Subsidy
Scheme, 1943, in Northern Ireland are
identical with those of the similar scheme
in Great Britain, and if not, in what respect do they differ; and whether he can
give particulars of the number of farmers
who have qualified for the subsidy and
the actual amounts paid to them to date.
I have put this question as very many
farmers have approached me about it.
They feel greatly aggrieved and discontented at the conditions of the Government Hill Subsidy Scheme. One of the
conditions that affect farmers in this area
to a large extent is that they must have
at least 50 acres of a holding before they
can apply under the Hill Subsidy Scheme.
In addition to that at least half of their
holding must be of the hill grazing type
in order to qualify. This Bill originated
in the British Parliament, and of course
those conditions applied to farmers in
Great Britain, but they certainly do not
apply to farmers in Northern Ireland.
Of the Northern Ireland farmers roughly
90 per cent. are under the 50 acres holding. As a rule they vary from 30 to 45
acres, and in my area I know many
farmers who approach the 50 acre standard. In fact in one case the farmer has
a holding of 48 acres, and in that 481
acres there are only two acres of arable
land. It is a great hardship for a man
in that position that he cannot avail himself of this subsidy scheme. It was the
intention, I believe, to improve hill
grazing. That was the reason for the
subsidy being applied, and in giving this
subsidy to the farmers you are only
making them a present of a very small
sum. The subsidy is £3 per head for
cattle, but you must keep the cattle for
at least six months on hill grazing. This
£3, I say, is not a gift, because there are
very strict conditions embodied in the
subsidy scheme. They must attend to
draining and fencing and also cut unnecessary shrubbery, bracken and that
sort of thing. It will be only a contribution to the farmer in order to make
these improvements. I feel it would be a
great inducement if it were extended in
some modified form or other to the farmer
in Northern Ireland, so that he might
take advantage of that subsidy.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): It is a very long answer,
so I think it better I should read it.
was
the
The Hill Cattle Subsidy Scheme was
introduced by the Imperial Government
in 1942 and was designed to improve the
hill grazings and to obtain the maximum
production therefrom. In 1943 the
scheme in Northern Ireland was operated
on identical lines to those in England
and Wales, i.e., subsidy at the rate of £3
per eligible animal
payable to
occupiers of land in respect of areas of
not less than 50 acres in extent, of
which not less than one-half consisted of
rough grazings, provided
provided that
animals were kept day and night on the
hill for a period of at least five months.
In Scotland to meet the peculiar difficulties of the crofting areas subsidy at
the rate of £3 per head was paid in
respect of each eligible animal irrespective of the area of the holding or
of the area of rough grazings. Exact
particulars of the number of farmers in
Northern Ireland who qualified for
subsidy in 1943 are not yet available,
but 3,000 are expected to receive payment amounting to £75,000. That
applies to 1943, and we were operating
the scheme in precisely the same way as
they were operating it in England.
For 1944 the scheme has been altered
so as to remove the acreage restriction.
The period during which animals must
be on the hill has also been reduced from
Housing (Requisitioning) Bill 138
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
All
five months
months to eighteen weeks.
farmers who have eligible land on which
not less than three animals are kept for
a period of not less than eighteen consecutive weeks will be entitled to apply
for subsidy.
Therefore the 1944 method of working
the scheme will meet a considerable
number of the objections which have been
raised by the hon. Senator. I had
better repeat the 1944 scheme. The
period has been reduced from five months
to 18 weeks, and the area restriction is
gone; also any eligible land on which
three animals are kept for not less than
13 weeks is entitled to the subsidy.

to hear of the concessions in this area

that as the object is to improve
hill grazing, you will scarcely get the
subsidy if you are not improving the
grazing, and it seems fairly obvious that
a proportion must be rough grazing. I
may be able to answer before the House
rises.

Payment of Allowances by Belfast Firms.

will remember, I think, that he asked in
regard to allowances paid by certain

ORDERS OF THE DAY.
SUPERANNUATION (TEACHING
SERVICES) BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The White
Paper distributed with the Bill is fully
explanatory and I do not think I need
detain the House with a very long speech.
Broadly speaking Clause 1 really only
affects accounting practice, the object
being to enable Parliament to have a
complete net and gross picture instead of
the figures being concealed in the accounts
of the Fund. Clause 2 is very fully
explained in the White Paper. Clause 3
is chiefly a matter of machinery. The
main object is to enable the Ministry
which pays the pensions to keep the
statistics, instead of their being kept by
the Ministry of Finance. Clause 4 is
self-explanatory. It confers
the
upon
Ministry of Finance the power enjoyed by
the Board of Education in England to
see that the benefits of the State pension
scheme do not inure to the undue advantage of persons who are also receiving
benefits of other schemes for the same
period of service. It is very unlikely that
occasions will arise; they will be very
exceptional if they do. It is obviously
undesirable that persons should draw a
double pension for the same services, and
this Bill gives the Ministry power to take
appropriate action. I do not think that
there is anything else that I can add,
and I beg to move.
On Question, Motion ("That this Bill
be now read a Second time') agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
HOUSING (REQUISITIONING OF
PREMISES) BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The object
of this Bill is to assist in providing houses
for those persons who are homeless. The
House is aware that at the present time
there is an acute shortage of housing
accommodation throughout the Province,
yet instances occur of houses lying vacant
139 Housing (Requisitioning) Bill-

18 APRIL 1944
Second Reading
for considerable periods. It is hoped by
the provisions of this Bill to put local
authorities in a position to use those premises for the purpose of housing houseless people. Before a local authority can
act by way of requisitioning, the assent
of the Ministry of Home Affairs must
be obtained. The local authority, having
requisitioned the premises, may adapt
them as far as may be necessary in order
to make them suitable for accommodation. That would apply mainly to the
dividing up of very large houses or to
the making of shops or business premises
suitable for ordinary residence.
Before the Ministry of Home Affairs
give their consent to the requisitioning of
premises they will require the local authority to give the owner of the premises
an opportunity of finding his own tenant
by insisting that a notice of intention to
requisition shall be served.
If the owner
can obtain a tenant the requisitioning
will not proceed because, of course, the
object of requisitioning will then have
been met and the house will be used
as a residence.
The houses which will be the subject of
the Bill will be dwelling-houses, whether
furnished or unfurnished, which are not
being used for residential purposes and
other buildings which are unoccupied. A
local authority may, therefore, requisition houses or shops or other premises
which can suitably be converted into
dwellings or may requisition large houses
and convert them into flats suitable for
two or more families. It is obvious that
they would not do so where the alterations would be very extensive or expensive. If the premises contain furniture
the local authority may require the owner
to remove the furniture or store it in
some part of the premises. If the owner
fails to comply with the order the local
authority may remove the furniture themselves, but if they do so they will be paid
the cost of the removal and the storage.
The local authority will be required to
charge for the accommodation provided
a rent which is reasonable having regard
to all the circumstances. That means
that where they give houses to persons of
small means they will charge a rent within the means of those people; thus, they
will be allowed to charge less than an
economic rent. In that case the local
authority will be reimbursed from Government funds.
The compensation provisions are in the
Schedule and on broad lines they follow
the previous Act. Any dispute regarding
the amount of compensation will be referred to the county court for determination. The Bill, as the House will see,
will not set free a very great amount of
accommodation, but in certain places
where accommodation is not required for
the armed Forces-my hon. and gallant
Friend (Major Perceval-Maxwell) behind
me has experience of this matter-there
will be accommodation available, and
under present circumstances every little
bit of accommodation that can be got is
some help. I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: Nobody in this House will have more knowledge of requisitioning than my right
hon. Friend, and I am sure that he
will sympathise with the points which I
have to make on this Bill. In the main
it is a good Bill, but I am in a little doubt
as to how it will operate where a factory
or firm has certain houses which are
usually occupied by workmen at that mill,
or factory, or firm. It is true that before
this Bill can operate the Ministry of
Home Affairs must give their consent,
but there is nothing in it to compel the
Ministry to make the inquiries always
found necessary when the military authorities are requisitioning houses in order
to see that no hardship is involved. I
think that something much more than an
official inquiry is required.
The right hon. Gentleman and I have
had a lot of experience of this kind of
requisitioning, and we have both found
that very often it is necessary for an
individual to investigate the question of
hardship at first hand, particularly as it
is provided by this Bill that premises
which are not necessarily residential premises may be taken. It might be that
some unsympathetic local authority
might take part of business premises, and
when one has had a good deal of experience of dealing at arm's length with a
Government Department and I say nothing against Governnient Departments,
because I have found them most helpful
in every way-in a matter of this kind it
might very easily happen that premises
required for the efficient running of some
business might be requisitioned purely
by accident, the local authority not realis-
141 Housing (Requisitioning) Bill—
Second Reading
THE SENATE

[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
ing the importance of the premises to the
business, and the Ministry of Home
Affairs not having the means by which
they can examine the matter closely.
I am sure that that is the last thing
that the right hon. Gentleman or any
Member of the Government wishes to
arise, and I trust that before this Bill
goes through the House words will be inserted which will make this matter abundantly clear. I think it is very desirable
to encourage good employers to build
good houses for their people when their
business is large enough to warrant their
doing so. I am afraid that this Bill may
make some hesitate to do this for fear
that a local authority, which might perhaps not be particularly friendly or
particularly sympathetic to a certain sort
of business might come in and requisition
part of the premises.
There is another point which it seems
to me it is desirable to clear up. Under
this Bill, as far as I can see, a local
authority may requisition part of a house.
The definition of dwelling-house is an inclusive and not an exclusive one.
It says,
"Dwelling-house" includes any yard,
garden, outhouses and appurtenances
belonging thereto or usually enjoyed
therewith.
It does not say that it applies to a home
or building only. I should like the
Government to make this perfectly clear,
because I am told that where people let
part of a house, in the neighbourhood,
say, of Belfast, where there is a big housing shortage, and have charged a very
high rent for that part of their house,
they are condemned as being profiteers.
In many cases they have only done this at
great personal inconvenience. Not every
owner would wish to let part of his house
to a person over whom he has no control
and when he has no say in the personality
of the occupier.
I am sure the Government do not intend this Bill to work hardship in this
way, but we are aware that many Acts
of Parliament define "dwelling-house" as
part of a house as well as a whole house,
and I should like it to be made perfectly
clear that in this case the local authority
cannot go down and requisition part of
a house, part of a factory, or part of
business premises unless the owner is
willing that this should be done. I think
that apart from those particular cases
there is nothing that anyone can object
to in this Bill, and it is certainly desirable
that houses which have been unoccupied
for long periods should not be kept unoccupied when people want houses. At
the same time I think the Government
will be very wise to look into these points
again and make sure that they are not
working hardship by this Bill.
There is, perhaps, another point which
should be considered by the Government,
namely, the part of the Bill which enables the owner of a house within thirty
days to put in a tenant. The meaning of
the word "tenant" is rather strictly
defined in law and in Act of Parliament,
and I am not sure that a person who may
be put into a house by the owner and is
not holding it at a rent and for a specified time, for example, an employee who
holds it merely so long as he is employed,
would be included in the expression
"tenant." I think the Government
should make it perfectly clear that if the
owner of a house within thirty days puts
in a person who is employed by him that
should operate as if the person was a
tenant. These may seem to some Members of the House to be small, niggling
points, but I do feel that it is necessary,
where you are dealing with property and
there are cases which may work hardship
if they are not carefully dealt with, that
these expressions should only be used with
the most strictly definite meaning. There
should be no possible danger of incorrect
interpretations being put on expressions
used in an Act of Parliament.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think I
had better deal seriatim with the points
that have been made. As regards Major
Perceval-Maxwell's first two points, these
are, I think, matters which could be dealt
with better by sympathetic administration
than by laying down definite rules in the
Bill itself. If the Ministry concerned
were to circularise the local authorities
pointing out the danger of hardship, and
the importance of care and sympathy in
these matters it would probably meet the
case.
The third point was as to the definition
of tenant. I should like frankly to consult those with more knowledge than
myself before I commit myself. One
has to remember that the man who
is trying to dodge requisitioning may
push in an employee simply to avoid the
house being requisitioned. If an Amendment is put down it will be considered
very carefully, but I cannot make any
promises on the matter.
As regards the points raised by Senator
Joseph Cunningham, I think they are all
covered in the Bill, with the exception of
the one about A.R.P. Premises which
are not being used for residential purposes
can be requisitioned by the local authorities if they consider them suitable. So
far as the point the Senator raised about
A.R.P. is concerned, I am not in a
position to speak with knowledge on
the matter. I think I cannot do
more than to say that the matter will be
brought to the attention of the Minister
of Public Security. He can look into the
question of A.R.P. accommodation and
see if any premises can be released.
Unfortunately in times of emergency
premises must sometimes be used in a
wasteful manner.
With regard to Senator Leslie's point,
we have to realise that this is an emergency
Measure. I do not think there would be
any justification for requisitioning if there
was not an emergency and, therefore, a
reasonable speed is essential. I think this
rules out the possibility of an appeal of
the type suggested. This is, after all, not
Ministries Bill-

MINISTRIES BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I do not
think I need take up much of the time
of the House in describing this Bill. It
was recommended in the excellent Report
submitted by the Select Committee on
Health Services that a Ministry of Health
should be set up. There is a very successful precedent for this. There has
been a Ministry of Health in England for
a good many years, and it has worked
successfully. There is a further important point. During the past 20 years the
number of matters which could be included in a Ministry of Health, Housing
and Local Government have increased in
importance and variety. There is every
possibility that in the reconstruction
period after the war they will increase
still further. That being so, there is the
serious possibility of overloading one
Minister who already has very important
and onerous functions in respect of the
maintenance of law and order.
I do not think it is necessary to deal
at length with Clause 1. As regards
Clause 2, it is obvious that when there is
a numerically small House a large number
of Ministries is undesirable. It is, therefore, undesirable to multiply Ministries,
and when creating a new Ministry it is
desirable that, if possible, an existing
Ministry should disappear. That does not
mean to say that all the various functions
which come under the Ministry of Public
Security are not still very important.
In connection with that Ministry, all the
organisation has been completed, and
there now remains only the supervision of
it. That being so, I do not think it unreasonable to put that duty on to the
Ministry of Home Affairs, particularly as
the other functions carried out by it are
reasonably akin to those of the Ministry
of Public Security.
So far as Clause 4 is concerned there
are really two points. The first is that
it is impossible to put into a Bill all the
details of the powers and functions conferred by successive Acts of Parliament
on the Ministry of Home Affairs which
will move to the new Ministry. That
being so, it is necessary to do that by
Order in Council, the principle having
been decided by the Bill. The second
point is that there has been very little
reallocation of functions of Ministries
during the past 20 years. During the
years the importance and volume of the
duties have altered, and in the reconstruction period after the war it may be
that they will still further alter, and
that it will be necessary to transfer functions from one Ministry to another for
the sake of more efficient administration.
The change might be a very small one,
and it would be a great waste. of time if
that had to be embodied in a Bill. It is
proposed, therefore, to carry that out by
Order in Council.
I come now to a point which is more
a point for the Committee stage, but as it
is one of principle, I think I should mention it on the Second Reading. In the
course of the debate in another place it
was stressed that Orders in Council, in
matters of this kind, should be reviewed
by Parliament and should not be recommended if Parliament expressed disapproval. This was considered a reasonable
attitude to take up, and the. Attorney-
General read out a draft amendmentit was very hurriedly prepared-in the
course of the debate. Owing to technical
difficulties, the, proposed amendment will
have to be moved in this House.
Attorney-General
would be moved.
The
undertook that it
Since that debate took place it has been
possible to consider with rather more care
the draft which the Attorney-General
read in another place. In the form in
which he read it in another place it
would have governed not only future
Orders in Council covering future
transfers, but also the Orders in Council
transferring all details of the functions

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: In
debating this Bill, I think the House is
under a certain handicap in that the Bill
is the result of recommendations made
by someone whose recommendations have
not been made public to the House. If
it had been possible for the Government
to carry out those recommendations without coming to the House it would have
been reasonable for them not to publish
the results of the Inquiry, but as they
had to come to the House to have this
Inquiry implemented they should, unless
there is some reason which is not known
to us, have disclosed the relevant portion
of the Inquiry. I do not think they took
the House or the country sufficiently into
their confidence in this matter. If it had
not been obvious to Members of this
House and to those outside it, that it was
high time there was a Ministry of Health,
I think this Bill would have stood in a
certain amount of danger. However, as
it has not taken any public Inquiry to
convince Members of this House, and
many people outside it, that a Ministry
of Health is an absolute necessity, the
only thing I will say on this Bill is that
not only will it be necessary to transfer
certain functions from the Ministry of
Home Affairs to it, but as time goes on
it may become necessary, to transfer to
it certain functions from the Ministry of
Education.
I say that because in the past, particularly in the case of mental ill-health,
the Ministry of Education have not done
their duty.
The only way by which
mentally defective children can be discovered is by the co-operation of the
Ministry of Education. That Ministry
have certain powers in regard to mental
defectives. I have stated in this House
before, and I now repeat, that the
Ministry of Education have not done
their duty in this respect. That is a very
serious statement to make. Now
you have
⚫mental defectives being admitted to
school with normal children. That is not
a satisfactory state of affairs to exist in
any country. It is not done in England,
and it should not be done in this country.
There is no proper and effective way of
dealing with a low-grade mental defective
except by sending him to Eire. I trust
one of the first things that the Ministry
of Health will do will be to take this
matter up and, if necessary, ask that the
functions connected with mental illhealth be transferred from the Ministry
of Education to them.
The other matter with which I think it
necessary to have very close liaison
between the two Ministries is in regard
to the question of nursery schools. It is
a big subject, and I do not propose to
go into it now. They are being
administered by the Ministry of Home
Affairs and the Ministry of Education.
That is a very unsatisfactory state of
affairs. Some are being administered by
the Ministry of Home Affairs-it may be
the Minister of Public Security, but I
am not sure which-and that has come
about through children being evacuated
from bombed areas. They do not know
what standing they have at all, what
finances they have. What is going to
happen to the nursery schools, and how
long are they going to continue? I speak
with some knowledge of one nursery
school, although not direct knowledge,
which is now under the Ministry of Public
Security. They are in a completely
anomalous position, and I think the
whole question of nursery schools should
be cleared up, at any rate temporarily,
before the new Education Bill or the new
White Paper is published.
