Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
also am strongly in favour of the Bill.
While the last speaker said that this
House was under a little difficulty or
handicap owing to the fact that none of
the Members served on the Select Committee, yet I think, on the other hand,
we are much more independent.
We
will be able to deal with the Bill and discuss it more frankly, because there are
not so many Members here aspiring to
the Ministry or to the Parliamentary
Secretaryship as there may be in another
place. I want to say that I think health,
housing, and local government should be
under one Ministry. As the Leader of
the House has said, Ministries sometimes
can be overloaded and not be efficient. It
appears to me that the Ministry of Home
Affairs must have been terribly overloaded in the last few years, because it
made a great deal of trouble with
local government boards. The greatest
difficulty that I have seen and the
greatest trouble that was caused was by
the way it dealt with the Belfast Corporation, and if this matter is put under a
Ministry.to deal with health, housing and
local government I think it will be able
to deal with those bodies properly, and to
follow more correctly the transactions
and the administration as they deserve to
be followed.
As far as the Ministry of Home Affairs
and the Ministry of Public Security are
concerned, Public Security was under
the Ministry of Home Affairs before, and
I believe that is the proper Ministry to
deal with matters of security. Therefore, I think that Ministry also will be
very suitable. I do think that it is very
wise for a Ministry of Health to be set up
to deal with health, housing and the
government of the people, and I do very
much support this Bill. As far as mentally defective children are concerned I
think the Government as a whole have
got to bear the responsibility. I do not
think the Ministry of Education is really
responsible. As a member of the Education Committee of the Belfast Corporation
I know that we have been pressing for a
long time for some provision to be made
for educating mentally defective children.
At the moment there is no proper provision or accommodation for them. That
is a matter for the Government as
whole to deal with. I do think this Bill
will make an improvement. I am glad to
say no more Ministries are likely to be
a
set up, but there is simply to be a reshuffle, which I think will be satisfactory.
Mr. LESLIE: I think the right hon.
Gentleman who moved the Second Reading reminded us that this Bill follows the
very voluminous Report of the Health
Committee which was set up by those in
another place. The House will remember that the membership of that Committee was entirely composed of Members
of Parliament who sit elsewhere. This
House was not represented on it at all.
The body that did sit did produce a very
voluminous Report. I do not know that
it was circulated to Members of this
House, but it was at all events obtainable
in the Library, and I got a copy of it. I
have not yet completed the reading of the
evidence. I have got some way through
it, and I have read the whole Report.
This Bill follows the issue of that Report
with really remarkable celerity.
Those of us who were concerned with
another committee on which both Houses
were represented, and which endeavoured
to deal with transport a year or two ago,
recommended, it may be recalled, that
the management of transport should be
allocated to a Ministry or Department of the Government. But that
has not yet been done. Unlike the
Health Committee, we did not say
that a new Ministry should be set up, but
we did say that some definite Ministry
should be placed in charge of the matter
of transport, just as the Committee to
which I am referring suggested that the
Ministry should be in charge of health
matters. I think I am right in saying that
the Health Committee stopped there. I
do not think they went so far as to suggest
that the new Ministry or the new Department, whichever it was decided to appoint,
should also take over housing and local
government. I think it limited itself to
health in direct recommendation. However, that Committee did recommend
definitely there should be a Ministry of
Health, and this Bill is one of the results
of that recommendation.

There is another recommendation in
that same Report, and I hope that will
be acted upon as speedily as may be, and
that is that the Public Health Acts should
be consolidated as soon
as possible. I
must say that the suggestion of the consolidation of the Public Health Acts was
made, I should think, 15 years ago, and
Ministries Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
I expressed myself then as very sympathetic but anxious not to have anything
to do with the job. Since then there
have been more and more Statutes relating to public health placed on the Statute
Book. I do hope that something will be
done at a not too distant date in the
matter of the codification of Acts of Parliament dealing with public health. It may
be that the preparation of these Orders
in Council, which we see in the Bill,
causes all the labour-and it will be a
very considerable labour that will be required to produce these Orders in Council
-may have some indirect bearing and
may be of some assistance when it comes
to consolidating the Public Health Acts.
I think I am right in saying that our
Public Health Acts only began in 1878.
I think they repealed anything before
that date. At all events, it is a matter
which has a very important bearing on
the whole thing.
The proposals for the new Ministry
are, I suppose a natural development—
again I think the right hon. Gentleman
suggested it-from the events of the last
20 years.
It may be remembered that
when this Parliament came into being it
was pointed out, and a good deal of
stress was laid upon it by the Cabinet of
that day, and credit taken for it, that
we had reduced the number of boards
which formerly existed in Dublin to a
very much smaller number, which we did.
Now, of course, we are moving a little in
the other direction, but we are still able
to act without increasing the net number
of Ministers before us. One hopes, of
course, that a great deal of good will
develop from this measure. I am afraid
that is what I said in dealing with
another measure, but still one does hope
-and I think with more justification on
this occasion-that real good will result,
and that it will not merely be a matter of
change. I do not know whether the
House at present recollects James Russell
Lowell, who was once referred to as that
distinguished Englishman, although he
was. in fact, Ambassador of the United
States. In one of his poems he remarks:
Change jes' for change is like them
big hotels,
Where they change plates and let's
you live on smells.
We do hope that this Bill is not going
to be anything of that sort.
We hope
that something effective is going to be
done, but I am very much afraid-and
again the right hon. Gentleman had it
in his mind, I think-that if you are
taking public health, which is regarded
as having been for some years the
Cinderella of the Ministry of Home
Affairs, and taking it down the stairs and
giving it glass slippers and so on, and
promoting it to be a princess of the realm,
you are not only taking the Cinderella,
but you are taking the ugly sisters on as
well when you are taking housing and
local government. Is not the effect of
that likely to be that this new department
is going to be overweighted? Have hon.
Members thought what the change involves? I looked through the Bill-and
I must say I looked through it with some
care- -to see whether anything could be
done to reduce the heavy burden which
will be cast on the new Ministry, and
again I am afraid we have to admit that
we cannot, at all events at the present, do
that. It must face the whole responsibility under each of those three headlines.
We have got to remember that it is
proposed, I understand, to continue to
act through the county councils. You
cannot have two authorities in charge of
the county councils. It is not practicable.
I remember many years ago, when I was
a member of a county council, some questions arose, and the chairman of that day
said his view of the duty of a county
council was to carry out the terms of the
Local Government Act of 1898. The Act
of 1898, as we know, has been extended
a good deal during the last 25 years or so,
and the burdens on the county councils
now are very heavy indeed. It is not
only the burden on the county councils
themselves, but the burden on the rates
from which those county councils derive
their funds, and from which rate any extra
expenditure will have to a great extent
to be met. There may be, I was going
to say Treasury grants, grants from the
Government which are really an allocation from the national income, but any
extension of local government, whether
under the public health head or any other
head, can only result in increased expenditure. As the House knows, I have
already expressed my opinion more than
once as regards the burdens which are
falling on the local rates. The rates do
not show any signs of diminution, and
the claims upon them seem to be in-
Ministries Bill-
Second Reading
18 APRIL 1944
creasing day by day. That is a matter
which will have to be most carefully borne
in mind.
Of the burdens that will fall upon the
new Minister, perhaps the most cogent at
the moment will be the one alluded to by
my hon. Friend on my left, the question
of the future of Belfast and the administration of the city. That is a problem
that undoubtedly does not belong to this
afternoon's discussion, but we cannot
shut our eyes to the fact that there is a
problem there which requires mature and
careful consideration. When the unfortunate Minister, whoever he may be,
has to deal with it, what time is he going
to have left to deal with public health
and the housing question except in so far
as he dovetails all these things into one
another. He is going to have a very,
very difficult time, but, as I say, I cannot myself see any immediate method of
reducing the burden. If he is to be
overweighted-and I am afraid he is
going to be overweighted-the Minister
of Home Affairs is going to be relieved,
except that he will have the Ministry of
Public Security with its still important
functions and everybody has laid stress
on the importance of the Ministry of
Public Security. These important duties
are to be added or returned, we will say,
to the Ministry of Home Affairs, but as
regards the net result of work the
Minister of Home Affairs is coming off
very easily, and the new Minister is not
going to have a light job. I think
Members are not disposed to shed any
tears over the disappearance of the
Ministry of Public Security.
The right hon. Gentleman has adumbrated an Amendment which will come
up at a later stage. I cannot help
wondering whether the effect of that
Amendment will not be to shift the main
interest of the discussion on this Bill
from the Committee to the Report stage.
That, however, we can deal with when
the time comes. As it happens, that
Amendment will, I imagine, be based on
Clause 4 of the Bill, but I will deal with
that when the Amendment appears on
the Paper. The whole structure of this
Bill is based on the assumption that such
changes as are to be made in the allocation of departmental activities require
the intervention of Parliament as such,
that intervention being distinguished, so
far as that is possible, from the mere
exercise of the Royal Prerogative. That
is one of the main fundamental principles
of the Bill before us.
This question of procedure has been
raised in this House before.
It was
raised, it may be remembered, in 1941.
On that occasion the penultimate predecessor of the right hon. Gentleman,
the Leader of the House, promised on
behalf of the Government of that day
that the matter would be considered in
due course, and he gave an undertaking
that that would be done. For one reason
and another, as the House knows, the
problems involved are not free from difficulty. As I now understand, we may
take it for granted that the promise given
in 1941 stands redeemed, and that the
matter has been considered. In fact, as
I understand, a careful scrutiny has been
made of judicial decisions and other proceedings during the last twenty-five years,
and from what I have been told about it,
and from what I have seen myself, I am
satisfied that the methods illustrated in
the Bill before us are in accordance with
the conventions and standards which are
now accepted.
Consequently those methods illustrated
in the Bill can be adopted without
prejudice to any of the checks which our
Constitution provides. Those checks
remain unimpaired. I am not going to
say I am altogether in sympathy with the
course these methods have taken from
their inception, but whether they started
on a right or a wrong course a good many
years ago now-a question which I think
will never be decided-is for our purposes
now unimportant. There is no question
that the course proposed to be taken in
this Bill is conventionally correct.
Whether the right hon. Gentleman proposes to tell the House any more about
that when moving the Amendment I do
not know. It may be that he will think
the House has heard enough about it and
will be content to accept it, the more so
as I gather that the tenor of the Amendment is likely to be in favour of extending
the control of Parliament rather than the
contrary. Parliament for this purpose
means the two Chambers of Parliament
and not Parliament as the word is properly used.
I think that everything will depend on
the man selected to administer the new
Department, and on the staff with which
he is furnished, whether by the creation

THE SENATE

[Mr. Leslie]
of a new Department or by transfer. A
tremendous amount of work will have to

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I think
the Government is to be congratulated in
acting so expeditiously on the report of
the Health Committee which they set up.
This is one noteworthy example where
action has followed very speedily on the
advice given by a Committee. But I am
not sanguine that the setting up of a new
Ministry will in itself transform the situation in any radical way. All will depend,
as the right hon. and learned Senator has
just said, on the man who is appointed
Minister of Health, and also on the
organisation which he is empowered to
establish in the provinces and in the
urban districts. It is in these areas that
the real work will be done, and not in
the Ministry. After all, while we have
not hitherto had a Ministry of Health,
we had a certain authority, and that
authority had power. The power was
not exercised because there was not the
proper organisation in the provinces to
carry out public health measures properly.
A good deal will depend on the status
and on the powers and on the ideals put
before the local medical authorities
throughout the area. Take, for example,
just one detail which attracted a good
deal of attention in the Committee. It is
well known to all the Members of this
House that a certain amount of ill-health
in the community is due to the production of impure, unclean, and unsafe milk.
I am sorry to say that the Committee
which considered health matters did not
speak with as positive and assured voices.
on this matter as I would have liked.
The future health of the children will
depend to a very considerable extent on
the ideals which are put before the local
Imedical authorities on such matters as
that.
Reference has been made to the neglect
of the Ministry of Education to deal in
any adequate or satisfactory way with
the education of the mentally defectives,
and with what was said in that regard I
have a great deal of sympathy. It ought
to be borne in mind, however, that a
former Minister did make a pretty brave
attempt to deal with the situation by the
establishment of a school at Haypark,
but I quite agree that that school was
not enough, and did not express the
proper method to deal with the situation.
That school was hampered owing to two
circumstances. First of all, it proved
very expensive on the local authorities,
and in addition to that it was discovered
that many parents were very unwilling to
part with their children who were in need
of such education as that school could
provide.
There is another method of dealing with
the needs of these unfortunate children,
and that method has been experimented
with to some extent in Belfast. At the
moment there is a section of a school in
Belfast given up to the education of a
group of these children, but it is only
one group.
There are in Belfast hundreds, possibly thousands, of such children
who could benefit by such classes, but at
the so-called Haypark School, now located
at Cliftonville, there are only fifty or
sixty children in attendance. In a city
comparable with Belfast, namely, Liverpool, there will be perhaps six or eight
entire institutions given over to the
education of these children. I had the
pleasure of seeing one of these institutions at work, and I may say, without any
fulsome boasting on my part, that this
was one sphere in the work of the Ministry in which I was intensely interested,
and in which I hoped to be able to achieve
something along these lines.
As Senator Leslie has said, a good deal
will depend on the Minister who is
appointed, and also on his staff. We
have in another place three medical men
representing constituencies, all very wide
awake, able, and experienced men. I do
not say
that any of them should be
appointed Minister of Health, because it
Ministries Bill
Adjournment
18 APRIL 1944
is not for me to suggest that, but if a
Parliamentary Secretary is needed, then
I hope a medical person with the proper
qualifications will be given that particular
responsibility.
Just one final word, and it is this. It
seems to me that this legislation is overshadowed by the tremendously extensive
legislation which is promised in the near
future in regard to the whole health
services of the community. What has
been foreshadowed in the Beveridge Report will to some extent become law. It
will affect us in Northern Ireland, and
will transform our medical services from
top to bottom. Indeed, the extensiveness of these reforms in regard to health
services are so immense, and will be so
expensive if they are adopted here to the
same extent as they will be adopted in
England, Wales, and Scotland, that
there is ground for thinking that this
legislation passed at Westminster ought
to be made applicable to Northern Ireland, and that the expense ought to be
borne as part of the United Kingdom
expenditure. I approve of the Bill. I
think it is a good Bill and that it will
help matters, but it all depends on how
its provisions are eventually carried out.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think the
Bill has received very general approval,
and it is scarcely necessary for me to reply
at length to the debate. As hon. Senators
have said, everything will really turn on
the Minister. I suppose a Ministry of
Health is always one of the key Ministries
of a Government. It is a very difficult
and very important Ministry. It will
be a
case not for one good Minister
only but for a succession
of good
Ministers,
because there is ahead
of that Ministry work which will extend
far beyond our time. While a heavy
load may be cast on that Minister, he
will have the advantage of having to
deal with problems which are
are linked
together. He will not have the same
difficulties as the Minister of Home
Affairs, who has had to switch over from
the totally different problems of the
maintenance of law and order to the
question of health and back again.
It is not quite so bad if the load is all of
one kind instead of being two different
loads.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
whole House for Tuesday next.

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
beg to move in page 2, line 29, after paragraph (5) (a) to insert a new paragraph:
(b) In respect of premises usually
occupied by a servant of the owner; or
I explained my objections on this point
on the Second Reading, and I do not propose to weary the House by any prolonged
explanation of it at this stage. But I do
163 Housing (Requisitioning) Bill-

25 APRIL 1944
Committee
want to point out that there are cases in
which a house, which has been in the past
occupied by an employee of the owner,
may remain unoccupied for many months
or even years because the owner has been
able to get a local man who already has
a house to fill the employment. Then
comes a time when he has to find someone
else to fill the place of this man,
and perhaps he cannot get a local man to do it.
In that case he may have to get a man
to come from a distance. That man may
be already married and he will not come
from a distance, however good the employment is, unless he can find a house for
his wife and family.
If the local authority find the house
which has been empty for some time they
may come to the employer and say, "Why
is this house empty? You have not filled
this house for a long time." The employer says, "I must keep this house open
in case I want someone from a distance
and cannot get a key man." The local
authority have no means of putting the
owner to a test as to whether this house
will be necessary in the future. They
must rely to a large extent on the judgment and decency of the owner in this
business. Now it is my experience, and
I think it is the experience of other Members of this House, that men who employ
a large amount of labour are nearly
always to be relied on to give a reasonable answer and not to keep a house unnecessarily empty. I think you must
allow such persons the benefit of the doubt
when it is a question of whether it is
reasonable or not. I think it is going to
be very
difficult for the owner of the
premises to prove that the empty house
may be of vital consequence to him in his
business.
Then again, it is a very great necessity
for the mobility of labour that people who
have a big business and key men should
be able to draw labour from other parts of
the country. You have a man say in
Derry who is a key man and who, through
no fault of his own, falls out of employment. There may be nobody in the
neighbourhood where he is living to give
him employment, and if he cannot move
to another part of the country he will be
another man coming on to unemployment insurance. Perhaps there will be a
man in County Down who would be quite
willing to employ him if he had a house
for the man to live in. If a man is a big
ememployer of labour and has a house on a
farm or in connection with his business it
is only right that that man should be
allowed a certain number of empty houses
so that when the business gets active
again he can, if necessary, draw
ployees from other parts of the country
where business may not be so active. I
commend this Amendment to the House
both from the point of view of benefiting
the industry, and from the point of view
of benefiting the workers themselves,
because I think it will enable the flow of
workers to be much more even over the
country, and a man who may find himself out of employment will be able to go
to some other part to find employment.
It may be that those words are not the
right ones to use. I shall sympathetically
consider any suggestion which the Government may make. If the House allows me
*
at
to withdraw the Amendment I shall do so
on the Government giving a satisfactory
assurance. That would leave me
liberty to move an Amendment at another
stage of the Bill, provided, of course, the
assurance given is not a satisfactory one.
I beg to move.
Mr. STEVENSON: While I am in
sympathy with the Amendment put down
by the hon. and gallant Senator, I think
it is drawn up rather broadly. The fact
remains that there are thousands of
houses in the North of Ireland that were
built and were kept solely for employees.
I would urge the Government to find
some means by which the rights of employers are safeguarded. I am not speaking from the point of view of my own firm,
but for the linen trade in general. I
know it would be a very great hardship
in many cases if houses were seized for
outsiders while the owners were looking
for suitable employees.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
the Government cannot accept the hon.
and gallant Senator's Amendment in its
present form. The Government consider
that it is too wide and would give a great
opportunity to those who were dishonestly
trying to prevent requisitioning. I do
appreciate the point made in regard to
the hardship imposed in the case where a
house is needed for a key employee. If
some form of wording could be found
which would narrow this Amendment so
that it would affect only the genuine cases
it would be better. I think we might find

CLAUSE 2 (Power of local authority
to permit occupation of requisitioned
premises).
Question proposed, "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill."
Major DOBBS: I should like to draw
the attention of the Committee to subclause (1) (b) of Clause 2. That subclause states:
The occupation shall be from week
to week and may be determined at any
time by the local authority giving to
the permitted persons at least seven
days' notice requiring him to give up
possession of the occupied premises.
It does seem rather hard on a person who
has gone into occupation of a house to
have to get out in seven days. If the
tenant is going to be sympathetically
dealt with I do not see any objection to
the matter, but can the Leader of the
House tell us if a person will be sympathetically dealt with? If he is not so
dealt with, then seven days seems very
short notice to give. Houses are very
difficult to get at the present time, and
such short notice does not give a person
a chance of getting other accommodation.
Such a person is given the house by the
local authority, and yet he has to clear
out in seven days. I wonder if the Leader
of the House could give us any information on that point?
Mr. LESLIE: Are we not rather in the
usual difficulty of a choice of two evils?
The unfortunate man, as my hon. and
gallant Friend has pointed out, would
like more than seven days' notice. He is,
presumably, put into the house because
he is homeless. At the same time the
house he is put into is not his own house,
it belongs to somebody else, and that
somebody else may have very proper
reasons they were adumbrated by my
hon. and gallant Friend-for wanting it
for other purposes. There is certain protection under Clause 3.. The Ministry of
Home Affairs, if that is the Department
that will be in charge ultimately, can give
the local authority instructions to get the
house cleared, and we presume that the
Ministry will have some regard to the
circumstances of the case.
It is a very
difficult situation. There is very much
in what my hon. and gallant Friend
stated. There would be great difficulty
in extending the period beyond seven
days. We must remember, I think, that
in agricultural and industrial matters
seven days is a fairly long period as regards the extent and length of a man's employment. Employment shifts very
rapidly, and sometimes there is very little
time to spare.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: This is one
of the cases in which we must trust to
reasonable, sympathetic administration
by the local authority. As the right hon.
Senator has stated seven days is the
normal notice in the case of weekly
tenancies. I do not see how the Government could give any undertaking, although I am sure that local authorities
will not be seized with a mania for turning people out at short notice. It is the
practice of local authorities to treat cases
Ministries Bill-
Committee
25 APRIL 1944
in a sympathetic manner. I think it
reasonable to suppose that that power will
not be applied harshly. There may, however, be cases where it would be necessary
to get a man out quickly, and they must
have the power to do so.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

CLAUSE 4 (Orders in Council for
assignment and transfer of functions and
other purposes).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:
At end to add the following sub-clause :
"(5) The Governor of Northern
Ireland shall not be recommended to
make an Order in Council under this
section until a draft thereof has lain
before each House of Parliament for
the statutory period, and if either
House of Parliament within that period
resolves that no further proceedings be
taken in relation thereto, no further
proceedings shall be so taken, but without prejudice to the preparation of a
fresh draft:
Provided that this provision shall not
apply to the making of any Order in
Council for the purposes of section one
or section two of this Act."
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: This is the
Amendment I foreshadowed on
the
Second Reading of the Bill, and it is consequent on the debate in another place.
I think the purport of the Amendment is
quite clear, but it is possible that some
hon. Members of the House, especially
learned Members, may have had some
apprehension that a Clause of this
type would infringe the prerogative of the
Crown. Sub-section (3) (b) of Clause 8 of
the Government of Ireland Act gives
power to the Parliament of Northern
Ireland to decide what Ministries there
will be. The subsequent decision of the
House of Lords in the case of the de
Keyser's Royal Hotel makes it quite clear
that the prerogatives of the Crown
having in any particular been transferred
to Parliament remain with Parliament.
That being so, the Constitutional position
is absolutely clear. It is curious that
while the Parliament of Northern Ireland unquestionably possesses these
powers there is some doubt whether the
Imperial Parliament could do it.
The other point to which I should like
to draw the attention of the Committee
is that this is not the usual practice to
which we are accustomed-Parliament
delegating its own legislative power to a
Ministry by regulation. This is the reverse position-Parliament taking from
the Executive powers which otherwise
would remain in the hands of the
Executive. That might lead to the fear
that this was an undue infringement by
the legislative side of Parliament of
Executive powers, a thing which has got
to be very carefully watched, because
there is no easier road to the destruction
of a democratic institution than the undue weakening of the powers of the
Executive. It should be remembered
that it was the persistent encroachment
Ministries Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
by the Legislature on the powers of the
Executive in both Germany and Italy
'which produced the conditions which
made Nazi-ism and Fascism possible. The
Legislature in both countries made
effective government impossible until a
dictatorship at last swept both government and Legislature away. I do not
think this Clause will cause that danger
here, and there is obvious advantage to
the Government that when they propose
a change in machinery they will have
the advice of Parliament. I beg to move
the Amendment standing in my name.
Mr. LESLIE: The Committee may remember that a few days ago, and also a
year or two ago, I made some observations
on somewhat kindred subjects. I said
the other day that perhaps the right hon.
Gentleman, the Leader of the House,
would tell the Committee a little more
about the detail, and I am glad to see that
he has availed himself of that opportunity.
He has told us something more about it.
Though personally, as I said before, I am
not happy in my own mind about the
practice, there is no getting away from
the fact that the practice is now quite
constitutional. . The right hon. Gentleman quoted to us Section 8, I think 3b,
of the 1920 Act, and he also quoted the
case of the de Keyser's Royal
Hotel Co. I am not quite
whether the case of the de Keyser's
Royal Hotel Co. did not precede the
Government of Ireland Act, 1920, at one
stage or other. I think it went through
every stage a case of that kind can go
through, until finally there was the
definite decision of the House of Lords.
I rather feel that that decision of the
House of Lords was, perhaps, before the
Government of Ireland Act.
sure
At all events, the point I want to
mention to the Committee is that after
the passing of the Government of Ireland
Act a case did arise and was referred to
the Judicial Committee of the Privy
Council in which Northern Ireland was
concerned. Before that Judicial Committee that same case of the de
Keyser's Royal Hotel Co. was cited
with approval and followed. Therefore I am afraid that nothing can
be said against the proposal on the constitutional aspect. The thing has now
become part of the Constitution. If the
Crown ever had the prerogative, and if
the Governor ever, so to speak, succeeded
to it from the Lord Lieutenant, which I
am afraid is rather doubtful, but if it did
happen, that power has been abrogated
by the Royal Assents given more than
once.
As regards the rest of the proposal, the
matter of interest seems to rest upon the
proviso; that is to say, the provisions
that as regards Sections 1 and 2 of this
Act the Government will not have to come
back to Parliament again to consider an
Order in Council, nor will they have to
go to any other court. That will, so to
speak, clear the decks and continue the
course which has been taken with Royal
Assents. I think there is nothing more
to be said, and there is certainly no objection to the insertion of this new subclause 5.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

which we find ourselves is that it is important that the Bills with amendments
should reach another place in time for
their sitting on 2nd May, in order to get
the Royal Assent by 9th May. These are
all urgent Bills, and it means that we
must take them on Thursday, so that it
will probably be impossible to circulate
the Amendment. I very much regret
having to hustle the House in this way,
but I am afraid that in this case it is
almost impossible to avoid it.

Major
*
*
*

*
PERCEVAL- MAXWELL :
The House will appreciate that the
Amendment on the Order Paper is a very
restricted form of the Amendment which
I put before the House on the last
occasion during the Committee stage of
this Bill, and which I withdrew to enable
the Government to consider putting down
an Amendment of their own.
There was
an understanding that I should place an
Amendment on the Order Paper in case
the Government failed to put down an
Amendment of their own. As it has been
impossible to circulate this Amendment,
with your Lordship's leave I shall read it
to the House, because it is very important
from my point of view, and from the point
of view of those who support it, that the
exact wording should be before the Members of the House. The Amendment is:
Page 2, line 29, and end (end of
paragraph [a]) to add:-
"(b) in respect of any premises reasonably required for some person engaged in the full-time employment
of the owner whom the owner is
unable to employ unless housing
accommodation is provided; or”
Some hon. Members may feel that in
restricting the Amendment in order to
try to get an agreed Amendment with the
Government I have taken out of it most
of what is important, but I do not think
that that is the case.
I have deliberately
restricted the cases in which this Act shall
not apply; at least I have suggested that
they should be restricted to premises
175 Housing (Requisitioning) Bill-

27 APRIL 1944
Report
reasonably required, that is to say, the
owner must show a reasonable cause for
getting the premises out of the Act. The
person must be in the full-time employment
of the owner. That is to prevent the
possibility of somebody dodging the Act
by putting in a person who is only a caretaker or is in the part-time employment
of the owner. Then, finally, the owner
must be able to show that he cannot employ this person unless he provides
accommodation.
I am as anxious as any Member of the
House to handicap in no way the efforts
of the Government to find houses for
people to live in, and I think it is quite
right that industry should bear some
portion of the burden that falls on all of
us to strain every effort to provide homes
for people until homes can be built for
them. I do not think it right that industry at this stage should be asked to bear
any unreasonable burden, and I have
drafted this Amendment to exclude any
case in which it would be unreasonable
for the owner to demand his own
premises.
It may be put to me by some Member of
the House that this would only operate
to exclude from the Act premises in cases
in which the owner was going to occupy
them with one of his employees in the very
immediate future, and I say to anyone
with
who urges that criticism that I agree
it. I do not think it would be right in
the present circumstances, and for the
next two or three years at any rate, for
any industry to have a large number of
houses unoccupied on the off-chance that
That
it might want one of those houses.
would be a scandal, and I do not think
we should encourage that to happen.
But if after some of these houses have
been taken up it then appears that a
mistake has been made and that houses
should not have been taken by the local
authority and that, these are required
urgently by a particular industry then
the owner has got a very strong case,
because he can point to the fact that if
circumstances such as I have mentioned
have arisen before the house was actually
taken the house would not come within
the Act at all. Therefore, he has a strong
case to go to the Ministry and local
authority and demand the return of his
house. The words which I have chosen
for the Amendment follow very closely
similar words in the Rent Restriction Act
of 1920. I think that Act is not one
which I would willingly follow on many
occasions because it has been adversely
criticised by almost every judge in the
United Kingdom, but still this particular
provision in the Act has worked reasonably well, and I have no hesitation in
recommending it to the House. I trust
that the Leader of the House will appreciate the pains I have gone to to find a
precedent for this Amendment.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I agree with the
general principle that all available houses
should be made use of to accommodate
the people who are so poorly housed at
the present time, and I think this particular Amendment has been worded in
a very careful and conservative way.
When we consider the position of the
business community to-day we find they
are suffering from many restrictionswhat they sell, how much they can sell,
and who they sell to. They are carrying
on under very great difficulties. I think
this Amendment will prevent injustice
and bring about results which will be very
desirable. It is a very reasonable Amendment. The hon. and gallant Senator has
gone to great care to explain it to the
House and give his reasons. The wording
of the Amendment should commend itself
to any reasonable legislative assembly.
As I said, it is a very desirable Amendment, and I support it.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I very much
appreciate the great care which the hon.
and gallant Senator has given to the
wording of this Amendment. I have the
very greatest sympathy with the object
which he is trying to attain; at the same
time I do not think we can accept the
Amendment in the form in which he has
moved it. I would however, like to
suggest a certain alternative procedure
which may not go the full way to meet
him, but a considerable part of the way.
In Clause 8 (1) there are general powers
given to the Ministry to make regulations.
The sub-clause states:
The Ministry may make regulations
prescribing the form of notices to be
given and records to be kept by a local
authority in the exercise of their functions under this Act and may make
regulations prescribing any other matter
which is to be prescribed and generally
of carrying this Act into effect.
177 Housing (Requisitioning) Bill—

THE SENATE
Report
[Sir Roland Nugent]
The Ministry give an undertaking that
if the Bill becomes an Act a Regulation
to this effect will be made:
re-
In any case where a local authority
receives from the owner
of any
quisitioned premises a notice stating.
that he requires the premises for bona
fide use as a dwelling-house by a person engaged on work necessary for the
proper working of an agricultural
holding, or with whom, conditionally
on housing accommodation being provided, a contract for employment on
such work has been entered into, the
local authority shall forthwith send a
copy of the notice to the Ministry, and
the Ministry, if satisfied that the
premises are so required, shall direct
the local authority to deliver up possession of the requisitioned premises under
section three of the Act.
That covers one case-the case of the
dwelling pertaining to an agricultural
holding and necessary for its working.
The wording is very close to the wording
of the Rent Restriction Act. This has
an important bearing on the matter, because it enables an empty house to be
used until it is required. A man who is
employed on a particular job may already
have a house, or the owner may find it
difficult to get a qualified man, and it
would be a pity, therefore, that the house
should not be used meanwhile. This
Regulation would ensure that the house
if required would be derequisitioned
promptly.
We have not covered the question of
the key factory worker. There the
difficulty in making a Regulation is to
find a form of words that cannot be
abused. The Minister of Home Affairs
asks me to point out that under Clause 3
of the Bill the Ministry has general
powers to require a local authority to deliver up any requisitioned premises. If
premises were to be occupied by a man
who would be regarded as a key-man in industry the local authority could, other
things being equal, be required to give
them up. I think this procedure by way
of Regulation has something to commend
it. If the Regulation is accepted the
Government can use an empty house until
it is wanted for a real purpose. I must
refuse the Amendment.

to that there is a time limit in the Bil

ORDER OF THE DAY.
UNEMPLOYMENT ASSISTANCE
AND SUPPLEMENTARY
PENSIONS.
Determination of Need and Assessment
of Needs.
The following Motions stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:-
Unemployment Assistance (Determination of Need and Assessment of
Needs) Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1943.-That the Regulations
made by the Ministry of Labour on the
30th day of December, 1943, under
Sections 33 and 46 of the Unemployment Assistance Act (Northern
Ireland), 1934, be hereby approved.
Supplementary Pensions (Determination of Need and Assessment of Needs)
Regulations (Northern Ireland), 1943.
That the Regulations made by the
Ministry of Labour on the 30th day of
December, 1943, under Sections 33 and
46 of the Unemployment Assistance
Act (Northern Ireland), 1934, as
applied by Part II of the Old Age and
Widows' Pensions Act (Northern Ireland), 1940, be hereby approved.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): There are two Motions
standing in my name on the Order Paper,
and with your permission, Mr. Speaker,
and that of the House, I will deal with
the two Motions together. They are to
secure the approval of the House to
Unemployment Assistance and Supplementary Pensions Regulations, which were
presented to Parliament on 3rd January,
1944, and are now in full operation. The
Supplementary Pensions Regulations embody in a single document the rules which,
for the time being, govern the grant of
supplementary pensions, and the Unemployment Assistance Regulations do the
same for unemployment assistance.
the process of consolidation the opportunity has been taken to make some
changes with a view to simplifying the
Regulations and effecting some improvements in the general standards of supplementary pensions and assistance. In the
main it is merely a consolidation of existing Regulations, and in some minor particulars the benefits accruing under them
have been increased. I beg to move,
In
"That the Regulations made by the
Ministry of Labour on the 30th day of
December, 1943, under Sections 33 and
46 of the Unemployment Assistance Act
(Northern Ireland), 1934, be hereby
approved.'
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Resolved, "That the Regulations made
by the Ministry of Labour on the 30th
day of December, 1943, under Sections
33 and 46 of the Unemployment Assistance Act (Northern Ireland), 1934, as
applied by Part II of the Old Age and
Widows' Pensions Act (Northern Ireland),
1940, be hereby approved."-(Sir Roland
Nugent.)

1 JUNE 1944
stages of the Trade Boards Bill."-(Sir

ORDER OF THE DAY.
TRADE BOARDS BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): This Bill proposes two
Amendments to the Trade Boards Act
(Northern Ireland), 1923, and their purpose is briefly as follows. Clause 1 gives
power to trade boards, at their discretion,
to fix guaranteed time rates of wages for
workers engaged in piece work, so that
these workers, while still entitled to whatever they can earn on piece rates, will
have a guaranteed minimum sum for each
hour spent on the job. The object really
is to meet difficulties which arise when a
skilled piece rate worker is moved to
another job and for a time cannot earn at
piece rates a reasonable remuneration.
This will cover the interval until he or she
has acquired sufficient skill at the changed
job to earn reasonable remuneration at
piece rates. It is already the practice of
many employers, in fact, to give a minimum time rate during this period, and it
is merely intended by this Clause to give
the trade boards power at their discretion
to enforce what is already the practice of
the better employers on those who do not
yet do it.
The second Clause is purely to relieve
trade boards from having to waste their
own time and that of others in what is
purely a formal settlement of an agreed
plan. I beg to move, "That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I am
very pleased to see this amending Bill
introduced. The trade boards have been
very successful, and they have been a
great benefit to the workers. They have
prevented greedy employers taking advantage of workers, and have required proper
rates to be paid to the workers. There is
it is
nothing strange about this, for
commonly known as a lieu rate. It is
paid in large concerns like the shipyards
and engineering works where, if a man
is not able to make a reasonable wage
at the piece rate, he is paid what is known
as a lieu rate. This is generally settled
by the foreman and gives the worker a
minimum time rate. Therefore, there is
nothing strange in this system, and it is
reasonably fair that in the case of a man
or woman being shifted from one class
of work, with which he or she is thoroughly
acquainted, to some other kind of work
with which he or she is not acquainted
or not so competent, they should be paid
the minimum time rate until they become
competent enough to earn sufficient on the
piece basis. I have much pleasure in
supporting the Second Reading of the Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed." (Sir Roland Nugent.)
On Question, Motion negatived.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
the Third time."-(Sir Roland Nugent.)
Resolved, "That this Bill do now pass."
-(Sir Roland Nugent.)
And a Message was sent to the House
of Commons to acquaint them that the
Senate have agreed to the said Bill without any amendment.

Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, "That this House do now adjourn."
Mr. WHALEY: Until when?
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: Until 6th
June, Tuesday next, for the Royal Assent.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at
Fifteen minutes before
Three o'clock until Tuesday next.

THE WAR.
INVASION OF WESTERN EUROPE.
Mr. LESLIE: May I ask the right
hon. Gentleman, the Leader of the
House, a question of which I have given
private notice? There is a general feeling that any information available would
be very acceptable. I want to ask the
right hon. Gentleman whether he has any
information to give to the House, in addition perhaps to the statement made, as I
understand, at Westminster in the Parliament of the United Kingdom by Mr.
Churchill, and to the statement made by
our own Prime Minister in another place
relating to events which are understood
to have occurred this morning.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I regret that I have no
information to add to that already given
on the wireless, and at Westminster by
the Prime Minister.
The MAYOR OF LONDONDERRY
(Mr. Simmons): Perhaps you would
repeat some of it. We did not all
hear it.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I have myself had the information only secondhand, but I understand that a landing
has taken place in Northern France, and
that the official statement is that so far
everything is proceeding according to
plan. I think that is about all the statement amounts to. Our Prime Minister
has just made a statement in another
place associating this Province with the
hopes and feelings of the United Kingdom
as a whole, and pledging us to do everything in our power to assist in the great
operations now in progress. I do not
think I can add anything to that.
Royal Assent.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I have to
report to the House, That His Grace the
Governor of Northern Ireland, in the
name and on behalf of His Majesty the
King, has been pleased to give his Assent
to the following Bill agreed upon by both
Houses:
Trade Boards.

THE WAR.
MESSAGE TO
GENERAL ALEXANDER.
Mr. SPEAKER: On behalf of the
Senate I sent the following message to
the Prime Minister of Great Britain,
asking that it should be transmitted to
General Alexander :-
"On behalf of the Senate of Northern
Ireland I have been requested to convey through you to General Sir Harold
Alexander their cordial congratulations
to him and to the gallant troops under
his Command, on the brilliant Victory
gained by them in Italy."
I have received the following reply:-
"Thank you for the kind message
from the Senate which I very much
appreciate. I acknowledge your congratulations on behalf of all ranks
under my command."

Flax and Flax Water.
The following questions stood on the
the
Order Paper in
of Major
Dobbs:
name
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state the proportion of
dam-retted flax to the total amount of

To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if our scientists
have been able to make any progress in
the last three years in finding a chemical
to neutralise the injurious effect of flax
water.
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the authorities
propose to take steps to provide small

farmers, to pump out the water of their

Major DOBBS: I expect the right hon.
Gentleman, the Leader of the House,
would like me to ask these three questions at once to save time. In regard to
the first two questions I think they are
quite simply worded, and I am quite
certain that the answer to each of them
will be heard with great interest by
everyone who has anything to do with
flax or the harvesting of flax. In regard
to the third question, I do hope that the
authorities have considered seriously the
question of providing some small pumping
sets or plants that could be used by
farmers to pump out the water from flax
dams on to surrounding ground. It would
not be expensive, and if the pump sets.

Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
were put in the hands of the local garage
man they could be got at any time by
the farmer who wanted to clear out his

that point because I think it is just as

grown for the first
or second time,

small streams. The ratio of flax water to

and I am always afraid that real damage

The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): The answer to the first
question is that I regret that it is not
possible, for security reasons, to divulge
the precise figures asked for by the hon.
Member, but he can take it that approximately 80 per cent. of the 1943 crop was
dam retted. In regard to the second
question, there has not been any research
work carried out during the past three
years by the Ministry of Agriculture, or,
as far as I am aware, by outside bodies
to discover a chemical to neutralise the
injurious effects of flax water, and even
if the technical staff was available for
the purpose they would mostly be doing
more important things at the present
time. In regard to the third question, it
is not the intention at present to provide
small pumps, but it is understood that a
certain number of these are available for
hire. Of course there is a considerable
difficulty in getting them at the present
time.
Major DOBBS: May I ask the right
hon. Gentleman if those small pumping
sets are on the market, or have they been
given out to various people?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: As I understand it they are available, presumably
in various tradesmen's hands through the
country, and it is possible to hire them,
but whether there is a large quantity of
them available I do not know. In any
case, they would be extremely difficult to
get hold of in the present circumstances.
Major DOBBS: We want them distributed so as to be at the disposal of the
people.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Perhaps if
the hon. and gallant Member took the
matter up with the Ministry of Agriculture it would be possible to suggest districts where a pump should be available,
and conceivably they might be able to
help in some way.
Major DOBBS: I would like to thank
the right hon. Gentleman for his answer.
Mr. BARCLAY: Seeing there are so
many of these flax dams convenient to
streams, is it not possible that the flax
water poured out on to the grass would
make its way to the streams, or does the
grass purify the water and take the poison
out of it?
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of what has
been said, might I ask the right hon.

who has great knowledge in these matters,
whether there is any magic about these
flax water pumps, or are they simply the

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Any small
pump, either power driven or with a
suitable handle, will do the job. I am
afraid I have not sufficient technical
knowledge to answer the question by
Senator Barclay, but if he likes to put
it down as a question on the Order Paper
I will see I get the information from
someone who does know.
Mr. STEVENSON: The pump which
I understand is used for the purpose is
the ordinary sludge pump, and most
effective it is. I believe there are quite
a lot of them to be had if they could be
secured, and they have been used for Government purposes. With regard to
Senator Barclay's question, the water
thrown on to the grass does not filter into
streams to any appreciable extent, nor
does it do any harm to the grass.
the time being, anyway, it is the best
way out of the difficulty with the ordinary
country dam.
Widow's Pension Claim.
For
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Lynch:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can say if there is any
remedy for a widow making a claim in
respect of her late husband's 20 years'
insurable employment, but finding that
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
18 JULY 1944
contributions had not been collected in
respect of them and that her husband
had not been an insured person;
whether she has a claim against the
employer who failed to stamp cards
during those years, or against the
Ministry, whose officers neglected to see
that the man was insured and only collected three years' contributions after
the death, and whether the National
Health Insurance Acts stipulate that a
person may sue an employer for benefits lost under like circumstances of
failure to stamp cards.
Mr. MAGUIRE: Senator Lynch has
been summoned to serve on the Grand
Jury to-day, and he is unable to attend
here. He asked me to put the questions
standing in his name. The first question
expresses itself fairly fully, but there is
one point to which I would like to draw
the attention of the Leader of the House,
and that is, in the case quoted, where a
workman has been employed for 20 years,
the labour exchange was only able to collect three years' contributions, thereby
preventing the widow from receiving the
widow's pension. Perhaps the Leader of
the House will explain why only three
years' contributions were collected if the
man was employed for 20 years.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In answer
to the first question, as it is a rather
lengthy answer I will read it. The
answer is:
It is clear that, in any case where no
contributions have been paid under the
Health and Pensions Acts until after the
death of an employee, his widow cannot
establish a title to a widow's pension
under the Contributory Pensions Act.
Section 5 (2) of the Widows', Orphans'
and Old Age Contributory Pensions Act
(Northern Ireland), 1936, provides that,
in determining whether the statutory
conditions have been complied with, no
account shall be taken of contributions
paid after the death of the person in
respect of whose insurance the pension
is claimed. There is a proviso to this
sub-section, but the proviso is not such
as to enable a widow's pension to be paid
where no contributions at all were paid
before the death of the husband.
Section 174 of the National Health
Insurance Act, 1936, provides that an
employee or any person claiming through
an employee may, by means of civil
proceedings, recover sums equal to the
benefits lost on account of an employer's
failure or neglect to comply with the
provisions of the Act in the matter of
the payment and collection of contributions. It has been held in the High
Court in Northern Ireland that the
terms of Section 13 of the Widows',
Orphans' and Old Age Contributory
Pensions Act (Northern Ireland), 1936,
are such as to make Section 174 of the
Health Insurance Act applicable to proceedings by a widow against her deceased
husband's employer for non-compliance
with the provisions of the Pensions Act.
Obviously the Ministry of Labour cannot be held responsible in any case where
non-compliance is not reported to its
inspectors until after the death of the
employee.
If I am right in my assumption-and
I must not mention names-I think the
case to which the hon. Senator refers is
one in which a man was not, in fact,
in insurable employment for 20 years.
He was connected with his employer's
firm but he was not in an insurable
capacity until towards the end of his life,
and I believe that is the reason why only
a certain number of years' contributions
have been collected. That is always
assuming it is the case I think it is.
You will see from that answer that the
position really is that probably-though
I cannot give legal advice-the widow
has a claim against the employer, but
she should consult her own legal adviser
as to whether she has or not.
Farmers and Old Age Pension.
Mr. MAGUIRE: The second question
in Senator's Lynch's name is to ask the
Leader of the House: :-
Whether he can state the number
of farmers in receipt of an old age
pension during recent years who were
found to be earning over £1,000 a
year, as stated by the Minister of
Labour on May 17th, and in what
electoral areas and counties they
reside.
I am greatly surprised that any
farmers are making £1,000 per annum.
I know it is not my experience of farming. I do not know how the labour
exchange has arrived at the profits of
these elderly farmers, because in many
cases since the war stocks have been
Questions to Minister
Local Government Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Maguire]
depleted, and very often it is a bank
account that is taken as a guide by the
labour exchange. Stocks are reduced on
account of the high tillage requirements
at the present time. The price realised
for many crops does not cover the cost
of production. It has been stated in this
House and in other places on many
occasions that the potato crop is produced
at a loss to the farmer of from £7 to £8
an acre, and, to my knowledge, that has
never been disputed. When you consider
the cost of sorting and handling the
potatoes, that is a fairly accurate estimate
of the loss entailed in the production of
potatoes.
Some small farmers who employ family
labour can very often make a profit
against the farmer who has to employ all
outside labour. Those small farmers make
no provision for their families, and they
accumulate a surplus in the bank, but
that is not a fair guide to take of farming. I remember that some years ago a
great friend of mine who was in the
licensed trade in Belfast asked my advice
on purchasing a farm. I gave him advice,
and he attended an auction and purchased
a farm. He wanted an outlet for some
of his family, and he also wanted the
farm for health reasons.
A few years
afterwards I asked him how he was
getting along. He said he had lost money
on farming, and if it had not been for
the licensed house he would have been in
the bankruptcy court. His opinion was
that every farmer should have a licensed
house as well as a farm. I do not think
the prospects are too rosy for farmers at
the present time.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In the
course of a special review of old
age
pensions carried out last year it was
found that a number of pensioners growing produce which commanded an exceptional market during the
war were
deriving incomes above the statutory limit
for receipt of a pension. In a few cases
the income exceeded £1,000 a year. The
information obtained by the Ministry in
the course of its inquiries is strictly confidential and I cannot, therefore, supply
the details asked for by the hon. Senator.

Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Local

Acts Amendment Bill, and the Appropriation Bill, to which they desire the

LOCAL GOVERNMENT (FINANCE)
BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The purpose of this Bill is to remove the restrictive maxima, imposed by the Act of 1936,
in respect of grants to local authorities
to make good the loss of revenue due to
derating, and to provide for the payment
of grants as follows:-(a) In the case of
urban authorities, a grant sufficient to
meet the actual loss of revenue. (b) In
the case of a local authority, other than
an urban authority-
(i) a consolidated grant, representing
the standard and supplemental
grants payable under existing practice, and calculated on the basis of
the amounts actually paid to such
local authority in respect of the
Planning Acts Bill ---
Second Reading
18 JULY 1944
year 1942-43, the latest year for
which audited accounts are available, and
(ii) an additional annual grant, not
exceeding 65 per cent. of the excess of expenditure by the local
authority concerned in the grant
year as compared with the basic
year 1942-43. The figure of 65
per cent., calculated with reference
to the proportion of derated hereditaments in the areas in question
is, together with the consolidated
grants, intended to recoup to the
rural authorities taken as a whole
the total loss in rate income attributable to derating.
In other words, this is a Bill, broadly
speaking, to restore to the local authorities the rating income they lost in consequence of the application of derating.
I beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.
PLANNING ACTS AMENDMENT
BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In 1931
urban and rural authorities were given
powers to be planning authorities for
their respective areas. For the purposes
of economy, co-ordination, and convenience, certain of these authorities
are
anxious that these powers should revert
to the county councils, and certain county
councils are understood to be willing to
have these powers. The object of the
present Bill is to make this possible, providing both bodies consent. It is obvious
that for the sake of convenience planning
should be done by the county council
rather than by the rural or urban district
council, and, in cases where both councils
desire that this should be done, the Bill
provides the necessary financial and other
arrangements for this to be carried into
effect. I beg to move, "That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: This very short Bill,
or, at all events, the first Clause which
is the main part of the Bill, is entirely
permissive. There is no compulsion on
either county councils or district councils,
whether urban or rural, to fall in with
the new proposal. Whether this proposal is one that has been promoted by
the Government or not I do not know,
or whether it springs spontaneously from
a desire of some of the county councils
to recover ground which they had
perhaps lost, or whether it springs from
a desire on the part of one or more urban
or rural district councils to get rid of
responsibilities they had, perhaps somewhat thoughtlessly, assumed I do not
know. The difficulty that occurs to me
is this, and it is a difficulty to which I
propose to direct attention at a later
stage of the Bill.
All these local authorities, both urban
and district councils, and also to some
extent county councils, are now somewhat over-ripe. We all know that, for
reasons we need not go into, it has been
necessary to postpone local government
elections for a period of some years, and
therefore we are somewhat without a
guarantee as to whether the county
councils of to-day really represent the
wishes of the constituents they originally
represented. Planning is new. Speaking generally, the country is sick to
death of it. They have heard so much
about planning and have seen so little
result in the way of houses, which is
what they do want, that they are not
inclined to attach too much importance
to any more they hear about it.
we want to make sure that in any retransfer of powers such as is suggested
in this Bill the consenting authorities do
represent the up-to-date feeling of their
constituents. The only way I can think
of in which this can be done would be
by drawing a little more on the possibilities of local inquiry.
But
I think it might be possible to draw
attention on the Committee stage to the
fact that before any of these retransfers take place the approval of the
Ministry of Health and Local Government should be sought, and reminding
the public that before such sanction is
given there should be a possibility of
holding the local inquiry which is provided for throughout the 1931 Act. If
you do that you are quite safe.
If you
get your two authorities to agree, and
if there is any disposition shown on the
part of any of those authorities to object,
Planning Acts Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
then it will be possible to hold a local
inquiry at which everybody concerned
can say what they want to say. If, on
the other hand, they do not take any
steps towards promoting the holding of
a local inquiry, then you may safely
assume they are quite content to fall in
with the desire of their own local council.
There is another smaller question to
which I would direct attention. I cannot help thinking that the method of
providing for the expenses of a rural
district council as designed in the Bill is
somewhat obscure. The explanation is
that you are thrown back on sub-section
(3) of Section 16 of the principal Act of
1931. The effect of that is that you have
your authorities in two separate Acts,
and I am inclined to think that that is
rather a nuisance from the point of view
of the individual ratepayer. In all
probability no one will ever look at the
Act as regards its financial grants, except
the class of financial authorities immediately concerned. I do think that the
House ought to look at that and see
whether anything can be done.
To
There remains the question of the proposed repeal of Section 12, I think it is,
of the principal Act. That was the
Section that provided that landowners
should receive notice of possible planning
schemes in their immediate neighbourhood, and they were empowered to
register their names with their local
authority in order that they might secure
for themselves the right to receive the
notices before anything was done.
what an extent an advantage such as this
was brought into effect I do not know.
My own opinion is that the provision was
put into the 1931 Act and then left in
the pigeon-hole. I do not think that it
was ever used, but the fact remains that
there is the possibility that somebody
may wish to use it, and if he does he
ought to say so. If not, I think we ought
not to hear any more about it.
I do not propose to the House to
take any steps in regard to repealing the
Section I have referred to, because I am
not satisfied that the repeal of the Section
will do any harm whatever. Its repeal
will, as indicated in the Bill, relieve some
of the local authorities. Someone may
think it an unnecessary burden, and it
may very well be that other Members of
the House, after their attention has been
drawn to this matter, may take a
different view and may think it right to
ask the House in Committee whether the
repeal of this Section is or is not desirable. Personally, I see reason to
object to the Bill; in fact, rather the
other way.
It does afford a possible
chance of accelerating procedure and
removing inconvenience.
no
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: The
right hon. Gentleman will correct me if
I am wrong, but I think that under the
present Planning Acts the local authority,
the urban district council, submits its
plan to the Government Planning Commission first and then it has to be sent
back to the county council for reconsideration. If that is right it may often
happen that the plan might have to be
started ab initio because for some reason
the county council had not been consulted
in the first instance and the plan might
be abortive. In this Bill, as far as I
understand it, this possibility is to some
extent avoided, because now the urban
district council can have their powers
vested in the county council, and so the
county council will have the first chance
of preparing the plan before it goes up
to the Planning Advisory Commission, or
whoever for the time being is the Government authority to consider this planning.
If I am wrong, I am sure the right hon.
Gentleman will correct me, and if I am
right I am sure the Bill will do a great
deal of good.
Mr. McALLISTER: When I read this
Bill, the same thought occurred to me as
occurred to Senator Leslie, and I share
his anxiety that it will enable the urban
and rural district councils who are not
desirous of doing their duty on this very
important question to shirk their responsibilities. I should like. if the right hon.
Gentleman, the Leader of the House, at
a later stage in the Bill could assure us
that this will not be so, and by what
means it is proposed to prevent such
authorities from failing to carry out
their duties. I feel, on this question of
housing, that no opportunity should be
allowed to pass without impressing upon
the Government the urgency, the magnitude, and the necessity of addressing
themselves to this particular question.
As has been said before, and been said
truthfully, there is a moral obligation
on the Government to attend to this
Planning Acts Bill
Adjournment
18 JULY 1944
matter at once. I go further, and say
there is a Christian duty on the Government to see that this housing question is
properly adjusted. If the Government
fail to carry out their duty on this question, Parliament, and each Member of
Parliament, should see to it that they are
not allowed, consciously or unconsciously,
to evade that duty. As I look upon it,
family life to be properly cultivated and

and as I also look upon it, family life is
the cradle for good or ill of every future
people and State, not only here. but
throughout the world. Therefore, I say
that this question of the housing of the
people is one of the most fundamental
duties to which this Government and
Parliament can be called upon to address
itself.

Some time ago a Bill was passed enabling the local authorities and the
Government to acquire and enter into
possession of proposed building sites.
quite realise that it is impossible for the
Government actually to construct the
houses required to-day; steel, bricks,
timber, and other materials are not available, but there is no reason why the
Government should not proceed with the
ordinary necessary amenities which must
be a preliminary to any building scheme,
namely, the construction of roads and the
provision of sewerage and water schemes,
electricity, and those other amenities.
When that Bill was before us, I said that
when the Government was empowered to
enter into possession of the land, practically without any authority, the House
would expect that the Government would
be able to render an account of their
activities at an early date.
I do not propose at this stage to ask

particulars of housing schemes. and
lands, and of the situation of the land
that has been acquired, of drainage.
schemes, and of the other amenities which
have been formulated and carried out,
or are to be carried out; but I do say
this, that I for one, when the autumn
Session starts, will expect to have some
definite information from the Government on the practical and active steps.
taken to meet this question of the shortage of houses which is going to be a tremendous problem immediately this war
is over.

Resolved, "That this Report be now
received."-(Sir Roland Nugent.)

PLANNING ACTS AMENDMENT
BILL.
Order for the House to be put into
Committee read.
Resolved, "That this House do now
resolve itself into Committee on the said
Bill." (Sir Roland Nugent.)
In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Amendments as to powers
of district councils).
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :-
Clause 1, after sub-clause (2) to insert
the following new sub-clause:
"(3) A resolution or consent for the
purposes of sub-section (1) or subsection (2) of this section shall not take
effect without the sanction of the
Ministry of Health and Local Government and the provisions of section
nineteen of the principal Act (which
relate to local inquiries) shall apply
with respect to the giving or withholding of such sanction.'
Mr. LESLIE: I gave some sort of
warning on the Second Reading that I
proposed to raise this point. In fact, I
am not sure that I did not go a little
further than I should have done on the
Second Reading in dealing with a Committee point, but knowing it was proposed
to take the Committee stage this afternoon I thought that perhaps the House
would pardon me. The point I want to
bring out by this Amendment is this.
Planning Acts Bill-
Committee
20 JULY 1944
At present our local councils, whether
urban or rural, really have been very
long in office. Normally they would have
been renewed before this, but owing to
prevailing circumstances. it has been
necessary to postpone elections and so on,
and therefore we cannot say with absolute
certainty that the council of to-day
represents the present feeling of its constituents. There is, it seems to me, a
possible danger that on this Clause action
may be taken which the electors of to-day
would not approve, and that action might
be taken without their having any notice
of it.
Accordingly, it seems to me desirable
that we should put in a sort of loophole
which may or may not be taken advantage of. I am afraid I am inclined to
think it may not be taken advantage of,
but the loophole should be there. The
only method I can think of is by making
the sanction which has to be given by
the Ministry of Health and Local Government definitely one of the powers and
duties of that Ministry, and so bringing
into play Section 19 of the principal Act,
which is more than ten years old, and
which enables a local inquiry to be held
if the Ministry think it desirable. The
effect of this Amendment would be that
without doubt it would be possible to
hold a local inquiry if the circumstances
seemed to require it. That would give
everybody an opportunity of saying anything, if they had anything to say.
Accordingly I beg to move the Amendment standing in my name.
Question put, "That those words be
there inserted."
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
the Government must resist this Amendment. The object of the Bill, as the
Committee will appreciate, is to encourage
and facilitate the transfer of these powers
back, as it were, from the district council
to the county council in cases where both
bodies agree, and it is of some importance
that, if this is to happen, it should
happen reasonably quickly. Planning
work is going on, and most of the counties
have appointed their planning officers,
and if these powers are going back to the
county councils it is desirable that they
should be transferred at a fairly early
date. To insert this Amendment in the
Bill would mean the possibility of delay.
It is true, of course, that the local
authorities are, in a sense, stale bodies,
but they still are elected bodies, and I
should be a little doubtful of transferring
the decision from an elected body to the
bureaucracy. I am a little surprised at
the right hon. Gentleman having made
the suggestion, but I am equally delighted
that he has done so, because it enables me
to pose as a good democrat.
I see very
few possibilities of a local inquiry eliciting anything of value. You will always
find some cross-grained person in any
district objecting to anything being done,
but I do not think you would get any
serious objection if the county council and
the rural district council concerned were
agreeable to the powers being transferred.
If we begin to argue that any elected
body is stale, and therefore it must not
act, we are going into very deep water,
because it might possibly be said that
Parliament is stale and should not legislate. We might eventually reach the
state where we should have to have a
referendum on everything. I appreciate
the right hon. Gentleman's point, but in
view of all the circumstances we feel we
cannot accept the Amendment.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I do not
suppose it is necessary to say anything
further after the statement of the Leader.
of the House, particularly as I agree with
him in the attitude he has taken up on
this Amendment. Like every other
Amendment put forward, there are pros
and cons, but I think that in this case
the cons have it. The Bill is intended
to facilitate the transference of responsibilities to the bigger body, and I think it
is an advantage that the bigger body
should exercise the right of future
planning rather than the smaller body.
The Amendment, while it introduces
certain safeguards, might hamper and delay that transference of responsibility.
There is just one remark I might make
in regard to the fact that these local
bodies might be out of touch with popular
sentiment at the present time. As the
Leader of the House indicated, there are
other bodies, and in particular the body.
that might have the final say in this
matter, that might be said to be even
more out of touch with local sentiment
than the rural authorities are. Indeed,
the only representative body in the
country at the moment which obviously
is not out of touch with sentiment is the
Senate.

Mr. LESLIE: I think it is pretty clear
that the opinion is that the necessity for
a safeguard is not of sufficient urgency
to run the risk of imposing a delay. I
think that is the general sense of the
Committee, and, that being so, I ask
leave to withdraw the Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
