Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
1, sub-clause 4, after the word "incurred"
(on account of expenses incurred) in line
14, to insert the words, "by the county
council". This is a much smaller matter,
and, I am bound to say, of very little
importance. It struck me in reading this
over that there was an obscurity in the
phrase. The difficulty is that a rural
district council is not, as the Committee
is
rate-making authority.
Expenses incurred by a rural district
council have to be met by a rate which
has to be raised by the county council
and specially imposed on the rural district
council concerned. Provision was made in
aware,
a
the principal Act of 1931 in two successive
sub-sections of Section 16 dealing with
the methods of raising funds, whether it
might be on rural or urban district
councils, and as long as the two subsections stood together the position was
perfectly clear. Now it is proposed to
repeal sub-section (4) of Section 16 and to
put in a new provision indicated by subclause (4) of the Clause before us, and by
that means divorcing the two provisions
from each other.
The result is that it is a little difficult
to follow, and I think it would be advantageous to insert these words. But I do
not suggest that the insertion of these
words will make any difference to the
sense. The intention is that things should
proceed as though those words were there,
and in fact I am afraid that the words
are really unnecessary. At the same time
I think it would be an advantage to put

may have to read the Section. Whether

Question put, "That those words be
there inserted".
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I regret
that the Government have to resist this
Amendment. We do appreciate the point
made by the right hon. Gentleman, but I
have discussed this at some length with
the Parliamentary draughtsman and we
think the words "expenses incurred" so
obviously relate to the fuller phrase in
the first line of the sub-clause that in fact
it should be clear to anybody reading it.
There is no question as to what the legal
interpretation should be, and we
reluctant to insert a small Amendment
which is not absolutely necessary, because
it will entail a certain amount of trouble
and time being spent on it in another
place. I quite agree that the Amendment does no more than underline what
has already been said in the Clause, but
we believe that, as drafted, the Clause is
sufficiently clear. Chiefly in order to
save time and trouble in another place we
prefer not to accept the Amendment.

Planning Acts Bill
Adjournment
20 JULY 1944
ambiguity, but I do think it exists.
Strictly interpreted, the words as they
stand would forbid the county council at
any time from including in their budget
the expenses that might be incurred by a
rural district council in carrying out the
work of planning. These rural district
councils are not, as the mover of the
Amendment has pointed out, rate-raising
bodies, and they have no other way of
securing money to meet their expenses
than by asking the county council to
approve their expenses and to raise money
for them. This Clause, as it stands,
states specifically that no sum shall be
raised on any district on account of expenses incurred. Those expenses may be
expenses of a rural district authority, and
those words as they stand, being strictly
interpreted, would seem to me to put a
ban on the county council, forbidding
them to raise any sum for meeting the
expenses which have been incurred by a
rural district authority.

""
""

the word

expenses

DRAINAGE ACT, 1942.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I beg to move:
That the Order made by the Minister
of Home Affairs on the 3rd day of July,
1944, with the consent of the Minister
of Finance, under sub-section (3) of
section two of the Drainage Act (Northern Ireland), 1942, postponing the
date prescribed under the said subsection beyond which grants towards
any expenditure incurred by a county
council shall not be made, be hereby
confirmed.
I should perhaps explain that the
Drainage Act, 1942, empowered the
Ministry of Home Affairs to make grants,
not exceeding 75 per cent., towards the
cost of drainage schemes undertaken by
county councils and designed solely to increase the acreage for food production.
during the war. A proviso in the Act
allowed the prescribed date, 31st October,
1944, to be postponed for not more than
two successive years by Orders made by
the Minister of Home Affairs, with the
consent of the Minister of Finance. These
Orders have to be confirmed by Resolution
of each House.
I think the Government may claim that
the Act has been a success-8,420 acres
have so far been reclaimed for food cultivation, the amount of the State grant
being approximately £107,000. Limited
engineering staff and equipment have precluded the completion of all the emergency drainage schemes within the prescribed period, and I am sure that the
House will agree it would be a great pity
to stop these now without completing
them. I may add that something like
ten schemes are outstanding.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I am very glad to
hear that this Order is to be extended
and I take it it will also include the clearing of shrubbery from hills.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Everything
provided for in the original Act.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I think the extension
will certainly help the farmers to keep
their workers on through the lean period,
that is, the winter period. It will also be
a great asset to the Government, for it
will mean that the ground will be prepared for production in later years. I
think it is very satisfactory that the Order
is going to be extended.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Business of the House.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: In moving
the second Resolution standing in my
name I am afraid I owe an apology to
the House. When we put this Resolution
down we hoped that the print of the Bill
would be in the hands of hon. Senators
at a slightly earlier date than it was.
Unfortunately there were one or two
drafting Amendments which slightly
delayed the final printing of the Bill.
Nevertheless it is a Bill largely of a noncontroversial nature, and I therefore move
"That Standing Order No. XXV be
suspended for this day's sitting for the
purpose of taking the Second Reading
of the Pensions (Increase) Bill when
received from the House of Commons."
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Message from the Commons.
The Commons have passed the Pensions
(Increase) Bill, to which they desire the
concurrence of this House.
PENSIONS (INCREASE) BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Pensions (Increase) Bill-
Second Reading
1 AUGUST 1944
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That the Bill be now read a
Second time." I need not detain the
House very long, as there is a very full
memorandum explaining the purposes of
the Bill attached to it. In short, the
object of the Bill is to grant certain
pension increases to civil servants,
teachers, local government officers, police,
including Special Constabulary; petty
sessions clerks and firemen, and follows.
mainly the lines of the recent Imperial
Government Act.
"
The first part of the Bill proposes to
grant to pensioners of slender means increases in pension, varying between 20
per cent. and 30 per cent. to meet the
general war-time rise in prices. These
increases are subject to an over-riding
income limit of £300 per annum in the
case of a married pensioner or one who
has at least one dependant, and of £225
in other cases. In the assessment of
means, however, the first £52 a year of
any income, other than pension, will be
disregarded.
The second part of the Bill proposes to .
grant varying increases up to 10 per cent.
in the pensions of civil servants who
retired since February, 1922.
Pensions
in this category, as the House will remember, contained a variable element, in
respect of the cost-of-living figure, until
1934, when it was stabilised on a figure
of 155. Since the outbreak of war the
cost-of-living figure has risen to 200, and
the proposed increases will compensate to
some extent for the rise in prices since
1939.
These are the two main parts of the
Bill. One is to relieve a definite hardship and therefore, because of this definite
hardship, there is an income limit. The
other part is to compensate in part
civil servants who are adversely affected
by having had their pensions stabilised at
the 1934 figure. It compensates them in
part, because we have all got to bear, to
some extent, the rise in prices caused by
the war.
Question proposed.
Mr. MCALLISTER: There are one or
two points in this Bill that I would like
to refer to. In the first place, I do protest against the fact that this Bill
differentiates financially between teachers
in Northern Ireland and teachers in England. I think that is a position which
the Government cannot stand over or
justify. I hold strongly the view that,
taking into consideration the relative
taxable capacity of the two countries,
England and this part of Ireland, the
present Imperial Contribution should not
be paid when our teachers and others are
placed in an inferior position owing to
that Imperial Contribution, and I am
confirmed in that view by Commissions
that have sat composed of men of sound
and sane views. I hold very strongly
that this position cannot be justified.
I know instances where men embarked
before 1922 on the establishment of
schools, and, to my mind, these men were
public benefactors. They established schools
in districts where there were previously no
facilities for secondary education and
which covered a thinly populated area.
They opened avenues of employment in
the Civil Service, in the medical profession, in the legal profession, and many
other avocations of life to which the
people of these districts could not have
had access had these schools not been
established. These men, to my mind,
were public benefactors. There is no
doubt about that.
Then the 1923 Bill came along and
their business as school holders was confiscated, and they were put on a salary at
Now by
great disadvantage to them.
this Bill they are put at a further disadvantage by being placed in an inferior
position financially to teachers in Great
Britain, who were in no way superior and
who had not to do the spade-work that
has been done by many teachers in Northern Ireland. I am sure no Member of
this House will try to justify the fact
this
that not so long ago teachers in
country started at under £30 a year, and
that the maximum they received was £90.
I do not think anyone would try to
justify that.
A means test under this Bill is just as
reactionary a procedure as the fact that
teachers were at that time paid under
£30 a year.
What will be the affect of
this means test? Boiled down, it means
that if a man, by frugal living, by constant industry, and by looking after his
small resources, has saved a small amount
of money to provide for the winter of his
life he is to be taxed by the State for that
industry and enterprise. I should like
the Leader of the House, when he comes
to reply, to try to justify the means test.
I respectfully submit to the House that
Pensions (Increase) Bill
Adjournment
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
this means test is unjustifiable, especially
in the case of the various services covered
by the Bill.
In conclusion, I should like to say that
we all recognise the value of education in
Northern Ireland. If we are going to
have successful education-I understand
a new Education Bill is to be introduced
-we must attract the very best men as
teachers. To do so we must make the
outlook sufficiently attractive. We can
only do that by putting them on a financial
basis similar to that which members of
the teaching profession enjoy in England.
A good teacher could hardly be compensated for the service he renders to the
community. By his efforts he opens up
for the children of comparatively poor
people avocations and fields of enterprise
in life that they could not take advantage
of without his unstinted service.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: First of all,
I should like to make it clear that this
Bill does not differentiate in the least between the pensions paid to teachers in
Britain and the pensions paid to teachers
in this country. In this Bill both are
treated alike.
Mr. McALLISTER: What about the
years of service?
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: This Bill
does not attempt or pretend to alter the
main basis on which pensions are paid.
That is a different matter altogether, and
does not come within the scope of this
Bill. This is a Measure to attempt to
remedy, in part, hardships suffered by
certain people, mainly the poorer class of
pensioners, owing to the rise in the cost
of living during the war. We hope that
this rise in the cost of living will be a
temporary thing. This is not a pensions
Bill in the sense that it affects the basic
rate of pension. So far as this Bill is
concerned, its provisions. are identical
with those in the Imperial Act. This is
also the explanation why a means test is
provided under the Bill. I am not
enamoured with a means test, but in this
case I think it is justified, because we
are seeking to remedy hardship. To do
so we must ascertain if there is hardship.
I hope I have made myself clear.
Mr. LESLIE: There is one point
which might be added to what the right
hon. Gentleman has stated. It is not
only the fact that the means are very
small that gives a person a right to an
increase, but also the fact that the demands on those means are exceptional.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I thank the
right hon. Gentleman.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.

(4) In this Act, unless the context
otherwise requires, the following
expressions have the meanings hereby
assigned to them, that is to say:-
"enactment" includes any public
general Act or local Act (whether
passed by the Parliament of Northern Ireland or the Parliament of
the United Kingdom) and any

such an Act;
*
"local authority" means the counciì
of any county, county borough or

Mr. McALLISTER: I beg to move in
Page 9, line 18, after the word "district"
("county borough or county district") to
insert the words "urban district". This
is purely a drafting Amendment and is
not in any way a contentious matter.
According to this particular Bill the
definition of the words "local authority"
means the council of any county, county
borough or county district, the commissioners of any town, the Belfast City
and District Water Commissioners, and
includes a committee of a local authority
and a joint committee of several local
authorities. It will be noted that urban
districts are not included within that
definition. If the Government are in a
position to give an undertaking that
urban districts are to be included and
will receive the benefit of this Bill I will
not press the Amendment.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I am advised that in
this particular Clause the term "county
district" includes an urban district.
Section 22 (3) of the Local Government
(Ireland) Act, 1898, provides that:
In this and every other Act (unless
the context otherwise requires) . . . the
expression "county district" shall
include every urban district, whether a
borough or not, which is not a county
borough, and every rural district.
County boroughs are mentioned in the
Clause. I think I can safely say that the
urban districts will be covered by the
provisions in the Act of 1898.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Clause ordered to stand part of the Bill.

233 Unemployment Insurance Bill- 24 OCTOBER 1914

Second Reading
UNEMPLOYMENT
INSURANCE
(INCREASE OF BENEFIT)
BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davison.)
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: As the
Minister of Labour is with us, I think it
would be. for the convenience and enlightenment of the House if he, rather
than I, dealt with this
technical
very
Bill. I beg to move,
"That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
The MINISTER OF LABOUR (Mr.
Midgley): I should like, first of all, to
express my appreciation of the privilege
afforded me of attending in this House
to very briefly outline the provisions of
this Bill.
The effects of this Bill, which has been
passed by the Commons without amendment, are set out in the. explanatory
memorandum which hon. Members have
received. Broadly speaking, the Bill
raises the rates of unemployment benefit
by from 20 to 33 per cent.-about 25 per
cent. on the average. Unemployment
benefits were last adjusted in 1940. Since
then there has been some increase in the
cost of living and, of course, we have had
the Beveridge. Report, and now recently
the British Government's White Paper
on comprehensive social insurance.
The
During the last four years the official
cost-of-living index has risen by about 11
per cent. of what it was in 1940.
meaning of such an index is perhaps
rather difficult to interpret in times of
scarcity and rationing, but when we
compare this 11 per cent. with the proposed increase of about 25 per cent. in
benefits, I think it is clear that there is
a comfortable margin. It is, indeed, not
so much as a cost-of-living adjustment
that we must regard the proposal before
us, but as an interim measure pending the
reorganisation of social insurance, designed to cover the period when many
workers will be leaving war work, or the
Forces, for other employments.
To recast unemployment insurance as
part of a comprehensive scheme is a complex task that lies ahead. But meanwhile,
with unemployment at a low level, it is
possible to raise benefits, so as to attain
in most cases the "White Paper" rates,
without requiring additional contributions. Under the Bill, an adult man gets
24s a week if single; 40s if married; 45s
if in addition he has one dependent child;
50s if he has two. These rates are the
same as those that were proposed in the
British Government's White Paper. So
also is the rate of 20s for a married
woman getting benefit in her own right.
This rate of 20s, compared with 22s for the
woman living independently, gives effect
to a new principle. Married women tend
to have poorer contribution records than
single women, and they are usually in
less need if unemployed. Sir William
Beveridge recommended different rates of
benefit for married and unmarried
women, and this principle is embodied
both in the British Government's comprehensive proposals and in the Bill.
The measure before us is similar to that
which has just been approved in Great
Britain, and there are good reasons,
known to this House, why we here should
keep our social insurance code uniform
with that of the rest of the United
Kingdom. It may, therefore, be superfluous for me to go now into further details of the Bill or its background; but if
hon. Senators desire elucidation of any
particular point in the Bill, I shall be
glad to afford it.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
would like to say that we are very pleased
to have the Minister of Labour with us
this afternoon and we are glad he has been
able to come along to explain the provisions of this Bill. So far as the Bill itself
is concerned, I for one am very pleased
that the Government has thought it advisable to introduce such a measure. As has
been pointed out, the cost of living has
soared very high without any increase in
unemployment benefit, and the increases
given by this Bill will have some little
effect in easing the burden of the increased
cost of living. I understand from the
speech of the Minister of Labour that it is
not only existing conditions that this.
measure is intended to provide for. It is
quite possible that when the war ends we
may have a number of unemployed in our
midst, and it is then that the increased
benefits enumerated in the Bill will be of
particular advantage to the men and
women who are unemployed.
I am sure I am expressing the view of
most people when I say it is to be hoped
that the unemployment figures will never
Unemployment Insurance Bill
Expiring Laws Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Joseph Cunningham]
again soar to the height they reached after
the last war. I trust that arrangements
will be made to prevent such a thing
happening at the conclusion of this war,
although I suppose there is bound to be
some degree of unemployment during the
change from war to peace conditions. The
fact that a single woman is to get more
benefit than a married woman is rather
peculiar, but the explanation may be that
a single woman is in more need of assistance than a married woman, who may
be
able to live on her husband's earnings,
whereas a single woman may have no one
to depend on. I am very pleased that the
Government has introduced this Bill, and
thus recognised the need for some increase
in unemployment benefit to combat the
increased cost of living.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
rise mainly to support the reference to the
appearance of the Minister of Labour in
this House, and to say how much we welcome him here. I do think there is a case
for an increase in unemployment benefit,
although the position cannot be put right
permanently until the whole new scheme
of social insurance is carried into effect.
This Bill may be regarded as a temporary
measure that may eventually be superceded by a greater scheme which, no
doubt, the Government are considering.
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me, and it
probably seems to other Members of this
House, that this Bill was almost inevitable. There are the contingencies which
have been alluded to by the Minister of
Labour, who is paying us a first visit,
to our great satisfaction, but besides
those there is the fact which I think I
am correct in stating, that our Unemployment Fund has got about £7,000,000
in hand. I think that is approximately
the correct figure, and there being a
certain amount of distress about, and the
danger of there being a great deal more
distress, it seems but natural that some
of that £7,000,000 should be made use
of. On the financial side there does not
seem to be anything to be said against it.
As regards the increase in agricultural
benefit, I cannot help feeling rather a
doubt that that arises not from the
material aspects of the case so much as
from the moral aspects. It has always
seemed to me that matters dealing with
agricultural benefit are always regarded
from the moral point of view rather than
the material, as being very different to
those applying to the industrial part of
His Majesty's subjects. The two things
are not quite the same. We have found,
I am afraid, in the past that an increase
in agricultural benefit has not resulted
in an increase in agricultural employment
or in agricultural output. The whole
position in the country is so different to
that in the town that there is every
reason to be afraid of too great an
attempt to assimilate the financial aspects
of the various schemes.
As regards the Beveridge scheme,
which is now the Beveridge scheme subedited by the White Paper in London,
we must reserve judgment until something more concrete is before us. At
first sight the scheme, although it has
many attractions, seems to me to have
certain dangers, but we need not cross
that bridge until we come to it. For the
moment there is no reason why anybody
should offer any opposition to the Bill
before us this afternoon.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Thursday next.
EXPIRING LAWS CONTINUANCE
BILL.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to

Second time."
The Bill is in the usual annual form,
providing for the continuance in operation of certain Measures for another year
ending 31st December, 1945. The
Measures it proposes to continue are set
out in the Schedule to the Bill.
The first part of this Schedule contains
the one Imperial Measure to which the
Bill relates the Harbours, Docks and
Piers (Temporary Increase of Charges)
Act, 1920.
Of
The second part of the Schedule contains the enactments of this Parliament
which it is proposed to continue.
these, the Local Government (Emergency
Powers) Act, 1921, has been continued
from year to year since it was originally
passed. The other four Statutes are
Statutes of a temporary nature, passed
Adjournment-
Business for Next Sittings
24 OCTOBER 1944
The
to meet certain war conditions.
Government naturally hopes that by this
time next year it may be possible substantially to curtail this part of the
Schedule. For the moment it is considered wise to continue these Acts on the
Statute Book.

ORDER OF THE DAY.
BEE PEST PREVENTION BILL
[SENATE ].
Order for Second Reading read.
""
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move,
That this Bill be now read a
Second time."
The
This Bill has for its objects the eradication of bee diseases and the improvement of the bee-keeping industry. It
will be realised that, apart from the
value of the honey produced, the bee is a
necessary agent in the pollination of the
fruit blossom and is thus a most essential
element in the cultivation of fruit.
prevention of disease amongst bees is
recognised in all progressive communities
as a duty which it is incumbent upon the
State to undertake. The existing Act,
which was passed 36 years ago The Bee.
Pest Prevention (Ireland) Act, 1908-is
in very many respects out of date in the
light of modern knowledge and, if beekeeping is to be encouraged, not only as a
rising industry but as an adjunct to the
important fruit-growing industry in
Northern Ireland, new legislation on the
lines of the present measure is urgently
required. Foul brood in bees, which
until a few years ago was found only in
isolated districts in Northern Ireland, is
now spreading, while acarine disease exists
in
a large number of areas and has
already caused serious losses. The existing powers under the 1908 Act provide.
only a limited degree of control over foul
brood, and none whatever over acarine or
any other disease of bees.
The importance of controlling disease
in bees has been recognised for many
years in the United States of America
and in the Dominions of New Zealand and
Canada, where honey is produced and
marketed commercially in very large.
quantities, and where fruit-growing is
carried out on a very great scale. In these
countries, and especially in New Zealand,
very drastic powers of control are in force.
The powers sought in the present Bill do
not go beyond, and in many cases do not
reach, the level of the powers conferred by
the New Zealand legislature upon the
authorities in that country.
Under the Bill it is proposed that all
bee-keepers shall be registered with the
Ministry of Agriculture. It has been
found in other countries that this is an
essential part of any system of control.
The whole structure of control rests upon
the knowledge of the location of bees
wherever they may be. Half measures, it
will be obvious, would be worse than useless. Following upon this, the Ministry
of Agriculture requires to be in a position
to have bees and appliances either
destroyed or treated if destruction or
treatment is considered necessary in order
to prevent the spreading of any disease
that may be discovered. The Bill, therefore, gives that Ministry authority to
make such Regulations as are best
calculated to achieve the desired control.
It will be observed that provision is
made for compensation to be paid to beekeepers who comply with instructions to
destroy their diseased bees and appliances.
This is a well-recognised principle in the
case of the destruction of animals which
have to be destroyed in the interests of
the community. Bee-keepers will themselves provide, by means of small annual
contributions to the Compensation Fund
to be established, part of the money
needed, and the State will, in certain
circumstances, contribute its share up to
a maximum in any year of £500.
I should, perhaps, add that representative organisations of bee-keepers in
Northern Ireland have for some time been
pressing for legislation which will enable
the Ministry of Agriculture to exercise at
greater degree of control over bee diseases
than is possible at present. These
organisations are in agreement with the
principles embodied in the Bill, which is
Bee Pest Bill [S.]---
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
now submitted with confidence to the
judgment of the Senate.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."
I
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:
strongly support this Bill. It is absolutely essential that bees should be under
proper control, so far as we can control
bees, and it has been found very necessary
in England to put bees under some form
of supervision. In this country there has
been a very alarming increase in foul
brood particularly. There are one or two
points in the Bill to which I should like
to draw the attention of the Government.
Bee-keepers are very much concerned
about the fact that inspectors go into a
place where there may be foul brood, and
immediately afterwards they go and inspect a perfectly clean colony of bees.
Where Isle of Wight disease is concerned
I do not think there is much danger of
contamination, but I believe there is considerable danger of contamination in the
case of foul brood where an inspector
visits a colony which is infected by the
disease and immediately afterwards, without being properly disinfected, he goes to
inspect a colony which is free from the
disease.
Then I think it is generally understood
that extractors are one of the most dangerous implements there are in spreading.
disease, and it does not always follow that
the extractor is owned by the bee-keeper.
It is quite possible for a general merchant,
or one who buys honey to buy an extractor
and hire it to the bee-keeper. Under this
Bill extractors are not under supervision
by the Ministry unless they are owned by
the bee-keeper, and I think the Minister
should consider making it compulsory
that all implements that are connected
with bees should be properly inspected,
and that notification should be given to
the Ministry by any general dealer or
other person who owns an extractor for
hire,
One of the greatest problems that beekeepers now have is the number of wild
bees in the country, and that is one of the
problems this Bill cannot possibly deal
with. But it is very difficult for people
who have old premises, or old houses, or
woods to keep any track of the swarms of
wild bees, and it is even more difficult to
find out where they are even if you do
find wild bees in the neighbourhood. I
think that perhaps the Government should
occasionally send round inspectors who
are experts in these things, because the
ordinary man who may happen to know
there is a swarm in his neighbourhood is
not always expert in finding out where
that swarm is.
Mr. LESLIE: It is really rather a
pleasure at this time of day to find that
that industrious and un-self-seeking
insect, the bee, is attracting a certain
amount of public attention.
It is all very
well to say, even of this industrious insect,
that he improves the shining hour, but
the complaint to-day seems to be that
he does not improve the shining hour at
all. He employs himself to a great extent in contaminating other bees in his
hours of pastime, and prevents them
following their natural avocation in the
production of good honey. The bee has
always been regarded as an unselfish
member of society. Many will remember
that admirable little classical quatrain in
pentameters attributed to Virgil, a matter
of nearly 2,000 years ago. Everybody
remembers the celebrated sic vos non
vobis, and it is in that that the line I am
thinking of occurs. You will remember
that the first line of the English translation was:
Not for yourselves your nests ye song
birds build.
The third line is the one we are concerned with-
Not for yourselves your hives ye bees
have filled.
That seems to argue that even in those
Roman days the self-sacrificing nature of
the bee was accepted, and he was regarded
as a useful member of society. It is
undoubtedly a fact that in 1908 an Act
dealing with bee pests was passed. As
someone has reminded us, that was 36
years ago, and the disease of foul brood
against which that Act was directed has
increased, and a new disease, the Latin
name of which I did not catch, but which
is commonly called Isle of Wight disease,
has developed and is entirely without
control. The question we have to consider is whether this Bill will produce any
effective control. If you could produce
a Bill which would effectually tend to the
eradication of bee diseases we should all
be for it, but here we have a Bill said to
be directed to that end, but involving a
system of registration and inspection of
Bee Pest Bill [S.]—
Second Reading
31 OCTOBER 1944
all the people in the Province who keep
bees, and that is a point on which I
would like to join issue.
I do not think the people of Northern
Ireland want any more registration, or
any more regulation, or the appointment
of any more inspectors. I think they
are sick to death of the whole thing, and
unless this House can be convinced that
there is really a popular demand on the
part of the people who keep bees, I think
we ought not to give this Bill a Second
Reading. It is all very well to say that the
bee-keepers' organisations, what there are
of them, want the Bill, but there are a
great many people who keep bees who do
not belong to any bee-keeping organisation. If everybody who kept a bee belonged to some sort of trade union of beekeepers, I think they could manage for
themselves most of what this Bill seeks
to produce. They could agree amongst
themselves not to procure colonies of bees
except from, so to speak, a certified
colony.
But there are things that cannot be
done. One of them is that whatever
Regulations you have got in Northern Ireland for preventing the access of unhealthy bees to healthy colonies, you cannot register effectively for the bees that
come across the border. I am told that
the ambit of the bee does not exceed two
to three miles, but you cannot provide
for bees that come across the border, and
unless you can do that effectively you
cannot stop a possibly diseased bee coming from across the border and infecting
a healthy colony. I do not understand
that there is any corresponding legislation which is anticipated in the Free
State. I think we are standing alone in
this respect, nor have I been able to
ascertain anything about those Regulations in England to which the Leader of
the House referred. In fact, I have not
been able to find any recent Act of Parliament dealing with bee pests in England
except this Act of 1908, which is only
applicable to Ireland. But I do know
that people object to registration. They
do not like it, and they are not prepared,
I think, to have any more of it.
Then again, the hon. and gallant
Senator opposite has pointed out that this
inspection has its own dangers-the possibility of an inspector himself introducing
disease into a colony previously free, and
difficulties about the extractors, and so
on. There are those difficulties. The
question is, do people want to be
registered, and by people I mean the
people as a whole who keep bees; not
only the comparatively small number of
organised people who are said to have
applied for this legislation. The right
hon. Gentleman told us, in introducing
the Bill, that requests to that end had
been received, and a statement to the
same effect appeared in the Memorandum
which accompanied the Bill. I must
digress for a moment to refer to that
Memorandum. I think it is the first
time a statement of that sort has borne
the signature of a civil servant, and I
venture to suggest it is not a desirable
innovation. That is, of course, a detail.
The main fact remains-that you have
got disease undoubtedly spreading in
Northern Ireland among such bees as
are left, and you have against that the
known dislike of the people to more

come to a decision on what it seems the

of keeping bees, and it seems to me that

Mr. MCALLISTER: The difficulty I
am in at the moment is this. We as
individual Members have no indication
as to what is the feeling of the Beekeepers' Association, and whom they
represent. But even if all the individual
bee-keepers and their Associations were
Bee Pest Bill [S.]--
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
in favour of legislation to deal with these
diseases I would object to the methods
I
which are adopted in this Bill.
associate myself entirely with the views
expressed by Senator Leslie-that people
are really worn out by these Rules and
Regulations, which are really an encroachment on their liberties. Under this
Bill we have arrived at the stage when a
person cannot keep a bee without getting
the consent of a civil servant. That is
an interference with the most elementary
rights of individual people. I object also
to the method under which this legislation
is to be carried out. I do not exaggerate
when I say that this Bill, when it
becomes an Act, will be merely a skeleton.
If the Bill is passed in its present form,
the whole idea underlying it will be placed
in the hands of a single Ministry to carry
it out by means of Rules and Regulations.
As long as I am in this House I shall
always maintain that Acts of Parliament
of this kind should set out the particular
offences of which a man can be guilty, and
that the system of registration and the
whole machinery by which the Act is to
be administered, should be set out in the
Bill, and that we should not delegate to
unknown individuals in the Civil Service
the right to frame the practical part of
the Bill instead of doing it ourselves.
There is another principle involved in
this Bill. Look for a moment at the
powers that are being handed over to the
staff of a Ministry established in an office
in this building. An official has the right
to deprive a man of keeping bees. He
has the right to revoke his licence. He is
expressly empowered by this Bill to do
those things, and there is no right of
appeal from the decision of the individual
in the Ministry. That is a position I
could not for one moment support. The
bee-keeper is expressly prohibited from
appealing on the question of the value of
the bees which may have to be destroyed.
Surely it is an old principle of law that
when a person's property has been confiscated or destroyed he has the right to
go to an independent tribunal to have
measured and assessed the damage he has
suffered. I do strongly protest against
this method of legislation, and as long as
I am in this House I shall always oppose
as strenuously as I can the delegation to
civil servants of powers we should, as
Members of this House, exercise ourselves.
Major DOBBS: I should like to support this Bill. Ten or fifteen years ago
in the locality in which I live there was a
lover of bees. He was what might be
called an amateur inspector, and everybody who had bees only had to get in
touch with him and he would be only too
pleased to give them all the information
he could, and to help them in any way.
I remember well that several times he
complained bitterly to me because,
although he knew that there were
diseases among bees in the locality, yet
nothing could be done because there was
no power. I think this Bill is very
necessary if you are going to save bees.
Whether the bees are worth saving is, of
course, another question..
As far as
honey is concerned, it is very nice, but I
do not think it is an important commercial business. But you must consider the
fruit-growing districts of Northern Ireland, and the man in the fruit-growing
district looks upon the bee, prima facie,
as a means of fertilising his fruit.
honey that the bee gives him is a subsidiary consideration.

be of benefit to these people, because if

only to be expected in war time. During

reasonable measure for the protection of
a great many people who find it profitable
to keep bees as a pastime.

that vested in him by his appointment as
an authorised officer, the house or other
premises of any subject living in any part

most reprehensible Clause to be included
in any Act of Parliament. It has always
been a well-established principle of law
that a man's home is his castle, and that
it shall remain inviolable unless for very
good cause under the law it is necessary
to invade that home. But under this
Clause it is proposed to give to
authorised officer, at all reasonable times,
power to enter upon any land or premises
where bees, honey, or appliances are
kept, or on which he has reason
suspect that bees, honey, or appliances
may be found or have, been kept.
an
to
I can only think that when this House

Rev. Professor CORKEY: I think
there is a good deal to be said for the
principle of registration. I do not see
how it is possible to control a pest such
as bee pest unless the authorities know
where the bees are, so that they can stamp
out the pest by methods which have been
proved to be effective. Therefore, as far

THE SENATE

milk were required to register, and power

who had neglected to conform to the

It will be within the recollection of all

the Bill would be improved if persons who
were aggrieved by the action of servants

to the courts. After all, the courts exist
to interpret and enforce the law, and that
is a great principle in British freedom.
Our courts are the ultimate guarantee of
our rights and privileges, and, therefore,
I think that paragraph (3) of Clause 2
might be extended in such a way as to
give this right of appeal from the decision.
of an official of the Ministry to the courts.
I think it is highly improbable that acivil
servant would ever do anything which
was obviously unfair or unjust, but, at
the same time, his opinion in regard to
the facts of the case might differ from the
opinion of the bee-keeper. Therefore, I
think, there ought to be some ultimate
court of appeal, and a court of appeal in
all other matters is a court of law. With
this comment I shall be prepared to support the Second Reading of this Bill.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: If I understand the position correctly, there will be

large number of hives is much greater

from my stormy past that I have considerable sympathy with anyone who objects
to registration, but I do not see how this

I think it is very necessary to

where stocks are that have been disposed
of to other people.
Then there is the other very important
point of the power to enter on premises.
As the House knows, that is a fairly
common feature of Acts of Parliament
nowadays, but in this particular case it
has a long ancestry, because that particular power is in the Act of 1908, and has
therefore been in existence for nearly 40
years. I thoroughly appreciate that the
House should be very vigilant about giving any additional powers to officers to
enter premises, but in this particular case
it is a very old power, and, as far as I
am aware, it has never been abused or
complained of.
I fully appreciate the weight of the
point Rev. Professor Corkey has made in
regard to the right of appeal to a court
of law. I seem to recollect having made
that point myself on previous occasions,
but I think that in this case it is a point
for the Committee stage. I cannot say
how the Minister of Agriculture will feel
about it, but if the right hon. Gentleman
would care to put down an Amendment
it will be considered.

mentioned here, the Register of Electors

under consideration in another place, and

That is why I have departed from the
Motion on the Order Paper.

FINANCE BILL.
Resolved, "That this Bill be now read
a First time."-(Sir Joseph Davidson.)
I beg to
Sir ROLAND NUGENT:
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time." The details of this Bill
are mainly of a technical nature, and
their chief object is to bring our legislation into line with that obtaining in
Great Britain. Each Clause in the Bill,
with one exception, embodies a concession. The exception relates to death
duties on enemy owned property, and
just because it is not a concession it will
probably commend itself to the House.
I will gladly give further explanations on
any point if hon. Members so desire, but
I feel sure that the House would not wish
me to take up their time by going through
the provisions of the Bill, item by item,
as it would take up a long time, and the
Clauses are, I think, self-explanatory.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Moved, "That this Bill be now committed." (Sir Roland Nugent.)

CLAUSE 1 (Prevention of Bee Pests
and Diseases).
(1) For the purpose of preventing
the spreading of pests or
diseases
among bees, the Ministry of Agriculture (in this Act referred to as "the
Ministry'') may by regulations provide
Bee Pest Bill [S.]—
Committee
THE SENATE
(a) for requiring notification
from
persons who keep or have charge
of bees, or who have involuntarily
acquired any bees, as to the
occurrence or suspected occurrence
of any such pest or disease affecting
bees as may be specified in the
regulations and for obtaining
information regarding any such
pest or disease;
(b) for requiring notification from any
person who keeps or intends to
keep or has charge of bees as to
the acquisition or disposal by him.
of any bees, and for requiring notification from any person as to the
death of any bees kept by him or
under his control, or as to the
existence of any bees which, to his
knowledge, have swarmed
or
settled on any lands or premises
occupied by him;
(c) for the inspection of bees, honey
and appliances used in connection
with bee-keeping;
(d) for prohibiting or restricting the
movement from, in or to, any area
of any bees, honey or appliances
and for that purpose to declare
any area to be an infected area;
(e) for prohibiting or restricting the
acquisition or disposal (whether by
way of purchase or sale or otherwise) of any bees, honey or appliances which are infected, or are
believed to have been exposed to
infection, with any pest or
disease specified in the regulations;
(f) for determining whether any bees,
honey or appliances are, or are
likely to become, infected, or have
been exposed to infection, with any
pest or disease specified in
in the
regulations, and for the destruction or treatment of any bees,
honey or appliances which are so
infected or are believed to have
been exposed to such infection;
(g) for prohibiting the exposure, in
such a way that bees can gain
access thereto, of honey or appliances which are infected or have
been exposed to infection with any
pest or disease;
(h) for prohibiting the keeping of bees
for such period as the Ministry
thinks fit upon any lands
or
premises on which any bees, honey
or appliances have been destroyed
in the exercise of any power conferred by or under this Act;
(i) for prescribing and securing the use
of a suitable type or types of hives
or other appliances;

with any bees, hives, combs or
honey, which he has acquired involuntarily or which may be found
on any land or premises in his

be prescribed;
(k) for such other matters as may be
necessary or expedient for all or
any of the purposes aforesaid; and
(1) generally for the purpose of carrying the provisions- of this Act into
effect.
an
(2) The Ministry or any officer
authorised by it (in this Act referred
to as "authorised officer') may
serve a notice in writing upon any
person keeping or having charge of, or
who has involuntarily acquired, any
bees or appliances which are infected,
or are in the opinion of the Ministry
or an authorised officer, likely to be
infected, with any pest or disease, requiring him to treat such bees or
appliances in the manner specified in
the notice or to destroy them within
the period specified in the notice, and
any person upon whom such notice is
served shall forthwith carry out such
treatment or destruction.
(3) If any person contravenes or fails
to comply with the provisions of this
section or of any regulation made or
notice or direction given thereunder,
he shall be liable on conviction to a fine
not exceeding twenty pounds.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in subclause (1), paragraph (a), after the word
"bees" ("disease affecting bees") to insert
the words "under their control." I
think the Amendment on the Paper is
almost self-explanatory. It seems to
me fairly obvious that you cannot expect
anybody, whether he is a keeper of bees
or a person in charge of bees, to do anything about bees which are not under
his control, and I think we ought to put
in, following the precedent of the Imperial
Act of Parliament of 1908, those words,
"under their control," in the position
indicated.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move on page
1, line 14, after the word "and" ("in

aware." This again is following the old
Act of 1908. As the words stand they
are a little too wide. They really require
these people who keep or have charge of
bees to report any disease which has happened to any bee whatever. There is no
limitation at all. That is not what we
intend. What we intend is that what a
man is required to notify must relate to
something of which he is aware, and it
must be something which relates more or
less to himself. You do not want him

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move to leave

is really a much more serious matter. We
are always ready to give credit for all
reasonable intentions, but I am afraid
that I must suggest that this is giving
the Ministry rather too much control.
There is too much power of interference
with a man as regards the use of his hives.
It is all very well for the Ministry to
prescribe and carry into effect, if they
can, provisions as regards the disinfection.
of hives, but it is a very serious interference with a man's power of discretion to
say what type of hive he has to use and
so on. There are a good many types of
hives, and perhaps the best known of them
is the C.D.B. hive. C.D.B. does not
mean contagious diseases amongst bees,
but Congested District Board. The fact
remains that there are numerous kinds of
hives, and if the Department should
allocate to itself the right to prescribe the
type of this or that hive that a man had
to use, it is not only undue interference
with his discretion, but it will inevitably
eliminate entirely the effects of individual
research. A man may invent for himself a new kind of hive, and if he is a
really expert bee-keeper he will have
studied the question, and I imagine that

7 NOVEMBER 1944
a man will not go in for bee-keeping
seriously unless he has made a study of
bees, and I am told they find it very

will be an unanswerable interference.
That particular paragraph should not be

rejection.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
we attach considerable importance to resisting this Amendment. The trouble is
this. I understand that it is practicably
impossible to examine bees unless they
are in some type of bar frame hive, and
the Ministry must have power gradually
to enforce some form of bar frame or
similar type of hive in which the bees can
be examined, or the whole process of trying to stamp out disease in bees would
come to an end. The Ministry does not
propose under present circumstances to
make Regulations which prescribe what
hives must be used, but it proposes to
start a process of education of the beekeeper in this matter, with the object of
insisting upon their use in due time, when
it is reasonably possible to procure bar
frame hives.
It might be of interest to the Committee to know that in New Zealand, the
most modern State in regard to diseases
and health of bees, they are prohibited
from keeping bees except in bar framed
hives, and if an inspector finds another
type of hive being used he may destroy
the hive altogether, with the honeycombs
therein, because it is not a regulation
hive. They seem to be rather an
arbitrary people in New Zealand, but
they are the last word in bee-keeping. In
the case of experimental hives, it will be
possible to obtain a licence from the
Ministry for a genuine experiment, but I
am afraid that we must stick to our guns
on this Amendment.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move at the
end of sub-clause (1) to insert as a new
sub-clause :
(2) Notifications under sub-section (1)
of this section shall be made in
writing to the Ministry or to any
authorised officer as hereinafter in
this Act defined.
This is rather a new thing. We have
said a lot about notification and that sort
of thing, but we do not give any notification as to who is to be notified, and I
think it is about time we began to talk
about that. The Committee will notice
that the last words of my Amendment
read, "to any authorised officer as hereinafter in this Act defined." I do not
think that those last words will ultimately pass into an Act of Parliament. The
point is this. This is the first intimation
we have in the Bill of the appointment of
an authorised officer, and that authorised
officer has got to be defined somehow.
Steps are taken to that end in later paragraphs of the Bill with which I need not
deal at the moment, but on this first
appearance of this officer it seems necessary to say something about the man.
The point that everybody is interested in
is not so much about the man being
authorised as about him being qualified.
That is really the point.
The greatest importance is attached to
the fact that these inspections shall only
be carried out by properly qualified
people. I think that by putting in the
Amendment in this way we are given an
opportunity of discussing the qualifications of the officer, and the Committee
will remember that in the 1908 Act it
was the local authority who were the
controlling people, and the local authority
were only to appoint such officers as had
such qualifications as were approved by the
Ministry. That came in under the 1908 Act,
following on the Technical Instruction Act,
1899, and the earlier Local Government
Act of 1898. Therefore I think we should
take this opportunity of saying who
the notification has to be made to. As
far as the officer is concerned, I do not
think I need move the words from "officer'
to the end of the Amendment, so that the
Amendment will stop at the word
"officer." It will not, therefore include,
"as hereinafter in this Act defined."
They are not strictly necessary, but I put
them in to direct the attention of the
Committee to the necessity of doing something, preferably at a later stage, and
especially insisting on proper qualifications for this officer. In the meantime I
beg to move the Amendment as I have
suggested it.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in

fine never could be £10. A maximum
penalty of £20 is high for some of these
offences. It is out of proportion. I
have not suggested a reduction of the
fine in any other case, because there
might be circumstances when a more
severe view should be taken of the offence,
but to put a fine of £20 on here for noncompliance with the provisions of some of
these Regulations seems to me to be entirely out of keeping with modern
notions of the liberty of the subject, and
to some extent also with the desirability
of ensuring that fines shall not be excessive. I do not suggest that any
ordinary bench of magistrates would
inflict a fine of £20, but nowadays you
have not got a bench of magistrates, but
man sitting alone. Accordingly I
beg to move the Amendment, which

has over and over again deliberately,
knowingly, and of malice aforethought,

that a fine of £5 would be no deterrent
to him. Why, just because he happens to
be a rich man, should he be fined a sum
which is no deterrent, whereas in the case
of a poor man a fine of £5 would be a
serious consideration. I think the Clause
should stand as it is.

CLAUSE 2 (Compulsory registration
of bee keepers).
(1) For the purposes of this Act the
Ministry shall keep a register of bee
keepers and, subject as hereafter in this
section provided, shall enter therein the
name of any person who keeps or has
charge of, or proposes to keep or have
charge of, bees, and applies to the
Ministry to be registered as a bee
keeper under this Act and who
furnishes the Ministry with such particulars and pays such fees as may be
prescribed under this section:
Provided that the Ministry may refuse to register any applicant-
(a) if the bees to which his application relates or any of them are,
in the opinion of the Ministry or
an authorised officer, infected with
any
infectious
contagious or
disease or with any other disease,
pest or defect which in the opinion
of the Ministry or an authorised
officer makes it inadvisable that
such bees should be kept in the
neighbourhood;
(b) if any appliances used by him in
connection with bee-keeping are,
in the opinion of the Ministry or
an authorised officer, infected with
any infectious disease; or
(c) if he has failed or refused to comply with any of the provisions of
this Act or of any regulation made
or notice or direction given thereunder by the Ministry or an
authorised officer.
(2) Any person who after the commencement of this Act keeps or has
charge of any bees shall, unless he is
registered in respect of such bees as a
bee keeper under the preceding subsection, be liable on conviction to a fine
not exceeding five pounds; and any
person who has been convicted of keeping or having charge of any bees contrary to the provisions of this Act,
shall, if after the expiration of seven
days following such conviction he keeps
or has charge of such bees contrary to
the provisions of this Act, be liable on
conviction to a fine of not exceeding
ten pounds; and if after such second
conviction he continues to keep or have
charge of such bees contrary to the provisions of this Act, he shall be guilty
of a continuing offence and be liable on
conviction to a further penalty not exceeding forty shillings for each week or
part of a week during which he is guilty
of the continuing offence.
(3) The Ministry shall have power to
revoke or suspend the registration of
any person as a bee keeper under this
Act, but such registration shall, unless
so suspended or revoked, remain in
force until the thirty-first day of
March occurring next after the day on
which the registration is granted, and
shall be renewable annually, and the
same provisions shall apply to the renewal of any registration under this.
Act as apply to the grant thereof.
(4) The fee payable in respect of the
registration for any registration year
of any person as a bee keeper under this
Act shall be such sum as the Ministry,
after consultation with such bee keepers'
organisations as it thinks fit, may prescribe having regard to the amount required to meet all claims which may
reasonably be expected to be made.
against the Compensation Fund established under the next succeeding section
of this Act.
(5) The Ministry shall, if so required
by any person registered as a bee keeper
under this section, grant to him, on
payment of such fee as the Ministry
may prescribe therefor, a certificate of
his registration as such bee keeper, and
any certificate so granted shall, unless
the contrary is shown, be deemed to be
sufficient evidence of the facts stated
therein.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause

If the Committee will refer
back to sub-clause (1) of the first Clause
they will see we talk about people keeping
or having charge of bees, and those expressions occurred in a certain Act of
Parliament which I shall not again instance. In Clause 1 of the Bill before us
the intention is to accept the possibility
that the man who keeps bees and the man
who has charge of them may be two
separate people. It is not an alternative
title for the same man, so that the expressions "keeping," and "in charge" are
meant to refer to two people. That being
so, we have to look at what is to be done
now.
This Clause deals with registration.
Under registration the Ministry has to
enter the name of any person who keeps
or has charge of bees. It is easy to register
the man who keeps them, but there will be
difficulty in registering the man who has
charge of them, or proposed to keep or
have charge of them. In the ordinary
case a man keeps bees in his garden, and
his gardener is the person who has charge
of the bees in the garden. The owner of
the bees may be abroad, or he may have
left the House on a Sunday to go to the
cinema. He could not do that in Belfast,
but he might go to church, or he might
have gone away.
How long has a man
got to be in charge of these bees? I
understand that the necessity for being in
effective charge of bees varies a good deal
according to the time of year and what is
happening in the hive at the time. I
do not see how we can consistently require
the registration of the person in charge
of the bees.
In the case of a man who has a gardener
in charge of his bees and who decides to
change his gardener, how are you going
to deal with that man? How can such
a
man furnish the Ministry with such
particulars as are prescribed under this
Clause? It seems to me that this expression is taking charge of us and the Bill.
Under this Clause I do not think it is a
workable possibility. You can get a beekeeper to be registered, but a person who
is in charge of bees may be a changing
person, and I do not see how he can be
registered on this bee-keepers' register.

in respect of the bees. In the case of
non-resident owners we must register the
man in charge of the bees. It is a question of administrative convenience, and it
would be extremely difficult to administer
the Act if you did not make the person
actually in charge of the bees responsible
for complying with the Regulations.

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: It
is very difficult to say who has charge

You may.
have a gardener who comes three days in

other days. I think the

the use of the word "keeping." It is not

the bees, because "keeping" does not
refer only to the man who owns the
bees but to the man who looks after them.
"Charge" is a very indefinite word,
particularly in regard to bees. You do not
licence a man to have sole charge of your
bees. You probably look after them
yourself part of the time, and it is going
to be frightfully difficult to say who is in

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The Act
would be completely and absolutely unworkable unless we could fix the responsibility on one person, and where the owner
is not there the person in charge must be
responsible. I would inform the Committee that while the words, "has charge
of" did not appear in the English, 1941
Act, they found it necessary to repair that
omission in the Regulations they issued
in 1942, because they found the Act was
unworkable without the words. The expression "having charge of" is one frequently used in these cases. Section 4 of
the Diseases of Animals Act, 1904,
mentions "every person having in his
possession or under his charge." Again,
Section 13 of that Act uses the expression,
"a person owning or having charge of."
I should apologise to the Committee
and to the right hon. and learned Gentleman for having rather loosely used the
expression "administrative convenience."
It is really a question of making the Act
workable. Normally, a person having
charge, and the keeper of bees, are the
same person. The object of this expression is to cover a case where the actual
owner of the bees is not resident in the
North of Ireland, and the bees are in
charge of someone else on the spot. In
that case it is not the owner we want to
get at, but the man who is looking after
the bees. If we did not have this provision it would make the Act practically
unworkable.
If we were unable to deal
with disease in a big stock of bees that
was in charge of someone other than the
owner, it might be a very serious matter,
and other hives might become infected.
Mr. LESLIE: The whole difficulty
comes from the Government insisting on
registration. If it was not for registration I should be entirely with the right
hon. Gentleman. I do not think it is
possible to give way on this Amendment.

Committee
interval between the passing of the Act

and any Regulations made under it.
Perhaps I should explain here why we
could not accept a later amendment for
the alteration of the date of commencement. We cannot accept 31st January
for the date of commencement, partly
because of the habits of the bee. In the
winter it is impossible to examine him.
Secondly, anyone wishing to register
would not know the number of bees they
would be registering until March. They
could not tell in January if stocks had
died.
Another reason is that it is very much
easier and more simple, where an Exchequer charge is going to arise, to make
the dates coincide with the end of the.
financial year.
As we should have to
stick to the 31st March we feel we have
plenty of time between now and that date
to explain the whole position to the beekeeper, and it is the intention of the
Ministry to do that. It will be appreciated that this Bill is only part of an
intensive campaign of education that will
be carried on, and we could not extend
the commencement of the Act to 31st
March and then give another three
months. In fact bee-keepers will get
three months, but it will be after the
passing of the Act.
