The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I have to report to the
House that His Grace the Governor of
Northern Ireland, in the name and on
behalf of His Majesty the King, has been
pleased to give his Assent to the following Bill agreed upon by both Houses:
Finance.
ORDERS OF THE DAY.
:-
PARLIAMENT (ELECTIONS AND
MEETING) BILL.
Order for Second Reading read.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The first and
most important part of this Bill deals
with the method to be adopted in the
immediate future for the compilation of
an electors' register for use at elections
to the Parliament of Northern Ireland in
all constituencies other than the community constituency. It is described as
making temporary provision therefor,
not primarily because the new system it
proposes to inaugurate is of a transitory
nature, but because the system is based
upon the National Registration System
which is itself a war-time measure
subject to abandonment at the end of the
war.
Prior to 1939 a combined register,
which in its various sections covered both
Westminster and Stormont franchises
and also the Local Government franchise,
was prepared and published annually.
This register was founded on a system of
house-to-house canvass, or other sufficient
local inquiry, and included the names of
the persons entitled to vote by virtue of
residence, or occupation of premises, in a
constituency on the last day of a qualifying period earlier in
the year of
compilation, continuous residence or
occupation being required throughout the
six months qualifying period.
By the Representation of the People
Act (N.I.), 1928, the register for use at
Stormont elections was declared to be the
register, prepared as I have outlined, in
force for the time being for Westminster
elections, subject to certain variations of
qualification in Northern Ireland, and at
the moment the register in force here is
that prepared in 1939, no registration
having been carried out since that date.
Having regard to the circumstances
ruling as the result of the war; for
example, the movements of population
and the shortage of labour, paper and
printing facilities, the Imperial Government decided that a reversion to the
suspended registration system was impracticable. They accordingly devised,
and the Westminster Parliament enacted
by an Act in 1943, and a short amending
Act in 1944, a new system of registration
which would come into force in supercession of the old 1939 register on an
appointed day.
That appointed day has been fixed as
first of December, and after that date
there will be no register in force which
could be used for a Stormont election
unless or until the Parliament of
Northern Ireland makes appropriate
Statutory provision.
Parliament Bill-
Second Reading
21 NOVEMBER 1944
It may be asked why the Westminster
register under the new system cannot be
used, as was that under the old system,
for Stormont elections. The answer is
that there will, at least for the present,
be no published register. The new
system provides for continuous registration in an index held by the registration
officers, and maintained from day to day
on the basis of information supplied to
the Electoral Registration Officers by the
National Register Office. A register will
be published only when required for an
election, and will be in force only for
that election. It could not, therefore,
be used for a Stormont election.
Now it may be arguable whether or
not the new system is ideal for Northern Ireland elections, but it is beyond
argument that if a register is to be prepared against a Stormont general or byelection in the relatively near future the
most convenient, if not the only
practicable, course to adopt is to utilise
the index which forms the basis of the
prospective Westminster registers as the
corresponding basis for Stormont
register.
a
The present Bill is designed to permit
this
of action.
course
It takes advantage of an enabling provision to that
effect in the United Kingdom Act of
1943. It provides that, subject to
the special Northern Ireland requirements in regard to electors' qualifications, those who are recorded as
potential electors for Westminster
elections shall be those entitled
to be registered for Stormont elections.
It provides for using the same machinery
for dealing with Service voters as is provided by the United Kingdom Acts for
Westminster elections. It applies, in
effect, all the provisions of the United
Kingdom Acts which will be of service in
the compilation of a register for Stormont
elections, and it makes provision for the
compilation and publication of a register.
here, as or when required, on the basis of
all this material.
It is obvious, in the. circumstances I
have outlined, that even did we consider
it desirable, which I suggest is, under
present conditions, extremely open to
doubt, to diverge. to any great extent
from the United Kingdom practice, we
could not do so without losing the very
important advantage of being able to use
all the vast amount of material ready to
hand. We should be driven back to
making our own arrangements from the
foundation up, and there would be no
possibility of doing this within the time
prospectively available before an election
confronts us. Moreover, the machinery
contemplated by the Bill is intricate and
is linked at every turn with the existing
machinery under the United Kingdom
Acts. Therefore, while it
while it would be
improper for me to attempt to restrict
in any way the liberty of Members of
this House, I feel justified in asking them
to be chary of suggesting amendments,
unless they have. probed to the utmost the
possible repercussions of such amendments
in their interaction on other parts of the
machine.
I do not suggest that the essential connection between the proposed system here
and the system established for Westminster election is so close or rigid that
no divergence from the United Kingdom
system is possible. That this is not so is
indicated by the fact that, as Members of
this House are doubtless aware, amendments on two important matters were
made during the passage of the Bill
through another
These
place.
two
matters were the length of time allowed
for the publication of the Register, and
the method by which occupiers of business
premises are to be incorporated in the
Register. The scope for divergence is,
however, as I have said, very limited if
we, even if only as a matter of expediency,
desire to take advantage of the machine
made available to us.
Apart from any question of expediency,
I have no hesitation in recommending to
the House, as an inherently good system,
the system proposed by the Bill. It has
number of advantages over the old
annual system of registration. Of these,
the three most important are, perhaps,
its flexibility, the use it makes of a very
complete system of National Registration,
and the freshness of the registers it will
provide. I do not think I need detain
the House by making any detailed
analysis of the Bill. I shall, therefore,
confine myself to mention one or two
salient points.
The first of these is the new residence
qualification. Under the old system a
person in effect became qualified by residing continuously in a constituency, or in
Parliament Bill--
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Sir Roland Nugent]
a circumscribed area around the constituency, for a period of six months
ending on a certain date in the year of
publication of the Register. Thus, for
an election in the summer of 1939, the
qualified electors would have been those
people who resided in the constituency
during the first half of 1938. At the
best, the Register was almost six months
out of date on publication, and at the
worst, that is at an election held shortly
before the publication of a new Register,
the election was fought on a Register
representing the position in a constituency
almost eighteen months before. The new
system, for the present, dispenses with a
residence period qualification and relies
upon residence, at a fixed date. The residence must be in the constituency in
which the vote is to be cast, and the
qualifying date is, at the longest, just
under two months before the need for the
election arises.
The Register on which an election will
be fought will therefore represent as
nearly as is physically possible, the position in the constituency at the actual
time of the election. It is obvious that
this is a great advance, but it may not
be immediately apparent just how great
an advance it actually is.
In
One of the surprising things which
have already come to light in the
working of the Index for the Westminster
register is the extent of the continual
movement of population that is going on.
The crude figure of movements amounts
to several thousands per week.
attempting to assess the net effect of these
movements in the electorate of a Northern
Ireland constituency many adjustments
have to be made and at the present stage
only an estimate is possible. The
position, roughly, is that the error due
to movements between the qualifying
date and the initiation of an election,
assuming in both cases the longest interval permitted by the respective systems
of registration, would in an average
Stormont constituency-about 17,000
electors be something like 200 under
the new system and 2,000 under the old
system.
In other words, considering the movement factor only, the new system permits
an accuracy of not less than 981
per cent., the corresponding figure for
the old system being about 88 per cent.
When other factors such as death, attainment of the age of 21 years, and so on
are taken into account, the disparity is
still further, and substantially, increased.
I should mention that these figures are not
based entirely upon the present abnormal
war-time movements. An allowance has
been made for a prospective stabilisation.
of population which may or may not
materialise.
The Westminster legislation provides
for a modification of the qualifications in
about a year's time, when there will be
reversion to a "period"
a "period" qualification.
This period will, however, be only two
instead of six months, and the qualifying date at the same time will be moved
forward by a month, so that the freshness
of the register will be, if anything
improved.
The second salient point in the first
Part of the Bill, and the last to which I
shall refer, is the alteration in the
election timetable. Formerly, with a
register always available, an election
followed the dissolution of Parliament
at once. It was, indeed, possible for a
new Parliament to meet within three
weeks of the dissolution of its predecessor. Under the new system a register
has to be specially prepared for each
election, and time has to be found for its
preparation and publication. Under the
United Kingdom Acts this time has been
restricted to 36 days, which is undoubtedly the minimum period within
which all the work of printing and publishing election lists, receiving and dealing with claims and objections and finally
publishing the register, could possibly be
completed.
For Stormont elections we have had
to allow an extra 7 days, making the total
time 43 days, because experience has
shown that claims and objections, particularly the latter, tend to be much more
numerous here than in Great Britain.
It has therefore been necessary to provide
in the legislation, both here and in the
United Kingdom, for a postponement of the
commencement of the election timetable
proper until the date on which the register is published. The date of the Royal
Proclamation, or, in the case of a byelection, the writ, will therefore be the
date of the "initiation" of the election,
but Parliament will not necessarily be
dissolved on the date of the Proclamation,
and the new Parliament will not meet
Parliament Bill-
Second Reading
21 NOVEMBER 1944
or the new Member take his seat until
between 8 and 9 weeks after the initiation date, instead of about 3 weeks later
as under the old system.
This is undoubtedly a disadvantage
inherent in the new system, but on
balance, and for practical purposes, I am
sure the House will agree that the advantages the new system provides far outweigh this solitary disadvantage.
The second Part of the Bill relates to
questions concerning the direction and
conveyance of writs for the election of
Members of the Northern Ireland House
of Commons. The present law on the
subject is obscure, and its observance
depends largely on practice. The opportunity afforded by this Bill has been taken
to clarify and simplify the law governing
this matter.
I beg to move, "That this Bill be now
read a Second time."
Question proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: It seems to me that in
the Bill before us there is really no constitutional precedent involved. There is
nothing, I think, so far as this House is
concerned, which deprives one of the
opportunity of saying that this is a
Measure which is really more of domestic
concern to those who sit in another place
than it is to us.
That is not to say that
it will not have certain repercussions on
the feeling of the people in the Province,
but I do not think that there is anything
in this Bill to which any reasoned objection can possibly be taken. It seems to
me that these domestic arrangements
which the Bill contemplates are both
practicable and economical, as the right
hon. Gentleman indicated. I think they
will be found convenient in practice, and
they certainly will not be unduly expensive. The proposals throughout the
Bill will make for what I should describe
as very desirable promptitude. They will
give an opportunity which has been lacking in the past for feeling the pulse of
the electorate up-to-date. That is, you
will get the feeling of the electors on the
appropriate date.
Whether that will be a good thing or a
bad thing it will be impossible to prophesy,
but for our purposes, strictly on constitutional lines, it is highly desirable that
the feeling of the day should be the feeling on which the electors proceed to vote.
If it might happen that in any given constituency the whole electorate was swayed
by some popular cry not supported by
pure reason, that we cannot help, but it
is the business of both Houses of Parliament to see that you get the up-to-date
feeling of the constitueney, and I do think
that this Bill provides a better means of
doing that than we had in the past, when
we had a very long period of registration
to make qualification. I think it has
been argued that the Register might be
17 months old before the election took
place.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: Eighteen
months.
Mr. LESLIE: That is even worse.
Eighteen months in these rapidly moving
times is out of the sphere of practical consideration if you are going to get a satisfactory indication of the feelings of the
electorate. I think I have already said
that the Bill will make for economy, and
I cannot see any serious difficulty that will
be put in the way. But I do not think we
can very well part from the Bill without
expressing our obligation for the very
admirable preparation which was gone
through before the Bill was actually presented. It is a complicated Bill in many
places, dealing
dealing with very technical
matters, and it requires a certain amount
of reference if you want to check every
word and dot every i and cross every t.
But, taking the Bill as a whole, it must
be recognised that it is presented in a
most admirable and concise form, and
made as easy to understand as possible.
We really are indebted to the Drafting
Department for the drafting of the
Measure.
Part II of the Bill, for the first time,
gives those immediately concerned with
the Register something of a Statutory code
to work on. I think everybody got along
very satisfactorily without a code, but
there must have been a feeling that some
of the preceding stages rather lacked
Statutory authority, and that will now be
obviated. I have pleasure in supporting
the Bill, and I do not think that in Committee there will be any disposition to
suggest anything in the nature of amendments. I very much doubt whether the
Bill could be amended.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.

the Parliament Bill becomes law these
gentlemen will, to some extent, resume

Bill provides that where they have earned

makes due arrangement for the remuneration of these officers. This is, mainly, a

twice, and, on the other hand, that they

ADJOURNMENT MOTION.
ACCIDENTS ON FROSTY ROADS.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I beg to move, “That
this House do now adjourn until Tuesday
next."
Question proposed.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I wish to draw the
attention of the Government to the speed
at which motorists drive at the present
time over roads in Northern Ireland.
During the winter months the roads with
a concrete surface are liable to become.
very slippery after a very short period of
frost. In fact, one or two hours of frost
will make them very slippery. On this
day week when coming to the meeting of
the Senate, four accidents occurred within
a distance of four miles on the road along
which I was travelling. I was held up
for a long period, and I had to assist in
getting some of the injured to hospital.
One of the accidents was to a bread lorry
that had overturned, and its wheels were
up in the air. Just in front of me
hearse with a coffin in it was stopped. A
heavy military lorry came along the road
at a very fast speed, and before it reached
the hearse it skidded two or three times
and banged into the hearse a few yards in
front of me. There were two other
accidents within a distance of two miles.
a
These accidents are occurring very frequently. They are generally due to the
fact that the vehicles are driven at too
great a speed. I am sure that other
Members of this House who travel by car
have witnessed similar accidents, and in
most cases they could be avoided by more
careful driving. During the winter
months the roads are very treacherous,
and I think some advice should be given
to motorists to be more careful and drive
an
more slowly. I can assure you that I
myself have to be very careful when driving on these treacherous roads, and I
always do take very great care. Other
vehicles glide past me at a very fast rate,
and often they come to grief later on. I
would like some warning to be given to
motorists through the Press, and
appeal made to them to take this matter
up with a view to trying to keep down
accidents during the winter months when
the roads are in a very treacherous condition. I think something should be
done in this direction immediately,
because these accidents are occurring very
frequently.
Major DOBBS: I think some of these
accidents are due to frost on the roads in
early morning, and on top of the frost are
a few drops of rain or moisture. This
makes the roads very treacherous.
Motorists are under the impression that
the road is merely wet, and they do not
realise that in addition to the road being
wet there is ice underneath, and this results in skidding. Motorists do not
realise what is happening. Only this
morning I myself noticed a three-ton lorry
travelling at a speed in excess of its
maximum of 30 miles an hour. It was
unloaded, and it is not uncommon to see
these three-ton lorries travelling at an excessive speed when unloaded, particularly
when trying to get back home in the evening. I think some of the accidents are
due to this cause.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I would like
to support the remarks that have been
made on this subject. Great care is
necessary when motoring in the winter,
but I do not think there is anything to
be done except to call the attention of the
Minister of Home Affairs to the matter.
I think that what is really needed is an
appeal to the public, but I will see that
my right hon. Friend is informed that
the matter has been raised in this House.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at
a Quarter before Three
o'clock until Tuesday
next.

QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
The Postal Censorship.
Major DOBBS: I beg to ask the
Leader of the Housewhether he will have representations
made to the appropriate authorities in
order that the stringency of the censorship between Great Britain and Northern Ireland may now be reduced.
a
I am sure all hon. Members will welcome the opportunity, not so much of
discussing this question as of hearing what
I am sure will be a lucid and full explanation from the Leader of the House
for the reasons for this stringent censorship at the present time. As far as I
am personally concerned, I have
considerable number of letters from across
the water, but not in one single instance
has any part of any of those letters been
either cut out or erased. At the same
time I estimate that something like 80
per cent. of the letters I receive from
across the water are opened and censored.
An envelope with the words "M. T.
Blank & Co., Solicitors, St. James's,
London," embossed in dark blue letters
on the back, is always opened. Again,
a periodic letter embossed "Barclay's
Bank, Ltd., Cambridge," is invariably
opened. If those letters came from
Portsmouth, or Chatham, or Hull, one
could realise that they would definitely
be opened, but why should all letters
which come from bankers and solicitors
always be opened.
Like most of us, I have quite a large
trustee and executor correspondence,
and within the last few weeks I have
had to resign one trusteeship, and in another case I have had to sign a blank
cheque, simply on account of the terrible
delays caused by the censorship. What
are the authorities afraid of? Are they
afraid that our friends in England will
write over here and say that the Black
Prince at Staines has been bombed by a
buzz bomb? Really I do not think that
if the Germans heard that the Black
Prince at Staines had been destroyed by a
bomb it is going to help the accuracy of
their shooting. I grant that the censorship work up to the present time has been
very important and absolutely necessary,
but I am a bit sceptical of its necessity
now, especially when we know that men
engaged on work of national importance
Both here and in England get periodic
leave to go back to Dublin and to the
South. You cannot censor their brains,
and you cannot censor their memories.
Then there is the question of these
small pictures and photographs. Is it
absolutely necessary now to stop them?
I came across a rather interesting case.
A printed picture, postcard size, of a
prize dog was sent in a letter to my wife
about November last year, and it came
through. A few weeks ago a similar picture was sent to my wife, and it was sent
back to the person who had forwarded it.
He is a famous breeder of dogs, quite a
respectable person, a a Minister in the
Parliament at Westminster, and a distant connection of my own. The picture
was sent back to him, and he wrote to
my wife apologising for the delay in
answering her letter. When his letter.
arrived, it was cut by having the address
of his kennels cut out of it.
I think there ought to be some discrimination in this censorship work.
I
would like to make my own view perfectly
clear, and I am sure, everyone will agree
with me. If this censorship is really
necessary, I certainly do not want it
altered. If it is unnecessary, then it
should be realised that it has become a
F

Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
disease and a nuisance, and should be
done away with.
Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
want to support the hon. and gallant

Those of us who are interested in business,
whether trustee or executor, very often
have to exercise options, and the companies concerned do not always allow for
the fact that some. of the stockholders
may be
in a blockaded country like
Northern Ireland. Consequently it is
sometimes very difficult to exercise an
option at the right time. At the present
time letters are very much delayed owing
to the censorship. Some very strange
things happen with the censor. The other
day a child of mine sent me a letter describing a dog fight. The censor chose
to regard it as indicating something that
had been done by air raids, and he erased
the whole account. These things simply
delay correspondence and
Government Department, concerned

make
the
a
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): If the hon. and gallant
Senator refers to the postal censorship, I
can only inform him that this is purely
a security matter, and one which is dealt
with by the Imperial Government. The
Government of Northern Ireland has
from time to time made representations
to the appropriate authorities as to the
inconvenience. and loss of time involved
by the postal censorship between Great
Britain and Northern Ireland, and will
continue to do so and to press for relaxation of the censorship as soon as security.
conditions permit.
The censorship which exists in regard.
to persons
Great
travelling between
Britain and Northern Ireland is also a
security matter, and this also has been
the subject of representations to the.
appropriate authority from time to time.
The continuance of emergency restrictions
generally is a matter which, I am informed, has already been carefully considered by His Majesty's Government, and
statements have been made by Imperial
Ministers from time to time that the
restrictions would be relaxed or withdrawn as soon as the conditions warranted
such a course.
The hon, and gallant Senator may rest
assured that this Government will do all
in its power to hasten the relaxation or
withdrawal of the censorship restrictions.
It must be appreciated that this is a
security matter, and that the decision as
to its necessity or otherwise rests with the
Imperial Government. The earlier the
Imperial authorities agree that the
security regulations can be relaxed, the
better this Government will be pleased.
Major DOBBS: I must thank the
Leader of the House for his reply. Some
may be diffident about saying this, but,
as I have. said, we do feel a little bit
doubtful whether this censorship is necessary at the present time, especially having
regard to what I previously said. However, I am very glad the Government is
going to keep pressing the matter.
Whitehouse Public Elementary School.
The following Question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Joseph
Cunningham:-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether arrangements can be made to
expedite the provision of accommodation for the serving of meals to children
in attendance at Whitehouse Public
Elementary School as arrangements
had been made for the provision of
meals for the children attending the
Roman Catholic School in this area
since September, 1943.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: The
question I have on the Paper refers to
a school on the outskirts of Belfast, in
the Greencastle district. It is an old
school that belongs to the Presbyterian
Church, and they have very kindly
allowed the Education Committee of
Belfast to have the use of this school for
educational purposes, although I know
they are very anxious to have it for youth
welfare work. As it is, the school is
overcrowded, and the Education Committee of Belfast have asked the Ministry
of Education for approval to put up a
Nissen hut in which the children can be
served with meals. They have the place
for the Nissen hut marked off, and the
plans have been submitted to the Ministry of Education and approved by them,
and the matter is now in the hands of
the Works Division of the Ministry of
Finance. All that was done in August
last during the good weather, and now
that we are in winter and the weather is
SO severe it is considered absolutely
necessary that this hut should be provided
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
5 DECEMBER 1944
as soon as possible so that the children
can be served with their meals.
The Education Committee of Belfast
have already decided to ask permission
to go on with the building of a new school
for which they have had a site selected
for the last ten or fifteen years, but there
has been tremendous delay. The school
is overcrowded, and there is not a classroom that can be utilised for serving
meals to the children. Therefore it is
essential that this Nissen hut should be
erected. What could be more aggravating in a small district like this than for
the parents to know that the Roman
Catholic children in the same district
have been provided with meals since
The names of the
September, 1943.
Unionist representatives are mud because
they have been unable to get something
done for the Protestant children. I
would urge the Leader of the House to
point out to the appropriate authority
that the job is so small that it would
take only a day or two to put up this
Nissen hut, and then everything would
be satisfactory, for a time at any rate.
It is very necessary that some action
should be taken immediately.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I should not
have spoken on this subject but for the
unhappy way the question is phrased, as
well as for the speech of Senator
Cunningham. As regards the hon.
Member's reference to the aggravating
position created by the Catholic children
receiving certain facilities which those
whose cause he advocates do not receive,
I should like to state clearly that the
Catholics want no different treatment. I
know that you, Mr. Speaker, would not
allow me on this occasion to go into and
develop the disadvantages and financial
embarrassments which Catholics suffer
under the educational system in regard to
the equipment and maintenance of their
schools. Knowing Senator Cunningham
as I do, I quite believe that he does not
begrudge the Catholic children getting
the facilities they are entitled to receive
under the law. While he always puts a
good case well, he props up a doubtful
case skilfully, and when he has a bad
case we can depend on him resurrecting a
skeleton from the cupboard. Notwithstanding the unhappy way in which this
question is phrased, and the speech of
Senator Cunningham, I think he has
made a good case, and I support him..
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think
Senator Cunningham has already given
all the information there is in connection
with this matter, but I will read the
official reply.
The arrangements for the service of
meals to children in public elementary
schools in Belfast are made by the Belfast
education authority. The authority
desires that the children of Whitehouse
public elementary school should be
brought within the scheme as soon as possible, but it is regretted that owing to
lack of suitable facilities it has not been
found possible to serve meals in that
school. It will be necessary to provide
dining accommodation, and tenders for
this work will be invited by the Ministry
of Finance at an early date. It is expected that the accommodation will be
available in about 11 or 12 weeks.
I have a little additional information
to add to that. I understand that it was
originally proposed that plaster board
huts should be provided as dining accommodation, but these would not have
proved satisfactory as the construction
was too light. It was then necessary to
change over to standard huts and this involved the preparation. of plans and
specifications and consultation in regard
to site and facilities such as gas and
water. These matters have now all been
settled and the work is being dealt with
as expeditiously as possible.
The Roman Catholic school in which
meals are being served is St. Mary's Star
of the Sea Public Elementary School.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time." By the Roads Act
(Northern Ireland), 1937, the Ministry
of Home Affairs was empowered to
borrow on the security of the Road Fund,
the amount which could be so borrowed
being limited to a sum which required an
annual payment of not more than
£50,000 to meet interest and repayment
of capital. It is proposed, while retaining this limit at the amount which can
be borrowed, to enable the Ministry to
borrow from the Government Loans
Fund and the Consolidated Fund, and
thus obtain the advantage of lower rates
of interest.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this Bill be now read a
Second time.
""
This Bill seeks to renew for a further
period of two years the powers conferred
upon the Ministry of Commerce by the
New Industries (Development) Act, 1937.
The extension of these powers granted
by the Act of 1942 expires on
December next.
31st
The House will appreciate that while
there has been a very radical change in
circumstances since the original Act was
passed, this change has only served to
emphasise the importance of every effort
being made to stimulate the growth of
new industries in Ulster, and that there
is very little doubt that the Government
will within the near future have to seek
more extensive powers for this purpose.
But at this stage of the war, and of
the preparations for reconstruction, the
future is not sufficiently clear to enable
plans to be made with the precision and
accuracy of detail which is necessary
before legislation could be drafted. A
very great deal of thought and work has
already been given to these plans, and
this work, together with the necessary
consultation with the Imperial authorities, will continue. Meanwhile it is
important that the Ministry of Commerce
should retain the powers conferred by
the old Act, which have been of substantial value in the past, and which are
still very useful.
Question proposed.
Mr. MCALLISTER: If my information is correct and if it is not the
Leader of the House will correct me-
New Industries Bill-
Adjournment
5 DECEMBER 1914
one of the deficiencies in this Bill amounts
to this. I understand that the interpretation put upon a "new industry" means
a new industry in Northern Ireland.
That is to say, an industry which has
existed in Antrim or Tyrone and has for
the first time been introduced to Down
would not be a new industry within the
meaning of these Acts. If I am correct
in that interpretation I would ask the
right hon. Gentleman to bear in mind,
when introducing future legislation, that
a new industry to any particular county,
even though it exists in another county,
may afford very much better facilities
for employment than an industry completely new to Northern Ireland, and it
may much more effectively prevent unemployment. I would ask the right hon.
Gentleman to bear that in mind when
framing any future legislation.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The House
will perhaps remember that I had a
certain amount to do with the working
of this New Industries Act before the
war as chairman of the Development Commission, and one of our biggest difficulties was to define a new industry.
It
is a most difficult thing to determine when
an industry is new and when it is not,
and that is one of the reasons why these
new powers are required. In the past
the Act has been somewhat cramped
because of this restriction, but I hope
that, with sufficient thought and care,
will be able to devise something
better in the future. The difficulty is
that if you once go beyond the narrow
limit of "new industry" you may be
using public money to foster competition
with an existing industry, and instead
of creating new employment we might
be shifting employment from one industry
to another. From past experience we
have learned a good deal in this respect,
and as a result I hope we shall be able
to improve on the existing Act.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

INCREASE OF FOOD PRODUCTION.
The following Motion stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Perceval-Maxwell:
"That in the opinion of this House
the food grown in Northern Ireland
could be increased, and for this purpose
the Ministry of Agriculture should
take more effective steps to ensure that
land is only used for the growing of
crops most suitable to it.”
PERCEVAL - MAXWELL :
Major
In another place the Minister of Agriculture has stated his policy for the
coming year, by which he has refused to
give any general relief to farmers, but
this refusal to allow an all round cut in
the tillage quota does not seem to prohibit him from making special arrangements which might involve the reduction
of the quantity of cereal crops in certain
areas or parts of certain areas if the
interests of the nation should demand
such differentiation. We cannot afford
at present to relax our efforts in any of
the activities that support the successful
prosecution of the war, and in drafting
this Motion I have taken pains to emphasise that our first aim must always be
to increase the production of home-grown
food.
The question of most importance, after
the statement of the Minister's policy, is
that of the organisation of that policy.
How is the policy of the Ministry to be
carried out so as to result in the greatest
possible advantage to the Allied war
effort? That is the only matter with
which I concern myself, or will concern
myself until the war is over. Every
practical farmer knows that a policy laying down as a fixed and immutable rule
that every farmer shall plough forty-five
per cent. of the arable land in his holding
is wasteful. It is wasteful in seed, wasteful of time, labour and machinery. It
may be an easy method for the Ministry
to employ, and it may seem fair to individuals, all of whom are treated alike.
Furthermore, it may provide valuable
political propaganda, but, as against
these advantages, it guarantees the lowest
average quantity and quality of food production at the greatest all round expense,
because it results in the production of the
most profitable areas being set off against
the produce of the areas that are incapable of producing anything of value.
In this country the available pool of
machinery is small. The pool of available labour is smaller by comparison with
the demands made on it. It is also of
very uneven quality. Under the present
system it is weakened and dispersed.
Good labour and good machinery is constantly being wasted by being used to
harvest crops which are of no food value,
Increase of Food Production
Increase of Food Production
7 DECEMBER 1944
or very little food value, because they are
damaged or grown under adverse conditions, and the farmer who can produce a crop of high food value loses
it through lack of means to harvest it.
In this country, unfortunately, we suffer
from tempestuous weather at a time when
fine weather is most needed for farmers.
The only way to overcome this inconvenience is to increase the man-hours
worked at the time when the weather is
more favourable and, as agricultural labour
already works as long hours as is possible,
we should reduce wasteful labour so that
it can be concentrated in the areas where
it is most useful.
We are now entering on the second
rotation of four years since the start of
the war, so it seems to be the appropriate
time to pause and consider the mistakes
that have been made in the past, to ensure that the next four years will provide
an added impulse to increased food production. In spite of the mistakes that
have been made, the farmers of Ulster
have certainly achieved remarkable results, and these results could never have
been achieved but for the hard painstaking and sympathetic work of the
Ministry of Agriculture, to whom the
farmers owe a very deep and lasting debt
of gratitude. But I believe that the rule
that every farmer must plough up 45 per
cent. of his arable land has been the cause
of great difficulty to the work of the
officials of the Ministry who work in areas
where the land cannot truly be said to be
arable at all; where it is poor, waterlogged, mountainous and surrounded by
trees. It must have been difficult for
these hard-pressed men to decide which
land should be called arable, and if their
choice did not prove always right no
blame should be imputed to them. They
were, after all, being forced to work a
difficult system, and as I maintain a bad
working plan.
However, the results have been good in
many ways, but they could have been
very much better if more attention had
been given to the cultivation and saving
of the crops and less to the number of
acres ploughed. There is nothing more
disheartening for a farmer who sows a
field than the thought that, apart from
the occurrence of a series of unlikely
flukes of good luck, he will never save
more than a fraction of the crop. Heated
cats ruin the digestion of stock. They
get heated, and this causes fermentation,
and fermentation creates acids. They
reduce milk production and cause a waste
of good food by using the energy produced by the good food in the simple
effort of digesting the bad. Bad food can
never produce stamina and the growth
that we require in young stock.
I am afraid the Minister takes far too
much pride in stating the number of
acres under cultivation. I maintain
that the acreage under cultivation is no
test of production and very little test of
war effort. What matters most is the
use to which these acres are put. What
is the use of acres producing nothing but
indigestible fibre?
I am afraid I must now go into the
somewhat technical matter of what
actually is produced. The digestible
constituent of oats should consist of 8
per cent. protein, 4.2 per cent. oil, 47
per cent. carbohydrate, mostly in the
form of starch. The dry matter should
be about 86.7 per cent. of the bulk. Of
the rest 14 per cent. is water, 2 per cent.
unavailable protein, and 10.8 per cent.
indigestible fibre. Oats grown on poor
land or badly heated lose their valuable
constituents with a consequent increase
of the indigestible qualities. The same
rules apply to barley and wheat, barley
having a slightly higher quantity of
starch and less oil and protein. If wheat
is cut before it is fully ripe it does not
contain gluten and is useless for milling.
All grain that contains more than 14 per
cent. water is rejected by the millers and
by the Port Area Authority, and is
almost useless unless the farmer keeps
enough stock to eat it. I would point
out that the ratio between the tillage
quota and the amount of stock maintained in this country is not comparable.
I would also point out that there are
better and cheaper ways of watering
cattle than by feeding them on wet oats.
I have gone rather deeply into this
rather technical matter to show that what
interests the farmer, the merchant, and
the miller is the quality of the grain.
rather than its quantity. The statements issued by the Minister of
Agriculture from time to time on the
acreage sown and the alleged increase of
production I take leave to doubt, because
the Minister makes out that our average
Increase of Food Production
Increase of Food Production
THE SENATE
[Major Perceval-Maxwell]
production is 18 cwt.
With great
respect I must say I am very surprised at
that, and I take leave to doubt it until
I know what the basis of his facts are.
The latest authorities give about one ton
to the acre as the average production of
oats throughout the United Kingdom,
and I cannot believe the land we have in
Northern Ireland is anything better than
the land of the United Kingdom in
general. However that may be, the
statements issued from time to time of
the acreage sown and the alleged increase
of production are, I think, apt to be misleading to those without expert knowledge,
and it is open to doubt whether these
statements serve any useful purpose except
propaganda, unless the Minister also
publishes the quantity grown that has
been rejected by the merchants, millers
and the Port Area Authority.
Recently, in a speech to his constituents, the Prime Minister took the credit
for reducing the tillage quota in Fermanagh. Being a County Down man
I may perhaps be forgiven for wondering
why it is that it is a more meritorious act
to reduce the tillage quota for farmers
in the Clogher Valley than for those in
districts such as the Mourne Mountains,
where oats do not ripen until September
or October, if they ever ripen at all?
Putting that query aside for the moment,
I would like to ask the right hon. Gentleman if this leniency has not more than
paid for the loss in quantity by the gain
in quality. The fact remains that notwithstanding the edicts of the Ministry of
Agriculture, crops grown in wooded or
hilly country or in country liable to
become waterlogged, will not grow as well
as they do in country where they get
plenty of sun and root nourishment.
These sort of considerations should be
given more weight by the right hon.
Gentleman when he is arranging his
tillage programme than the very obvious
emphasis and I think over emphasis
on the propaganda effect of saying that
so many hundred thousand acres have
been ploughed up.
Before I sit down-and I have very
little more to say on the general subject-
I want to say a word about the difficulties
that the Ministry's policy raises for
farmers in the period in which they should
grow a root crop. Most provident
farmers arrange that under normal conwar
ditions at least one-third of their tilled
land is sown with some root crop which
rests the land and cleans it. Under the
conditions brought about by our
policy this is an impossibility. A root
crop entails almost all hand labour thinning and cleaning it, and it requires more
labour than farmers can obtain. Most
of it is only able to be used on the farm
on which it is grown.
Apart from
potatoes, there is no appreciable market
for turnips and mangolds, and there is
very little market, and a very unstable
one, for carrots, parsnips and cabbages.
And unless the farmer has a large dairy
farm it is not worth while growing very
much kale. For this reason, and not for
the reason given by the Minister in
another place, some farmers have left
their land too long in the cultivation of
cereal crops.
The Ministry has determined that this
land must be sown down to grass this
year. The decision is probably right.
although wheat has been grown successfully at Rothamstead in one field for more
than 100 years, and in Lincolnshire
potatoes are sometimes grown for as many
as seven years without a break. I think,
perhaps, the climate and soil of this
country would not as a general rule allow
such a thing to be done here.
In any
case, in England no one grows potatoes
as a general practical measure unless his
land produces ten to sixteen tons to the
acre. Here, I should
should imagine,
imagine, the
Howaverage is seven tons to the acre.
ever that may be, in England they have
a very much better chance. They have
sugar beet and other crops which they
can grow as alternative crops to oats, and
in this country it is very difficult for a
farmer to rest his land or clean it.
I would point out, however, that the
Minister should remember that grass is
a cereal crop of essentially the same
nature as oats or barley, and by compelling farmers to continue cereal cropping
without resting the land or reviving it by
organic manure or cleaning it, he is laying up great trouble for the future.
suggest that this branch of farming
should attract the Minister's attention
much more fully than it has in the past
four years.
I
In case I should be called a destructive
critic, I have, a suggestion to make for his
consideration; that in suitable cases the
Ministry should encourage farmers to
Increase of Food Production 7 DECEMBER 1944
Increase of Food Production
fallow their land in the spring, plough it
late, and sow a crop of winter green
herbage, such as rape or Italian rye
grass, on which they can fatten sheep or
catttle, or graze their dairy cows in the
winter. By this means the farmer can
use his stock to spread his manure on the
land. It is rested in the spring and
cleaned by the. late cultivation, and by
the rape, of which there is no better
cleaning crop in existence. I do not want
the Minister to think I am merely giving
this technical advice. from theory. I have
tried it, and tried it most successfully,
and it works. The land lies down just
as well as it would do if there were
ordinary rotation.
Under the present policy of the Ministry, weed control is impossible. You cut
your weeds because you have to, and you
lose your crop, and if you do not cut your
weeds you are prosecuted. What is the
wretched farmer to do? I think it is the
duty of the Ministry, in carrying out the
Minister's policy, to differentiate between
farm and farm, and parts of a farm if
necessary, if this will result in more food.
I do not know if this policy will result
in a reduction of the acreage ploughed,
and, to tell you the truth, I do not much
care so long as it produces more food.
I fear, from some of the statements of the
Minister of Agriculture, that he is more
concerned with the value of the. propaganda than the value of the food, and it
is to the end that the supply of food may
be increased, even at the risk of losing
some political propaganda, that I move.
this Motion. I beg to move.

the food grown in Northern Ireland

Major DOBBS: I should like most
heartily to support the Motion put down
by the hon. and gallant Senator. May I
first of all say how very pleased we all are
to see the right hon. Gentleman the
Minister of Agriculture here to-day?
(Hon. Members: Hear, hear.) I certainly think that this question should be
approached from the angle from which the
hon, and gallant Senator has approached
it; that is, how can we increase the food
That is
grown in Northern Ireland?
really the crux of the whole matter. The
weather cannot be altered by Parliament,
nor can it be altered by the Minister of
Agriculture, but that is no reason why
we and the Minister of Agriculture, should
not take the weather into consideration.
I was over some land in country near
where I live about five or six weeks ago,
and it was quite. pitiable. That land was
not hurt by the flooding, but simply
through the weather and so on.
A quarter of the grain crop was carted, a quarter
was hutted, a quarter was in stooks, and
a quarter was uncut, absolutely ruined.
If you go back to last year, I think the
proportion that was ruined was very much
the same.
What is the reason for this, apart from
the weather? One reason, I think, is
that the farmer has got to do, and he
certainly is trying to do, a little more
than he can do. There are one, or two
other points which hit him very hard.
There is the question of harvesting.
When I am talking about harvesting, I
am not talking of this season, but of the
last few seasons. The grain has got to be
cut by binder, but we have got to realise
that a binder cannot start in some of
these regions before 1 or 2 o'clock,
or even 3 o'clock in the afternoon.
We cannot start the binder until the
grain is sufficiently dry. The binder cuts
the grain, ties it, and throws the sheaves
out. These sheaves have to be stooked.
We cannot put these stooks into our barns
or make them into ricks immediately, because if we did there would be a fire,
and the barns or the ricks would be
burned. We have to leave the stooks
long enough to dry. It very often
happens that the weather is such that the
stooks have to be shifted and re-stooked
once or, perhaps, twice.
The Ministry say I think it is quite
a good idea that we should hut these
stooks at once. We cannot do that until
they are fairly dry. On the way to Larne.
I saw some stooks which had been hutted.
They were smouldering because they had
been hutted too early. In some cases the
stooks have to be re-stooked. When the
weather is good we bring the stooks into
the barn or put them in ricks. If that
cannot be done we hut them, and then
take the huts in later on. There is a
considerable amount of labour expended
in doing that. The tremendous amount
Increase of Food Production
Increase of Food Production
THE SENATE
[Major Dobbs]
of grain that is lost through falling on
the ground, owing to the number of times
the stooks have to be moved, is not
sufficiently realised.
In the South of England all that work
can be done in one day. I have seen it
cut and carted in in the one day. With
the machines and the men most farmers
have at the present time they would be able
to do their allotted quota if they could
do all the work in one day. They cannot
do so.
They have to do the work over
and over again.
I wish to accentuate another point in
connection with harvesting. Our harvest
starts six weeks later than the harvest in
England. If for one reason or another
the harvest in England takes longer than
usual it does not matter, because the
farmers there can look forward to plenty
of good days, but farmers here-they
start six weeks later than the farmers in
England-must get their harvesting
finished before the weather breaks. They
have no extra fine days to which to look
forward. That being so, everything has
to be rushed at harvest time.
Here is a point to which I should like
to draw the attention of the Minister of
Agriculture. Why should the grain crop
not be cut by a binder, and by a trailer
taken at once to a drying plant, dried,
threshed, the grain saved, the straw
baled, and so on? If that were done
much labour would be saved. The whole
crux of the matter is the drying plant.
In the near future I intend to table a
question in regard to that matter. I
think it is a matter which should receive
the serious consideration of the Minister
of Agriculture.
am
a
Let us leave harvesting for a moment
and go back to the starting pointploughing. I
firm believer in
ploughing being done in the autumn so
that the frost may get into the ground:
Last year I could not plough from the
month of September until the middle of
February. This year I could not plough
from the month of August, and it may be
weeks before I will be able to do So.
When I use the word "plough" I mean
in addition to grubbing and cultivating.
What is the result? It means that I cannot plough until the early part of 1945.
That means late sowing. I should like
to grow beans, but I am told that I
should not do so unless I can sow them in
the middle of February. If I cannot
plough the land until then what chance
have I of growing beans or like crops?
I hope the Minister of Agriculture will
face up to the facts as we find them at the
moment. He has started doing so in connection with certain places. He and his
Department should be guided by what can
be done in Northern Ireland, not by what
can be done in Essex, Hampshire or Wiltshire. It seems to me that too much is
ordered to be done for the purpose of
making a good effect on paper. I suppose
that is only natural. When people hear
that 100 acres have been ploughed they
say it is wonderful, but if the ploughing
of those 100 acres means only 50 tons of
grain then it is not wonderful, it is
miserable. Would not 75 acres giving 50
tons of grain and the remaining 25 acres
used for producing beef or milk not be of
better value both from the point of view
of the farmer and of the war effort? I
heartily support the Motion moved by
my hon. and gallant Friend.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I shall be very brief.
I wish to support the Motion proposed by
the hon. and gallant Member, and supported by my hon. and gallant Friend
(Major Dobbs). This has certainly been
a very strenuous year for the farmers.
Owing to the flooding that took place a
short time ago crops were damaged to a
terrific extent. In my area-the Glens of
Antrim-I should think that half of the
potato crop is still in the ground. The
corn is still out in huts, while hay is also
lying out in the fields and is deteriorating.
The farmers are unable to save them owing
to the bad weather. Something should
be done in the matter.
The time is ripe for a change to be
made in regard to the 1945 harvest. There
should be a reduction in the percentage
of land to be tilled. If that were done
it would help the farmers. If that is not
done then the differentiation which the
hon. and gallant Member has referred to
should be applied generally. The mover
of the Motion put up a very strong case
for a change to be made. I heartily support him, and maintain that the time is
ripe for a change to be made.
The MINISTER OF AGRICULTURE
(Rev. Robert Moore): To begin with, I
should like to say that I understand that
in regard to legislative matters it is an
Increase of Food Production
Increase of Food Production
7 DECEMBER 1944
axiom that law which is based on exceptional cases is bad law. This year is an
exception to all rules in regard to the
climatic conditions with which our farmers
have had to battle. To get panicky
because things have been difficult this
year and to jump to conclusions on the
strength of that evidence alone is not
advisable to say the least of it. None of
us has the gift of prophecy. It may be
that the summer of 1945 will be a record
one for dryness and drought. For all we
know the grain harvest in 1945 may not
be eighteen inches in length when harvest
time arrives. We are living in a period
of risk. They are, I submit, legitimate
risks, and having regard to all the facts
and circumstances I submit that I am
taking a most legitimate risk in adhering
to the figure of 45 per cent. for another
year.
I should like to draw the attention of
this honourable House to the situation
that confronted the nation. We were
faced with the necessity of providing a
reserve of food not only for the support
of our livestock but of providing a reserve
of food that, if the worst came to the
worst, could be diverted from cattlefeeding purposes to human use. That
meant that the food we grew had to be
in a certain form. In normal conditions
our farmers market the bulk of their
crops, if we except potatoes. Even there,
I think, the rule should apply, too, because on the other side the accusation is
made that the £10 per acre payment is
unjust in the case of Northern Ireland
because our farmers persist, even in wartime, in feeding an undue percentage of
the potatoes they grow under the acreage
payment arrangement to their live stock.
and, that, therefore, they are not available for human food.
The broad point I want to make is this.
The national necessity was so urgent that
the Ministry and the farming community
had so to arrange. the economy of our
farming that the utmost food would be
produced in a form that, if necessary,
could be diverted to human use. I am very
glad to know-I am sure this honourable
House is equally glad-that the worst
part of that risk is probably past.
I should like to say that the policy my
Ministry has pursued, and the policy I
am pursuing, in this connection has got
nothing to do with propaganda. It never
once entered my head. I do not know
what the hon. and gallant Member who
moved the Motion had in his mind when
he talked-he returned to the point again
and again-about propaganda. Propaganda for what? Propaganda to whom?

What did the acreage figures mean except
propaganda?

Rev. ROBERT MOORE: I

tainly never entered my mind.
have to do is to try to get the utmost
quantity of food grown during the period
of the war. Now that the necessity for
having a reserve in the form of cereals and
potatoes that could, if the need arose, be
diverted to human consumption is almost
passed, I am still confronted with the.
necessity of growing the utmost food our
land can produce, because roughly 85 per
cent. of the total income of the farming
community of Northern Ireland is derived
from live stock or live stock products.
But how are we going to maintain and
support that live stock if we do not grow
the food to feed it? It is a comparatively
simple matter to carry a certain amount
of stock in the summer during the five
grazing months, but anyone who has experience knows that a tremendous proportion of the live stock population has to be
carried right through the winter, and you
can only do that, and you can only carry
out satisfactorily the rearing, apart from
the mere fattening, of livestock, if you
have the necessary food to carry those.
livestock right through the whole year.
When Mr. Hudson was here, he thumped
the table and assured all his listeners that
not in the lifetime of anybody to whom
he was speaking at that meeting would
there be the importation of cattle-feeding
stuffs we had been accustomed to pre-war.
We are now faced with circumstances in
which we must keep up our live stock
population, and as we cannot get the
imported feeding stuffs, we simply must
get the food for them from our own land.
Apart from those broad general considerations, I do not accept the dicta of
the hon. and gallant Senator who moved
this Motion. I am confronted with
evidence which is contrary. We have a
system of conacre letting which is
peculiar to Northern Ireland. In my
own immediate neighbourhood this very
week there have been I do not know how
Increase of Food Production
Increase of Food Production
THE SENATE
[Rev. Robert Moore]
many farms put up for conacre letting,
and I know of only one field that was
not let. You see the point I am making?
I admit there is a certain amount of
speculation in this business. You have
mill scutchers, you have ordinary farm
labourers, you have sometimes even
people from the towns speculating and
taking some of this land in conacre, but
by far the greater proportion of that
land is taken by bona-fide farmers, by
good farmers.
What I cannot understand is why those
farmers, if they are already in it to the
neck and are not able to grapple with the
task I have set them on their own farms,
should of their own volition go and take
more land on a neighbouring farm and
bid up the land for oats up to, as it was
in my neighbourhood this week, £11 and
£12 an acre, and the land for flax to as
much as £16 an acre. These are the
facts. I am not convinced, with great
respect to the mover of this Motion, that
if we had a normal season again the job
I have laid before the farmers of Northern Ireland for 1945 is not well within
the range of their ability to cope with.
I admit that we could still do with considerably more farm machinery, although
there is no part of the United Kingdom
that has made such strides in
mechanisation of farming since the war
started than Northern Ireland has done.

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL:

Rev. ROBERT MOORE: I know the
hon. and gallant Senator did not, but it
has been hinted in other quarters that
that was the case. I quite appreciate
that the hon. and gallant Senator never
suggested anything, but I want, when I
have the opportunity, to make my point
clear. County Fermanagh is in a category
by itself, and how, even in peace-time,
the majority of the small farmers in that
county were able to eke out a living from
the kind of land there is there God only
knows, because I do not. I have been
over it. It is a sort of sticky, impervious clay, and if a beast in an
ordinary grass field puts down its foot
there is a pool of water which will stay
there until the sun evaporates it.
Drainage is useless. Phosphates are
nearly non-existent. It would be simply
waste in County Fermanagh to go on
ploughing up the land simply because we
had a ploughing quota. It was purely
consideration of the local conditions that
induced us to reduce the quota for
County Fermanagh from 45 per cent. to
30 per cent. To that 30 per cent. quota
in the hope of doing something to lift
the fertility of County Fermanagh soil,
we have imposed conditions which have to
be carried out. I do not agree that the
yields have suffered materially throughout the course of the war.
I would refer the hon. and gallant
Senator who moved this Motion to some
figures in one of the monthly reports
which I have here, but from my own
experience I keep in close touch with
farming and farmers in County Derry
and County Antrim, and as far as I can
over the Six Counties-I submit that in
my own county, and it is a late part of
the country being far north-I myself
live at the foothills-if I had said to the
farmers of Northern Ireland that in regard to 1945 I was not going to impose
any particular quota but would leave it
to themselves, the vast bulk would have
ploughed at least 45 per cent. Many of
them have never been affected by the
ploughing quota at all. They were
beyond it, and are still beyond it, and
only that the abnormal rainfall this year,
which is leaching all our land and washing all the substance out of it, there is
farm after farm where so far from the
fertility having been lowered it is, I know,
actually higher and better to-day than it
was in 1939.
I would point out to this honourable
House that in spite of this ploughing we
are to-day carrying more live stock than
we were when the war broke out. That
surely means that those animals are being
fed, and the resulting farmyard manure
is going back into the land. Then the
use of fertilisers and artificial manures
has gone up by leaps and bounds.
1939 the average in the whole Six Counties
was only 40,000 tons, whereas in the year
1944 it had gone up to 180,000 tons. I
cannot think of that as being wasted; I
In
Increase of Food Production
Increase of Food Production

7 DECEMBER 1944
can see it resulting in our being able to
support an increasing stock of cattle.
This, in turn, consumes the produce of
our fields, and the manure is going back
into the soil in organic form, and where
a proper rotation of cultivation has been
observed I am satisfied that no real harm
has been done or is being done.
The trouble is that there are certain
areas in the country where the soil lends
itself to certain uses, and there was a
comparatively easy way of farming in
some districts. I am not reflecting on
the farmers who have resorted to that
way. It has not been mentioned in this
debate, but there were areas where the
custom was to wait until a suitable time
and bring up a lot of cattle, say from the
Free State, put them on the land, and
graze them there for maybe eight weeks,
or whatever the period might be. They
were practically fat when they were
brought up. During those eight weeks
they added practically nothing to themselves, but they were at least in as good
condition as they were when they were
purchased. With the seasonal
movements of prices the owners of those cattle,
without doing a tap, were able at the end
of those eight or ten weeks to make £2
or £3 a head, or whatever the figure
might be.
That is not food production; that is not
contributing anything to the national
necessity. I admit it is a source of income
to the farmers, but it brings me back to
what I said at the start, namely, that
we had to plan, even if it did drag the
heart out of our land, just as the soldier
on the battlefield has to take the risk,
even if it means his death. We were
faced with an emergency such as never
confronted the world before.
We were
asked to produce the utmost that our
fields could produce and to get the utmost
out of our whole farm economy in the
growing of flax and all those other things.
Having regard to everything, I submit
that our policy has succeeded even
beyond the rosiest dreams.

Mr. LENNON: The difficulty with
regard to the application of any all-over
45 per cent. quota appears to be that it
disregards completely the tremendous disparity that exists in the land of the Six
Counties; and whilst it is perfectly true,
as has just been stated by the hon. and
gallant Senator, that there are areas of
land which could quite readily bear the
quota of 45 per cent., there are vast areas
of land which could not bear anything of
the kind. The Minister has said that
this year has been exceptional, and withthat I may say at once I most heartily
agree. The weather throughout the, harvest period this year, and right on until
now, has been unprecedented. But the
trouble there is that his Department proposes to apply during 1945 the 45 per
cent. quota. That 45 per cent. quota
being applied during 1945 totally disregards the fact and this is a matter I
ask him to take particular notice of-that
we have just passed through a most unprecedented period of wet weather, which
undoubtedly diminished the value of the
land and its capability for the production
of crops.
Minister's
There is attached to the
Department probably one of the finest
services that this Government can give
to the community, and that is the Research Department which is carried on, I
believe, at Elmwood Avenue. And if the
Minister and his Department were to
take more steps to let the farmers know
of the services that are available through
that Department, then his Department
would be doing a tremendous service to
the farming community. But his Department, I suggest, might readily make
more use of the Research Department by
ascertaining which lands are the more
suitable for crops, and therefore avoiding the trouble that has been mentioned,
namely the production of crops of an
unsuitable kind on farms which will not
therefore produce as good food, nor
nearly as much food, as if this all-over
quota of 45 per cent. was not of general
effect but was to be applied with discrimination after ascertaining first of all
the particular areas and the particular
lands suited for the production of certain
types of crops. It would then become
manifestly clear that this all-over quota
of 45 per cent. is not a suitable method
in the Six Counties.
The Minister's Department has in its
research laboratories some of the finest
men of science that are available, and
they carry out an essential work, but his
Department does not, to my mind at any
rate-and I make the suggestion with the
greatest possible respect-appear to take
sufficient steps to let the farmers know of
the services that are there available, with
the result mentioned by the mover of the
Motion, that crops are being grown
which are not economical, the work of the
men employed is wasted, and the money
for seed is wasted. Might I suggest to
the Minister that a mere casual view of
the lands of the Six Counties to-day shows
that crops are now being rotted and
wasted on the land, potatoes are undug
and acres of oats in stooks are lost irredeemably? And why? Because too much
was asked from the farmer at a critical
time when the labour was not available.
Might I suggest that the Minister's
Department might profit from the experience it has had in this most unprecedented season, and, as was suggested
by the hon. and gallant Senator in
moving the Motion, that elasticity is what
is required. Discrimination is required.
An all-over quota is unsuitable and uneconomical.

expenses of producing a potato crop. The
potato crop essentially follows after a
green crop. The trouble is that there is
such disparity in the land. There are
vast areas in South Armagh, areas in
Tyrone, and areas in Down that are hilly.
Areas are being ploughed over a thousand feet above sea-level. It is my submission it is a complete waste of time and
a waste of the money and effort that

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I have some
difficulty in speaking after my right hon.
Friend, who knows a great deal more
about the subject than I do, but I think
there is one side of the question it is just
possible the House has overlooked, and
that is the fact that this war is not being
fought to make money for the Northern
Ireland farmer. We all know, or most
of us know, that if we were able to farm
as we should do in peace-time we should
all in all probability be making more
money. I know I should. Again, we all
know that if we had exact choice of what
we would plough and 'what we would not
plough we should probably plough much
less.
But there is a war going on, and
we farmers of Northern Ireland and the
right hon. Gentleman are all people working under orders from a higher command.
That higher command is in London, where
they have all the statistics and all the
knowledge and information which they
cannot make public-they cannot give it
to us; I doubt if they can give it to the
right hon. Gentleman-of what the food
position is for the months to come all over
the world. They put forward certain requests to us which are important. These
requests are based on information which
cannot be given to us. We dare not dispute it-the right hon. Gentleman dare
not dispute it.
It is the some. position which may
happen at any time to the troops. I
remember reading the other day of one
of the incidents in Wavell's attack on
Tobruk. A certain unit had to attack
far into the line. It must have. looked
hopeless to the men and everybody concerned, but they attacked, and they had
as many as 80 per cent. of casualties.
By that attack they held very nearly a
Division of reserves there, and the big
attack went through ten miles away.
It may be that we are being asked to
some extent to sacrifice ourselves because

ADJOURNMENT MOTION.
WHITEHOUSE PUBLIC ELE-
MENTARY SCHOOL.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, "That this House do now adjourn
until Tuesday next."
Question proposed.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I promised
Senator Joseph Cunningham at our last
meeting that I would give the House
some further information, if I could
obtain it, in regard to the provision of
dining accommodation at Whitehouse
Public Elementary School. It has a long
and lamentable history. Originally a conference in April last decided that temporary dining accommodation must be
provided for various elementary schools
that were too crowded. Under that
general agreement, application was made.
in August last by the educational authorities for the provision of accommodation
at Whitehouse. The application was
approved by the Ministry of Education
and was forwarded to the Ministry of
Finance. At that time the Ministry of
Finance had available. certain plasterboard huts, not Nissen huts. These were
approved by the architect to the, education authority, and they would have been
comparatively quickly erected on a site
already selected.
On further examination it was found,
however, that these huts were not of sufficiently robust construction to stand the
wear and tear of a dining-room, and it
was therefore necessary to plan for a more
substantial form of hut. That planning
entailed a reconsideration of the site and
required plans and specifications of the
normal type. That work, as the House
will appreciate, always takes a certain
amount of time, but it is practically completed now. The huts will be of prefabricated concrete construction-concrete
panels fitted on to a concrete base. It is
also necessary to arrange for water and
gas accommodation, because, there will be
a scullery and a small kitchen attached.
That accounts for the delay up to the
present date.
The estimate of twelve weeks given in
my answer on Tuesday last is made up in
this way.
Tenders have to be asked for
and, when received, have to be considered.
This will take roughly two or three weeks
from now, and that will leave about eight
weeks for the contractor to build. Eight
weeks, I am informed, is not an unreasonable time for the erection of a hut of this
substantial form of construction, though
it is hoped it will be built more quickly;
but we do not like to give too short a
date, because at this time of year build-
Whitehouse P.E. School
7 DECEMBER 1944
Adjournment
ing may be stopped by inclement weather
and the Christmas holidays.
I should, in justice to the Works
Division of the Ministry of Finance, say
that they are terribly overworked and
are very short of qualified staff. Various
works arising from the war, although
fewer than some months ago, are still
throwing an altogether abnormal load on
them, and plans do necessarily take
longer than they usually do to go through.
At the same time the House will probably realise that in normal private building construction it is not unusual for four
or five months to elapse between the
original decision to build and the commencement of the work. The intervening time is taken up by the preparation
of plans, the invitation of tenders, and
so forth. Therefore, although at first
glance this seems to be an unusual delay,
it has not really been a very serious one.
