dining-rooms for a considerable number
of years to come, and, of course, a Nissen
hut, after the first bloom of youth goes
off-and it goes off pretty quickly-is an
unsatisfactory dwelling, as some of us
who have lived in them know only too
well. I think it is considered that a more
permanent type of accommodation should
be put up. We do appreciate the importance of getting ahead with this work,
and it is most unfortunate for the people
that the other form of hut proved not to
be suitable. That is the real reason of the
delay.
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: Thank
you.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at Five
minutes before Four o'clock
until Tuesday next.
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Tuesday, 12th December, 1944.
The Senate met at Half after Two of
the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the Chair.
QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
Drying and Threshing of Grain Crop.
The following Question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state if the attention
of the Minister of Agriculture has been
drawn to the speech made by Colonel
G. P. Pollitt at the Crop Driers' Conference at Winchester, putting forward
'the suggestion that the grain crop
should be cut, placed direct into an
accompanying trailer and taken at
once to a drying plant, dried, and then
immediately threshed; whether he will
have this proposed method of drying
the grain and straw thoroughly considered as a solution of our harvesting
difficulties; and whether he will consider the erection of some of these
drying plants in Northern Ireland.
Major DOBBS: I think I should be
showing scant courtesy to this House if I
did not try to give the reason underlying
this Question. Some years ago, from
experiments in England, it was found
that if grass was cut young and then
dried in a special drying plant, its feeding
values were very, very much greater than
the feeding values of ordinary straw.
Many people were interested in those
experiments, and I think the right hon.
Gentleman the Leader of the House was
one of those who was definitely interested.
It was generally felt, I think, that the
great cost of the special drying plant made
it practically prohibitive unless this was
carried on on a very large scale. The
farmers here in Northern Ireland, at
harvest time, are unable to carry their
sheaves immediately they are cut into a
rick or into a barn, and this is one of the
great drawbacks. There is a great
amount of work entailed because they
cannot do that as soon as it is laboured,
and there is a great loss in grain from its
multiple movement. Colonel Pollitt suggests that directly the sheaves are cut
they should be carried straight away to a
special drying plant. This, I would like
to point out, is different to what is happening at the present time, for after it
has been threshed in a combined harvester
or ordinary threshing machine it is taken
to a communal drying plant. In that
case it is the grain only that is dried.
The point I am trying to put forward
is that the grain and straw, that is the
sheaves themselves, are dried together.
If a drying plant could be constructed
that could deal both with young grass,
and later on in the season with the
sheaves and grain, the. objection of its
high cost would be partly mitigated. As
Colonel Pollitt points out, the harvest
field, if worked in this way, would become
cleared at one operation as it was cut, and
the field would be. immediately available
for cultivation and for a catch crop.
Another point, which is rather important,
is that the crop carries off with its grain
and straw a maximum proportion of weed
seeds, and that ensures that the field is
left in a much cleaner state than it would
be in the ordinary way.
I would like to point out that Colonel
Pollitt is one of the chiefs of Imperial
Chemical Industries. Imperial Chemical
Industries, Ltd., quite apart from altruistic reasons, are. very keen on farming and
its importance, on account of the great
amount of artificials they produce. They
have had a great deal to do with these
grass drying machines as well as with
other farming processes.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): The Minister of Agriculture is aware of the suggestion made
by Col. G. P. Pollitt to the effect that the
grain crop should be artificially dried as
soon as it has been cut and threshed
immediately after drying. The Minister
is of the opinion that apart from the
many practical difficulties involved, the
expenditure necessary for providing and
operating the driers would not be
economic, and he is not prepared in existing conditions to adopt the proposal.
The real difficulty is the high cost of
transport, extra handling, and so forth.
Speaking from my own slight knowledge
of these things I may say it might conceivably and, in fact, it would be
economic in a year, such as the present
year, with the quantity of grain we have
in the country under abnormal circum-
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
12 DECEMBER 1944
stances, but it is very unlikely it would
be economic in a more normal year when
there is a more normal amount of grain.
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of that, I
would like to ask whether, in considering
the matter, the average size of the field in
Northern Ireland has been considered.
The point is, of course, that there are,
not exactly on the market in this country
but certainly on the market in the United
States, and I think also in Canada, what
I would describe as a harvesting outfit,
where the whole business is carried out in
one operation. That is not quite the
same thing as artificial drying to which
my hon, and gallant Friend refers, but
it is a conceivable method of dealing with
the difficulty if you have only got a field
big enough to allow your equipment to
operate, and if you can satisfy yourself
that the cost involved is an economic
proposition. Whether that has been considered or not I do not know, but it
strikes me it would be of interest to hear
what the Leader of the House has to say
on that point.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I am afraid
this information is only from my own
personal knowledge, but I can and will
get more information from the Minister
of Agriculture. I do know that a certain
number of combined harvesters have been
tried since the war, and I myself have
been to see one or two of them in operation. They are operating with moderate
success, and when I went to see these
demonstrations I was informed they were
operating fairly effectively in a reasonably
sized field. I did not see any in a small
field, but in fields of from 10 to 15 acres
they seem to work effectively. The
some
difficulty here, I think, is partly the hilly
nature of the country. The harvesters
work much better on flat land. However, the difficulty in most cases is that
our grain is not dry enough here, so
that it is necessary to instal
form of drying kiln as well. I have not
pursued the matter much further, but
there is a great deal of data on the subject
in the Ministry of Agriculture, which I
will be glad to supply if the House wants
it. It is in its experimental stage here.
Major DOBBS: Thank you.
Coal Supplies from "Bellmen."
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I
wish to ask the Leader of the House
whether arrangements can be made to
supply coal to those people who usually
purchase their supplies from the "bellmen" on the street in view of the fact
that these "bellmen" cannot be relied
upon to provide the housekeeper with
the quota allowed.
I put this Question down because I
am satisfied that a considerable number
of people in Belfast are suffering severe
hardship as a result of the fact that they
cannot get coal. My attention was drawn
to this a long time ago when an old
couple returned to Belfast from the
country after being evacuated. While
they had a permit for coal there was no
coalman to supply them. They were in
a difficulty. A day or so ago I had
occasion to meet a young couple who are
living in one room, and they told me that
while they had a permit for coal there
was no one to supply them with it. They
had terrible trouble getting a small
quantity of coal brick. In fact, they had
to go from one place to another to get the
coal brick. They broke it up to light a fire
in the room. One can readily understand
that it must be a severe hardship during
weather like this if there are families left
without coal.
I was told the other day of another
case where there was sickness in the home
and they were left without coal because
the "bellman" could not supply them. I
imagine the Government are really to
some extent responsible for seeing that
these poor people are supplied with the
quota of coal they are allowed or are
entitled to get. There is no use giving
them a permit if you cannot get someone
to supply the coal, and that is really the
position to-day. That is the reason I put
this Question down. Surely when the
Government give a permit to these poor
people to enable them to get coal they
should recommend someone to provide
them with coal. There must be a considerable number of cases in Belfast and
probably in other parts of the North of
Ireland. In Belfast I know of a number
of cases, and something requires to be
done to remedy the position especially during this terrible cold weather.
Mr. BARCLAY: I would like to
support the hon. Senator in this Question.
I am not going to refer to Belfast. I am
going to refer to the counties of Northern
Ireland. Last year at the close of the
season two complaints were made to me
and I was asked if I could do anything
Message from the Commons 354
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. Barclay]
to get coal for some of the poor people in
towns outside the city of Belfast. For
instance, Magherafelt was mentioned. In
the district of Magherafelt the poor could
not get coal or anything in that line except
by going out and cutting down thorn
hedges and timber, which they used as
fires to cook and boil their food. I
inquired if these people had anything that
would enable them to get coal, and I was
told that some of them had permits but
that the merchants had sold their coal to
people who were better off but who had no
right to it. Thus, when these poor people
went for their coal they could not get it.
This is a question that should be looked
into. The coal merchants get a certain
amount of coal and the poor people are
entitled to a certain quota of that coal.
The coal merchant, however, has other
customers who come in for articles other
than coal, and when they want coal he
gives it to them without a permit. I would
like to see something done for the poor
people. Only Magherafelt was mentioned
to me, but I understand there are several
other towns and districts suffering from
the same complaint.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I cannot
accept the implication in the hon.
Senator's question that "bellmen" are
not maintaining a proper distribution of
the coal to their registered customers.
The main body of evidence in the Coal
Control shows the contrary to be the case.
The "bellmen" have priority in regard
to obtaining supplies out of the domestic
coal imported into the country, and they
distribute this coal impartially amongst
their customers. Frequent inspections of
the conduct of business run by "bellmen"
are carried out, and in a great majority
of cases it is found that an equitable
distribution is in progress. I may say
that this principle has been followed for
a considerable time past and the Coal
Control is satisfied that the system is
working well. It may be that the hon.
Senator has in mind the past few weeks
when inclement weather has prevented
the regular importation of coal, but this
is a factor which affects not only the
"bellmen" but all coal supplies in Northern Ireland. If, however, the hon.
Senator has some particular cases in
mind, would he let me have a note of
them and I will see they are investigated
at once?
I think the chief point is that the
"bellmen" have absolute priority on the
Ministry's supplies. If anyone gets coal
the "bellmen" do, and therefore if anyone gets coal the "bellmen's" customers
do. "Bellmen" are inspected as to
equitable distribution amongst their
customers. Of course, it is inevitable
that there are mistakes, but actually, I
am informed, the cases of complaint as
to failure to distribute are, in fact, not
coming forward in any great numbers at
the present time, and they are substantially fewer than they usually are at
this time of the year. I am afraid I
would have to have notice before I could
answer Senator Barclay's question, but
there again what I have said to Senator
Cunningham applies to him, too. If he
knows of definite cases I shall be very
glad, if he supplies me with particulars,
to have them investigated. Generally
speaking, as the House knows, one 18
supposed to register with a coal merchant
and one is then entitled to a supply of
coal, but one does not always get it because the coal is not there. I should be
very grateful to both Senators if they
would give me particulars of any instance,
and I am sure it will be looked into at
once.
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
would like to thank the Leader of the
House, and I will supply him with the
names of those cases of which I know.
I think the main point I made was missed,
and that was that the "bellmen" may
supply their regular customers all right,
but in Belfast there are a considerable
number of people moving about from one
district to another seeking apartments.
Although they have a permit for coal
they cannot get a "bellman" to supply
them.
Mr. SPEAKER: The hon. Senator is
not entitled to make a second speech, but
only to ask a supplementary question.
Mr. CUNNINGHAM: Thank you. I

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That the Commons' Amendments

I think the House will be glad to know

of the financial Clauses, with which this

CLAUSE 1 (Prevention of bee pests
and diseases).
(1) For the purpose of preventing
the spreading of pests or diseases among
bees, the Ministry of Agriculture (in
this Act referred to as "the Ministry'.')
may by regulations provide
(a) for requiring notification . m persons who keep or have charge of
bees, or who have involuntarily
acquired any bees, as to the occurrence or suspected occurrence of
any such pest or disease affecting
bees under their control as may be
specified in the regulations and for
obtaining information regarding
any such specified pest or disease;
*
*
*
*
*
*
Commons Amendment: Clause 1, subclause (1), paragraph (a), leave out the
words, "or
who have involuntarily
acquired any bees."
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, "That this House do agree with
the Commons in the said Amendment."
's
The omission of these words will not
materially affect the powers of the Ministry to deal with bees which were involuntarily acquired or found on a person's
land, because under Clause 1, sub-clause
(1), paragraph (b) the Ministry has power
to require notification of the existence of
any bees which, to his knowledge, have
swarmed or settled on any lands or
premises occupied by him. This gives an
opportunity of ascertaining whether the
disease which breaks out in the district
has been occasioned by bees acquired involuntarily, and that is the only power
the Ministry really needs to carry out the
provisions of this Clause.
Amendment agreed to.
CLAUSE 3 (Establishment and operation of Bee-keepers' Compensation Fund).
Commons Amendment: Clause 3, after
sub-clause (2) add:.
(3) If in any registration year the
amount standing to the credit of the
Compensation Fund is insufficient to
meet the payments required to be made.
out of that Fund during that year
the deficiency shall to the extent
mentioned in the next succeeding subsection be defrayed out of moneys provided by Parliament and any further
deficiency then remaining shall, subject
to the provisions of sub-section (5) of
this section regarding temporary
advances made by the Ministry of
Finance, be defrayed out of moneys
subsequently standing to the credit of
the Compensation Fund.
(4) The total amount to be defrayed
out of moneys provided by Parliament
for the purpose of the last preceding
sub-section shall not, unless the Ministry
of Finance having regard to any
circumstances
exceptional
otherwise
directs, exceed in any registration
year-
(a) one-half of the compensation payable out of the Compensation Fund
during that year; or
(b) five hundred pounds;
whichever of these amounts shall be
the lesser.
(5) The Ministry of Finance may at
any time temporarily advance out of
the Consolidated Fund of Northern
Ireland to the Compensation Fund,
such sums as may from time to time be
required for meeting any claims against
the Compensation Fund for which no
moneys standing to the credit of that
Fund are available and any sum SO
advanced, together with such interest
thereon (if any) as may be determined
by the Ministry of Finance, shall be
charged on the Compensation Fund and
shall, as soon as may be possible, be repaid to the Exchequer of Northern
Ireland out of moneys subsequently
standing to the credit of that Fund.

Glens of Antrim-Increased Tea Ration.
Mr. MAGUIRE: I wish to draw the
attention of the House to the inconvenience caused to old-age pensioners and
residents over 70 years of age in the
Glens of Antrim district. I refer to the
distribution of forms for the increased
ration of tea. There is a sub food office
in Cushendall, but the main office is in
Ballycastle, 16 miles from Cushendall and
24 miles from some residents of the rural
district of Ballycastle in the Glens of
Antrim. They are unable to get the forms
of application from the sub-office. The
food office in Ballycastle have refused to
send the forms to the sub office. The
sub office is carried on by ladies of the
W.V.S. who give very reliable, very efficient and good service voluntarily. Why
the Food Office have done that I do not
know, but it is putting these old people
to great inconvenience. They are greatly
adverse to writing letters and correspondence. Afterwards they have to send the
ration books and application forms filled
in to Ballycastle. There is a great deal of
delay. They would have two writings,
sending for the form and filling it up,
whereas if they could utilise the sub office
in Cushendall they could get assistance
in filling up the forms and getting the
application forms sent on.

It is very
This sub office also until this
tributed emergency ration
visitors coming to the district.
helpful to people coming for a week or
two. Very often they had to send to
Ballycastle to get the emergency cards,
and very often the emergency cards did
not arrive back until the holidays were
finished. I feel that if the attention of
the Ministry were drawn to this matter
they certainly would provide facilities for
these old people and help them in every
way they can. The Glens of Antrim are
situated in a very out-of-the-way place
and transport facilities are very bad.
There is no bus connection between
Cushendall and Ballycastle, a distance of
16 miles. Anyone wishing to attend in
Glens of Antrim Tea Ration
12 DECEMBER 1944
Adjournment
Ballycastle cannot engage a local taxi
owner to take him to Ballycastle. He has
got to take the local taxi owner a distance
of ten miles and arrange with a taxi
owner in Ballycastle to meet it half way.
That is very inconvenient for people in
that district. There is no bus service at
any period between these two points.
a
I will just give an instance that
occurred with myself within the past two
months. I had occasion to come to
Belfast on a Sunday. There is no bus
service out of Cushendall on Sunday until
6-30 p.m., and the distance to Ballymena,
where I could get a train, is 20 miles. I
could not use my own car, so I had to
get in touch with a taxi owner half way
between Cushendall and Ballymena and
arrange for him to come ten miles for
me, without a passenger. He picked me
up and took me 10 miles to the station at
Ballymena to get a train to Belfast.
After leaving me at Ballymena he had
to drive 10 miles back, without
passenger, to his place between Ballymena
and Cushendall. In the evening he had
to drive back 10 miles to Ballymena,
again without a passenger, to take me
back to Cushendall, and then he had to
drive 10 miles back, without a passenger,
to his own garage. Altogether he had to
drive 40 miles without a passenger.
district like that I think some concession
ought to be made to taxi owners. It is
20 miles distant from any railhead, and I
think the attention of the responsible
authority should be drawn to this matter
with a view to facilitating the residents
in some way. I hope also that the local
food sub office will be enabled to distribute the forms I have referred to, as
well as supply emergency ration cards
during the holiday period.
In a
Mr. MCALLISTER: Knowing the
district to which Senator Maguire has
referred I think he has established a very
decided grievance in regard to these oldage pensioners who have given their lives
to the service of the State. Their diet is
very cramped as a result of rationing,
and this House should not tolerate for a
moment red tape and regimentation to
compel these people, many of whom are
illiterate-
Mr. SPEAKER: I am sorry to interrupt the hon. Member, but he can speak
only once on the Motion for the Adjournment, and he has already spoken.
To
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
revert to the question of the Motion
moved on the last sitting day by Senator
Perceval-Maxwell, I certainly am of the
opinion that the explanation given on
that occasion by the Minister of Agriculture was quite satisfactory. I was in
favour of supporting the Minister of
Agriculture, but I understand the Motion
was declared carried. I do not think
that is right.
Mr. SPEAKER: The Motion was
carried, but there was a mistake in the
Minutes, which is being corrected.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think hon.
Senators who spoke legitimately, and the
hon. Senator who spoke too often, will
not expect me to give any information on
the point to-day. I can assure them that
the matter they have raised will be
brought to the notice of the proper
authority.
Major DOBBS: I should like to ask
the Leader of the House if we will be
meeting in the immediate future for the
purpose of giving the Royal Assent to
Bills.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: No, we shall
not.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

minutes after Three o'clock
until such date as Mr. Speaker
shall appoint.

THE SENATE
THE SENATE.

Thursday, 14th December, 1944.
The Senate met at half after Two of
the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in the Chair.
Royal Assent.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Sir
Roland Nugent): I have to report to the
House, That His Grace the Governor of
Northern Ireland, in the name and on
behalf of His Majesty the King, has been
pleased to give his Assent to the following
Bills agreed upon by both Houses:-
Parliament (Elections and Meeting),
Register of Electors (Temporary
Provisions),
Exchequer and Financial Provisions
(No. 2),
Appropriation (No. 2),
New Industries (Development).
Business of the House.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I venture to
suggest that, with your approval, Mr.
Speaker, it might be for the convenience
of the House to adjourn during pleasure
in the hope that the Housing Bill may
reach us from another place in a very
short time. That would enable us to take
the formal First Reading, and the Bill
would be in the hands of Members over
Christmas.
House adjourned during pleasure.
House resumed.

Ministry of Agriculture:
Agricultural Enquiry Committee.
Interim Report of the Committee appointed by the Minister of Agriculture.
Transport.
Presented (by Command) and ordered
to lie on the Table.
QUESTIONS TO MINISTER.
Town and Country Planning.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Sir Roland
Nugent:
To ask the Leader of the House
whether it is the intention of the Government to establish a planning division of the Ministry of Agriculture as
has been done in Great Britain, and,
if not, what steps the Government propose to take to prevent the alienation
of good agricultural land to other
uses and generally to protect the interests of rural land and rural inhabitants in connection with town and
country planning.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: The question standing in my name on the Order
Paper refers to a matter of some importance. In England, I understand,
they found it necessary in connection
with planning to institute an advisory
body in connection with the Ministry of
Agriculture, which consults the planning
authorities during the period in which
plans are being prepared to prevent unnecessary harm being done to agricultural land and agricultural interests. It
is a matter of very considerable importance, because I think that we tend to
devote our minds too much to the harm
somebody may do to planning.
It is quite possible that the planners
themselves may unwittingly do harm, because very frequently the very land most
suitable for building upon, with easy
access to roads and water and so on is
also the most valuable agricultural land
in the neighbourhood. The experts who
advise the planning authorities are town.
planners; that is to say, they are mostly
gentlemen, architects by profession,
whose minds are devoted mainly to the
building of towns, and, as a general rule,
they would not appreciate the agricultural objections to what they are going
to do. In many cases it will be necessary
to provide a compromise.
I am anxious to ascertain whether the
Government has taken this point into
consideration and whether it is prepared,
so to speak, to put somebody up to fight
the agricultural corner on this matter.
I beg to ask the question standing in my
name.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): As the hon.
and gallant Senator no doubt is aware,
the Planning Board, of which the Vice-
Chancellor of Queen's University is
chairman, has already made considerable
progress with the task allotted to it. In
addition, the Agricultural Enquiry Committee under the presidency of the Right
Hon. Lord Justice Babington is at present engaged in examining the whole
future of the agricultural industry. Until
both these bodies have completed their
labours, it would be premature to make
any statement, but the hon. and gallant
Member may rest assured that the preservation of good land for agricultural
purposes and the general protection of
rural interests and amenities will receive
careful and unremitting attention from
the Ministry of Agriculture and other
interested Departments. I can give the
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
4 JANUARY 1944
further assurance that the Ministry of
Agriculture would, of course, be consulted before any appreciable area of
suitable land is diverted from agricultural purposes.
In
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I rise to ask
a supplementary question. Could the
right hon. Gentleman give me in rather
more detail and rather more definitely
the safeguards which it is proposed to
adopt? In England special expert gentlemen are detailed for this purpose.
this country where agriculture is of
infinitely greater importance we are put
off with the statement that the Ministry
of Agriculture will undoubtedly do something in the more distant future. I am
afraid I cannot accept that answer
final. What I want is some definite plan
by which agricultural interests will be
taken into account when the plan is being
made. That apparently is not being done.
I would ask the right hon. Gentleman
kindly to elaborate the answer more.
as
Rev. Professor CORKEY: I am afraid
the only elaboration I can venture on at
the moment is to say that there is some
point made by the hon. and gallant
Senator in his supplementary question.
But I, unfortunately, am not able to
supply him with the further information
which he wishes. I would suggest he
should put down a further question on
the subject, when I will endeavour to get
him the information which he requires.
Christmas Day Bus Service.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state under what
authority the Northern Ireland Transport Board withdrew public transport
on Christmas Day for religious services,
and thus deprived the Catholic citizens
of Northern Ireland of transport
facilities to attend Mass; whether the
providing of transport for religious
services on Sundays and holidays is just
as important as providing transport for
public functions; why did the Transport Board not provide the Catholics
of Lislea and Milford with transport
as heretofore to Mass at Armagh on
Christmas Day as directed by the
Petroleum Officer; whether this depriving of Catholics of transport to attend
Mass is a contravention of the Government of Ireland Act, 1920; what
number of special buses were provided
from Armagh to Keady on Christmas
Day and for what purpose; why the
ordinary services as on weekdays were
provided from Armagh to Newry;
whether it is a fact that there were
only two passengers on one of these
services; whether the Government will
direct the Transport Board to provide.
public transport for Catholics in rural
districts to attend Mass on Catholic
Church holidays where the numbers
justify such transport provision; and
whether he will place on the Table of
the House any alleged agreement
between the churches and the Transport
Board to withdraw transport for
religious services on Christmas Day and
New Year's Day, and any correspondence connected with this matter.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: In putting this
question I may mention that a few days
before Christmas, Rev. Father Quinn,
administrator of Armagh, 'phoned me to
say he had heard that the Transport
Board were withdrawing the buses from
Lislea and Milford to the Catholic Mass
on Christmas morning. Having heard
that I got into touch immediately by
'phone with the Transport Board authorities, and I was informed that it was due
to the action of the Ministry of Fuel
Petroleum Officer that they were withdrawing these buses. I wrote to the
Traffic Manager immediately and on
Christmas morning I received a reply
from him dated 23rd December. It
reads as follows:
Dear Sir,-Replying to your letter of
the 22nd instant, it is regrettable that
owing to the continued necessity for
economy in the use of petrol and rubber
the Board cannot see its way to operate
any special 'buses to Churches or
Chapels at Christmas time or at the
New Year. This decision has the
approval of the appropriate Government Departments-
I would like very much to know what
Departments they are.
and is applicable to all areas in Northern Ireland.
Referring to Northern Ireland, I should
say that Northern Ireland consists of nine
counties. Three of them are under the
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
jurisdiction of the Eire Government, and
in those three counties they have no reason
whatever to complain of the transport
supplied to religious services.
It should be mentioned that the
ruling herein conveyed does not in any
way affect the usual omnibus services
operated each Sunday to facilitate
attendance at places of worship. It is
presumed you will be good enough to
advise all those interested of this
decision.
The member of the Board had not the
courage to advertise the withdrawal of
this 'bus. I wrote in reply. I may say
that not having received an answer from
the traffic superintendent on Christmas
Eve I phoned the Petroleum Officer and
explained the position, and also explained
that heretofore these Catholics had been
supplied with an omnibus to attend Mass
on Christmas Day. When the Petroleum
Officer heard the position explained he
very kindly agreed to direct the Transport
Board to put on the usual service
Sundays for the Catholics of Lislea and
Milford districts and the Transport Board
refused. I immediately replied to the
traffic superintendent, who got my
letter at Christmas, as follows:-
as
"Yours of the 23rd inst. was received
by me yesterday (Christmas) morning.
I must say I was surprised at contents
of same. On 24th inst. I 'phoned the
Petrol Officer, Ministry of Fuel, and
after my representations he agreed to
issue directions to your Board to provide
public transport services to the Catholics.
of Lislea, Milford and districts on
Christmas Day same as services supplied
on Sunday. As a public representative
I now demand an explanation of the
refusal of your Board to carry out these
directions, and under what authority
your Board deprived these people of
public transport to convey them to
Mass on Christmas morning. Does your
Board not consider the providing of
public transport for religious services
just as important-in my opinion it is
more important-as providing transport
for public functions, markets, fairs,
cinemas, dances, etc. I note your Board
also intends to withdraw transport for
religious services at the New Year.
Again, I ask for your authority to
deprive Catholics of the Six Counties
once
rural districts of the transport to convey them to Mass at the New Year
and all Catholic holidays of obligation.
I demand a decision and
a direct
answer to this question. Should your
board persist in their refusal to provide
such services, I will see that action is at
taken by the Catholic people
under the jurisdiction of the Six County
Government to protect their rights
under the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, and that your board, appointed
by the Government of the Six Counties,
shall provide all transport required to
convey our Catholic people in rural
districts to Mass on Sundays and all
Catholic holidays of obligation.
I also understand that your board
when asking for the approval of the
petrol officer, Ministry of Fuel, stated
that "the withdrawal of transport for
religious services was agreed to by all
the Churches." This is not correct,
and I would point out most emphatically that it had not the approval of
his Eminence Cardinal MacRory or
the bishops, nor were they consulted.
You presume I will be good enough to
advise all interested of your board's
decision. I will do no such thing. I
will do everything I can to see that
Catholics are provided by your board
with transport to convey them to Mass
on all Catholic holidays of obligation.
In conclusion, I suggest that you pass
this on at once to the chairman of
your board, Mr. Clark.
In reply the assistant manager, Mr.
Macve, took the matter up. I suppose
he thought the superintendent was not
strong enough for me and that he would
tackle me himself. His letter, dated 28th
December, stated:
I have received your letter of 26th
instant, addressed to the traffic superintendent. I wish to inform that
you
while the petroleum officer can issue
petrol to permit us to operate services,
he has no jurisdiction over this board
which would force it to operate any
particular service.
The policy in regard to church services on Christmas Day this year was
the same as that adopted in 1942, and
conformed with the arrangements
made throughout the British Isles that
no extra services would be made available. By some mischance last year
buses were provided at Milford and
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
4 JANUARY 1914
Lislea for the conveyance of those who
wished to attend a midnight Mass, but
by no means could this be considered a
precedent to operate an omnibus service to a particular place for 8 o'clock
on Christmas morning. No services
were, in fact, run for any denomination on Christmas Day and, therefore,
the Catholics in Lislea and Milford
districts were treated in exactly the
same way as everyone else throughout
the Province.
The Transport Board is one of the
boards that I have complained of. It is
what I might call a non-Catholic board,
as it consists of seven members without
any Catholic representation whatever. It
is one of the most important boards appointed by the Government, yet there
is not one representative on that board of
the 35 per cent. minority of the population. What are the facts in regard to
this Transport Board? In 1938 when a
new chapel was opened in Irish Street,
Armagh, for the district I am referring
to and for that end of the town, we asked
the Transport Board to supply buses for
the people to come in to 8 o'clock Mass
on Sunday mornings and on holidays.
At first they refused. They put forward
every argument possible and eventually
the last leg they had to stand on was that
the service would not
immediately said that it would pay and
guaranteed that it would pay. I told
them that if the service did not pay they
could take it off.
pay.
We
The fact is that that bus service taking
the people from Milford and Lislea to
Armagh Chapel is the best paying service.
that the Transport Board have. So well
do they pay that on Sunday morning last.
the buses were packed to their utmost
capacity. If the Transport Board had
any consideration for the Catholic people
of that district they would put on enough
buses to serve the people who require
them, but instead of that the people are
packed into the buses, both sitting and
standing. I say that that shows the consideration of the Transport Board for the
people attending Mass.
Now, in regard to this question of
supplying extra buses on Christmas Day,
I ask a question with regard to the Keady
and Armagh buses. I find that in addition to the Sunday service to Keady-I
defy contradiction of what I am going to
say the Transport Board ran a special
bus from Keady at 4 o'clock in the afternoon for the pictures. Better go to the
pictures than to Mass. In addition to
the ordinary bus from Armagh to Keady
at 8-25, that is the ordinary bus on
Sunday evenings, they ran a special bus
from the cinema at 10 o'clock for civilians
and another special bus at 10-30 for the
military. So much for the saving of
petrol and rubber. It is all right to save
them and keep people from attending
Mass, but do not save them as far as
amusement is concerned. Let the people
go to the pictures or wherever they
like.
I come now to the Christmas Day
service from Armagh to Newry. You
would have thought they would have run
the ordinary Sunday service on Christmas
Day from Armagh to Newry; but, no, it
must be remembered that they are in
competition with the Great Northern
Railway. What are the facts in regard
to that service? Instead of running the
Sunday service they ran the ordinary
weekday service, and one of the buses
actually had only two passengers on it.
Again, so much for the saving of rubber.
I do not wish to detain the House too
long with this question, but I do say,
and I think the Leader of the House will
agree with me, that it is just as
important, if not more
not more important, to
encourage people to attend religious
services as it is to encourage them to
attend public functions, because if the
people keep to their religion they will be
good citizens. From that point of view
I suggest to the Leader of the House
that every facility as far as possible
should be given on Catholic holidays of
obligation to the people to attend Mass.
It must be remembered that on all
holidays of obligation Catholics are
bound, as far as they possibly can, to
attend Mass.
With regard to the agreement that has
been alleged between the churches, I have
already stated that neither Cardinal
MacRory nor any of the bishops were
consulted. There was no agreement. I
have no hesitation in throwing the lie
back to the Transport Board. They went
to Government Boards as well as to the
Ministry of Fuel and told them that there
was an agreement on the question with
the different churches. I say there was no
agreement. I stand by that, and I
challenge contradiction.
Questions to Minister
Questions to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. McLaughlin]
Take the question of discrimination and
what occurs in England and Scotland. I
think it will be agreed from the point of
view of religion that Ireland is not to be
compared with England, Scotland, or
Wales. Thank goodness, we have a bit
of religion left here; let us keep it as
long as we possibly can. I will go further
and say that as far as buses for religious
services on Sundays and holidays are concerned those services are used, and the
users of them are 98 per cent. Catholic.
I say that emphatically. If the Government of Northern Ireland wish to have
the minority looked after as far as their
religious views are concerned they should
insist that the Transport Board provide
proper facilities for the people to attend
Mass on Sundays and holidays. It will
be agreed that the Transport Board has
gone just a little bit too far in regard to
this question, and I would suggest that
they should not be allowed to trample on
the liberties of the people
the people too much,
because, after all is said and done, they
are a public body appointed by the
Government and their capital is the
money of the people of the Six Counties.
It is, therefore, up to them to provide
proper transport for all religious services.
as
Now, the Leader of the House is both
a minister of religion and a Minister of
the Cabinet, and as a minister of religion
I am sure he will consider it his duty to
see that an adequate transport service is
provided on Sundays and Church holidays.
for religious services, and I am also sure
that he will agree that attendance at
Church services is as important
attendance at public functions. Then, as
a Minister of the Cabinet, I am sure he
wishes to see that all sections of the
community get fair play and equal treatment. If that is to be so, then Catholics
must be provided with adequate transport to convey them to Mass, not only
on Sundays but also on Church holidays.
In conclusion, the Transport Board should
deal fairly with the public. If people
are deprived of going to Mass on Christmas morning for want of transport, and
transport is supplied on Christmas evening to take people to cinemas and bring
them home again, I say
there is discrimination. I beg leave to ask the
question standing in my name.
Rev. Professor CORKEY: The hon.
Senator opposite has asked a longish
question, and he wishes an explanation,
and I think that when he hears the explanation he will agree that the situation
is not as bad as he has assumed it to be.
He has put his case well and barristerlike, but it often happens that a case
sounds very strong until the other side
has been heard. First of all, in regard
to the authority under which bus services
are augmented or restricted, I desire to
point out that the Ministry of Home
Affairs has no power to direct the Transport Board as to what services they shall
run. The Board is in the first instance
the sole judge of what services are reasonable and necessary, but it is then open
to any persons who are dissatisfied to
appeal to the Ministry, and where such
appeals are received, the Ministry has
certain powers.
In practice, however, the Board generally consults the Ministry before making
any material alteration of bus services,
and on
the 7th December, 1943, the
Board asked for the Ministry's views on
the question of road transport facilities
for special religious services at Christmas
and the New Year, and were informed
that the supply position did not warrant
any improvement on the facilities which
were afforded in 1942. The Board
announced its Christmas Day services in
due. course, and no appeals were received
by the Ministry. The hon. Senator, however took up with the Board the question
of transport between Lislea, Milford and
Armagh on Christmas morning. I understand that the Board informed him that
no exception could be made to the general
policy of running no special Christmas
Day services. The hon. Senator then got
into communication with the Petroleum
Officer and informed him that a service
which had been run on Christmas Day,
1942, was going to be. withdrawn on
Christmas Day, 1943, owing to the petrol
restrictions. The Petroleum Officer informed the hon. Senator that so far as
he was concerned, he would allow petrol
in 1943 for any service that was run in
1942. The Board, however, considered
that as the three buses which were on this
service in Christmas, 1942, had been put
on in error, a similar arrangement could
not properly be made in 1943. The
Petroleum Officer, at the time he spoke
to the hon. Senator, was not aware that
the service had been put on in error in
1942.
Planning Bill—
Committee
4 JANUARY 1944
In reply to the other parts of the hon.
Senator's question, I am informed that
no special buses were provided on Christmas Day on the Armagh-Keady route.
With regard to the Armagh-Newry
route, five runs each way were provided,
but these served three different routes. It
is possible that at some given point on
one of these routes there were only two
passengers in the bus, but it is impossible.
to check whether this was, in fact, the
case or not. It is, however, certain that
on all these routes more traffic was offered
than could be carried by the buses serving
them.
No agreement was made, I am told, between the Churches and the Transport
Board with regard to transport for
religious services on Christmas Day and
New Year's Day.
That is the answer or rather the explanation to the hon. Senator's inquiries,
and I may supplement it by saying that
we in Northern Ireland are in a better
position as far as Sunday services are
concerned than are the people across the
water, where in most areas the bus
services on Sundays do not begin before
one o'clock p.m. There are, therefore,
no travelling facilities by bus for morning church services for denominations.
any
As to the general principle to which the
hon. Senator made appeal, of course I
may say that I am in hearty agreement
with it, but we have got to remember that
both petrol and rubber are scarce owing
to war reasons.

CLAUSE 1 (Application of planning
resolutions to land not subject to planning
schemes or resolutions).
*

*
Provided that the Ministry may, by
order made at any time before expiration of the said period, direct that this
section shall have effect in relation to

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in page
2, after line 5 ("as may be so specified'),
to insert:
"(b) without prejudice to the generality of anything contained in the
principal Act, and notwithstanding
anything contained in this Act, this
section shall, on the expiration of two
years after the commencement of this
Act, cease to have effect in respect of
any ward or district electoral division
wherein a planning scheme is not in,
or has not come into, operation within
the said period of two years."
This Amendment is, of course, an
amendment to the principal Clause in the
Bill. As the law stands to-day any local
authority as defined in the principal Act,
that is a county borough, urban district
council or county council, has powers to
make planning schemes. They have had
those powers since 1931-a period of 13
years. Speaking generally, I may say
they have not made much use of those
powers. The proposal now is to bring
in a new Bill under which any land,
whether a scheme has been propounded
for it or not, is deemed to be subject to
a resolution for a planning scheme.
According to the Bill the waiting period
is to be indefinite. It seems to me that
that is an impossible position. When a
man is restricted in the use of his land,
be the portion great or small, in the
public interest as it is alleged the Bill
is in the public interest-when that restriction is imposed it is reasonable to
Planning Bill-
Committee
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
expect that the period of restraint shall
be as strictly limited as possible, that is
to say the local authority should not be
given any further opportunity of simply
postponing action unless, of course, the
case is that they do not desire to take
action, which may be the explanation of
a great deal of the non-carrying out of
schemes in the past.
As the Bill stands before us there is no
limit whatever on the period which may
elapse between the coming into operation
of the Bill and the preparation of the
scheme. I need not go into detail on that.
It is referred to in sub-clauses (3), (4),
(5) and (6) of Clause 2 and in Clause 4.
Under sub-clause (6) of Clause 2 the
question of costs becomes important, but
I need not lay stress on that. I cannot
help thinking that an expert might have
shortened the wording of my Amendment. What I am endeavouring to do
by the Amendment is to provide that if
a local authority do not bring in a scheme
under the new Bill within two years then
they must proceed under the powers contained in the principal Act. I think
that this limitation on the period of restraint is really necessary.
The MINISTER OF HOME AFFAIRS
(Mr. Lowry): I greatly regret it is
impossible for the Government to accept
the Amendment proposed by the right
hon. and learned Senator and when he
has heard my comments on the matter I
think he will see that his fears are
groundless. Provision is made in the
Planning Schemes Procedure Regulations
made under the principal Act requiring
local authorities to proceed with the
various stages in the preparation of
schemes within the prescribed periods.
unless the Ministry agrees to an extension of these schemes.. It is certainly
not the policy of the Government to agree
to such extension as would extend indefinitely the interim development period.
I am sure the right hon. and learned
Senator is familiar with the various
stages set forth in the Regulations. I
entirely agree with his observations as to
the little that was done since 1931 in
respect to the operation of the principal.
Act, but in fairness to the local authorities it must be borne in mind that if one
searches that Act very carefully one will
find there is a curious absence of anything
dealing with financial aid or assistance
on this matter.
It is not contemplated that that very
cogent reason will remain for the future,
and I am very glad to say that many of
our local authorities, without any pressure at all from the Ministry, have set
about procuring skilled guidance on this
matter of planning, and I hope that the
good example set by at least a dozen of local
authorities will be followed generally over
the country. The right hon. and learned
Senator alluded to the number of planning
authorities, mentioned in Section 12 of
the original Act-the two county
boroughs, the six county councils, all the
urban district councils and the rural
authorities. This is a matter in which, I
hope, we can count on the support of the
county councils. In any case, they can
delegate their powers to the rural
councils, and I hope that will be done
fairly generally.
There will, of course, be great difficulty
in obtaining an adequate supply of skilled
men. I am
sure the Committee are
familiar with what is happening in Scotland, where schemes have been undertaken, notably in the Clyde Valley and
the south-western and south-eastern
parts. Those schemes will create a demand for skilled people. I think there
are signs of an awakening to the necessity
of this matter, and I do not think it is
necessary to apply something in the
nature of indirect coercion on the local
authorities at the present juncture. The
Government, in seeing that the time limit
set forth in the principal Act is adhered
to as closely as possible, will prevent that
delay that the right hon. and learned
Senator fears. The Government consider it absolutely essential that plans,
particularly in the rural areas, should be
completed at the earliest possible moment,
in order that the rural authorities may
be in a position to go ahead with housing
the moment the materials are available.
It was suggested, in the course of the
Second Reading, that planning would
hold up the provision of houses, but, in
fact, the contrary is the case.. With costs
at the level they are going to be at for
some time, it will clearly be impossible to
work on the principle of bringing water
and other services to houses, and it will
be necessary to bring houses to where the
services are. This means that before any
large-scale housing can be undertaken,
the line and lay-out of services must be
arranged, and there is no way in which
Planning Bill-
Committee
4 JANUARY 1944
this can be done except by methodical
planning.
Further objection to requiring schemes
to be completed within a hard and fast
period is that the period which would be
reasonable for one area might, for obvious
reasons, be quite inappropriate for
another.
In
The Amendment would have the effect,
in the absence of a fresh resolution, of
decontrolling development in any area in
which a scheme had not been brought into
actual operation within a period of two
years from the commencement of the Act,
that is within twenty-one months from
the coming into operation of a resolution
under paragraph (1) of Clause 1.
many cases this period would be quite
inadequate, and much of the value to.
the community of the operation of Clause
1 would be lost if the resumption of unrestricted development were to be
permitted before a scheme could be
brought into operation. Moreover, any
expenditure incurred by the planning
authority during the interim period would
be rendered nugatory. In any event, the
effect of the Amendment might be to
encourage local authorities to prepare
plans with undue haste, a procedure
which might result in the acceptance of
ill-considered proposals and the imposition
or attempted imposition of undesirable.
restrictions. Such plans, when submitted
to the Ministry, would require considerable modification and the result would be
to cause much uncertainty in the minds
of property owners.
I can, however, give an assurance that
the Government have in mind the difficulty to which the hon. and learned
Senator has drawn attention, and will
ensure, in so far as it can, that there is
no unnecessary delay on the part of local
authorities in the preparation of plans.
In other words, in so far as it is possible
and is reasonable, close adherence to the
time-table set forth in the Schedule to
the principal Act and the regulations
made thereunder will be enforced. It is
only reasonable that in the case of a local
authority which is doing its best power
should not be taken out of the hands of
the Ministry to act with consideration.
Although past experience of the Act does
not, perhaps, encourage one to think that
there will be a general setting about
planning, I have every hope that it will
be so. I am sure the excellent example
set by some local authorities will become
general, and that we can rely on the
time-table set for in the principal Act.

Mr. LOWRY: The hon. and gallant
Senator, who has just spoken, forgets that
the 1931 Act was a purely voluntary Act.
If a time limit of two years is put on this
Bill at the end of that time the effect of
sub-clause (1) of the Bill would cease to
operate, and you are back again to the
same position as you are in to-day, namely
the operation of the Act will be purely
voluntary. The effect of sub-clause (1) of
this Bill is that every piece of land becomes the subject of a planning resolution.
Thereupon every local authority is in the
same position as one that has passed a
planning resolution, because it is only
after the passing of a planning resolution.
that the further powers under Regulation
4 and Regulation 9 come into operation.
From the moment that this Bill becomes
law the six months will begin to run, and
within that time every local authority
must set about preparing their preliminary statement.,
The various local authorities will
emerge at different times through the
operation of this Bill, and we will know
those which are active and those whose
problems are comparatively simple.
Doubtless the Bill will tell us at an early
stage the local authorities who through
the complexity and difficulty of their
problems may need longer time. One
would see much more clearly at the end
of six months after this Bill has been
operating as to whether or not there is
going to be a good hearty working of
the Bill. At the end of that time I
venture to say that the fears uttered here
to-day by Members of the Committee will
prove to be groundless. In the first place,
to put on a time limit would destroy the
whole usefulness of sub-clause (1);
secondly, it will be hampering the welldisposed, active local authorities; and,
thirdly, it would put an undue burden
upon them, and it might really lead to
perhaps hasty and ill-considered decisions. The effect of sub-clause (1),
coupled with the effect of the Regulations, to my mind, is a sufficient safeguard against the fears that have been
voiced here this afternoon by hon.
Senators.

citizens of Northern Ireland who happen
to have land, in the air, so preventing
them from having any form of control
until such time as something happens. I
think that this is an Amendment that
must go on. If this Amendment goes
through, and if the Government propose
on the Report Stage to bring in a saving
Amendment giving them the chance of
giving extra time in special cases, I do
not think anyone will object. That would
be a side-show directed to particular circumstances. I would not object to that
at all, but what I do object to is citizens
being held up for an indefinite period in
order that schemes which they may not
like may be forced upon them. I do not
feel I ought to withdraw the Amendment.
Question, "That those words be there
inserted," put.

Mr. LOWRY: I regret that the

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in Clause
2, to leave out sub-clause (9). This is a
question of what is called dumps. Like
most other landowners, I strongly object
to them, especially the unofficial dump
which comes over your wall or railings
without consent. But the question is
whether it is justifiable to pass this subclause, with due regard to the convenience
of the citizens of Northern Ireland, and
I have some regard for the convenience of
the general public. I have much more
regard for that than I have for administrative convenience, or for the fact that
the Home Office is able to get through
some proposal of its own merely because
it is its own. I know a similar subclause is imported from the English Act,
having regard to the. big dumps in the
mining villages in the Midlands which
are extremely unsightly, but there is
nothing of that sort in any quantity in
Northern Ireland. There are a certain
number of dumps, but not very many.
Even if there are any, if they are existing
now you must not superficially add to
them under this sub-clause. You may
go on adding to the height of them, but
the sort of stuff you put on them has a
habit of falling down, so that in the end
it would extend superficially.
If you have any minerals worth anything and are going to open a new mine,
Planning Bill—
Committee
4 JANUARY 1944
what is the owner going to do? He cannot put the refuse back into the mine.
Does the Minister propose that he should
waste his mine because he cannot mine
his waste? If you look at Clause 9, there
a man can apparently put up a saw mill
in his back garden, because that would
be for the purpose of a plantation or a
wood, and he might require to have a
sawmill, but what is he to do with the
saw dust? Then again, there is the case
of a man who cleans out a ditch and casts
up the refuse on the side. All these
things become dumps. There are, I believe, houses in Belfast which are founded
on dumps. Again, in the country what
are you to do with your refuse? Generally in the country you have not got
dustmen or an incinerator, although you
can perhaps erect an incinerator.
think that, on the whole, this sub-clause
will do us more harm than good, and,
therefore, I move that it be left out. I
do not think it is going to help us.
I
Mr. LOWRY: I regret that the Government cannot accept this Amendment.
The Government's view is that control
should be exercised over dumping
grounds for waste materials and refuseparticularly in
in developed areas and
that power should be available to prevent
the extension of existing dumps. I can
assure the right hon. and learned Senator
that it was not mining we had particularly in view when we framed this subclause. What we had particularly in
mind, because we are constantly reminded
of it, was the fact that these dumps are
not merely of an unsightly character, but
invariably they are breeding places for
rats and all sorts of pests.
Apart from certain proceedings under
the Public Health Act, in general,
especially when a local authority is the
offender-as not infrequently happensnothing short of a full Chancery action
has any real effect in abating this nuisance. For these reasons I would ask
the right hon. and learned Gentleman
not to press his Amendment.
If coal or
any other valuable mineral is discovered
I am perfectly certain that this House
will be very ready to facilitate the person
concerned, but, in the meantime, we are
death on the dumps.

sort of thing will not be prevented.

CLAUSES 4 (Power to enforce interim
Development Control), and 5 (Powers of

CLAUSE 6 (Compensation and contributions in certain cases).
(1) Where any application made
under an interim development order,
being an application for permission to
complete or carry out a building or
work begun or contracted for before
the date on which the resolution to
prepare or adopt a planning scheme
took effect, is refused or is granted
subject to conditions, then, without
prejudice to the provisions of subsection (5) of this section, if
person has
(a) where the resolution was in force
at the date of the commencement
of this Act, before that date;
any
(b) where the resolution took effect
after that date, before the resolution took effect,
incurred expenditure in carrying out
any work which is rendered abortive
by the refusal or conditions, or entered
into a contract for the purpose of any
work which is abandoned by reason of
the refusal or conditions, he shall be
entitled to recover from the local
authority by way of compensation an
amount equal to the expenditure so
incurred or, as the case may be, to
any sums reasonably paid by him in
discharge of any liability arising under
the contract in respect of the abandonment of that work.
(2) Where any permission for the
development of land granted under an
interim development order or order
made in pursuance of section four of
the principal Act is revoked or modified
by an order under section three of this
Act, then, without prejudice to the
provisions of sub-section (6) of this
section, if any person has, before the
date of the last-mentioned order, incurred expenditure in carrying out any
work which is rendered abortive by the
order, or entered into a contract for
the purpose of any work which is
abandoned by reason of the order, he
shall be entitled to recover from the
local authority by way of compensation
an amount equal to the expenditure
so incurred or, as the case may be, to
any sums reasonably paid by him in
discharge of any liability arising under
the contract in respect of the abandonment of that work.
(3) For the purposes of the foregoing provisions of this section any expenditure incurred in the preparation
of plans for the purpose of any work,
or upon other similar matters preparatory thereto, shall be deemed to
be included in the expenditure incurred in carrying out that work, but
except as aforesaid no compensation
shall be paid under sub-section (2) of
this section in respect of any work
carried out before the date on which
the permission was granted, or in respect of any liability arising under a
contract made before that date.
(4) Any claim for compensation under
the foregoing provisions of this section.
shall be made within twelve months
after the date of the final determination
Planning Bill—
Committee
THE SENATE
of the interim development application
or, as the case may be, the date of the
order revoking or modifying the permission.
(5) In any case where-
(a) an interim development application
is refused or is granted subject to
conditions; or
(b) a permission for the development
of land is revoked or modified by
an order under section three of this
Act;
then, without prejudice to the provisions of sub-section (6) of this section,
the applicant or the person to whom the
permission was granted (as the case may
be) shall, if he makes a claim for the
purpose within three months after the
decision of the interim development
authority, be entitled to obtain from
the local authority such compensation
in respect of any injurious affection of
his property by reason of the aforesaid
decision as he would be entitled to receive under the principal Act if such
injurious affection had been suffered as
a consequence of the coming into
operation of the planning scheme; and
any question as to whether a person is
entitled to such compensation as aforesaid, or as to the amount of any such
compensation, shall be determined in
like manner as in a case where a person
makes a claim under sub-section (1) of
section nine of the principal Act, and
the provisions of sub-section (4) of that
section and section forty-six of the
principal Act shall apply accordingly.
(6) In any case where-
(a) an interim development application.
is refused or is granted subject to
conditions; or
(b) a permission for the development of
land is revoked or modified by an
order under section three of this
Act;
and the applicant or the person to
whom the permission was granted (as
the case may be) shows to the satisfaction of the local authority that he is
likely to suffer any damage or expense
by reason of the decision of the interim
development authority, then the local
authority may, if they think fit, make
a contribution, the payment of which
shall not be unreasonably delayed,
towards the said damage or expense.

6 JANUARY 1944
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, on page 11, line 19, to leave out
from the word "and" to the word
"expense" inclusive, in line 26, and
insert:
"any person who suffers any damage or
expense by reason of the decision of
the interim development authority
shall be entitled to obtain compensation
from the local authority, and the
amount of that compensation shall be
assessed, after hearing the said person
and the local authority, by an independent person to be appointed by the
Ministry for the purpose. In assessing
the amount of such compensation no
account shall be taken of any profit
which it might be anticipated would
have accrued to the applicant or to the
person to whom the permission was
granted from the development if the
interim development authority had not.
acted as set out in (a) or (b) of this
sub-section, but the local authority
may, if they think fit, make a contribution towards any loss of such profit."
The compensation Clause in this Bill
is going to be very difficult for a layman.
like myself to work out, but, as far as
I understand it, while there is ample
provision for actually ascertained losses,
there is no provision for what I may call
consequential loss. Consequential loss
seems to me to be divided into two classes.
One is loss of property, which I think in
the circumstances of this Bill the owner
would probably not wish to have compensated. But there are cases of
consequential loss of the following types.
We will say someone has started the
development of a property, and in the
course of doing so has pulled down
various buildings which have a certain
value with the intention of erecting other
more valuable buildings in their place.
At the point when he has pulled the
buildings down and started to rebuild
the interim development authority
intervenes, and he is left minus his
buildings, which had some value, and is
not allowed to erect buildings. In that
case he may suffer appreciable loss. As
I understand it, he could not be compensated for that except purely at the
option of the local authority. I think in
cases of that kind it should be mandatory. My Amendment provides that
an independent person be appointed
valuer to assess the compensation.
Another case of loss which might arise
would be where an owner of agricultural
land had begun to prepare it for building, that is to say, removed a considerable
quantity of the top soil for the purpose
of making roads and drains and so on.
He may
have completely ruined that
land. Again he is stopped by the
interim
and
development authority,
there he may be left with what may
amount to an annual loss of £50 or £60
an acre. There again, as I read the Bill,
he can obtain compensation only by grace.
from the local authority. It is that sort
of cases I seek to cover by this Amendment. In order that I shall not at the
same time cover loss of prospective profits
I have added the tail end of the Amendment. I am afraid it is clumsily worded,
as I am not a skilled draughtsman.
I see no objection to my Amendment
except this. It is just possible the local
authority, having first of all decided on
development, might proceed to change
its mind and so not proceed. In that case
claims for compensation could arise and
compensation be paid, and when the local
authority changed its mind again the
owner may have been compensated for
loss he did not eventually suffer. I may
point out to the Committee that continual change.of mind on the part of the
local authority would argue that at least
one of its decisions had been taken hastily.
We do not want in a matter of this kind
the local authority to make a sort of snap
judgment and say, "We can make it all
right afterwards." We want the local
authority to do this with a certain
amount of care and caution, and if they
do it with care and caution I do not
think there will be repeated reversals of
their judgment. I think I have covered
everything I wish to say with regard to
this Amendment.
Mr. LOWRY: I regret that the Government cannot accept this Amendment.
My right hon. Friend, the Leader of the
House, in dealing with this Clause in
his Second Reading speech emphasised
the importance of the distinction between
damage or loss that had been suffered and
prospective damage. or loss which a
person was likely to suffer. The Bill, as
it stands, gives a right to compensation
in respect of injury which has been
suffered, but as regards prospective
injury or damage it empowers the interim
developing authorities to make contributions.
Planning Bill—
Committee
THE SENATE
a
[Mr. Lowry]
might be open to abuse, that the local
authorities might take it into their heads
to be very liberal with money in this
respect. I do not think that that is the
common experience, and there are always
plenty of people to sound the alarm if
any such measure were contemplated.
For those reasons I regret that the Government cannot accept the Amendment.
In so far as the proposed Amendment
relates to damage which has been suffered
it is the view of the Government that
adequate provision for the payment of
compensation is made in sub-clause (5)
of this Clause. The two examples which
the hon. and gallant Member gave are
both covered, as I shall show in a
moment. He instanced the case of a
man pulling down a building. That is
that
case of what is known as injurious
affection, and the hon. and gallant
Senator will know that under sub-clause
(5), at line 46 or thereabouts, he is
entitled to obtain from the local authority
compensation in respect of any injurious
affection of his property. Another instance that was suggested at an earlier
stage of the Bill was the case of a man
who had gone the length of laying down
a concrete road over a piece of land, and
perhaps destroying or seriously injuring
the land for agricultural purposes.
Just like the building that has been
pulled down that, in my opinion as a
lawyer, is equally a case of injurious
affection. It is the same as if the man
had started to make radical changes in
his land by laying down a concrete road.
An endeavour has been made to cover
every sort of damage that one can reasonably anticipate. It is clear that where a
man's scheme has been interrupted and
he has ended a contract for carrying
something out compensation is payable as
a right. It is payable as a right where
there is injurious affection, an instance
of which the hon. and gallant Senator
gave.
Now, there may be other things. It
is very hard to anticipate where it will
be only right and reasonable that some
sort of compensation should be made,
but it is very hard to visualise every case,
and I think that Members of this Committee who have experience of local
boards will know that very often a case
turns up when good neighbourliness alone
will demand that something ought to be
given to a man for some interference with
his property. It is very hard to anticipate every sort of case that will arise,
and I think that the discretionary power
given here to a local authority will prove
of value. Real accrued damage can be
measured under the provisions of this
Bill and of the principal Act, and I
think that owners are fully protected.
Of course, it has been suggested that this

I am not in the least questioning his

not be decided by him, and I should like

It is really a case of the use of very
wide terms. "Injurious affection'' may
mean a very great deal, or it may mean
very little, and I am not at all sure
that the construction which the right hon.
Gentleman has put on those words is the

It is like the case of the
every case.
lady who was told by a Government

Sir Roland Nugent has put forward and
others which may be suggested to the
right hon. Gentleman by those who have
practical experience of building.
I am not at all sure how a person who
has contracted to build a house and has
not yet completed the contract would be
affected by this Measure, but I am very
much afraid that if we are not careful
some of the provisions of the Act will
make people very chary of taking land for
building in parcels at some future date.
If a man is going to develop all the land
at once, I think he can fairly say that
he is protected by the Bill. I am not at
all sure if he is going to develop the land
over a number of years and build one
house here and another there he is fully
protected. I should like that to be made
perfectly clear, because I think it would
be an absolute tragedy if after the war
were unable to build houses with
reasonable speed.
we
England is going to build an enormous
number of houses after this war, and in
rural areas we must have houses, and decent houses, for agricultural labourers
and young farmers to live in. I am afraid
that most of these Bills have a bearing
entirely on Belfast, and the more I hear
of the remarks in connection with these
Bills the more convinced I am that they
are Belfast Bills, and that the other areas
are not really being given proper consideration when the Bills are being
drafted. I admit there is a very important building problem in Belfast, but
it is not one that I know much about,
and so I am not going to refer to it. I
do, however, understand the building
problem in the country districts. There
are cases of farmers not being able to
marry because the whole family, mother,
sisters, and brothers, have to live in one
house because there are not enough houses
in the rural areas.
The right hon. Gentleman the other
day said that you cannot build houses
until you can bring water to the site.
That is a complete and utter fallacy in
country districts. If we are going to wait
until we can bring water into every
country district we shall certainly not
have houses for years and years after this
war, and if the Government do anything
now which will prevent or make difficult
the building of houses in rural areas they
are storing up for themselves trouble
which they will deserve. I would ask the
right hon. Gentleman to be very careful
before he forces this Bill in its present
form through the House, unless he makes
it quite clear that the words "injurious
affection" are going to affect everyone who
is injured, and to the full extent of his
injury.

In England their minds are full of town

the land is owned by relatively few

vast number of people, mostly poor. I

Mr. LOWRY: The hon. and learned
Member who spoke before the last speaker
was in some doubts as to the phrase
"injurious affection' and the last speaker
was right in saying he was not taking my
opinion. It is not my opinion I am
going on solely.
The expression "injurious affection" has been common since
1845 and has been the subject of many
judicial decisions. It has well defined
and well-known meanings, and I have no
hesitation in saying by reason of these
decisions that the very instances the hon.
Senator mentioned are covered by the
expression "injurious affection."
With regard to the matter raised by
the hon. and learned Senator as to contracts having to be abandoned that is
covered in sub-clause (2); which states:
if any person has, before the
date of the last-mentioned order, incurred expenditure in carrying out any
work which is rendered abortive by
the order, or entered into a contract
for the purpose of any work which is
abandoned by reason of the order, he
shall be entitled to recover from the
local authority by way of compensation
an amount equal to the expenditure
so incurred or, as the case may be, to
any sums reasonably paid by him in
discharge of any liability arising under
the contract in respect of the abandonment of that work.
There is only one other point raised
by the right hon. and learned Senator
who instanced the case of works that had
been commenced even before the war.
That is covered by sub-clause (1) of the
Bill. Thus matters raised by both
Members are all covered. I do admit
that this was an extremely difficult Bill
to draft, but the hon. Member need not
have apologised for any inexpertness in
draughtsmanship. I think, however, one
thing is clear; that is, that the hon.
Member's Amendment would be merely
repeating in somewhat different terms
the other provisions that are to cover
the matters he has in mind.

the ratepayers.

CLAUSE 9 (Interpretation).
(1) In this Act the following expressions have the meanings hereby assigned
to them, that is to say:
"Development," where used in relation to land, includes any building
operations or rebuilding operations,
and any use of the land or any building thereon for a purpose which is
different from the purpose for which
the land or building was last being
used:
*
Provided thatwhether
(i) the use of land for the purpose of
as arable
agriculture,
meadow, pasture ground or orchard,
or for the purposes of a plantation
or a wood, or for the growth of
saleable underwood, and the erection or use for any of those purposes
of any building which may be or is
occupied together with land so
used, shall not be deemed to be a
development of that land or building; and
(ii) the use of land within the curtilage
of a dwelling-house for any fresh
purpose other than building operations shall not be deemed to be a
development of that land if the
purpose is incidental to the enjoyment of the dwelling-house as such;
"Interim development" means development between the date on which
the resolution to prepare or adopt a
planning scheme took effect and the
date of the coming into operation of
the scheme;
"Interim development application"
means an application made under an
interim development order for permission to develop land;
"Local authority" means the local
authority for the purposes of the
principal Act;
"Planning Commission" means the
persons named in the Warrant signed
by the Minister of Home Affairs on
the seventeenth day of August, nineteen hundred and forty-two, and
thereby appointed to be a Commission to prepare planning proposals
and make recommendations, and such
other persons as may from time to
time be appointed by the said Minister as members of that Commission;
