Mr. LESLIE: The object of this new
Clause is to protect the interests of any
man who may be abroad serving his
country as regards disability arising from
his absence, and also to protect him as
regards disability affecting the land or
premises. So far as I can make out in
the principal Act there is no protection.
No man who is abroad on active service
should be in any way embarrassed by any
attack made on his property in his
absence. That I think is the main point.
There is a secondary point. A good many
men have land and premises which, at
the moment, are sequestered, and as to
which they cannot exercise the ordinary
rights of property. I suggest that we
ought to protect such men, and I think
the words I have on the Order Paper
will be sufficient for that purpose. I beg
to move, "That the Clause be now read a
Second time.'
""
Mr. LOWRY: I regret the Government cannot accept this Amendment.
One effect of its adoption would be to
cause considerable uncertainty in the
minds of planning authorities since they
would, for example, be unaware of the
extent to which interest in property
covered by the proposed scheme were held
by persons absent from the British
Islands on account of the war. As such
authorities would, therefore, be faced
with the prospect that long after schemes
had been prepared and approved they
might be faced with claims which they
had never anticipated, it cannot be
asserted that this state of affairs would
be conducive to the encouragement of
local authorities to proceed expeditiously
with the preparation and submission of
schemes one of the purposes which this
Bill is designed to effect. That is one of
the purposes, and, indeed, a purpose
which the right hon. and learned Senator
has very much at heart. On Tuesday last
expedition was his war cry, if I might
respectfully say so.
the
The Amendment would have the effect
of rendering extremely difficult
operation of Clause 4-it relates to the
enforcement of interim development
orders if local authorities are to be prevented from taking action under the
Clause pending any possible appeal under
the provision which it is now proposed
to add. If planning authorities are not
so prevented the extension of the period
for the appeal to a court of summary
jurisdiction would not be of any value
at all to owners of property who are
absent on active service abroad.
The invariable practice of property
owners who have reason to go abroad is to
appoint an agent to give a power of
attorney would be quite an easy matterto act for them in their absence, and everything should be done to encourage this
practice. The regulations made under the
principal Act specifically provide for the
service of notices upon such agents in
order that the interests of owners, whether
abroad on active service or for some other
reason, should be adequately protected.
to
I think it would be of interest to the
Committee to point out that in the corresponding legislation in Great Britain there
is no such provision, and yet one is bound
say that very many more owners of property are abroad from England, Wales
and Scotland. The Scottish Bill is not
through yet, but so far as the draft is
concerned there is no such provision as
this. At the same time, if the right hon.
and learned Senator does not press his
new Clause now, I undertake, before the
Report stage, to examine the possibility of
embodying additional safeguards in a
regulation under section six of the
principal Act so that no person absent for
the good reasons that the right hon. and
learned Senator has indicated shall not
be thoroughly safeguarded. I will make
a careful examination and consult with
our learned draughtsman on the subject.

simple matter took eleven months to get
the necessary signatures. I quite agree

quoted. I think it is Section 6.

In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair]
CLAUSE 1 (Quality of milk sold for
human consumption.
(1) In any area or areas which the
Ministry of Agriculture (in this Act
referred to as "the Ministry") may by
order determine a person shall not,
either by himself or by any servant or
agent, sell or offer or expose for sale
any milk for the purpose of human consumption except milk of the quality
indicated by one of the designations (in
this Act referred to as "grades")
which are specified by the order.
(2) The grades to be specified by an
order under this section shall be such
of the grades described in sub-section
(2) of section one of the Milk and Milk
Products Act (Northern Ireland), 1934
(in this Act referred to as "the
principal Act") as the Ministry may
determine.
The following Amendment stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :
Page 2, after line 12, to insert as a new
sub-section-
"(2) Milk sold outside any such area as
aforesaid and brought within that area.
shall, for the purpose of this section, be
deemed to have been sold within that
area."
Mr. LESLIE: The Amendment on the
Paper should have the word "delivered"
instead of the word "brought" so that it
will read :
"(2) Milk sold outside any such area
as aforesaid and delivered within that
area shall, for the purpose of this
section be deemed to have been sold
within that area."
The point is, of course, that the
object of the Clause is not so much to
prevent the sale of this milk, which is
merely a means to an end, as to ensure
that this milk shall not be consumed
within the selected area. That very
desirable point of view seems to me to be
defeated by sales contracted outside the
area and subsequently consumed within
it. I think the words on the Paper with
this alteration will, so to speak, fill the
Bill.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: The right
hon. Gentleman the Minister of Home
Affairs has kindly undertaken to reply.
The MINISTER OF HOME AFFAIRS
(Mr. Lowry): I have great pleasure in
accepting the Amendment moved by the
right hon. and learned Senator.
Amendment agreed to.

'B' or 'C.'"

CLAUSE 2 (Licences for the sale of
milk and conditions applying thereto).
(1) A person shall not sell any milk
unless he holds a milk licence under
this section (in this Act referred to as
a "milk licence"):
Provided that a person who holds a
licence under section two of the principal Act for the sale of milk of Grade
A, Grade B or Grade C shall be deemed
to be the holder of a milk licence.
(2) The Ministry may, on application being made, grant a milk licence
to any person upon such conditions as
the Ministry may determine.
(3) It shall be the duty of the person to whom a milk licence has been
granted to comply with the conditions
applying thereto and with such directions as may be given by or on behalf
of the Ministry with respect to-
(a) the cows from which milk is taken;
(b) the premises at which milk is produced; and
(c) the methods of the handling, treatment and disposal of milk intended
for sale.
(4) A milk licence may be revoked or
suspended by the Ministry by reason
of the non-compliance of the holder of
the licence with the conditions applying
thereto or with any directions given to
him under sub-section (3) of this
section.
(5) The Ministry may refuse to grant
a milk licence to an applicant by reason
that he was formerly licensed under
this section or section two of the principal Act and that his licence has been
revoked.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in page
3, lines 31 and 32, to leave out the words
"treatment and disposal" and insert the
words "and treatment.'
I mentioned this point on the Second
Reading. What I pointed out was that
the Clause as it then stood seemed to me
to bear the possible interpretation that it
gave the Ministry power to throw the
creamery industry entirely out of action.
It gave the Ministry power to order the
disposal, which means sale, to anywhere
they like. Therefore the creamery was
out of the picture altogether.
That, I understand, is not the intention, and I think we can get over the
difficulty by leaving out the word "disposal." Disposal is a word with a very
wide variation of meaning. It may mean
sale. It may mean something much less
than sale. I am afraid you cannot get
away from the fact that in these cases
it may mean sale, and that the Ministry
may give orders where the milk should
be sold which would put the creameries
and other manufacturing concerns
entirely out of the picture. That is not
intended, and leaving out the word disposal will make it clear.
Mr. LOWRY: The ambiguity so
clearly pointed out by the right hon.
Gentleman on Second Reading is now
cleared up if I may say so, and the
Ministry and the Government have great
pleasure in accepting the Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.

I foreshadowed this Amendment when
I spoke on the Second Reading of this.
Bill and I explained my reasons then. I
very much dislike the principle that is
creeping into a great deal of modern
legisation both in this country and overseas of transferring what should be the
powers of the court to punish, to the
Ministry. I think it is very unfortunate
both from the point of view of the
liberty of the subject and also from the
point of view of the standing and wellbeing of the Ministries themselves. In
this particular case, it is in a sense a
small point. But these things appearing
in Bill after Bill are cumulative in effect.
I think it is quite time a stand was made
against it. In this particular case there
may be various circumstances where there
is a genuine honest difference of opinion
between the Ministry and the owner of
the business. There may be a case
where one inspector may think a byre
quite suitable for cattle producing grade
"B" milk, but in time he is removed to
another district and the new inspector
immediately insists upon the expenditure of several hundreds of pounds before he will pass the byre as suitable.
That is obviously a case where two experts have taken a totally different view
on a point which is important under this.
Clause.
Before a licence is removed there
should be an appeal to an impartial
third party, otherwise, the court. I do
not anticipate that there will be many
such appeals. Most people are willing to
accept the Ministry's judgment in a
technical matter, but such cases may
arise and I do not think that this Committee should leave it that a man cannot have an appeal to a court in a matter
which may affect his livelihood. I feel
it would be much better for the Ministry
themselves, as they would feel freer in
exercising their functions if they knew
that if anything arose they could say to
the man aggrieved, "If you are not prepared to take our opinion you have a
right to appeal, and the matter will be
settled by an impartial court." I beg
to move.
Mr. LOWRY: I am very sorry that I
am unable to accept this Amendment.
This question as to whether this is the
discharge of a judicial or administrative
function was discussed at great length
elsewhere, and may I point out to the
the hon. and gallant Senator that precisely these powers have been exercised
by the Ministry for over 10 years now
with general consent. I do think that
the consideration of the question as to
the sort of cow the milk has been taken
from, the condition of the byres, or the
way the milk producer handles the milk
is much more suitably dealt with administratively than judicially. One sees the
contrast in very marked fashion when
one considers the next Clause of the Bill
where there is a possibility of legal proceedings beforehand, and I do suggest
that quarter sessions is not a suitable
tribunal at all to decide this question as
to the health or otherwise of the cows,
the suitability of byres, the conditions of
cleanliness, or of the handling of milk.
I will give an illustration. Take a
case where it is established that there is
a typhoid carrier engaged in the handling
of milk. It is essential that the Ministry
should be in a position to act promptly,
whereas the quarter sessions conclude in
the month of June and there may perhaps
be no further sessions until October, so
there
may be
a delay if the only
way of dealing with the matter is by
appeal to quarter sessions. The most
famous typhoid carrier was Typhoid
Kate, who was discovered 40 or 50 years
ago in America, and because there was no
means of dealing with the case of a carrier
in those days, she had to spend the rest of
her life on an island in the St. Lawrence.
Is Typhoid Kate to go on gaily handling
milk from June to October because there
is no quarter sessions court during that
time?
I think a practical consideration like
that shows that in administrative functions the Ministry must have a fairly free
hand, and in order that there may be no
apprehension about it, for over ten years,
as was conceded on all hands in another
place, the functions of the Ministry in
respect of the revocation of licences relating to byres or handling have been
carried on in a fair, impartial, and helpful manner all the time. Therefore,
there need not be the apprehensions that
the hon. and gallant Senator entertains.
As to this matter of a constant endeavour
to exclude the courts, personally and professionally, if I thought there was any-

Major PERCEVAL-MAXWELL: I
am rather surprised that the Government
have not accepted this Amendment.
am even more surprised at the speech of
the right hon. Gentleman. It seems to
me that although he made an excellent
point, it is not the point which the mover
of the Amendment is seeking to make by
his Amendment. Those of us who have
experience of the production of milk
appreciate the importance of having clean
and healthy milk, not only for the people
who have to drink it, but also for the
people who have to produce it. I do not
think there is anything in this Amendment that questions the right of the
Government to take action by regulation
to see that healthy milk is produced. All
that the Amendment seeks to do is to
ensure that if someone is accused of not
having complied with the Government's
regulations before he has his licence withdrawn the courts of this land should have
a chance of saying whether, in fact, the
conditions of the licence were infringed.
A great deal depends upon the evidence,
and the courts are there to decide these
matters and to sift the evidence very carefully. The Minister can appoint anyone
he chooses to sift the evidence. It may
be he may appoint a man who has no
previous experience of sifting evidence.
This man may be very good at knowing
about cows, but may not be the slightest
use in deciding whether a person is telling
the truth or not. Then, again, the
Minister is judge, jury, and advocate in
his own case. Surely the most elementary
rule of law is that you cannot be judge,
jury and advocate in your own case.
is a matter of great importance to the
dairy farmer that if a particularly bad
case arises in which the rules of the Ministry are infringed the greatest publicity
should be given to the case. The way it
It
is done at present is that the whole question is settled behind closed doors in
secret, and no one hears whether a man's
licence has been withdrawn or not.
me
There are three particularly bad cases
which the Ministry have to decide. There
is the case of byres which my hon. and
gallant Friend raised. That is a case of
which I have had considerable experience.
I think the Minister said they settled that
question ten years ago, and it has been
done most satisfactorily. I wished to
upgrade my own herd, and it took me two
years to do so. I had six different inspectors, each differing from the other,
with the result that the whole thing cost
a great deal more money than I
originally intended to expend. That is a
personal matter, and I should not have
referred to it but for the fact that the
right hon. Gentleman said it had been
working perfectly for ten years. I can
assure the House that though the
Ministry make the rules, they do not
promulgate them. They leave them in
the hands of their inspectors, and ultimately they may come into the hands of
the farmers. That is not the right way
to promulgate rules, and I think that the
first thing the Ministry must do, before
they have the right to remove anyone's
licence, is to make sure that the rules
have been properly promulgated in a way
that a court of law would approve of.
In the second place, there is grave
danger in the way the milk is at present
dealt with. It is sent in unsealed tins
over great distances round the country
before it reaches the distributor. It very
often goes through quiet country roads
or lanes, and I have very good reason to
believe that when it arrives at the distributor in some cases it is not untampered with since it left the producer. I
am informed that there are other people
who have the same opinion. Whether
that is so or not, it obviously is a matter
of fact that milk arrives at the testing
station in an unsatisfactory condition.
Whether the milk left the producer in
that condition, or whether some person
other than the producer was responsible
for the damage done to the milk, again,
is a matter which only a court of law
skilled in sifting evidence can ascertain.
The third point is, that in the testing
of milk a great deal depends on the personal element. Some people think that
Milk Bill-
Committee
11 JANJARY 1944
you can test milk for distribution by
putting it into a test tube and then
putting some liquid in, with the result
that it turns different colours according
to its condition and quality. That is not
the case. It requires a great deal of
personal knowledge and expert personal
handling. I should mention that some
milk is turned down because the tester
considers the taste is not suitable. That,
again, is entirely a personal matter.
Those are matters which, I think, do require, or may require, the expert help
of the court. I am not in the least
seriously suggesting that the Ministry will
often withdraw a man's licence.
It has been said that we must increase
our milk production in the next ten years
by 25 per cent. That is going to be a
very difficult thing to do. It is certainly
not going to be the Ministry's business to
take away a man's licence if they can
avoid it. It is not only necessary that
justice shall be done, it is necessary that
justice shall be seen to be done. If the
milk producers get it into their heads
that there is danger of their licences
being withdrawn without proper investigation, then I think there would be a
danger of a great many of them going
into other forms of agricultural effort and
stopping the production of milk. I would
regard it as an absolute tragedy in this
country if the milk production were to
fall. There might be a milk famine, and
that would be one of the most disastrous
things that could strike this country at
the present time or, in fact, at any time.
I believe that if the Ministry will
allow this Amendment to go in it will do
a very great deal to remove the apprehension which many farmers feel at the
present moment, notwithstanding what
the right hon. Gentleman has said. I
have talked with farmers, and I have
found that they feel they are now SO
tied up with regulations that it is very
difficult for some of them, particularly
those not very well educated, to upgrade their herds. That is a thing which
I should regard as a very serious danger
in this country. For that reason I will
ask the Government to reconsider their
view, but I do not think it will really
have very much practical effect. Occasionally it may. It will certainly make
it necessary for the Government to produce proper Regulations, which they
have not yet done, and to promulgate
them in a proper manner.
It will give
the farmer encouragement and make him
feel that in the last resort he has the
support of the courts to fall back on.

They may range from a slight to a high

because there was an appeal to a Court of

Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 3 (Sale of adulterated or
impoverished milk.)
(1) Any person who sells or offers or
exposes for sale adulterated or impoverished milk shall be guilty of an
offence under the principal Act which
may be prosecuted before a court of
summary jurisdiction; and for the
purposes of this provision milk shall
be deemed to be adulterated or impoverished if, when tested in the prescribed manner, it has been found to
be mixed with any other substance, or
any part of it has been found to be
abstracted, so as, in either case, to
affect injuriously its quality, substance
or nature.
(2) Where a person who has been
convicted of an offence in relation to
milk under the foregoing provisions of
this section or under section three,
section six or section nine of the Sale
of Food and Drugs Act, 1875, is the
holder of a milk licence, or a licence
under section two of the principal Act
for the sale of milk of Grade A, Grade
B or Grade C, the Ministry may revoke or suspend the licence.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
3, page 4, at the end of sub-clause (1)
to add the words:
Provided that this sub-section shall
not apply to buttermilk or separated
milk bona fide sold, or offered or exposed for sale, as such.
I cannot but think that this is an
other matter that has slipped through
I am sure it was never intended that
the Bill should interfere with the sale of
buttermilk or separated milk, but unfortunately the wording of the Bill,
which speaks of milk being adulterated
or impoverished if it has been found to
be mixed with any other substance, or
any part of it has been found to be
abstracted, so as, in either case, to affect
injuriously its quality, substance, or
nature. That is throwing separated milk
out of the category of milk which can
properly be sold. Therefore it is necessary to protect buttermilk and separated
milk vendors, although there are not so
many of them now as there were. I
suggest that the words on the Paper
will protect them, and I beg to move
accordingly.

Mr. LESLIE: I am bound to say I
think the hon. Senator who has just sat
down is right. It seems to me the passage
before us is two-pronged. If there were
only one prong I think it would not be
necessary, but there are two. The subclause is directed against the person who
has been convicted of an offence under
the foregoing provisions of this section.
That is an offence the Ministry would
know all about, because the Committee
will remember the offence cannot be prosecuted without the consent of the
Ministry of Agriculture. They know all
about that. But then there is a second
problem, the possibility of a man being
prosecuted for an offence under a section
of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act.
That is not a matter for the Ministry at
all. If the man is prosecuted in the one
case the Ministry must know. In the
other case the Ministry may not know.
But if you give power to the court, that
is the magistrate, in the first instance
that magistrate has heard both. He has
got half the pronged fork. He has heard
the case of offence against the Ministry
regulations. He has also heard the other
case under the Food and Drugs code. He
must necessarily know, and therefore he
is the proper person to decide whether the
circumstances are such as to require the
man's licence to be revoked. Licences
under this Act are free. There is not
even a registration fee. At all events I
must say I do think the magistrate is the
man to decide in this instance and not
the Ministry. I do not think there is
anything I need add to that. The arguments for leaving these matters to the
court are sound. This is a special case.
It may be one or the other. In each case
the magistrate knows. I must support
the Amendment.
Mr. LOWRY: I am sorry I cannot
accept this Amendment, and when the
reasons are heard the Committee will bear
in mind the protection that is proposed
to be introduced by another hon. and
learned Senator. It is unnecessary and
undesirable to make the court the revoking power as well as the convicting power.
As to the first part of the Clause that
creates the offence of adulterating or impoverishing milk. That would be heard
by the magistrate and there will be all
the safeguards suggested. The other
offence under sub-clause 2 is under
sections 3, 6 and 9 of the Act
of 1875. That again is
done in
Milk Bill-
Committee
11 JANUARY 1944
open court. In both cases if conviction
follows either under sub-section (1) or
sub-section (2) the Ministry may take a
further step. They may revoke the
licence. Now one sees how the revocation of a licence works in certain cases,
for example in the licensing court. After
two convictions the property is gone for
good and all. Under certain sections of
the Road Code a man loses his licence for
the arbitrary period of 12 months. If
you hand over this power to the courts
there must be some limitation as to time
of revocation. What is to happen on a
second conviction. He. is wiped out
altogether and the man is disqualified.
But the main object of this Bill is to
make provision for the improvement of
the quality of the milk supply, and the
primary object of the Ministry is this
improvement of quality. It is not to act.
as a continual source of chastisement and
reprimand for farmers. It is to try by
example, by precept, by lecture, by
information and expostulation, and if
the Minister himself were here he might
even add by prayer, to improve the milk
supply. That being so, it does not necessarily follow that in every case of conviction the man is going to have his
licence revoked for ever, or even that it
will be revoked at all. Where such a
penalty as revocation follows, it is much
better to have a discretionary power left
to the Ministry to act in a proper case,
because these cases will vary very much
from negligence right up through gross
negligence until you come to the point of
deliberate and wilful criminal fraud.
These are difficult cases, where a discretionary penalty exists, for any magistrate
to decide, and decide fairly, whereas in
the case of the Ministry they will know
the man's milk record, so to speak,
whether he is a man who really made a
genuine mistake, a man who, under great
difficulty, is trying to do his best. We
leave the discretionary power with the
Ministry.
One of the main objects of the Bill is
to secure, as far as possible, the willing
co-operation of the farmers in improving
the milk supply. I think that can best
be secured by leaving this discretion to
the Ministry, together with the limitation.
suggested in the amendment lower down
on the Paper, which I think will meet
the suggestions which are made, namely,
that revocation shall not take place only
where the offences are of such a character
as to render the holder an unsuitable
person to hold such a licence. With that
qualification, there is nothing dangerous
about Clause 3 whatsoever. It does not
oust the jurisdiction of the court in the
main matter as to whether the man is
guilty or not guilty to say that the consequences that can follow should be left to
the discretion of the Ministry which is
endeavouring, as this Bill itself is the
best possible evidence of sincere desire, to
improve the quality of the milk
supply.

hon. Gentleman that it is better to give
discretionary powers to the Ministry to
try and get a licence saved, or prevent it
from being annulled or suspended. I
think it is a far healthier form of administration if a man can prove in court that
he is not guilty of any criminal intent,
and that the courts should have jurisdiction, in exceptional circumstances, to say
whether a licence and a man's livelihood
should be preserved rather than that it
should be confiscated at the whim of any
individual in an office here, no matter
how honest he might be.

importance of this undertaking. I think
these words of the hon. and gallant
Senator will make clear all the grounds
on which revocation would be enforced.

Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move, on page 4, line 15, after the word

the words,
The holder of a licence which the
Ministry may propose to revoke or

within twenty-eight days of being informed by the Ministry of their intention to suspend or revoke his licence,
give notice of appeal to Quarter
Sessions, and his appeal shall be heard
and determined by the Court of
Quarter Sessions for the district in
which the appellant resides held next
after the expiration of the said notice.
This is the amendment which I referred to as being consequential. I do
not think I need make that point again
at any great length. This is another
case where we consider that a man
should not have his livelihood taken away
without the power of coming before the
courts. In this particular instance I
think it is even a more important case,
because a man will already have received
punishment for his crime in a court of
summary jurisdiction. Therefore, why
should he get a further punishment unless
he is a person unfit to hold a licence? If
he is a person unfit to hold a licence the
Ministry propose to remove his licence.
It is then for the court to say whether
the Ministry are right or not. It seems
to me that it is very important to give
the accused a chance of getting before a
court. Here is, I think, a very much
stronger case for getting before, a court
than in the previous amendment which I
withdrew. There, I think, one might
reasonably have argued that there was a
question of policy which should not be
left to the decision of the Ministry, but
here it is quite different. This is an
offence sufficiently serious to justify the
taking away of a man's livelihood, and
we think that only the court should be.
the arbiter of that.
Mr. LOWRY: So much has been said
on this appeal to quarter sessions that, I
think, it is not necessary to weary the
Committee once more by going over some
of my considerations, but I should suggest
that the hon. and learned Senator with
the Amendment he has already got, which
sets forth in unmistakeable fashion the
principles which should and must guide
the Ministry in revoking a licence, has
accomplished all that the most careful
man most anxious to preserve the milk
producer could desire. May I point out
that whether it be the offence in subclause (1) or the offences in Sections 3, 6
and 9 of the Food and Drugs Act, they
are all punishable on summary conviction
and the right of appeal to quarter sessions
will then come in? If, therefore, a man
is not convicted the power to revoke a
licence under Clause 3 does not arise.
Now, if by some fatality or by his not
having his case properly prepared, or from
inexperience of the court of first instance,
that is the petty sessions court, the case
comes on at quarter sessions, of which the
hon. and gallant Senator seems such an
advocate, one of two things must happen.
If the conviction be affirmed and if you
prove to the satisfaction of the Ministry,
twice perhaps, that he is not a person
who should have a licence, can anybody
say that the licence ought not to be revoked? If, on the other hand, he is
successful on appeal, then the power to
revoke the licence disappears. Therefore, on
the mere question of revocation, the Committee having already resolved that this
is not the best way of treating revocation,
I respectfully suggest to the hon. and
gallant Senator, having got in the matters
in which character is to be the guiding
feature as to whether or not the licence
should be revoked, he should not press
this Amendment. In any
event the
Goverment are bound to resist it.

NEW CLAUSE (Right of appeal).
Where a person is aggrieved by any
decision of the Ministry or any order
or regulation made by the Ministry
under this Act he shall have a right to
appeal from such order, decision or
regulation to the next Quarter Sessions
for the district in which he resides.

prove vexatious and hinder the efficient
administration of the Bill by the Ministry. Therefore, I ask leave to withdraw.

CLAUSE 8 (Provisions as to orders).
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I beg to
move, at the end of Clause 8, to add the
following sub-section :
(3) Sub-section (1) of section six of
the principal Act (which relates to
procedure regulations) shall have effect
so as to enable the Ministry to make
regulations providing for the extension
of the times for lodging appeals or
making applications or claims under
the principal Act or this Act in cases
where a person, or the interest of a
person, might be prejudiced by reason
of his absence from the United
Kingdom for any purpose due to the
present emergency, or by reason of the
occupation of the land or premises
concerned for any such purpose.
Mr. LOWRY: On the Committee
stage of the Bill the right hon. and
learned Senator drew my attention to
this matter, as he had found by experience the inconvenience caused by absence
from the country on war service or by
the requisitioning of houses during the
present emergency. He asked that some
extension of the notices and so forth
should be given, and consequently it is
proposed that this sub-section should be
added. I think the right hon. and
learned Senator did agree with me that a
Regulation under Section 6 of the principal Act would be the more suitable way
to deal with the matter, because one
would have to consider very carefully a
variety of circumstances, and more
especially the representative or agent on
whom notice might properly be served
with a reasonable hope that it might be
brought to the notice of the party interested, and that he would have power to
deal with the matter.
Mr. LESLIE: I rise only to express
my thanks to the right hon. Gentleman
for the kindly manner in which he has
dealt with the suggestion made in connection with this matter on the Committee
stage. I quite agree that it is much more
convenient to deal with this matter under
the comparatively more elastic procedure
of Regulation than by hard and set terms
in the Act. I am very glad that he has
met us in this way, and I recognise he
also met us in the matter of the dumps,
for which we thank him. I have much
pleasure in supporting the Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.

CLAUSE 3 (Sale of adulterated or
impoverished milk).

(2) Where a person who has been
convicted of an offence în relation to
milk under the foregoing provisions of
this section or under section three,
section six, or section nine of the Sale
of Food and Drugs Act, 1875, is the
holder of a milk licence, or a licence
under section two of the principal Act
for the sale of milk of Grade A, Grade
B or Grade C, the Ministry may, if
satisfied that the offence or offences are
of such a character as to render the
holder an unsuitable person to hold
such a licence, within twelve months
after such conviction, revoke or suspend
the licence.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I beg to
move in Clause 3, sub-clause (2) on page
4, line 24, to leave out from the word
"may" ("C, the Ministry may") to the
word "within" ("within twelve months")
in line 27. This is purely a drafting
Amendment, and I do not think it affects
the meaning of the Clause. It will be
within the recollection of the House that
when I was moving the Amendment to
this Clause in the Committee stage I
suggested it might be better to move the
words down to the end of the sentence in
which they occur. On further reflection,
and having taken advice, I feel quite sure
the Clause would read better so, but
unfortunately it needs two amendments.
The first is in line 24, and the other is
in line 28, to add the words "if satisfied
that the offence or offences are of such a
character as to render the holder an
unsuitable person to hold such a licence.”
In other words, it lifts the words which
I inserted last time and puts them in,
roughly speaking, about a line and a half
further down.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE
(Rev. Professor Corkey): The Government agree to this Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.
Amendment made: Page 4, line 24 at
end add, "if satisfied that the offence
or offences are of such a character as to
render the holder an unsuitable person
to hold such a licence.'

QUESTION TO MINISTER.
Suppression of "Orange Terror."
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
McLaughlin :-
To ask the Leader of the House
whether he can state the reason for the
suppression of the publication "The
Orange Terror "; whether the facts it
contains have been collected from
Government and other official documents, some of these documents being
photographed; whether any reply to it
was possible, and, if so, could the new
Propaganda Department under the
supervision of Mr. Cooper not have
issued one; whether the English censor
has issued any order against this
publication; and whether thousands of
copies have been sold and circulated
amongst all classes in Great Britain.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: The publication
I refer to in the question is published by
the Capuchins, a religious order whose
members are highly respected and command the confidence of every Irishman.
They would print nothing that they cannot verify. The Unionist Press of the Six
Counties made no attempt to contradict
any of the statistics or facts given in the
publication. What liberty is left to the
Press if publications based almost entirely
upon official statements and figures are
banned in this way, without any indication as to the reasons for the ban. Many of
the most damaging instances of intolerance
quoted are reproductions of photographs
of original official documents.
refusal of one well-known firm to employ
a Catholic girl is quoted. When asked to
state their reasons in writing, the firm
boldly inserted "Religion." These and
other documents are given as photographs
from the original documents. Why, then,
is this book banned? Simply because the
Government do not wish the true state of
affairs to be known.
""
The
Mr. BROWNE: Before the Leader of
the House answers the question, I should
like to ask him if he has seen in the
Belfast News-Letter of last Thursday a
letter from the Dean of Belfast, in which
he says:-
The pamphlet, "Orange Terror," is
such a ludicrous piece of gross misrepresentations and glaring falsehoods
that its reading is likely to be useful.
I believe its circulation should be encouraged as evidence of the deceptions
to which opponents have to resort, and
as a revelation of the methods of the
campaign against Northern Protestants,
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
25 JANUARY 1944
I would like to see every Orange Lodge
provided with a copy. Besides,
"Orange Terror" is confessedly designed
for propaganda in England and abroad.
It should be countered by argument and
exposure a very easy task.
Those are the words of Dean Kerr, who
apparently agrees with Senator McLaughlin that "Orange Terror" should not be
suppressed. Of course, the reasons which
have induced the Dean to come to that
conclusion are entirely different from
those in the mind of Senator McLaughlin.
I should like to know if the Leader of the
House considers that during a terrible war
in which the whole world is involved a
pamphlet of this description, which is calculated, if not intended, to create ill-will
and to stir up strife and turmoil, should
be circulated in Northern Ireland.
Furthermore, is not its very title, "Orange
Terror," a wicked libel on a worthy and
honourable, a world-wide institution
which stands for civil and religious liberty,
and which has been in existence for 150
years?
I
Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM:
agree with the Dean of Belfast that it is
a mistake to ban this publication. I think
it is necessary that the people, and
especially those who have been attacked in
this book, should have an opportunity of
examining it very carefully instead of it
being banned. Some time ago I thought
it was most ridiculous when the Government in Southern Ireland banned a certain Sunday paper because Miss Dorothy
Crisp had written an article dealing very
honestly with the position in Southern
Ireland. As a consequence, the Government of Eire banned that particular newspaper for several weeks. This is a similar
case in Northern Ireland. I think it
would be much better that a book like
this should be freely circulated amongst
our people to give them an opportunity of
reading the false impressions that are sent
abroad regarding them. Then they
would be able to counter them and enlighten the world as to the true facts.
Sir ROLAND NUGENT: I think that
in war time, when there is a necessity for
a considerable censorship on expressions of
opinion, that the censorship should be
exercised with the greatest possible discretion when it is used on a pamphlet
which, I gather, is simply an expression
of political opinion, and not having anything to do, as far as I can gather—I have
not read the book-with the war. I would
also urge on the Government that if pamphlets of this nature are allowed to
appear, they would automatically take
their proper place in the mass of propaganda literature which appears at the
present time as the public is able to
judge of its own knowledge as to the
weight, or lack of weight, that should be
attached to it.
If, on the other hand, the censorship
steps in and prevents publication, there is
at once a feeling that it is an extremely
important document and that they must
get it at all costs.
The LEADER OF THE HOUSE (Rev.
Professor Corkey): A variety of questions.
have been asked, but I shall confine my
reply to the question which has been put
on the Order Paper.
are
The reasons for the prohibition of
the book entitled Orange Terror
fully set out in the Order made by the
Minister of Home Affairs dated the 7th
January, 1944, which was widely circulated in the Press.
Some of the statements in the book may
have been gleaned from Government or
official sources, but nevertheless the
publication in its entirety is definitely
considered to be prejudicial to the preservation of the peace
in Northern
Ireland.
If the book was issued for propaganda
purposes it is a very poor effort, and it
would be beneath the dignity of any
Department to attempt to reply to the
scurrilous articles which it contains.
The British Censor does not exercise
any functions in Northern Ireland under
the Civil Authorities (Special Powers)
Acts under which the book was prohibited.
I have no knowledge of the sales of the
book in Great Britain, but I would
imagine they are inconsiderable.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I think everyone
with an open mind must agree at once
that the attempt made by the Leader of
the House, briefed by the Ministry of
Home Affairs, to answer the question is
a very poor effort to meet a difficult
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
THE SENATE
[Mr. McAllister]
situation. I should have liked to have
known, before I received the Parliamentary Papers to-day, that this question.
was to have been put down, because one
cannot do justice to a matter of this kind
unless one has the time and opportunity
to produce the authenticated data on
which the statements in this paper are
founded. They are founded on Government statistics and Government records,
but people like myself cannot produce this
data at a moment's notice. The statement has been made by the Leader of the
House that it would be beneath the dignity
of a Government office and a Government
official of this parochial Parliament of
Northern Ireland to reply to a document
published by a body widely known
throughout almost the entire world, and
respected in every place except Northern
Ireland; a body carrying weight whose
word is accepted as the truth throughout
almost the entire world. Yet it is suggested that it would be beneath the
dignity of an official in the Ministry of
Home Affairs to deal with a paper
published by a body of that reputation.
With regard to the reference that has
been made by Senator Browne to a letter
to a newspaper from the Dean of Belfast,
far be it from me to attack any clergyman. At the same time, I would like to
point out that he has failed to do the
very thing he suggested should be done;
that this document should be circulated
and should be replied to by means of
criticism. In his letter he makes one
general statement, and he does not
attempt by one single argument to controvert or repel one single statement in
that book as published. I read this book
some time ago, and I have no hesitation
in saying that practically the entire book
is made up from official records. It is
also largely contributed to by men who
have spent time and energy in trying to
preserve the peace in this very city. It
points out that by gerrymandering
Fermanagh a Nationalist majority can
secure only one Parliamentary seat, while
the Unionist minority can secure two.
It points out a similar state of affairs in
Londonderry and Tyrone and South
Down, and all over Northern Ireland.
Are those official records likely to lead to
a breach of the peace?
The Leader of the House has not attempted to reply to the question. He
has not given one single reason why this
document should be banned. He has
not pointed out one single statement in
it that might lead to a breach of the
peace, which is the reason he has alleged
for the banning of the book. After all,
a general statement made by the Leader
of the House is very hard to deal with,
but he knows as well as I know that if
he put up one single argument against
any statement in that book he would not
be in a position to sustain it, because all
the statements and figures in the book are
compiled from official records of the
Government, of which he is a member.
Is it likely to lead to a breach of the
peace to expose this so-called democracy
in Northern Ireland? It goes to the very
fount upon which democracy must be
founded, because the mouthpiece of
democracy is the ballot box, and if you
gerrymander constituencies you stifle the
voice of the people and render alleged
democracy only a mockery, as it is in
Northern Ireland.
I have no hesitation in saying, and I
say it with absolute assurance, that the
reason for banning this book was not, as
alleged, to preserve the peace in Northern Ireland. What interest can the
minority in Northern Ireland have in
trying to promote a breach of the peace?
Is it not in the interests of the minority
to maintain law and order? Are we not
the people who have suffered when there
has been a breach of the peace, as pointed
out in this book? That can be borne out
by the records of the courts, and the
number of our people who have been
burned out of their homes. A general
statement of the type made by the
Leader of the House carries us nowhere.
The reason why this book was banned
by the Government of Northern Ireland
is because it is an absolute condemnation
of the partisanship displayed by the
Northern Government and the destruction of democracy in their midst, and of
the biased legislation and administration
of the Government. It is to prevent
people in Northern Ireland, and the
visitors who are here at the present time,
ascertaining the true position of the
minority under the Government of Northern Ireland. I personally join with
those who suggest that this book should
be circulated. It has been banned by
Question to Minister
Question to Minister
25 JANUARY 1944
the Northern Government, but as a public
representative I say I hope that by some
means and through some source this book
will be circulated far and wide throughout England and America, and wherever
it can be circulated, in order to bring
home to the outside world the partisanship and the gerrymandering that exists
in this area.
This book brought back to me one recollection. It refers to the horrible
tragedy of the McMahon murders, when
a whole family, in the sanctity of their
own home, were deliberately murdered at
night. No one has been brought to
justice for that crime. No one has ever
been prosecuted. If that statement is
wrong it can be contradicted, but it is
well to let the world know that that sort
of thing can occur in Northern Ireland.
I do not want to trespass further on the
time of the House, because I have not
got the necessary figures and data with
me to repel the general statement of the
Leader of the House, but I think any
fair-minded person must agree that no
reason whatever has been given, or attempted to be given, for the banning of
this book.
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: I have read
this book, and it is full of statements that
are given without any proof whatever.
The last hon. Senator who spoke referred
to the McMahon murders. He made no
reference, nor does the book, to the fact
that quite a large number of policemen
have been shot in the city of Belfast in
the exercise of their duty, and no person
has come forward to give evidence against
the murderers. I would like to know if
either Senator McLaughlin or Senator
McAllister can give me any information
with regard to how this Capuchin Press
became possessed of a document issued by
the County Grand Lodge of Belfast over
my name some years ago.
Mr. MCALLISTER: What was that
document?
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: It is a
document sent out by the County Grand
Orange Lodge of Belfast to its members,
and reproduced from photographs in the
book referred to.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: What does it
state?
Sir JOSEPH DAVISON: The document only stated that we object to our
members supporting Roman Catholic
publicans in the city of Belfast, and that
we would take action against our members
if they did so. I am not ashamed of
that. There are also in this book reproductions of speeches made many years ago.
Many years ago I was speaking in County
Armagh on the infiltration of persons
from Southern Ireland. That speech, or
part of it, is reproduced, stripped of its
context. My statement then-and I am
not going back on it to-day-was for
Protestants to employ Protestants. That
is one of the things that is reproduced in
this book. There are also some old statements made by the late Lord Craigavor,
by our present Prime Minister, and by
the Right Hon. J. M. Andrews when he
was Prime Minister. In regard to all
these statements there is no proof given.
Of course, everyone of us was horrified
at the McMahon murders, but there have
been just as atrocious murders committed
in Belfast since then, and there were
bound to be people who could give
evidence, but they were terrorised and
prevented from doing so. There are
articles in the book by twenty writers,
including the Roman Catholic Bishop of
Down and Connor, and some of these
writers advocate the use of force to put an
end to the partition of Ireland. That is
what the book is really published for, to
end the partition of Ireland, and that is
the one thing our opponents on the other
side are working for all the time. This
propaganda is going on extensively from
day to day, and, according to these people,
the power and influence of the Orange
Institution is to be broken. The Orange
Institution has been in existence for 150
years, and we are going to keep going on.
There is one statement in this book I
personally can contradict. That is the
statement that the riots which occurred in
Belfast in 1935 were brought about by
the Orangemen on their return from the
demonstration on 12th July. I was at
the head of that procession, and I saw
the troublesome element at Cromac
Square at the end of the Albertbridge
Road. The head of the procession had
not passed that corner more than ten or
fifteen minutes until there was an attack
made on the procession. That attack was
made, not by Protestants, not by Orange-
Question to Minister
Prorogation
THE SENATE
[Sir Joseph Davison]
men
on Orangemen, but by the forces.
that were out against Orangeism and who
want to put an end to partition in Ireland. It was an unprovoked attack, and
it led to a great deal of disturbance in
the city of Belfast. We all regret that
incident and the riots that ensued afterwards.
I have my own opinion with regard to
this book, but when a publication is
issued by anybody, surely the names of
the writers of the articles should be given.
They should not be given, for instance,
under the Gaelic name of Uladh, or
Ulsterman. I say this book is full of
misrepresentation. There may be some
truth in some parts of it.
The writer of
the main article states that his people
were chased from the city of Belfast and
deprived of their business. That be
may
so, but why had the writer not the courage to put his name to the article? With
regard to the statement I made in County
Armagh in 1933, I have not gone back
on it, and I do not intend to go back on
it under any circumstances as long as the
same conditions prevail in Northern
Ireland.
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Referring to
the resolution passed by the County
Grand Orange Lodge of Belfast
August, 1935-
Mr. SPEAKER: Is the hon. Member
asking a question?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: Yes, I am asking
a supplementary question. Is it not a
fact that that resolution read to the
effectthat any member found frequenting
Roman Catholic public-houses is guilty
of conduct unbecoming an Orangeman,
and a charge to that effect may be
brought against him and dealt with
according to our laws.
Reference has been made to the
murders of policemen and officials.
Mr. LESLIE: On a point of order,
does this really arise out of the question
on the Paper?
Mr. McLAUGHLIN: It arises from
the question that has been introduced
about the murders of policemen. As a
public representative I wish to condemn
anything of that kind. With regard to
the articles that are printed in this book,
we welcome the Dean of Belfast's expression of opinion that the book should not
have been banned. No ill-will is intended, and only the true facts are
stated. If they can be denied let them
be denied, as well as any documents that
are alleged to be untrue.

PROROGATION OF PARLIAMENT.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have it in command
from His Grace the Governor to read
forthwith in this Chamber to the Senators
and Commons of Northern Ireland therein
assembled His Grace's Proclamation proroguing this present Session of Parliament.
Resolved: "That the acting Gentleman
Usher of the Black Rod be commanded to
inform the Commons that the Proclamation proroguing the present Session of
Parliament is to be read forthwith, and
inviting the Commons to attend in order
hear the same."-(Rev. Professor
Corkey.)
to
The Commons, being at the Bar with
their Speaker, the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
His Grace's command), as followeth :-
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR OF
NORTHERN IRELAND.
A PROCLAMATION
For Proroguing the Present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by section.
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
Prorogation
Prorogation
25 JANUARY 1944
certain enactments amending the said Act,
that the Governor of Northern Ireland
shall, in His Majesty's name, summon,
prorogue and dissolve the Parliament of
Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the first
day of February, one thousand nine
hundred and forty-four:
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
DUKE OF ABERCORN, KNIGHT OF
THE MOST NOBLE ORDER OF THE
GARTER, KNIGHT OF THE MOST
ILLUSTRIOUS ORDER OF SAINT
PATRICK, Governor of Northern Ireland, by this Proclamation do publish and
declare His Majesty's Pleasure that the
said Parliament be, as from the reading
of this Proclamation in the Parliament of
Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday
the first day of February, one thousand
nine hundred and forty-four.
AND I do hereby further declare His
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to be
that the said Parliament shall on the said
first day of February, one thousand nine
hundred and forty-four, assemble and be
holden at Stormont for the despatch of
divers urgent and important affairs, and
the Senators and Members of the House
of Commons of Northern Ireland are
hereby required to give their attendance
accordingly.
Given this twenty-fifth day of January,
one thousand nine hundred and fortyfour.
(Signed by his own hand),
ABERCORN.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
End of the Sixth Session (opened
